这份目录列出了70本关于林学的书籍，其中包括森林科学、树木栽培、生态学、遗传学、分子生物学等多个方面的内容。以下是对这些书籍的简要概述：

1. 《可持续农业中树木的作用》（R.T. Prinsley 编著）- 探讨了在可持续农业中树木的重要性。
2. 《木质植物体细胞胚胎发生：松类》（S.M. Jain, P.K. Gupta 和 R.I. Newton 编著）- 专注于松类植物的体细胞胚胎发生研究。
3. 《木质植物体细胞胚胎发生：历史、分子和生化方面以及应用》（S.M. Jain, P.K. Gupta 和 R.I. Newton 编著）- 研究了体细胞胚胎发生的各个方面及其在树木培育中的应用。
4. 《木质植物体细胞胚胎发生：被子植物》（S.M. Jain, P.K. Gupta 和 R.I. Newton 编著）- 探讨了被子植物的体细胞胚胎发生。
5. 《森林学：科学、政策和实践》（F.L. Sinclair 编著）- 分析了森林学的科学、政策与实践。
6. 《欧亚北部针叶生态系统中的火灾》（J.H. Goldammer 和 V.V. Furyaev 编著）- 研究了火灾对欧亚北部针叶生态系统的影响。
7. 《树木的体细胞遗传学和分子遗传学》（M.R. Ahuja, W. Boerjan 和 D.B. Neale 编著）- 探讨了树木的体细胞遗传学和分子遗传学研究。
8. 《利用遥感建模森林生产力》（H.L. Gholz, K. Nakane 和 H. Shimoda 编著）- 研究了如何使用遥感技术来评估和模拟森林生产力。
9. 《生物多样性与改善森林规划评估》（P. Bachmann, M. Kohl 和 R. Piiivinen 编著）- 探讨了如何利用生物多样性评估来改进森林规划。
10. 《全球变化对树木生理学和森林生态系统的影响》（G.M.J. Mohren, K. Kramer 和 S. Sabate 编著）- 分析了全球变化对树木生理学和森林生态系统的影响。

以上仅为部分书籍简介，其他书籍也涵盖了林学的多个方面。希望这些信息能帮助您更好地了解林学领域的知识。

Foreword 
The papers in this book were 'in a preliminary version' presented at an international con-
ference May 21-25, 2002 in Gilleleje, Denmark. It was a joint event, namely the biennial 
meeting of the Scandinavian Society of Forest Economics and the 3rd Berkeley-KVL Con-
ference. 
The Scandinavian Society of Forest Economics (SSFE) was established in 1958 as a 
forum for forest economists in the Nordic countries to meet and exchange ideas on research 
and education. Alternating between Denmark, Finland, Norway and Sweden, biennial 
ordinary meetings have taken place ever since. The number of participants has increased 
from 10-15 in the first decade to more than 80 in 2002. In the last two decades prominent 
researchers from outside Scandinavia have been invited to present papers at the biennial 
meetings and also to participate in ad hoc working groups. 
The Berkeley-KVL part of the conference is based on a research collaboration between 
The Royal Veterinary and Agricultural University (KVL), Copenhagen, University of Cali-
fornia at Berkeley, and Oregon State University. It was initiated in 1993 within the frame-
work of a research programme at KVL: 'Stochastic Decision Analysis in Forest Manage-
ment' and since 1996 extended to the programme 'Economic Optimisation of Multiple-Use 
Forestry and Other Natural Resources'. 
Participants in the conference were given the option to have their paper published in the 
SSFE proceedings or submit it for publication in a potential edited volume, after selection 
and peer reviewing. The outcome is the present book in which no paper has been previously 
published. 
The editors express their sincere appreciations to Samnordisk Skogsforskning and The 
Danish Agricultural and Veterinary Research Council for indispensable financial support 
to the conference. We also thank the authors who took on the extra work needed for such 
publication, and the reviewers who accepted to accomplish the task fast and efficiently. 
Copenhagen, October 2002 
Finn Helles 
Niels Strange 
Lars Wichmann

Preface 
Finn Helles, Niels Strange and Lars Wichmann 
The papers in this book deal with applied forest economics. With regard to topics it is 
a heterogeneous book- even within each of its three parts: Resources, Industry, Markets, 
the topics differ. It demonstrates recent accomplishments within the broad discipline of 
modern forest economics, applied on a wide spectrum of problems using many different 
approaches. 
The book does not pretend to show the entire state-of-the-art in forest economics, but 
nonetheless it is claimed to illustrate many recent accomplishments. 
Part I: Resources starts with a paper by Colin Price who demonstrates that the 
world-wide trend towards 'near-nature forestry' may not be as self-evidently advantageous 
as is usually claimed by the public, politicians and even forestry professionals. The eco-
nomics of transformation from even-aged to uneven-aged (continuous cover) forestry is 
examined on the basis of Douglas fir in Wales. It appears that economic justifications 
such as improving profitability, or gaining early, well-distributed or flexible revenue are 
spurious, and that environmental advantages are equivocal. Suggestions are offered as to 
the choice of locations for continuous cover forestry in Wales - the arguments may well 
be applicable to other geographical locations. Guillermo A. Navarro scrutinises the theo-
retical problems affecting calculation of the land capital asset value in forestry, i.e. using 
the land expectation value (LEV) formula. Three different basic LEV formulas are iden-
tified in the forest economics literature, the topic having been debated for close to 200 
years. The paper will hardly bring the debate to an end, but it is elucidated that much 
disagreement is due to different and often unclear assumptions, and criteria are presented 
for improving the application of the three LEV formulas. Richard J. Brazee also deals 
with the Faustmann formula or model, but shows that the 'Volvo Theorem' provides an 
alternative model of optimal timber harvesting. Instead of choosing to harvest at the age 
that maximises net present value, under the Volvo Theorem landowners harvest when faced 
with a non-forestry expense such as the purchase of a new Volvo. A simplified model is 
used to describe behaviour when a landowner faces differential interest rates depending on 
asset position. And an assessment is made of the Volvo Theorem as an alternative to the 
Faustmann model. Henrik Meilby, Bo J. Thorsen and Niels Strange apply Monte Carlo 
simulation to evaluate the consequences of an adaptive optimisation approach to spatial 
harvest planning under risk of windthrow. Such strategy is demonstrated to have the dou-
ble advantage of (i) producing realistic solutions that imitate the adaptive behaviour of 
the forest manager, and (ii) producing realistic solutions that circumvent the tremendous 
combinatorial dimensions of the topical stochastic interaction problem. Stale St¢rdal and 
Sjur Baardsen perform an econometric analysis of Norwegian microlevel timber harvesting 
data, demonstrating significant differences in stumpage values over regions with respect to 
both the characteristics of the timber and the valuation of the different grades. The paper 
provides a framework for analysing individual harvests by regions, season and size that de-
composes differences between regions. If the findings can be generalised, moderate rather 
than large-scale harvests should have minimal impact on the economy of a particular har-
vest - an interesting result, e.g. from the point of view that small clear-cuts are often more 
environmental friendly than large. Bo J. Thorsen and Nikolaj Malchow-M¢ller extend the 
vii

--- Page 7 ---

field of real-options theory. Instead of considering land-use decisions under uncertainty as 
a problem of replacing an existing land use with one alternative use, the paper analyses the 
problem of decision-makers having more than one investment option available, and by exer-
cising any of them, they sacrifice all other options. A simple two-period model is presented, 
a continuous-time model outlined and results given of a numerical solution procedure for 
the case of afforestation. Signe Anthon applies the hedonic pricing method and data from 
two residential areas in Denmark to calculate willingness-to-pay (WTP) measures for two 
afforestation areas. Modelling the environmental attribute with the continuous variable 
'distance' is likely to be the best representation of the marginal values that proximity to 
an afforestation area has in the two cases, and it is concluded that there is a positive WTP 
for afforestation projects in both cases. Niels Stmnge, Vilis Brukas, Finn Helles and Peter 
Tarp analyse the performance of several optimisation models, using genetic algorithms, 
simulated annealing, and mixed integer programming, when applied to the multi-criteria 
problem of delineating conservation areas in a case forest in Lithuania. The study indicates 
that mixed integer programming is relatively time-inefficient for complex problems that 
involve a large number of sites and consider spatial connectivity. Peter Bogetoft and Kurt 
Nielsen discuss the pros and cons of using Data Envelopment Analysis (DEA) to evaluate 
and enhance the efficiency of natural resource management. The DEA modelling is particu-
larly useful in a natural resource setting because of its ability to handle multiple inputs and 
outputs, to work with flexible production structure, to incorporate local variables, and to 
work with limited or no preference information. Moreover, the need in DEA for good data 
from several similar units is often possible to fulfil. The DEA approach can also be useful to 
cope with delegated production and incentive problems. Some basic results on DEA based 
incentive schemes are reviewed. These schemes can be used in motivating landowners to 
take desired decisions. It is indicated how a DEA based procurement procedure could be 
used to select farmers and forest owners for a programme, e.g. to enhance environmental 
qualities. The authors believe that developments along the lines of the DEA based auctions 
may lead to new approaches that can solve real problems using sound theory. 
Part II: Industry. Sjur Baardsen estimates input demand, output supply, returns 
to scale, and technical change in Norwegian sawmills, employing a flexible, restricted and 
normalised translog profit function on observations for 1974-1991. Focus is on the estima-
tion of the profit function, on the derived elasticities and on aggregation issues rather than 
on possible implications for policy. Matti Flinkman analyses the use of sawnwood within 
six end-use sectors and nine related producer sectors involved in the West German wood 
supply chain for construction. The time-series cover the period 1970-2000 and is used for 
econometric analysis and forecasting the wood use within the subsectors. By aggregation 
over the subsectors, a projection for the total consumption of sawnwood in construction is 
derived. Lars Lonnstedt investigates, on the basis of six cases in the Swedish forest sector, 
arguments for and against backward vertical integration, i.e. integration from pulp- or 
sawmills to timberland ownership. The results give strong support for the transaction cost 
theory. Backward vertical integration of the forest industry may be explained, not least for 
the pulp and paper industry, by transaction specific capital, i.e. high quasi rents. 
Part Ill: Markets. Mikael Linden and Jussi Leppanen investigate, on the basis of 
Finnish regional data 1983-2000, the impact of government investment assistance on forest 
investment and timber supply. It is argued that the private forest owner has a low incentive 
for self-financed forest investment due to low forest capital return and postponed consump-
tion. It is shown in a theoretical model that government cost sharing (loans and grants) has 
positive timber supply and investment effects. Peter Berek and Sandm Hofmann consider 
different methods and issues in estimating employment changes from preservation of forests 
and other natural lands in California. The conclusion is that job multipliers exist, but are 
surprisingly difficult to pin down. Different simulation and time series methods result in dif-
ferent empirical estimates for job multipliers. James Turner and Joseph Buongiorno review 
differences between the concepts and information used in current global forest sector mod-
viii

--- Page 8 ---

els. The need for such models is stressed; however, there are few models with sufficiently 
detailed coverage to answer specific questions. Adequate representation of timber supply is 
a particular weakness of existing global forest sector models. Anders Baudin investigates 
inventory and price movements of coniferous sawnwood in Europe (Sweden, the UK, Ger-
many). A method is shown by which an indicator of consumer inventory change can be 
obtained. It is also shown that dynamic models can be specified and estimated for the re-
lationship among price and inventory variables. Ritva Toivonen and Eric Hansen examine 
quality dimensions of wood products as perceived by German organisational customers, and 
compare wood and other construction materials with respect to those dimensions. Product 
quality appears to take somewhat different dimensions regarding different products. Prod-
uct information and perceived quality are found to be separable dimensions. Particularly 
with regard to wood, environmental considerations did not seem to form a separate quality 
dimension. Using Nepal as case country, Carsten Smith Olsen and Thorsten Treue inves-
tigate the commercial trade in the medicinal plant product "chirait", mainly consisting of 
the herb Swertia chirayita, and show that this is an important income generating product 
for rural households. It is emphasised, however, that before medicinal plants can be used 
in longer term larger scale attacks on poverty in the Himalayan region, it is important to 
establish harvest rates and to ensure that national policies, legislation and administration 
support such initiatives.

Part I 
Resources

--- Page 12 ---

The economics of transformation from even-
aged to uneven-aged forestry 
COLIN PRICE 
Abstract 
The Welsh Assembly's recent target of converting 50% of Welsh forests to uneven-aged struc-
ture within 20 years is not based on careful economic analysis. Economic justifications such as 
improving profitability, or gaining early, well-distributed or flexible revenue are spurious. Falling 
timber prices do justify shifting to lower-input silviculture. Even without firm data, rational 
decision making is aided by knowledge of trade-offs between extra harvesting costs, saved es-
tablishment costs, improved revenues and environmental advantage. Manipulating a standard 
yield model allows these to be explored, along with costs of sub-optimal felling age during trans-
formation. For group selection, the results suggest that transformation will reduce profitability. 
Single-tree selection offers a logical possibility of increased long-term profit, but the lead time 
makes transformation unprofitable except at low discount rates. Environmental advantages are 
equivocal, and, except in selected locations, unlikely to outweigh costs. 
Keywords 
Continuous cover, trade-offs, environmental value 
Introduction 
The National assembly for Wales (1999) has recently declared a policy target of transforming 
50% of Wales's forests to uneven-aged structure over the next 20 years. This follows both 
a world-wide move in favour of "near-nature forestry", and a local dissatisfaction with the 
perceived results of clear cutting. 
Clear cutting is the dominant regime of commercial forestry in the UK. This results 
partly from rapid afforestation, mostly of poor upland sites, during the 20th century, which 
raised forest cover from 5% to 12%. Importantly, on such sites the high windthrow risk 
limits how, and how much, transformation may realistically be attempted. 
Of course, the last professional to be consulted on the transformation policy would be a 
forest economist. Work from the 1980s onwards had modelled uneven-aged forestry in both 
biological and economic terms (Buongiorno & Michie, 1980; Haight, 1987), and such work 
has re-emerged in Europe during the 21st century (Buongiorno, 2001; Hanewinkel, 2001). 
However, it may safely be hazarded that politicians are unfamiliar with much of that. 
In spoken English "uneven-aged forest" is hard to distinguish from "an even-aged for-
est", and the term "continuous cover forestry" is widely used, in contradistinction to clear-
cutting forestry. That usage is observed hereonwards. However, the debate in the UK has 
been clouded by uncertainty as to what "continuous cover forestry" actually entails (Mason 
et al., 1999). Strictly, it denotes only forest structure, without implication of other elements 
of "near-nature forestry". In Wales, most interest has been shown in group selection, the 
boundary between this and small-scale clear felling being taken as somewhere between 0.1 
and 0.25 ha. This is the most likely form for continuous cover forestry to take. 
This paper uses for illustration thinned Douglas fir, yield class 20 (m3 per hectare 
per year), with volume out-turn based on Forestry Commission yield models (Edwards & 
Christie, 1981). A partially shade-bearing species, Douglas fir is suitable for regeneration 
by small coupes. It has typically been planted on sites where windthrow risk is low enough 
for the crop to be grown to, and beyond, its risk-free optimal financial rotation. This gives 
flexibility in scheduling transformation. 
3 
F. Belles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 3-17. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 13 ---

Revenue data, where required, use the price size relationship prevalent during the period 
1985-1995, on the grounds that 
• the low prices received in the late 1990s have not yet become established as permanent, 
• UK prices are affected by an overvalued £ , and 
• quality Douglas fir has in any case maintained its price better than UK timber gen-
erally. 
In all cases the criterion of profitability is the net present value (NPV) of cash flows per 
hectare over an unlimited time horizon. 
Why change the present silvicultural regime? 
Several economic arguments purport to favour continuous cover forestry over clear-cutting. 
Superior profit? 
Knoke & Plusczyk (2001) detail a case where transformation from a clear-cutting regime 
improved the profitability of a long-rotation crop, because it yields some early revenues. 
Figure 1.1 shows that such an analysis can give a misleading impression of the causes 
of increased profits. Transformation, beginning at 32 years, from a 77-year-rotation clear-
cutting regime to continuous cover forestry on a much shorter rotation, may indeed improve 
profitability (from £5,980 to £6,361 per hectare). But one could do better still (£7,406) 
by reducing the clear-cutting rotation to 4 7 years.

What continuous cover forestry actually offers here is a means of escaping the strait-
jacket of over-long rotations imposed by silvicultural tradition. Because the concept of 
rotation is less clear in an uneven-aged structure, and because early removals can be rep-
resented as "stand development" rather than "premature felling" such dramatic changes of 
rotation may be politically acceptable. 
The profile of revenue 
It has often been claimed that transformation allows an early flush of revenue (as an end 
in itself, rather than as a means of increasing overall profitability). Subsequently, there 
would be a more evenly spread cash flow. This argument is specious. Early cash flow, if 
desired, may be obtained by premature clear cutting, without suffering the cost penalty 
4

--- Page 14 ---

that results from harvesting individual trees or small groups. Regular cash flow may be 
realised by converting the forest to a normal age class structure, which does not require 
intimate mixture of age-classes within each stand. Flexible cash flow, claimed as a further 
advantage of continuous cover forestry, can also be achieved by delay or advance of clear 
cutting, in those stands for which the optimal rotation has been nearly reached. Or (a 
risk-neutral forest economist would say) a bank loan could be obtained when funds were 
required, against the collateral of a forest to be felled at its optimal rotation. 
There is, however, one way in which continuous cover forestry allows greater early 
revenue. Early clear cutting entails removal of many small and low-value trees: on the 
other hand continuous cover forestry could be initiated by removing the largest and most 
valuable trees throughout an even-aged crop. In this sense it has the same advantages as the 
extreme thinning type explored by Price (1987, 1989), designed to concentrate increment 
onto the trees best able to benefit from it. Figure 1.2 shows that it is not always the largest 
trees that offer the best rewards from thinning release. 
Dls.count 
,.,. 
Troeslzo(m') 
Figure 1.2: The benefits of allocating increment to different sizes of tree 
The difference between removal of the largest trees as a heavy thinning and doing so 
as a step in transformation is that improved increment on remaining trees is the prime 
objective of the first, and encouraging regeneration is the prime objective of the second. 
If referred to as "high-grading" or "creaming", this means of initiating transformation 
would be less acceptable. On the other hand, if early revenue is the main objective, then 
this should be plainly stated. Continuous cover may result, but, as shown later, this does 
not necessarily enhance long-term profitability. 
The cost versus revenue trade-off 
It has been argued recently that low timber prices oblige UK foresters to seek cheaper 
means of regeneration, such as natural regeneration under continuous cover. An economist 
might respond that a profit-maximising forest manager should always seek cheaper means 
of regeneration, regardless of how large or small the revenues are. However, lower costs may 
entail reduced or delayed revenues, for example through incomplete or uncertain natural 
regeneration. Figure 1.3 shows the determinate profitability (LEV) of artificial regenera-
tion and the mean expected profitability (MEV) of natural regeneration which has a 0.3 
probability of success in any year (see Price (1999) for an elaboration of the calculation 
procedure). 
As prices have fallen, the importance of gaining revenue relative to saving cost has been 
reduced. Natural regeneration may therefore become more profitable than planting. But 
natural regeneration need not depend on continuous cover forestry: it is abundant on some 
large clear-cutting sites in the UK. Nor is it always successful under continuous cover. Nor 
is it necessarily desirable, when it occurs sparsely, patchily or in superabundance.

Non-market benefits 
Following centuries of destructive logging, with palpable environmental costs, the intuition 
has arisen that "it must be right to do things more like nature"; or else more explicit claims 
have been made that environmental benefits arise from that particular kind of forestry. This 
issue will be revisited, quantitatively, when financial matters have been reviewed in more 
detaiL 
Illustrating the trade-offs 
Not unusually in the annals of government, Wales's continuous cover forestry policy arrived 
before the emergence of any firm evidence on which it could reasonably have been based. 
This problem is Europe-wide: as Bergen ((in press)) has indicated, there is a dearth of 
scientifically collected data about the costs and revenues of continuous cover forestry. 
This paper therefore addresses questions of relative profitability by using a range of 
costs and benefits, compared with those of clear cutting. Then trade-offs are explored: 
supposing (for example) the costs of harvesting under continuous cover forestry were £3 
per cubic metre greater than under clear cutting, what saving in establishment cost, or 
increase in revenue, or level of environmental benefit, would be required to compensate for 
that? 
Even if no firm figures are forthcoming within the 20 years over which the policy is 
intended to be fulfilled, making trade-offs is something that politicians are meant to do. 
They might as well enter the multi-criteria era in an informed way (Hjortso & Stnede, 
1999). 
Reasons for higher costs 
Continuous cover forestry probably involves higher unit costs, because of: 
• fixed costs (e.g. in setting up cable systems) spread over few units of output; 
• dispersed working (e.g. costs of supervision, load concentration, diffuse road network); 
• slower working due to more awkward and care-demanding operations; 
• limitations on suitable technologies (e.g. continuous cover forestry may oblige use of 
chainsaws rather than cheaper harvesters). 
For the same reasons thinning entails higher unit costs than clear cutting: in the fol-
lowing calculations a thinning penalty of £5/ m3 applies to both continuous cover and 
clear-cutting regimes. 
6

--- Page 16 ---

The extra costs of small-scale harvesting benefit, not the current crop, but its successors. 
Hence there is a long delay between those costs and the accrual of benefit, although the 
mixture of age classes within a stand renders the time lapse less easy to identify. The 
delay is particularly significant, because transformation is a protracted process. Costs are 
incurred from the beginning of transformation, but are only fully realised when the results 
of transformation are realised, up to two rotations later. 
Doing it with Douglas fir yield class 20 
Continuous cover forestry is initially examined in the form of small group felling and re-
generation. Consideration is given later to single-tree selection systems. 
Figures 1.4 show the improvement in performance, relative to clear cutting, required to 
offset realistic harvesting penalties, once transformation has been completed. Evidently, the 
higher the discount rate, the less significant does a given improvement in future performance 
become, so the greater is the improvement required to offset a given harvesting penalty. At 
the higher rates, and with mid-range harvest penalties, the improvement required is beyond 
any reasonable expectation. One might ask, in fact, why any improvement in either volume 
or timber quality would result from group regeneration: for example, the expected effects 
on the form of edge trees would be negative.

Figure 1.4: Enhanced performance required to justify increased harvesting costs 
The greater improvement required in volume production (Figure 1.4b) as opposed to 
price of product results because the harvesting penalty must be paid on a greater timber 
volume. In Figure 1.4a, at any given discount rate the required price increase rises linearly 
with successive increments of harvest penalty. But in Figure 1.4b the required volume 
increase rises more rapidly. The effect is such that at a 7% discount rate a £5jm3 harvest 
penalty could not be offset, no matter how great the increase in volume. 
Including the costs of transformation 
During transformation premature and delayed felling cause reductions in net revenue, com-
pared with that from clear cutting at the optimal rotation. Figure 1.5 shows the discounted 
cash flow implications of transformation phased over one rotation. The model selects time 
of initiation of transformation and length of subsequent felling cycles so as to maximise 
NPV of all future cash flows. 
With no harvest cost penalty and a 4% discount rate, the most profitable option is a 
42-year rotation, with transformation beginning at age 32. Figures 1.6 and 1. 7 show how 
discount rate and harvesting penalty affect respectively the optimal rotation, and the most

be expected for small group fellings.

with establishment costs equal to those in clear cutting (the case between "Estab= 80%" 
and "Harv.Pen=£1"), any increase in revenue would suffice to offset transformation costs. 
In most circumstances, however, a substantial or even implausible increase is needed.

Single-tree selection 
Genuine financial advantage from continuous cover forestry is most plausibly achieved 
through better assortment of products. Single-tree selection makes it theoretically pos-
sible to harvest all the increment in trees of "final felling" size that is, in the highest value 
category (Matthews, 1991). This is accomplished if only trees expected to grow to this size 
are allowed to occupy growing space. Once transformation is complete, "thinning" of the 
canopy is achieved by occasional removal of trees of this size. 
Economically, this system combines the advantages of narrow spacing (full utilisation of 
site and narrow initial ring width) with those of wide spacing (few trees being established 
and large individual size at removal). As analysed below, however, it does represent an 
optimistic extreme: even with natural regeneration there will be costs in manipulating 
conditions so that the right number of trees regenerates, and so that no tree will annex 
increment unless it is destined to achieve the target size. In practice it may be impossible 
to ensure that no significant increment accrues to trees removed at a younger age and in 
smaller size classes. 
In the calculations and trade-off figures following, it is assumed that all trees are har-
vested at the largest size that would be reached, over the optimal rotation, on the heavy 
thinning and clear-cutting regime adopted so far as a base-line.

Figure 1.10 shows the capitalised difference of annual cash flow per hectare between 
a continuous cover forest and a forest of normal age class structure under a clear-cutting 
regime, once each regime is up and running. Even if establishment costs are double those of 
clear cutting, the better assortment from continuous cover gives it considerable advantage, 
unless the harvest penalty is exceptionally high.

Single-tree selection and transformation cost 
The picture looks different when the costs of launching the regime on previously unplanted 
land are included. Now there are opportunity costs in leaving sufficient growing space for 
later cohorts of trees. Figure 1.12 should be compared with Figure 1.10, which excludes 
launch costs. 
It looks different again if the costs of transformation from an even-aged forest are in-
cluded. Ideally the results would be based on single-tree modelling. However, such models, 
though advancing in sophistication, may not give reliable predictions of future stand condi-
tion. To maintain comparability with the (empirical) clear cutting model, transformation 
is represented by using values derived from that model. 
Tree removals are characterised as follows. 
• The whole stand is thinned according to normal prescriptions.

Figure 1.12: Performance of single-tree selection with launch costs 
• Once the start-time for transformation is reached, additional trees are removed. The 
extra volume removed per intervention is equal to the current main crop volume, 
divided by the number of interventions in the transformation period. Removed trees 
have the mean size for the main crop on average larger than the thinnings; but smaller 
than the largest trees remaining, which in practice might be the ones targeted for 
single-tree removal. Thus the assumed price/m3 would be less than could be achieved 
by removing the largest trees. 
• However, as Figure 1.2 showed, these are trees which might in any case be targeted by 
a radical thinning scheme, so the revenue difference between conversion to continuous 
cover forestry, and adoption of the radical thinning scheme, might in practice be 
similar to the one calculated here. 
• The additional light made available by thinning is mostly annexed by the remainder 
of the maturing crop, as in the clear cutting model. 
• The additional light made available by early selective removals is primarily used for 
the benefit of regeneration. 
• Any benefit of additional light accruing to first crop trees surrounding significant gaps 
may be balanced by the negative productivity effect resulting from retention of trees 
having lesser vigour than the ones removed. 
Figures 13 a and 13 b show the further increase in revenues required, over and above that 
resulting from assortment, for single-tree selection to be as profitable as clear cutting. As 
11

--- Page 21 ---

expected, without discounting single-tree selection is superior to clear cutting: the on-going 
assortment advantage must eventually outweigh the one-off transformation cost. Indeed, 
it is sufficient to outweigh a harvesting penalty of up to £7jm3. At a low 1.5% discount 
rate, the situation is reversed, even with costless establishment and no harvesting penalty. 
The effect of discounting is severe because a whole rotation elapses before any advantage 
of superior assortment begins to be felt. Comparison of Figures 1.13 shows that at high 
discount rates the poor performance of single-tree selection is somewhat mitigated if the 
costs of nurturing regeneration are spread through the life of crop trees.

The dysgenic effect 
Not only is removal of individual trees likely to incur a high cost penalty in the short 
term: in the long term it may also cause a dysgenic effect (Cahalan, (in press)), since 
genetically superior trees potential seed trees are likely to be removed first. In the model 
this dysgenic effect is included by lengthening subsequent rotations in inverse proportion 
to reduced productivity. Illustratively, a 20% reduction in yield following transformation 
is inserted. There should be no further dysgenic effect once transformation is complete. 
During transformation the largest trees are probably genetically superior: once the process 
12

--- Page 22 ---

is complete, the largest trees are probably the oldest, not the genetically superior ones. 
Nonetheless, Figure 1.15 shows that there is a dramatic effect, compared with Figure 1.11, 
from a one-off reduction in productivity. Only under very favourable circumstances is 
single-tree selection superior to clear cutting, even once the system is up and running.

Figure 1.16: Impact of dysgenic effect on the required revenue increase 
As it happens, without discounting no significant change in revenue is required for the 
case interpolated between "Estab=80%" and "Harv.Pen=£1", where establishment and 
harvesting costs are equal for both regimes. The 20% loss of growth rate is just balanced 
by the advantage of superior assortment. Any greater cost disadvantage under continuous 
cover would require a revenue increase over and above that achieved by superior assortment. 
Environmental advantage? 
The belief is widespread that uneven-aged forests are more environmentally benign than 
those managed under clear cutting. But the case is not as unequivocal as has been supposed. 
Carbon sequestration 
In the 1990s, officers of the [then] Countryside Commission in England argued that con-
tinuous cover forestry keeps a constant quantity of carbon locked up. By contrast, all the 
gains in carbon during the growth of even-aged crops are dissipated after felling by rapid 
13

--- Page 23 ---

volatilisation of the wood products and biomass. But this sequence of gain and loss is com-
mon to clear cutting and continuous cover: only the spatial scale is different. Figure 1.17 
shows that a hectare of Douglas fir yield class 20 managed under group selection eventually 
maintains about 150 tonnes of carbon in trees and in products. Any given stand under 
clear cutting offers fluctuating storage. But if the storage of three, then six stands within 
a normal age-class structure is aggregated, the outcome, averaged across space, is closely 
similar to that for continuous cover. 
Cerbon in 
syotom 
jtonnes) 
Figure 1.17: The effect of age-class structure on carbon sequestmtion 
The case is somewhat different under single-tree selection. Recall that, under the lim-
iting assumptions made above, there are no planned removals as thinnings; the [individual 
tree] rotation remains unchanged from the clear-cut regime. An easy intuition is that the 
absence of thinning with a given rotation assures a much higher volume on a given area 
under single tree selection. The intuition is however misleading. Although no pattern of 
increment has been specified for single-tree selection, it is likely (from the low light levels 
beneath the canopy) and desirable (to give even ring width) that increment will accrue 
slowly in the early part of the tree's life. Thus with an equal number of trees in each age 
class and constant ring width, the accrued-carbon profile for single-tree selection, along 
with that for thinned and unthinned crops, is as in Figure 1.18. 
,...;-y-,,--,-.,_._~--- DO GLAS 
FlR YC20

eye'-. 
Figure 1.18: The effect of regime on carbon sequestered through a growth cycle 
The numbers following the regime labels are the tonnes of carbon locked up, averaged 
through the growth cycle. While single-tree selection does perform somewhat better than 
clear cutting, it is not by the large margin that the unthinned regime suggests. And if in 
practice some trees are removed during the growth cycle, single-tree selection's advantage 
might disappear altogether. 
14

--- Page 24 ---

Environmental costs? 
The effects on other environmental qualities may not be benevolent at all. For example, 
loss of water by evaporation from conifer canopies has been a serious economic problem in 
the UK (Collet, 1970; Barrow et al., 1986). Recently Robinson (1998) has indicated that 
losses may be less serious under a clear-cutting regime, because the phases of "bare" ground 
and incomplete canopy entail less evaporation. By contrast, evaporation from a continuous 
canopy persists through time, and indeed may be increased by edge and turbulence effects 
induced by the irregular canopy structure. 
As for landscape, it is easy to see why monocultural even-aged blankets and the devas-
tated tracts of clear cutting have evinced a negative response. And yet, seen from within 
the forest, clear cutting allows a constantly changing immediate environment, and views 
ranging from vignettes to panoramas. In marked contrast continuous cover forestry offers 
great variety at one point, but little alteration in that variety as a journey progresses. And, 
except where they are deliberately created (at further cost to production), there are no 
outward views. 
Having spent forty years of my life campaigning for a more aesthetically sensitive ap-
proach to clear-cutting regimes, I begin to fear that I shall spend the remainder of it 
campaigning for more clear cutting. And that, 80 years from now, forest visitors will be 
wishing that the Welsh Assembly had thought through the implications of continuous cover 
forestry before launching their policy. 
Despite these reservations, continuous cover forestry undoubtedly offers environmental 
advantages in specific locations. But how much advantage would be needed to compensate 
for the costs incurred by the regime? In the calculations below, environmental benefit 
increases pro rata, as the proportion of crop under continuous cover forestry increases 
through the transformation. Thus halfway through transformation, only 50% of the final 
environmental benefit has been achieved. 
Figures 1.19 and 1.20 show that at high discount rates a large cash value of annual 
benefit per hectare is required, even with no harvesting penalty and under reduced cost 
of regeneration. This result is caused, once again, by the long delay from the costs of 
initiating transformation, until full environmental benefits are derived. Except in favourable 
circumstances, the cost runs to hundreds of pounds per hectare per year. This is so under 
single-tree selection even at quite low discount rates if a dysgenic effect is included. 
DO GLAS 
FIR YC20 
thinned 
Figure 1.19: Level of environmental benefits required to compensate for the costs of transforming to 
group selection 
Such levels of benefit might be expected in areas used intensively for recreation or 
with specific conservation purposes. For example, millions of visitors pass through the 
immediate forest environs of Betws-y-coed in the Snowdonia National Park each year, and 
a benefit of a small fraction of a pound per head would justify transformation. However,

for stands remote from tourist centres hundreds of poundsworth of benefit would be a quite 
unreasonable expectation. Applied to 50% of the forested area of Gwydyr Forest, the cost 
mounts towards £1 million per year. In much of that area, the only visitors to benefit 
would be the relatively few (100,000 or so per year) who venture from their cars. 
Conclusions 
Under the dispassionate scrutiny of economic analysis, the case for continuous cover forestry 
looks less self-evident than might be expected from the number of feel-good words that are 
associated with it in the public, the political, and even the professional mind. 
If the Welsh Assembly had taken the unusual step of hiring a forest economist to advise 
on matters of forest economics, what might that advice have been? (and what parallels 
could be drawn for other European nations, which are also stampeding down the near-nature 
forestry trail?). Where should continuous cover forestry be located in Wales, to minimise 
potential damage to environment and economy, while selectively achieving benefit? 
Firstly, it should be applied to small, discrete, much-loved wooded features of the topog-
raphy, for which a sense of aesthetic continuity is important, and where continuous cover 
would not be so extensive as to create tedium. 
Secondly, it should be located on valley slopes seen in full-frontal-view, especially at 
topographical focuses. Such locations offer several advantages. 
• They are the places where clear cutting appears in its least sympathetic, plan-like 
view. 
• Reduction in scale of variation will emphasise the topographical focus. 
• High yield classes, and species amenable to single-tree or small group selection, are 
as it happens concentrated on such sites. 
• The windthrow threat entailed in transformation is relatively low. 
• The steepness and roughness of the ground may preclude highly mechanised harvest-
ing anyway, so that continuous cover forestry imposes no further cost on this account. 
• This is where the people who complain about clear cutting to the Welsh Assembly 
will see it, and will perhaps be thereby persuaded to desist from baying for further 
expensive and not-necessarily environmentally friendly extension of this little-tried, 
little-understood and little-costed regime. 
16

--- Page 26 ---

As for the applicability of these arguments to the rest of Europe, I leave that for others 
to decide.

Re-examining the theories supporting the 
so-called Faustmann formula 
GUILLERMO A. NAVARRO 
Abstract 
This article deals with some theoretical problems affecting the calculation of the land capital 
asset value in forestry in regard to the Konig-Faustmann or land expectation value formula 
(LEV). Several conditions have produced the co-existence of different LEV formulas, which 
generate different land asset values for the same forest management problem. This problem 
was probably generated given different economic traditions, forest management concepts and 
scientific paradigms which illustrated the even-aged clear-cut (EAC) stand management prob-
lem in different ways with the LEV formula. The aim of this research is to make an explicit 
distinction between these different LEV formulas based on different land rent theories, interpret 
the assumptions behind the mathematical models, and state the management problems that 
they want to define. Thus, it is intended to present a unified framework for the applications 
of these LEV formulas in theoretical and empirical forest economics and to support managerial 
decisions. 
Keywords 
Faustmann formula, land expectation value, value-in-use, forest economics 
Introduction 
This article deals with the theoretical problems affecting the calculation of the land asset 
value in forestry because several Faustmann or LEV formulas have been found in the 
literature, which produce different land asset values for the same valuation problem without 
being properly differentiated in the forest economic theory. Theoretical forest economists 
have not bothered to explicitly recognize this difference because the formulas produce the 
same optimal intertemporal cutting decision, and the differences do not affect the result of 
their applications. Thus, this article attempts to make a contribution in this field by re-
examining the economic and forestry theories behind the different LEV formulas of the so-
called Konig-Faustmann tradition. Moreover, a unified framework is proposed for improving 
their utilization in the theoretical and empirical applications in the economics of even-aged 
(EAC) stand management by providing criteria for using the LEV models. 
This article identifies three different basic LEV formulas in the forest economic literature 
over the last 189 years, which have not been properly identified and classified and are a 
source of ambiguity in the economic analysis that uses this method. Moreover, this study 
presents evidences that the so-called Konig-Faustmann formula is an incorrect term because 
the Konig and Faustmann formulas are theoretically different LEV models, which belong 
to separate soil rent theories. 
"Konig-Faustmannism" as a land rent theory for forestry seems to be an aggregate, a 
digest theory that summarizes the main classical economic theories, and it bears several 
problems. By answering this research question, it would be evident that mathematics alone 
can not be the sole basis of a functioning economic theory. 
Conceptual framework: Calculation of the Asset Value in Forestry 
Value is a human perception for the utility or satisfaction that any asset can yield to an 
individual under specific circumstances. Basically, the measure of asset value is conceived 
19 
F. Belles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 19--38. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 28 ---

in terms of other capital assets that an individual would be willing to give up in order 
to obtain it. By considering the intertemporal decision problem in forestry, an investor 
would have to resign to the present enjoyment of other assets in order to obtain the desired 
asset. The forest investor who makes such a decision expects that this investment asset 
would yield even more satisfaction in the future (Davies & Johnson, 1987, p. 371). Thus, 
the value of the asset is calculated based on the forgone opportunity of holding another 
asset, this is what economists call opportunity cost. Therefore, the value of an asset is 
always subjective because it is defined as the investor's willingness to pay (WP) for an 
asset considering the investor's unique inputs (Klemperer, 1996, p. 206). This type of asset 
valuation is called value-in-use. 
The value-in-use is basically an individual oriented valuation in which individual buyers 
and sellers assign a value to a good or service as a function of expected use (Davies et al., 
2001, pp. 397-398). The value-in-use is a necessary instrument to determine the WP of an 
asset that does not have an established market or to determine the best possible land asset 
value when examining different investment alternatives on it. 
The net present value (NPV) is a very common method for calculating the value-in-use 
of an asset in money terms and using the interest rate as the cost of capital. The land 
asset in forestry is a capital good that will allow future income. Thus, the NPV can be 
mathematically expressed as the perpetual periodic series for representing the cash flow of 
a typical rotation period that is replicated in perpetuity. This technique has been proven to 
be the proper way to determine the land asset value under forestry (Ohlin, 1921; Gaffney, 
1957; Samuelson, 1976; Johansson & Lofgren, 1985). 
Determining the land expectation value (LEV) has been the spinal cord of classical 
forest economics. As early as 1813, Gottlob Konig applied a LEV formula, and was the 
first to introduce discounting in forestry (Speidel, 1967, p. 95). Moreover, Faustmann 
(1849) proposed the use of the LEV formula for the calculation of the value of a piece of 
land projecting an even-aged forest on it in perpetuity in an independent way from Konig. 
Therefore, the LEV formula is traditionally known in forest economics by the name of 
Konig-Faustmann or simply Faustmann. Equation 2.1 shows how the original Faustmann 
formula looked in this example, without intermediate treatments and annuities for the sake 
of simplicity. 
L _ RH -Ca(1 +if 
0 -
(1 + i)T -1 
(2.1) 
In Equation 2.1, L 0 is the expected value or WP of the forestland without trees calculated 
by simple capitalization of the forestland rent. RH is the revenue of the timber harvest (H) 
net from the harvesting costs, minus the afforestation costs (Ca) compounded to the end 
of the rotation (T) with the discrete interest factor (1 + i). These Ca, which were referred 
to as establishment costs by Faustmann (1849), "occur at the start of the first year and 
recur every T years" (Faustmann, 1849, p. 11). The numerator (RH- Ca(1 +if) is a 
future value in which "we can compound to the end of the rotation all the incomes and 
expenditures which do not occur annually" (Faustmann, 1849, p. 9). This total is then 
discounted to the present by the expression (1 +if - 1, which is known as "the initial 
moment of land availability" (Hyde, 1980, p. 199), and accounts for the opportunity cost 
of invested capital (money, land, and labor). 
The Problem 
189-Years of Confusing Debates over Konig-Faustmann Formula 
There is confusion as regards the "Konig-Faustmann" formula because for the same EAC 
forest management problem three different LEV formulas are found in the literature under 
the same concept produce different asset values. This problem might be better explained 
with a numerical example. Adapting the example of a loblolly pine stand in the Southern 
20

--- Page 29 ---

United States (Klemperer, 1996, p. 217) helps represent more clearly three different "Konig-
Faustmann" formulas co-existing in forest economics. It can be found that for the same 
optimal rotation age one model reports a land value of $761.4/hectare, a second formula 
reports $655.0/hectare, and a third one reports $937.4/hectare as shown in Figure 2.1.

Figure 2.1: Three LEV formulas share the same rotation ages and generate different land assets

Table 2.1 arranges the three different LEV formulas found in the literature, as well as the 
compact version of the LEV model that is equal to the NPV of the perpetual periodic series. 
A sample of reviewed articles and chapters that used each of the LEV models appear to 
the right of each formula1. The LEV formulas are presented with their discrete discounting 
factors for the sake of simplicity. In theoretical forest economics, the critic of the LEV model 
is about the oversimplification or incompatibility of the Faustmann rationale to describe 
the forest management problem (Heaps, 1984; Walter, 1980). Moreover, in applied forest 
economics, the critic suggests "that the very abbreviated nature of the Faustmann formula 
leaves rather too much to the imagination of those who must use it within the context of 
practical every-day forestry rather than from the detached academic viewpoint" (Grainger, 
1968, p. 174). 
Model I: The Faustmann Formula 
The original Faustmann model, which has already been described, is also presented in the 
first row of Table 2.1 as formulas (2.2) and (2.3), that represent the two ways to characterize 
the model I of the LEV formulas (LEV-MI). In his 1849-article, Martin Faustmann proposed 
the use of the "land rent formula" for the calculation of bare land value for an even-aged 
(EAC) forest stand. Pressler (1858) developed the soil rent theory for forestry, in which 
the Faustmann formula became the milestone of this theory. Later, Friedrich Judeich and 
others incorporated Pressler's ideas into forest management. In this way, Faustmann's 
article and his formula entered the tradition of forest economics. The economic context of 
the German society in the mid-nineteen century demanded that forest sector and the newly 
born forestry science created a land rent theory for supporting economic based decisions 
and value forest investments (Markus, 1967, p. 13-14). 
In the 1840s, a new era of private capitalistic enterprise was promoted, and the individ-
ual was permitted to follow one's own self-interest, which was then considered part of the 
general prosperity and nation's wealth. These ideas were based on the classical school of 
economics that promoted economic freedom and proclaimed the laissez faire, laissez passer
