Table 2.1: Classification of references in respect to threee different LEV formulas 
ideology, in which Adam Smith and David Ricardo's economic theories influenced forestry 
(Markus, 1967, p. 14). 
Alternative investment opportunities developed, coal replaced wood as fuel, and the 
expansion of agriculture was renewed at the cost of forestlands, which caused timber values 
to decrease, and contributed to timberland shifting away from the productive units towards 
marginal lands (Hyde, 1980, p. 199). Thus, the public authorities wanted a valuation 
method in order to pay forestland owners a fair compensation for conversion into agriculture, 
and this is where Faustmann article came onto the scene (Newman, 1988, p. 7) (Oderwald 
& Duerr, 1990, p.170). 
On the other hand, the forestry strategy in this new liberal era was to offer profit in 
return from investment, and profit making was now the main goal of forestry. The transition 
of forestry from simply providing wood for the national economy to a commercial oriented 
activity confronted forestry with the problem of how profit making could be accounted. 
The management question was now economical and was formulated as follows: How long 
should trees be allowed to grow in order to achieve the highest profit in terms of land value? 
This became the basic question of forest economics (Markus, 1967, p. 14). Evidently, the 
original Faustmann model does not determine the land value for purchase, because this is 
always decided on the basis of the market value. Up until that point, the concept behind 
the Faustmann model stated that it calculated affordability for any given piece of land 
when it was converted into forestry. This was necessary because, unlike cropland, there 
was not an established market for forestland (Grainger, 1968, p. 169). 
The Faustmann formula was introduced with an applied and normative approach. The 
land rent theory in forestry was developed from the basic idea of soil rent from the school 
of classical economics as a basic tool to measure the rentability. 
This article supports the suggestion of Markus (1967) that the Faustmann formula uses 
Ricardo's definition of soil as an indestructible quality (fertility) with the exclusion of any 
improvements put in the soil in the course of land cultivation. From Ricardo's definition of 
soil it is clear that nothing but the bare ground is meant to be considered fixed land capital 
22

--- Page 31 ---

when determining soil rent. All other land attributes were considered capital outlays, and 
their profit is determined by the market interest rate. In 1826, Johann H. von Thiinen in his 
book The Isolated State calculated the land rent for a forest, and he acknowledged Ricardo's 
authority in this matter. However, Thiinen (1826) criticized the Ricardian concept of rent 
because he wanted to accentuate the soil rent as a location theory (Kooten, 1993, pp. 
18-19). 
Faustmann must have asked himself: How much could I afford to pay for bare land or 
non-forested land, probably agricultural land, if I were to start planning a forest stand from 
zero? (Gregory, 1972, p. 286). The view of Faustmann (1849, p. 442) was that forestry 
was a type of agriculture with the only difference in the planing horizon of the activities 
just as Edmund von Gehren (1849) claimed, and therefore he suggested to calculate interest 
charges on all capital used in the investment. Faustmann (1849) also coined the term forest 
land bare of trees, which also complemented the idea that the land was usually bare of 
the desired trees to be grown in forestry. At that time, German forestry promoted fast 
growing exotics. Forest species such as Picea abies or Pinus spp. were preferred over native 
broadleaves such as Quercus spp., Betula spp., and Fagus spp. (Dr. Deegen, pers. comm.). 
The principle behind the Faustmann theory is that of all the main capital goods of 
forestry (infrastructure, growing stock and pure land) only soil is considered fixed capital. 
The determination of the value of the fixed capital signifies the profit of the forest enterprise, 
which is the income net of all production and capital (interest) costs (Markus, 1967, p. 15). 
In the Faustmann MI-formula, the investment evaluation is made before the establishment 
of the new forest crop because Faustmann (1849) assumed that the establishment occurs 
at the start of the first year. Faustmann's statement may consider converting land into 
forestry, which is suited for forestry, which has been under other competing land uses (i.e. 
agriculture, cattle raising or denuded land). On the other hand, this denuded land might be 
a forestland, which is bare of trees at the evaluation moment because all trees were removed 
in the previous harvest, but the cost of restocking has been considered to be part of this 
new investment. Whatever would be the right answer to these open-end concepts in regard 
to the condition and use of the land, Faustmann evaluated an intermittent EAC system 
very much like an agricultural activity regarding the handling of the genetic production 
factor. 
However, the nature of forestry is very different. Only in the special case of plantation 
forestry with artificial regeneration does forestry have similarities to an intensive agricul-
tural system. The Faust mann formula has been set up for an isolated piece of bare land that 
has the potential to hold a stand that is about to be planted. Faustmann considered that 
afforestation cost ( Ca) had to be subtracted from the harvest proceeds like in agriculture, 
where the sowing (planting) is considered circulating capital tied up for the length of the 
growing period. Thus, the character of the growing stock in this soil rent theory applied to 
forestry is also considered circulating capital, and at the end of the rotation the investment 
is completed with bare ground. 
Intermittent EAC forestry was the system used to explain the land value formula in 
forestry. In the 1800s, the classical forestry paradigm considered the production of an 
even flow of timber goods based on stands managed under this clear-cut system within the 
normal forest context. Today, this production concept is considered simplistic, intensive 
and a contributor to degradation of the environment especially due to impacts of clear-
cutting. This type of forest production is known as tree farming, a type of agriculture, and 
it was the silvicultural system in which the traditional Faustmann formula was built. 
In the Faustmann Ml-formula, subsequent rotations are assumed to be completely iden-
tical to the first rotation. Harvesting ( RH) and replanting occur in different years, the 
former at the end of the rotation year and the latter at the beginning of the first year of the 
new growing period. This way of looking at the EAC system is possible when the restocking 
is not part of the production system and after the harvest of the crop the bare land can be 
sold or used in another alternative use. This rationale also follows the traditional intensive 
23

--- Page 32 ---

production concept of tree farming, in which the only possibility to regenerate the stand is 
by artificial regeneration considering exotics or genetically improved seedlings. 
Figure 2.2 shows the deterministic future line assumed in Faustmann model and shows 
the repeating series of cash flows every T years. Annual cash flows, thinnings and other 
periodic treatments are not considered for an easier graphical representation. 
yr. 
c. 
~--------------------------------·~ 
Figure 2.2: Organization of the net harvesting revenues (RH) and planting costs (Ca) in the planning 
horizon of the original Faustmann MI-formula 
Many forest economists such as Davies & Johnson (1987, p. 432); Filius (1992, p. 
123); Terreaux & Peyron (1996, p. 184) among others do not accept that afforestation and 
subsequent restocking occur at the beginning of year-1 (year-0); thus the main variant of 
a corrective nature made to the Faustmann formula is to account for afforestation cost at 
the end of year-1 as shown in formula (2.7). 
Lo = RH- Ca(1 + i)T-l 
(1 + i)T- 1 
(2.7) 
The Faustmann theory assumes that formula (2.3) can be mathematically transformed 
into formula (2.2) by transposition of the planting costs. However, mathematics alone 
can not be used to prove how sound an economic theory may be. The fact that formulas 
(2.2) and (2.3) are regarded as the same Faustmann MI-formula gives evidence to the 
oversimplifications and the basic theoretical failures of the land rent theory created for 
forestry. This article shows that considering these two formulas as analogous models, it 
evidences the ambiguity nested in the Faustmann theory. The meanings of the expressions 
in the formula are essential because they represent the postulates and definitions of the 
silvicultural systems and the economic theory that are being represented in a mathematical 
way. 
The original Faustmann model assumes that the investment starts with bare land, 
which would be afforested at the end of the rotation after the harvest is completed, but 
it is considered part of another growing cycle. The site is only restocked because infinite 
numbers of rotations are considered in the future (Newman, 1988, p. 7). However, the 
infinite rationale allows us to postpone the bare land problem to infinity and eliminate it 
through the myopia effect of the model, which means that when the problem can not be 
seen any more in the horizon it has been solved! 
Model II: The Konig Formula 
In the second row of Table 2.1, formula (2.4) represents the second LEV formula (LEV-
Mil). This LEV-Mil model represents another idea of the land rent different from the 
Faustmann MI-model. Basically, formula (2.4) considers afforestation of (bare) land within 
the context of real land-use conversion into permanent forestry use. LEV-Mil formula 
rationale has the potential over the LEV-MI that it can consider not only costs associated 
24

--- Page 33 ---

with land improvements (Ce), but also clearly differentiates the initial establishment and 
afforestation costs (Ca) from the restocking costs (Cr), which occur at the end of the 
rotation (Klemperer, 1982). 
In a historical context, the first author found in the literature to have presented a soil-
rent model and discounting applied to forestry was Gottlob Konig in 18132. Konig (1813) 
calculated a discounted cash flow of a pine plantation considering both annual and periodic 
events in perpetuity on a small piece of cropland. This cash flow included an initial seeding 
cost, an annual tax payment, and a periodic cash flow of revenues coming from different 
thinnings and the final harvest. All periodic payments are compounded to calculate a net 
future value, which is used to calculate the PV of the perpetual periodic series. However, 
the initial establishment costs were not compounded to the rotation age, and were left as a 
present value that was subtracted from the PV of the discounted cash flow of stand forest 
management. 
The Konig Mil-formula is not an extension of the LEV-MI model as several authors 
have described it, but it represented a different forest management problem, which pre-
sented another definition of the land asset, and it improved the accounting of the forest 
activities in the LEV formula. However, the original Konig Mil-formula (2.4) was presented 
with two minor simplifications given the characteristics of the example used. The Cr were 
not accounted for because natural regeneration was assumed to connect one rotation with 
the next one; and the initial establishment costs (Ce) were not considered because it was a 
small piece of land (Konig, 1813, pp. 257-259). Konig (1813) used his formula to evaluate 
alternative land uses (agriculture and forestry) for the same property. However, the impor-
tance of this LEV-Mil formula is that it considers a real land-use conversion scenario from 
agricultural land into permanent forestry while the Faustmann MI-formula begins and ends 
with bare land. 
The formula directly derived by Konig has been historically and ideologically isolated, 
and attempts at bringing it into the main forest economics arena have not been successful 
because it has not been presented properly by his supporters. Speidel (1967) presented 
the Konig formula as being exactly the same as the LEV-MI model, in which theCa were 
compounded to the end of the rotation as Faustmann (1849) had suggested. This misin-
terpretation of the LEV-Mil formula diminished the impact of its discovery, and changed 
the original logic of the LEV model, which is highlighted in this discussion. In addition, 
Oderwald & Duerr (1990) suggested that the initial growing stock should not bear interest 
as a critique to the traditional LEV-MI model, which they called "Konig-Faustmann" for-
mula, without being aware that the LEV-MI and Mil represent two incompatible soil rent 
theories, and they presented the LEV model as if Konig and Faustmann produced identical 
formulas, without knowing that Konig promoted the same idea that they were suggesting 
in respect of the treatment of the initial growing stock. This article is another attempt to 
bring a successful discussion on this unresolved Konig-Faustmann debate by differentiating 
and showing the relevance of the Konig Mil-formula in forestry. 
Konig published his LEV formula 4 years before Ricardo's 1817-soil rent theory. Thus, 
the soil rent theory used by Konig must have been the one developed by Adam Smith in 
1776. Smith's soil rent theory considers a long arrange of soil improvements as indivisible, 
inseparable parts of the pure land itself; consequently, the land capital represents the reim-
bursement of infrastructure and improvements. Moreover, Smith qualified the seed as fixed 
capital following the iron rule of agriculture, in which the harvested crop must provide the 
seed of the next crop, and the seeds never leave the productive ground, and therefore can 
not be considered circulating capital (Markus, 1967, pp. 22-23). Thus, there is no doubt 
that Konig used Smith's land capital concept for treating the valuation problem. 
The second author found in literature that showed mixed evidences that he was aware 
of the soil rent theories of Smith and Ricardo in respect of treatment of the growing stock 
as a variable or as a fixed capital, was Berti! Ohlin in 1921. He acknowledged several 
2The formula was corroborated on the original text of Konig (1813, pp. 257-259) 
25

--- Page 34 ---

possibilities of treating the initial growing stock either as a fixed capital or a cost to be 
compounded to the rotation age, depending on the management objectives. 
Ohlin's awareness of the co-existence of the two soil-rent theories in respect to growing 
stock provides a special functional rule for using Konig Mil-formula (2.2). Ohlin (1921) 
explained that in order for the forest system to be evaluated one should differentiate between 
initial afforestation and restocking costs. In addition, Ohlin recognized the growing stock 
as fixed capital when natural regeneration is considered, and regeneration costs could be 
considered as exploitation costs because the legislation ensures the continued growth of the 
forest in the productive sense as in the LEV-Mil formula. He also recognized that there 
are cases in which investment costs in afforestation should bear interest as in the LEV-MI 
model. In this case, Ohlin saw the possibility for changing from LEV-Mil to LEV-MI 
rationale and vice versa. However, in the example calculation of his 1921-article, Ohlin 
used the LEV-MI formula with respect to the growing stock. 
Traditionally only costs associated with stand establishment have been taken into ac-
count. Nothing has been done about the cost of capital such as buildings and other infras-
tructure. In this respect, Ohlin (1921) sided with Smith's soil-rent theory in recognizing 
that land improvements and infrastructure should be considered fixed capital fitting un-
doubtedly in the LEV-Mil idea3 . Ohlin re-defined within the context of the LEV formula 
other attributes of the land asset (land improvements, infrastructure, and initial establish-
ment costs) which are added attributes of the pure land fixed capital. In general, these 
magnitudes (land improvements and infrastructure) are likely to be omitted by the original 
Faustmann concept because the LEV-MI logic does not consider the valuation procedure 
against a background of large-scale and technologically managed EAC forest systems4• Hi-
ley (1956) suggested that one way to account for the infrastructure is to consider them as 
annual rent with administration costs. These costs were intended to include costs regarding 
any capital asset not accounted for elsewhere in the formula. To account for these capital 
assets in the formula would reduce the land value normally calculated by the original Faust-
mann formula. Grainger (1968, p. 168) provides a strong argument supporting a LEV-Mil 
rationale by stating that "the (Faustmann MI-) formula has a pronounced tendency to ex-
aggerate [ ... ] the land expectation value (LEV)". Representative data for every significant 
item of expenditure based on per unit of area should be selected before applying the LEV 
method. However, it is not sure that all costs have been included unless the forest manager 
has a relevant accounting system, and this has not always been the case. Grainger (1968) 
also emphasized that there is a significant difference between cost of establishment of the 
initial crop and the estimated long-term cost of replacement crop, and this difference must 
be accounted for. 
Klemperer (1982) discussed the difference between replanting cost and land-use estab-
lishment costs, which remains to be a continual property value element. He recognized 
that the net periodic income reflects EAC stand management with harvest and reforesta-
tion every T years. In addition, Klemperer defined Cfc in formula (2.4) as a non-recurring 
land-use establishment cost that in forestry consists of site preparation, drainage, roads, 
bridges, and permanent buildings (Ce) plus the afforestation cost (Ca), (Ca + Ce = Cjc)· 
Even reforestation may be considered as a non-recurring land-use expense if natural regen-
eration is considered at the end of the first and subsequent rotations (Klemperer, 1982, p. 
295), which is exactly the idea that Konig developed 189 years ago. CJc corrects LEV-MI 
formula (2.2)'s simplistic assumption that Ca = Cr in value and nature; therefore, it is no 
longer possible to transpose it into the Faustmann MI-formula (2.3). Instead, C fc trans-
forms LEV-MI formula (2.2) into a LEV-Mil formula (2.4). The LEV-Mil model represents 
clearly the case of land-use change from cropland into permanent forestry land use. 
30hlin (1921) and Klemperer (1982) independently suggest that land improvements and establishment 
costs are non-recurring costs and should be considered investment or fixed capital 
4When Faustmann developed his formula, the tractor, the motor vehicle and the chain saw did not exist. 
The forest activity was very much labor oriented and the system of forest records and accounting was more 
simple than nowadays (Grainger, 1968, p. 174) 
26

--- Page 35 ---

In the Konig MII-model, the non-recurring land improvement costs (Ce) have a place for 
being accounted for, and afforestation costs ( C a) are regarded as different from reforestation 
costs (Cr) not only in nature but in magnitude as well. These initial land improvement 
and afforestation costs (Cjc) are not compounded to the rotation age because they are 
considered fixed capital, not circulating capital like in the previous Faustmann MI-formula. 
These fixed costs can be regarded as the cost of land-use conversion ( C Jc) from non-forestry 
land uses into forestry. In this way, the cash flow associated with these one-time costs are 
discounted to the present in an independent way from the perpetual periodic series segment 
of the formula, and can be considered a PV in formula (2.4). In addition, C fc do not affect 
the maximization of the land value (Heaps, 1981, p. 698), but they can affect an investment 
decision in capital budgeting and the willingness-to-pay calculations. 
Figure 2.3 presents the idea of this LEV-MII formula, and shows how the initial Ce and 
Ca, and subsequent harvest revenues RH and Cr are organized in the deterministic planning 
horizon projected with the Konig Mil-formula. Moreover, McConnell et al. (1983, p. 239 
and p. 297) discussed the land-use change form cropland into forestland as the LEV-MII 
model. They recognized that switching from agriculture to forestry is not cost-free, and 
the initial establishment (Ce) and afforestation cost (Ca) differ from reforestation cost (Cr) 
after harvesting because the landowner must take major one-shot expenditures right at the 
beginning of the investment. 
€ 
yr. 
~·~ 
~ 
~ 
~ 
~ 
~-----------------------------------~ 
Figure 2.3: Organization of the net harvesting revenues (RH ), establishment costs (Ce) and planting 
costs (Ca and Cr) in planning horizon of the Konig Mil-model 
LEV-MI and LEV-MII formulas have co-existed in forest economics without much dis-
tinction. The idea of starting and ending with bare land was considered to apply to both 
models. Konig Mil-formula had the intention to value the non-forest land assets consid-
ering a permanent land-use conversion into forestry, and creating a place for accounting 
correctly for the fixed magnitudes of the land capital. 
Model III: The Ostwald Formula 
Differing from LEV-MI and LEV-MII formulas, a third LEV formula (LEV-MIII) was found 
that assumes a pure forestry scenario, in which the immediate past and present has been 
forestry land-use. The LEV-MIII formula does not use the idea of bare land or land use 
conversion. 
The first LEV-MIII formula that appeared was the one presented by Eugen Ostwald 
(1915) as a critique to the Faustmann-Pressler's soil rent theory, in which he suggested 
that the growing stock must be considered of a different economic nature. He defined two 
major groups of capitals in forestry: rent capitals (Rentenkapital), in which growing stock 
and pure land are the most illustrative examples, and interest capitals (Zinskapital), which 
correspond to circulating capitals like operative costs. Ostwald (1915) considered the soil 
27

--- Page 36 ---

and the growing stock as gifts of nature (Naturgaben), which belong to the same category of 
capitals and constituted joint and indivisible rent capitals. He even considered the growing 
stock more important than the soil because the soil derives its value from the quality of wood 
growing on it and there is no way to correctly separate the forest rent into soil rent and 
wood rent. Ostwald mathematically transformed the traditional Faustmann MI-formula 
(2.3) into formula (2.2) by mathematical equivalency, and then he transposed theCa term 
into the right to leave the right-hand-side of the equation equal to the LEV-Mill in formula 
(2.5). 
L 
C _ 
RH -Cr 
0 + a -
(1 + i)T - 1 
(2.8) 
The term (Lo + Ca) on the left-hand side of Ostwald MIII-formula (2.8) means that 
the soil is already planted with seedlings, and this term can be denoted with the term 
forest value (FLo). Formula (2.8) right-hand side represents the capitalized value of the 
forest rent coming from the discounted production cash flow (circulating capital ( CC)) 
during the rotation. In this Ostwald Mill-formula, Ca and Cr were theoretically equal 
in amount; however, Ca can not be considered afforestation costs, but the reforestation 
costs of a previous rotation, and are part of the fixed capitals of the forestland value 
(FLo = Lo + Ca)· Moreover, Cr are due the same year of the final harvesting, and they 
are considered production costs (CC). Like with the Konig Mil-formula, Ostwald (Hll5) 
brought back Smith's soil rent ideas, in which the growing stock including seeds must be 
considered fixed capital. Therefore, Ostwald's FLo represents the forestland rent of these 
magnitudes to be calculated, in which pure land and the growing stock, that are fixed and 
bounded capitals, are assumed in situ. This FLo formula has faded because its author 
would be identified with his 1931 relative forest rent theory5(Markus, 1967, pp. 20-22). 
Colin Clark (1976, pp. 269-270) proposed a new version of the Faustmann formula, 
which took into account the forest as an ongoing entity, but this LEV formula was de-
veloped using the Ostwald Mill-concept. Clark (1976) gave an explanation different from 
Faustmann to arrive at his LEV formula. He explained that at each period T 6 the existing 
forest is harvested and immediately replanted with a young stand (RH- Cr)· In addition, 
Clark denoted that the cost of harvesting and replanting should be accounted for together 
in the Cr term. The idea of a continued forest ecosystem is here considered assuming that 
the previous, present and future conditions of the land have been and will be forestry, t = 0 
is the time of the first planting, and also of the first felling, considering production, and 
market conditions remain constant. He proved his viewpoint as follows: "consider the prob-
lem facing the forestry manager immediately following the first felling at t = 7\. Because 
we assume that all parameters remain constant for all future time, this second rotation 
problem is identical to the original problem faced at time t = 0. Identical problems have 
identical solutions, so T2- T1 must be equal T1 (i.e., T2 = 2T1). The same argument applies 
to all future rotations" (Clark, 1990, p. 269). Consequently, Clark expressed the LEV-Mill 
model as in formula (2.5). He explained that the forest economics problem is to choose a 
rotation age, in which the value for T1 , T2 , ... and so on maximizes this LEV-Mill model 
where all T8 are of equallength7 . However, the problem with Clark's LEV-Mill formulation 
is that the model was not properly differentiated, and it was also named Faustmann or site 
value formula, meaning also bare land! (Clark, 1976, p. 270). 
The LEV-Mill and LEV-Mil models differ from the original LEV-MI model in which 
the revenue coming from timber harvest is net of harvesting costs and replanting costs 
5The LEV-Mill formula should not be confused with Ostwald's relative forest rent formula (Waldreinter-
trag) that was developed later in his 1931-book Outline of a Forest Rent Theory. Ostwald (1931)'s relative 
forest rent formula does not consider the land cost and the time value of money capital (Filius, 1992, p. 
123) 
6 Assuming a sequence of times of T years: T1 < T2 < ... with the property that at each time Tk the 
forest is felled and a new forest is planted (Clark, 1990, p. 269) 
7 "This is not an easy problem, it is formally posed as the sum of present values" (Neher, 1990, p. 68) 
28

--- Page 37 ---

because immediate restocking is assumed. Therefore, LEV-Mill model assumes replanting 
costs at t = 0, but they do not have to be accounted because they were already accounted 
in the preceding rotation To, and under the NPV concept, they are considered sunk cost. 
When assuming planting in t = 0, this formula locates the evaluation time immediately 
after restocking and right at the beginning of the growing period with either a seeded 
land or an already established young stand regenerated naturally or artificially as shown 
in Figure 2.4. Heaps (1981, p. 689); Hyde (1980, p. 199); Johansson & Lofgren (1985, p. 
85) also used the LEV-MIII formula by avoiding the bare land concept and using instead 
the seeded land or land in which a crop of trees had already begun to grow. Both ideas 
recognized an initial level of growing stock coming from natural regeneration without any 
silvicultural effort (instant regeneration). Likewise, Nautiyal & Fowler (1980, p. 214) did 
not use the bare land concept but an existing young forest growing in the land for T years. 
Hartmann (1976, p. 52) already considered a forest growing on a given plot of land that 
is going to be grown for a rotation of T years, which means that the standing forest was 
0 years old (Hartmann, 1976, p. 56, equation 8). Finally Yin & Newmann (1995, p. 479) 
also defined a LEV-Mill formula in which the landowner used land which has been devoted 
to timber production (no bare land).

Figure 2.4: Organization of the net harvesting revenues (Rn) and planting costs (Cr) in the planning 
horizon of the Ostwald MIII-model 
Moreover, Garfitt (1986) discusses whether to include or not the cost of initial planting 
of the growing stock in the LEV calculation. He addressed this point as follows: "If the 
matter is looked at historically, therefore, it will be seen that where restocking of felled 
woodland is concerned, as opposed to the afforestation of non-forestry land, the cost of 
replanting should in equity be a charge against the previous crop or, in wider terms, a part 
of the cost of maintaining a continuous forest. That being so, the costs involved should be 
deducted from the proceeds of the sale which should be a net figure. The costs of restocking 
is thereby removed from the account" (Garfitt, 1986, pp. 34-35). 
Confusing LEV formula 
Formula ( 6) in Table 2.1 represents the NPV of perpetual periodic series that can represent 
any of the three different LEV formulas when different assumptions or simplifications are 
used. The term p represents a future value that represents the compounded net income 
at the end of the rotation period. When the three LEV models assume instant natural 
regeneration, they end up being the same formula; however, this is an oversimplification 
because natural regeneration is a product of a silvicultural effort and it should be reflected 
in the cash flow. This reduced formula makes it more difficult to find out what kind of 
idea is behind the simplified model. In other situations, it is not possible to know what 
kind of soil rent theory was used to represent the forest management problem because the 
29

--- Page 38 ---

source only mentioned that the LEV or Faustmann formula was used, and only results of 
the calculations are presented. 
Searching for a unified framework 
Land-use determinants 
The investment asset in forest economics has been defined as bare land by the traditional 
Faustmann formula, but there must be a more comprehensive concept of the land asset in 
respect to the expected cultural use of the land, which includes other associated capitals 
also responsible for defining a land use. Thus, a land use is composed of all those fixed 
capitals that would allow a productive activity such as forestry. Genetic material is an 
important part of the land asset in agricultural and forest production. That is, the tree 
is the production factor and the crop at the same time due to the dual nature of forestry 
(Gregory, 1987, p. 4). This genetic stock could be considered part of the land at any state 
and growing conditions such as sowed seeds, established seedlings, or at any state of the 
growing stock depending of the silvicultural regime. Other capital goods of the forest land-
use asset are land improvements and infrastructure, the biotechnology (know-how), and 
production money capital (circulating capital). Three LEV formulas (Faustmann, Konig 
and Ostwald) found in the literature consider different initial and final land-use conditions 
due to different land rent theories, and forest management problems. 
Bare land as the only land-use determinant: The Faustmann MI-model (2.3) describes 
a land rent concept based on the Ricardian 1817-soil rent theory. Ricardo (1817, p. II.2) 
considered that land rent was ''that portion of the produce of the earth, which is paid to 
the landlord for the use of the original and indestructible powers of the soil." The original 
quality of the land, bare ground, was highlighted to prove that soil was the only bearer 
of land rent, which was traditionally used for agricultural and pastoral land uses. Land 
improvements and growing stock were regarded as production capital, in which profits were 
measured with a capital cost using the market interest rate as the minimum required profit. 
These land improvements and growing stock were thought to change from time to time, 
while the ground was fixed and kept indestructible powers such as fertility. 
Nowadays, it is accepted that particular land uses on certain terrains deteriorate or erode 
soil fertility, reducing revenues and increasing production costs. The idea of bare ground 
as the only fixed capital in agriculture and pasture has been part of agricultural economic 
traditions. The calculation of the bare land value could be done with the LEV approach in 
agricultural production under both periodic (pastures, vineyards, horticulture, and coffee 
plantations) and annual (grain and vegetable crops) cycles in which the production ends 
with the removal of the genetic material leaving bare ground. 
Ricardo's soil rent concepts were not only included in the 1849-LEV formula applied 
to forestry by Faustmann, but also later by Presseler and Judeich who developed a more 
comprehensive soil rent theory for forestry. They tried to prove the profit-making capabili-
ties of forestry, but they considered forestry a type of agriculture. The idea of the soil rent 
according to the Faustmann MI-formula is regarded as the capitalized sum that could be 
paid for bare land (Lo) if it was put into forest production (Ward, 1963, p. 788). However, 
the investment ends with the removal of the trees at maturity, leaving bare land (Lr) be-
hind for sale or for starting a new investment cycle (see Figure 2.2). Any planting after 
harvesting would be considered a reinvestment (Comolli, 1981, p. 301). 
It is valid to use Faustmann MI-formula in cases where the productive objectives and 
some conditions allow for the application of Ricardo's soil rent theory in forestry. However, 
those conditions should be very specific, and should be made explicit in order to justify not 
only accounting for Ca in subsequent rotations, but also compounding these Ca to the end 
of the rotation. Many even-aged (EAC) forestry investors are not happy with the idea of 
permanently remaining in forestry and they would like to see the investment analysis with 
30

--- Page 39 ---

the possibility of selling bare land at the end of the cutting cycle. This is called the bare 
land value or the willingness-to-pay for land considering only one rotation and the future 
land market price (Klemperer, 1996, p. 208). In practical applications, Lr is the crop. 
or pasture land price which is the most likely markets used as reference for rejecting or 
accepting the willingness·to-pay for bare land to be used in forestry. In this application, 
the compounding of the afforestation costs may be justified. 
However, if the Faustmann MI·formula is projected for infinite cycles, and in each cycle it 
is assumed that planting costs belong always to the next growing cycle, and are compounded 
to the end of the rotation, the calculation bears a high cost for the management of the 
growing stock. This application may be justified when there is a use of high technological 
systems such as in tree farming in which it is assumed that improved genetic seedlings 
must be planted after each clear felling in order to maintain a high level of production. 
Thus, the permanent capital cost on Ca may represent the opportunity cost for managing 
a genetically improved and overstocked forest that sustains an exceptionally good growth 
and yield of timber products. 
Bare land, growing stock and land improvements as land use determinants: Smith 
(1776) also proposed a liberal soil rent theory, which considered that the land yielded a 
rent to the landowner not only from the soil qualities, but from the soil improvements and 
the seeds. Smith's idea of the connection between land and growing stock included also the 
idea of sustainability that he had on the continued production of the land. He also was of 
the opinion that replacing the seed had to be done at the expense of the proceeds of the 
harvest of the crop (production costs). 
This sustainab!lity concept of Smith was based in the mercantilist idea that the products 
from the land were under obligation to supply material and food to the nation's economy 
(sustained yield) (Markus, 1967, p. 14). Today, the sustainability idea envisions a delib. 
erated and managed coexistence of society and nature (Davies et al., 2001, p. 13) where 
the land rent should not only strive for the supply of goods and services for the survival of 
society, but also for the existence of forest as a living and ongoing ecosystems. In practical 
terms, Smith'!! ideas fit into this new concept of sustainability, which moves away from 
forestry as a type of agriculture, and more into foref!try as an ecosyf!tem management. 
Smith understood that the seed or genetic material is bounded to soil qualities as well 
as geographical location and distance to markets, which also play an impprtant role in the 
land·use determina,tion. In this respect, Smith wrote that "the rent of land not only varies 
with its fertility, whatever be its produce, but with its situation, whatever be its fertility" 
(Smith, 1776, p. !.11.13). Moreover, Smith (1776, p. !.11.15) explained that fertility is 
only one of the attributes of land, and it is not an absolute determinant of land use. The 
adaptability of the growing stock to the land'11 biophysical characteristics and utility for 
individualfl were far more important. Thus, Konig Mil· and Ostwald MIII·form~,!las fit 
better in the Smith's soil·rent concept. 
Konig (1813) presented the LEV formula to explain how a productive piece of cropland 
could be valued when deciding whether to continue with the annual cropping land use or 
switch into ptJrmanent forest land use, Konig used a piec(l of land that had been used 
previously a.s cropland. ThW!, an agricultural crop and a pine plantation were examined 
on the same ground ~t~~ alternative land uses (Konig, 1813, pp. 257·259). Konig (1813) 
unmistakably applied Smith's land rent theory because he remarked on other land attributes 
that must be considered for selecting the best ecpnomic use of the land before a deci!lion 
can be made for allocating the resources in the function of a land use. He stated that the 
decision to choose forestry m> a land use should not only be based on the soil quality, but 
also the surrounding land uses and probably its accessibility in respect to the market. 
Konig MII·formula (2.4) considers a real land·use conversion scenario from cropland 
into permanent forestry where the investment starts with cropland (Lo) and ends with an 
ongoing forest stand recently planted (FLr) (see Figure 2.3). After all, land-use conversion 
is not cost. free. If land improvements and afforestation costs have to be made, they should 
31

--- Page 40 ---

be considered one-time investment (fixed capitals), which would become bounded to the 
land capital according to Smith's soil rent theory. However, Konig Mil-formula is trying to 
answer the question: What is the maximum that should be paid for cropland considering 
the land-use conversion costs? In order to answer this question, the land-use conversion 
costs (Cjc), (CJc = Ce + Ca), should be expressed as PV and subtracted from the PV of 
the perpetual net periodic series in the right-hand side of the formula (2.4). Thus, the 
result is the calculation of the cropland value under future forest production, which is 
also the maximum cropland price that could be paid for converting cropland into forestry. 
Therefore, the criterion for accepting the forest investment is that the calculated cropland 
value (Lo) is higher than or equal to cropland market price. 
In Ostwald's Mill-formula (2.5), the process starts immediately after the removal of 
the mature stand and replanting of a new one (Markus, 1967, p. 23). The cost of removal 
and restocking are not considered in the forestland value (FLo) calculation because in the 
recently established EAC system, the land use magnitudes (growing stock and pure land) 
are already contained in situ as fixed capitals, and are subject to valuation. Since the land-
use was previously under forestry, land improvements and infrastructure are also considered 
to be in place. Thus, the FLo is determined by the capitalized value of the net yield of the 
forest activity (Ohlin, 1921, p. 96). The FLo fits in Smith's soil rent definition because the 
forestland asset contains in itself the three land magnitudes described above. When the 
calculation considers only one cycle, the investment also ends with the immediate restocking 
of the stand. In this case, leaving a young stand at the beginning of a new growing cycle 
(FLT ), which is assumed that the forest grows in a continuous and interrupted development 
(see Figure 2.4). 
The Silvicultural Effort: Afforestation, Reforestation, and Natural Regen-
eration 
Three broad regeneration concepts as products of different silvicultural efforts are explored 
and clearly differentiated from one another for the correct use in applied and theoretical 
forest economics of EAC systems: afforestation, regeneration and natural regeneration. 
A silvicultural system is a controlled method by which crops from a forest are managed, 
harvested and perpetuated (Matthews, 1989, p. 3). The latter concept is achieved through 
the application of regeneration methods. The regeneration establishment is a product of 
a silvicultural effort either for initiating or continuing a forest. The silvicultural effort 
may consider the use of artificial regeneration or the use of management practices for the 
successful establishment of natural regeneration. Selection systems best conceptualized the 
harvest-regeneration idea when it is not possible to separate restocking costs in the forest 
stand management (Garfitt, 1986, p. 34). 
Afforestation, understood as an artificial regeneration of non-forested land, is used in 
situations similar to the ones described by the Faustmann MI- and Konig Mil-formulas. 
The idea of the Faustmann theory regarding afforestation is that any planting after har-
vesting would be considered a reinvestment, which means that replanting costs are always 
considered afforestation costs (Ca), but these Faustmann-Ca are regarded as circulating 
capital because they bear interest. 
The Konig Mil-formula also considers afforestation as part of the conversion of non-
forested land at the beginning of the growing period. However, at the end of the first 
rotation and in subsequent ones, the cost involved in the restocking should be deducted 
from the proceeds of the harvesting as reforestation costs (Cr) (Garfitt, 1986, p. 35). In 
other words, this Ca are fixed capital investment cost and reforestation costs are circulating 
capital or production costs. Ohlin (1921) knew the existence of different LEV models based 
on different ways of defining the growing stock either as fixed or circulating capitals. In the 
case of the afforestation costs as circulating capital, Ohlin (1921, p. 97 footnote) suggested 
that it should be treated with compounded interest as interest capital. 
32

--- Page 41 ---

Reforestation has been practically regarded as an artificial restitution of the previous 
stand that has been clear-felled. This is the idea behind the Konig Mil- and Ostwald 
Mill-formulas because in the two models, there is a projection in the future of an ongoing 
forest. From the accounting standpoint, reforestation takes place at the same time as 
logging (Heaps, 1984, p. 132). It is not possible to separate harvesting and reforestation 
costs because they happen in the same period (year). 
In Smith's soil rent theory, the cost of replanting should be charged against the previous 
crop, as production costs of no-investment character (Smith, 1776; Ohlin, 1921; Markus, 
1967; Garfitt, 1986). Ostwald (1915) suggested that Cr represent the continuity of the 
system because they restore natural growth production forces, which were eliminated by 
harvesting the old stand (Markus, 1967, p. 22). Thus, Cr are not accounted at the beginning 
of the growing cycle in the LEV-Mill formula, which removes a reasonable stress from 
forestry, which has been traditionally regarded as an inefficient industrial process (Garfitt, 
1986, p. 35). 
Natural regeneration occurs in a phase that may take several years. However, through 
silvicultural efforts, this natural regeneration lag is reduced considerably (Hyde, 1980, p. 
202). Natural regeneration can be the product of higher intensities of thinning programs, 
which reduce the net revenues of the final harvest compared to fully stocked stands that 
use artificial restocking methods. Traditionally, the assumption of considering natural 
regeneration in the analysis of the stand was done in theoretical applications with the 
intention of simplifying the LEV calculations as shown in formula (2.5) without taking into 
account the silvicultural effort incurred in achieving a successful natural regeneration. 
Many applications that used this particular natural regeneration assumption considered 
that bare land was always seeded or had an instant natural regeneration capability. Thus, 
it is assumed that valuable fast growing forest species would grow instantly from the bare 
ground. In this way, the Faustmann MI-formula has been transformed into an Ostwald 
Mill-model, but keeping Faustmann MI-rationale. "It is assumed that the site was bare at 
time 0 at which time a crop of trees began growing on it. This crop is logged at timeT, 
a second crop is then allow to grow on the site, which will be logged at time 2T, and this 
process of logging successive crops ofT-year-old trees is carried on indefinitely" (Heaps, 
1981, p. 689)8 . 
According to this rationale, an over-simplification was acceptable because the cutting 
decision was not mathematically affected. Many forest economists have thought that this 
assumption was the idea behind the LEV-Mill formula, considered to be a simplification of 
the Faustmann Ml-formula as described by Heaps: "On the model used by Clark (1976, p. 
259) one can obtain the above result without any reference to regeneration" (Heaps, 1981, 
p. 687). Moreover, there is a theoretical flaw in this instantaneous natural regeneration 
assumption. For those using the Faustmann MI-formula, when it is assumed that forest 
seeds are contained in the land asset; then this assumption changes the definition of the soil 
rent theory from Ricardo to Smith. The instant regeneration assumption is impractical if 
land has not been previously cleared (Strang, 1983, p. 580). Similarly, the Konig Mil- and 
Ostwald Mill- formulas assume that after a stand is harvested, a seeded land or a young 
stand is left in place. 
Land Infrastructure and Improvements in Forestry 
Traditionally, the calculation of the LEV did not properly account for the infrastructure 
and improvements made to the forestland, thereby making the valuation too simplistic. 
This has been a constant criticism made in the field of applied forest economics when using 
the LEV formula for valuation and investment analysis (Grainger, 1968, p. 174). The 
forest was treated as a sum of solitary, identical sizes, and equally located forest stands 
8The seeded land simplification is also found in Johansson & LOfgren (1985, p. 85); Hyde (1980, p. 199) 
among others 
33

--- Page 42 ---

in respect to markets without considering land improvements. This initial setting caused 
miscalculations and exaggerated valuations because the traditional LEV calculations did 
not Coflllider the large scale forest enterprise perspective, which is composed of stands of 
different 5izes, productivity, and improvement needs (roading). 
Berti! Ohlin was one of the first economists who recognized this problem, and criticized 
that administration, taxes and land improvement costs usually are not taken into account. 
"Land improvements costs [ ... ] have not been touched upon, despite their influence on the 
proceede of the operation. [ ... ] Such capital investments are integrated in the land itself 
• they can hardly be separated from the land value. It is usually hard to determine how 
much of the yield is interest from paid-up capital and how much is land rent" (Ohlin, 1921, 
p. 94). 
(Ohlin, 1921) argued that it is difficult to deal with the land value in its pure sense 
of bare ground and considered that additional initial infrastructure should be treated as 
fixed capital investments. The land capital asset is an initial fixed cost that defines the 
asset characteristics. These characteristics enable forestry to be considered a productive 
activity, which can yield a desired capitalized value over time. Thus, the general LEV 
formula should consider land infrastructure and improvements for present applications in 
order to obtain a more realistic asset valuation of the forestland asset. Moreover, regarding 
infrastructure and land improvements as components of the fixed land capital, they also 
should not be separated from the pure land value. Land infrastructure and improvements 
must not be compounded or mixed with production cash flow (circulating capitals). 
Criteria for Improving the Application of the Three LEV For-
mulas 
Figure 2.5 presents a flow chart for the adequate selection of the formulas for any specific 
application when working with EAC forest investment analysis. All LEV formulas in this 
chart calculate the land asset value for only one rotation and consider the sale of land asset 
(FLo/Lo) after harvesting. This is done with the intention of showing the condition of 
the land asset in respect to its attributes at the start and at the end of the first growing 
period, and how these attributes are treated in each formula either as fixed capitals (FC) 
or as circulating capitals (CC). If there is an interest in a forestland (at the beginning of 
the growing period), then the Ostwald MIII-formula can be applied. The Ostwald MIII-
formula can be referred to as the willingness-to-pay for forestland (W P FLo) or forestland 
value (FLo). 
On the other hand, if there is an intere11t for valuing a piece of cropland for tree farming 
or conversion into forestry land use, then the upper part of the Figure 2.5 could be followed 
as a guide for choosing between the Faustmann MI- and Konig Mil- formulas. Likewise, 
W P Lo form should be used when there is an interest for buying cropland, which does not 
have an established market price for conversion into forestry. In the case of tree farming, 
the Faustmann MI-model derived from formula (2.3) and adapted for a single rotation is 
the correct model for evaluating this activity. Formula (2.3) represents the original way 
that Faustmann (1849) formulated the land value calculation problem, in which the cost 
of afforestation (Ca) is accounted for at the end of the first year, compounded to the end 
of the rotation age, and subtracted from the timber revenues. This view is applied when 
the forestland improvement costs are not required or considered circulating capital (C.), 
and can be handled together with Ca. When the willingness-to-pay for cropland (WPLo) 
is calculated for a land-use conversion into EAC forestry as an on going ecosystem, then 
the Konig Mil-formula is suggested as presented in formula (2.4). The land-use conversion 
is not cost-free and initial land improvements, site preparation and planting should be 
accounted as capital investments in PV terms (Go).

The Volvo Theorem: From myth to 
behavior model 
RICHARD J. BRAZEE 
Abstract 
The standard Faustmann Model of forest management assumes that landowners harvest timber 
at the age that maximizes the Net Present Value (NPV) of forestland. Although until recently 
more folklore than a model of forest management, the "Volvo Theorem" provides an alternative 
to the standard Faustmann Model of optimal harvesting. Under the Volvo Theorem landowners 
harvest timber when faced with a non-forestry expense such as the purchase of a new Volvo 
rather than choosing to harvest at the age that maximizes NPV. Previous research on the Volvo 
Theorem analyzes credit limits, in situ values and bequests. Here a simplified model is used to 
extend previous analysis to describe behavior when a landowner faces differential interest rates 
depending on asset position. In addition the adequacy of the Volvo Theorem as an alternative 
to the Faustmann Model is assessed. 
Keywords 
Faustmann Model, credit limits, forest bequests, life cycle model, differential interest rates 
Introduction 
A, if not the, fundamental question in forest economics is when to harvest a stand of trees. 
For a simple setting, the Faustmann Model (Faustmann, 1849; Samuelson, 1976) provides a 
clear answer, i.e. landowners should harvest when the marginal benefits of delaying harvest 
from an increase in volume equal the marginal costs of delaying harvest from interest forgone 
and land rents. For more complex settings the dominance of Faustmann type models is 
virtually complete. Newman (2002) reports that a total of 278 works can be traced back 
to Faustmann with 85% of this literature published between 1979 and 1999. 
Within the folklore of forest economics the "Volvo Theorem" or "Volvo Effect" provides 
an alternative answer to the question of when to harvest a stand of trees (Johansson & 
Lofgren, 1985). Under the Volvo Theorem landowners harvest when they need a new Volvo 
or other non-forestry expense. That is, rather than borrowing, when they need to meet an 
expense, landowners harvest. Landowners harvest rather than borrow because of imperfect 
credit markets. Although long a part of informal discussions among foresters and forest 
economists, only recently have the Volvo Theorem and credit constraints been formally 
analyzed. The two most important streams of this analysis may be characterized as two 
period and Faustmann type rotational models analyses of imperfect credit markets and 
asset preferences. 
In addition to its theoretical value, accurately describing landowner harvesting behavior 
is important in forest policy design. In many countries an important component of forest 
policy design is focused on encouraging small non-industrial landowners to manage their 
forest holdings to more optimally produce timber. More accurately modeling harvesting 
behavior is an essential component of designing effective policies for encouraging active 
forest management. Analysis of the Volvo Theorem is particularly timely given the free 
financing of new autos offered by the US auto companies during the last 3 months of 2001. 
A reasonable hypothesis generated by free financing of new autos seems to be that if the 
Volvo Theorem holds in the sense that credit constrained landowners harvest before the 
Faustmann harvest age, can we expect credit subsidized landowners to harvest after the 
Faustmann harvest age, i.e. does a "reverse" Volvo Theorem hold? 
39 
F. Belles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 39-48. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 48 ---

The purpose of this paper is two-fold. The first goal is to assess the usefulness of the 
Volvo Theorem as an alternative to the standard Faustmann Model. The second goal is to 
present a simplified model of forest management, and then use this model to characterize 
a set of new results that describe optimal land-owner behavior, when the landowner's 
borrowing interest rate differs from her/his lending interest rate. In the next paragraph 
six general questions regarding the Volvo Theorem are posed. Following are sections on 
relevant previous research, the simplified model of forest management, and the derivation 
of basic theoretical results when borrowing and lending rates differ. The paper ends with 
discussion of the new results and tentative answers to the general questions posed. 
Six general questions regarding the Volvo Theorem are: 
1. Under what circumstances does the Volvo Theorem hold? 
2. When does the "reverse" Volvo Theorem hold? 
3. What are the implications of the Volvo Theorem? 
4. Should the Volvo Theorem be investigated further? 
5. Is the Volvo Theorem a useful alternative modeling paradigm to the Faustmann 
paradigm? 
6. Given the complexities of analyzing the infinite rotational models with credit con-
straints, are simplified versions of the Faustmann Model a desirable approach? 
Previous Research 
Previous research uses both the two-period (Koskela, 1989; Kuuluvainen, 1990; Kuulu-
vainen & Salo, 1991; Kuuluvainen et al., 1996), and Faustmann type rotational models of 
optimal harvesting (Tahvonen, 1998; Kuuluvainen & Tahvonen, 1999; Tahvonen & Salo, 
1999; Tahvonen et al., 2001) to address how landowners optimally harvest under credit 
constraints and bequest preferences. Note that Fina et al. (2001) also derive a Volvo type 
result with perfectly functioning credit markets, but with a desire to pay the loan at a fixed 
time without refinancing. The two-period model is used to examine life cycle models, derive 
comparative statics results, welfare loss, and virtual prices, describe timber supply under 
risk, and identify landowner characteristics with harvesting behavior. The rotational mod-
els derive the Faustmann solution as a special case with a non-binding credit constraint. In 
situ preferences are studied extensively. Comparative statics results of the rotation period 
are derived. 
Limits on credit are universal. Few individuals are granted an unlimited or virtually 
unlimited access to credit. Four common types of credit limits are debt ceilings, borrowing 
rate limits, collateral requirements and down payments. A debt ceiling requires that total 
indebtedness be less than a specified level. Under a borrowing rate limit the landowner 
cannot borrow faster than a specified rate. If collateral requirements are required, the 
landowner's debt ceiling is a function of her/his net worth. Similarly, a down payment 
may be thought of as a purchased saving rate. Credit limits will only impact landowner 
behavior if binding. When credit limits are binding stumpage should be harvested when 
the marginal benefits in the per hectare rate of return from forest growth is greater than 
the interest forgone plus the decrease in bare land value, i.e. marginal benefits of delaying 
harvesting are increasing faster than the discount rate. In comparison with Faustmann 
type models, this harvesting pattern decreases land values and implies that production and 
consumption decisions are no longer separable. The lack of separation between production 
and consumption decisions implies that owner specific and not just market characteristics 
impact production decisions. 
40

--- Page 49 ---

Landowners routinely value non-market goods and services associated with standing 
forests. When landowners value, the standing forest stumpage should be harvested when 
the marginal benefits in the per hectare rate of return from forest growth is less than the 
interest forgone plus the decrease in bare land value, i.e. marginal benefits of delaying 
harvesting are increasing slower than the discount rate. Similar to credit constraints, when 
the standing forest is valued, there is a lack of separation between this and consumption 
decisions. An important result is the development of an alternative to the Hartman Fonunla 
under which benefits of the standing forest are not treated as financial returns but enk. 
directly into a landowner's utility (Hartman, 1976). 
Approach 
Theoretically, Faustmann type rotation models should usually be more useful in analyzing 
credit constraints than the two period models, because rotational models describe harvest-
ing behavior over many to infinite periods (Tahvonen, 1998). However, due to jumps in the 
state variables at harvest under credit constraints and the desire for an infinite planning 
period particularly with finitely lived agents, rotational models are extremely difficult to 
analyze. The solution approach adopted by Tahvonen et al. (2001) is to examine an infinite 
chain of consumption problems that include net forest income. Even in adopting this ap-
proach the current level of mathematical analysis required is extremely high. For example, 
(Tahvonen et al., 2001) extend a lemma by Pontryagin et al. (1962) to derive their results. 
This level of technical expertise significantly limits additional analysis. 
The approach adopted here is to develop a life cycle model with a single rotation with 
a fixed bare land value. This approach highlights the determination of the optimal har-
vesting age for the current rotation but avoids the necessity of solving an infinite chain of 
consumption problems. Due to space limitations and simplicity only results for the con-
tinuous portion of the state variable path will be presented. State variable jumps will be 
discussed and not formally derived. These simplifications give rise to a mining model with 
an appreciating resource. 
Basic Model 
In temperate and sub-artie areas of the world optimal forest rotations are long enough 
that many landowners only have the opportunity to harvest at most one rotation. Most 
landowners in these regions inherit or purchase standing forests rather than bare land as 
modeled in the standard version of the Faustmann Model. For example, Kuuluvainen & 
Salo (1991) estimate that the average Finnish Non-Industrial Private Landowner (NIPF) 
holds a standing forest for approximately 30 years. Since rotation periods in Finland vary 
from 50 to 130 years many if not most landowners inherit a standing forest, and do not 
harvest many of their stands. Over their lifetimes most landowners have income generated 
outside of their forest holdings. The following landowner welfare function captures these 
characteristics: 
max W(Aa, La)

the number of hectares available for harvest before the end of the planning horizon, N.

Asset Dependent Discount Rates 
Landowners may face different discount rates depending on their net asset position. Two 
observed differences in discount rates depend strictly on whether the landowner is a net 
borrower or saver. It is common that consumers including landowners pay higher interest 
rates as borrowers than they receive as lenders, i.e. credit is expensive. Note that both 
Kuuluvainen & Salo (1991) and Tahvonen et al. (2001) call for investigation of expensive 
credit. A second and less common situation is when consumers may finance part of their 
indebtedness at a rate lower than at what they receive on savings, i.e. credit is cheap. An 
example of cheap credit in the U.S. during the last 3 months of 2001. Following September 
11, 2001 the U.S. automobile companies offered 0% financing on new automobile purchases. 
First consider a landowner facing expensive credit. With differential interest rates, the 
landowner's objective function of 3.1 is replaced by equation 3.16:

t, Ao is redefined as the landowner's initial gross level of financial assets, and Do is the 
landowners gross level of indebtedness.

The landowner optimal harvests when the per hectare returns from harvesting grow at 
rate between rA and rD. The introduction of the non-negativity constraints on A(t) 
and D(t) generates four cases to analyze, i.e. A(t) > 0, D(t) < 0; A(t) > 0, D(t) = 0; 
A(t) = O,D(t) < 0; A(t) = O,D(t) = 0. Note that 1(t) = (>)0 when A(t) > (=)0, and

Table 3.1: Impact of Expensive Credit 
The first 3 columns of Table 3.1 identify the case and the status of A(t) and D(t). The 
fourth column describes what per hectare return rate equals when stumpage is optimally 
harvested. Case 1 in which the landowner is simultaneously indebted and has positive 
financial asset level is suboptimal, as s/he can arbitrage to a more profitable position. If 
the landowner's net balance is positive, then s/he will only hold the financial asset. If the 
landowner's net balance is negative, then s/he will not hold any of the financial asset, and 
will reduce her/his debt as much as possible. 
Cases 2 and 3 are symmetrical. In Case 2, the land-owner is holding the financial 
asset, but has no debt, while in Case 3, the landowner is not holding the financial asset, 
and is indebted. If timber is harvested, then the returns from harvesting would either be 
invested in the financial asset or used to reduce the level of indebtedness depending on 
whether the landowner holds financial or is indebted, respectively. The relative discount 
rate to determine when to harvest depends on how the harvesting returns will be used. 
If the returns will be invested in the financial asset, then r A is the relevant rate. If the 
returns will be used to reduce indebtedness, then rD is the relevant rate. In Case 4 the 
landowner neither holds financial assets nor is indebted. The relevant discount rate is r A 
or r D depending on whether the returns from harvesting will be used to add assets or avoid 
debt, respectively. 
One scenario that could arise from cases (2)-( 4) could generate two or more rather 
than one optimal harvest times. An indebted landowner would optimally harvest when 
d~;t) =rD. Suppose that the harvest of part of the landowner's forest lifts her/him out 
of debt. If some of the remaining harvest returns will be invested in the financial asset, 
then it is optimal to stop harvesting and wait until either d~;t) = rA, or harvest more 
to avoid debt in the interim. This could happen several times during the period in which 
rA < d~~t) <rD. Note that since this is a continuous time model in the limit the landowner 
could employ a chattering control, i.e. there could be an infinite number of optimal harvest 
times during the era that r A < d~~t) < rD. This is an illustration of the rational underlying 
why some of the previous work directly addresses jumps in the state variables, see Tahvonen 
(1998); Tahvonen & Salo (1999); Tahvonen et al. (2001). 
With the cheap credit rA >rD. When conditions 3.27, 3.22-3.25 and 3.26 are analyzed, 
it is easy to show that due to arbitrage the landowner will maintain the maximum debt 
level possible at every time t. Since there is no debt limit in the current formulation, the 
landowner will borrow an infinite amount at rate r D to invest in the financial asset at r A, 
which will generate an infinite profit. Although this problem is poorly defined in the sense 
the borrowing, and hence returns are unbounded, more sophisticated problem formulations 
that include debt limits for all or a portion of the debt, installments plans for repaying the 
debt, and/or the existence of two goods only one of which can be purchased using cheap 
credit, cannot fully escape arbitrage. In each of these formulations, the landowner seeks to 
maximize her/his indebtedness at the lower interest rate and transfer those funds to assets 
with a higher rate. Since the landowner's behavior with respect to inexpensive credit is 
determined, cheap credit does not impact harvesting decisions. 
45

--- Page 54 ---

Note that the landowner can face variable borrowing and lending rates, e.g. the bor-
rowing rate could increase as function of landowner debt, or the lending rate could increase 
as the landowner invests more in the financial asset. With variable borrowing and lending 
rates, 3.29 and 3.30 are replaced by: 
dF(t) 
/(t) = rA(A(t)) if A(t) > 0 
(3.31) 
or 
dF(t) 
__.!M_ = rD(D(t)) 
F(t) 
if D(t) < 0, 
(3.32) 
where rA(A(t)) is the average market rate of interest on investments and rD(D(t)) is the 
average market rate of interest on debt. 
Discussion 
The six questions posed in the introduction are used to frame the discussion. 
1. Under what circumstances does the Volvo Theorem hold? 
Previous research demonstrates that the Volvo Theorem holds when credit is constrained, 
or when refinancing is precluded. The new results of the previous section indicate that the 
Volvo Theorem also holds when a landowner faces a higher borrowing than lending rate. 
Previous research also demonstrates that Volvo type results follow when the standing forest 
has value. 
2. When does the "reverse" Volvo Theorem hold? 
As discussed in the previous section, the "reverse" Volvo Theorem in which inexpensive 
credit postpones harvesting is unlikely to occur since optimal behavior suggests that owners 
will arbitrage by borrowing as much as possible at the lower rate and transferring the 
borrowed funds into the financial asset. 
3. What are the implications of the Volvo Theorem? 
Previous research derives three important implications of the Volvo Theorem that contrast 
with the implication in the Faustmann Model. First, when the Volvo Theorem holds there 
is a loss of separation between consumption and production decisions. Second, this loss 
of separation and other factors generate different and more complex comparative statics 
results. Third, long term forest dynamics under the Volvo Theorem are also much more 
complex than a normal forest under the Faustmann Model. The Faustmann normal forest 
can be derived as a special case in which credit is unconstrained, and in situ values are 
ignored. 
4. Should the Volvo Theorem be investigated further? 
Although the Volvo Theorem has moved from the realm of myth to refereed research, there 
are several needs for additional research. Areas in need of additional theoretical research 
include savings constraints, collateral requirements and down payment requirements, forest 
bequest assumptions, and asset preferences for holding wealth in either forests or financial 
assets. Debt limit constraints, in situ values and differential interest rates have been rela-
tively thoroughly examined. Areas in need of additional empirical research include all types 
of credit constraints including debt limit constraints, in situ valuations, forest bequests, as-
set preferences and differential interest rates. In addition since the previous empirical 
analyses are based on Finnish data, it is important that data on landowner behavior from 
other parts of the world are examined. 
46

--- Page 55 ---

5. Is the Volvo Theorem a useful alternative modeling paradigm to the Faust-
mann paradigm? 
In certain respects previous research on the Volvo Theorem has much to offer. First, the 
research is very carefully executed; both the theoretical and econometric analysis are first 
rate. Second, as described above the Volvo Theorem provides different and more complex 
explanations of landowner behavior. Third, at times landowners are credit constrained, and 
it is plausible that in situ forest values are important to numerous landowners. Tahvonen 
et al. (2001) suggest that Finnish credit markets were rationed between 1935-85 and during 
this period the average lending was -1.3%. 
However, there are two additional tests that the Volvo Theorem must pass before it can 
be considered a truly useful alternative to the Faustmann Model. First, do the expected 
landowner behaviors predicted by the Volvo analysis match actual landowner behaviors? 
Second, how common are credit constraints, differential borrowing and lending rates, and 
bequest and asset preferences? Unless the Volvo Theorem accurately predicts behavior 
based on conditions faced by landowners, it is of limited value. 
6. Given the complexities of analyzing the infinite rotational models with credit 
constraints, are simplified versions of the Faustmann Model a desirable ap-
proach? 
The analysis of differential interest rates in the previous illustrates the value of a simplifying 
the standard Faustmann analysis to highlight the first rotation decision, when adding addi-
tional complexity such as life cycle modeling. It is possible that this type of simplification 
may produce results in other areas of forest economics as well. Given the prevalence of the 
Faustmann Model, it is seems likely that the marginal returns to effort in Faustmann anal-
ysis eventually will decrease. A possible niche for the type of simplified model presented 
here may be in positive analysis, i.e. the Faustmann Model may be used to derive optimal 
landowner behavior, while the simplified model may be used to describe actual landowner 
behavior. 
Acknowledgments 
I am greatly indebted to Professor Finn Helles of KVL and the Scandinavian Society of Forest Economics 
for inviting me to the SSFE meeting in Gilleleje in May 2002, and for obtaining funding through the 
Council of Nordic Ministers, so that I might attend. Without this invitation, I would never have started 
working in the area. Special thanks to Lizzie Rohde and Niels Strange for service above the call of duty 
to make Ann and my trip to Denmark go well.
