Adaptive spatial harvest planning under risk 
of windthrow 
HENRIK MEILBY, Bo J. THORSEN AND NIELS STRANGE 
Abstract 
Optimising the economics of forest stands faced by risk of windthrow becomes difficult when 
the risk depends on spatial position and shelter created by neighbouring stands. The number 
of harvest age solutions to be considered is exponential in number of stands and polynomial 
in number of periods. Moreover, similar conditions apply to the number of possible courses 
of events (scenarios) that must be evaluated to calculate the expected present value of each 
suggested harvest age solution and, therefore, the computational effort virtually explodes as 
the number of stands and periods increases. However, instead of preparing a long-term harvest 
plan it may be satisfactory to determine which stands should be harvested during the next 
period and which should be retained. This adaptive approach reduces the computational effort 
considerably. On the other hand, as the result depends on stochastic events it does not provide 
any idea of the expected forest value or rotation age distribution. Therefore, in this paper we 
apply Monte Carlo simulation to evaluate the consequences of the adaptive optimisation - or 
stepwise forward dynamic programming- approach. This strategy has the double advantage of 
(i) producing realistic solutions that imitate the adaptive optimisation behaviour of the forest 
manager, and (ii) partly circumventing the tremendous combinatorial dimensions of the topical 
stochastic interaction problem. 
Keywords 
Monte Carlo simulation, dynamic programming, harvest scheduling, spatial interdepen-
dence, simulated annealing 
Introduction 
North-western Europe occasionally experiences destructive gales that lead to severe wind-
throw events, particularly in coniferous stands, and cause significant economic losses to the 
forest sector and Society as a whole. Sometimes large coherent areas of forest are blown 
over, leading to serious damages to forest structure and increased costs of reforestation due 
to, e.g. unfavourable micnrclimatic conditions. Such damages remain visible for decades. 
Windthrow is an important cause of economic risk in some parts of the world and in 
other parts forests are subjected to the risk of forest fire or catastrophic defoliation by 
insects. Hence, it is not surprising that the economics of forest management under risk of 
some calamity has been analysed in several studies, both at the stand level (Reed, 1984; 
Reed & Apaloo, 1991; Haight et al., 1995; Thorsen & Relies, 1998) and at the forest level 
(Reed & Errico, 1985, 1986, 1987; Lohmander, 1987). Except for Lohmander (1987) all the 
studies use a single-stand approach to risk modelling and the forest-level models are essen-
tially aggregates of stand-level models where interdependence is created by introduction of 
forest-level constraints. Lohmander (1987) analyses optimal harvest scheduling under risk 
of windthrow for a small forest with three stands and applies explicit modelling of wind-
throw hazard based on empirical work by Lohmander & Relies (1987). There is, however, 
only one wind direction in his model, and the forest consists of uniform stands. 
In real forests the risk of wind throw varies in time and space and depends on topography, 
soil conditions, stand management, forest structure, and previous damages. For any given 
stand, the risk depends not only on stand characteristics but also on the spatial structure 
of the surrounding forest. Each stand benefits from the shelter provided by neighbouring 
49 
F. Helles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 49--61. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 58 ---

stands, and each stand offers shelter for other stands. Shelter is in other words an ex-
ternality, which is (usually) not accounted for in single-stand models. Hence, independent 
stand-wise harvest decisions might imply a significant economic loss at the forest level. This 
problem was investigated in a previous paper (Meilby et al., 2001), analysing the optimal 
spatial harvest planning problem when forest stands are faced by risk of windthrow and 
the risk depends explicitly on the features of each stand and its neighbours as well as on 
the geographical structure and orientation of the forest. We showed that shelter affects 
the optimal rotation age significantly and, hence, when the number of stands (forest size) 
increases, the average expected present value and the optimal rotation ages increase as 
well. However, the problem is exponential in number of stands and polynomial in number 
of periods. In addition, the number of possible courses of events to be considered is also 
exponential in number of stands and - depending on the chosen stochastic dynamics model 
- either polynomial or exponential in number of periods and, therefore, the problem easily 
becomes computationally intractable even for low numbers of stands and periods. 
In Meilby et al. (2001) we basically assumed an anticipatory planning approach, common 
in the literature. However, it may not reflect the real-life decision problem faced by the 
forest manager. The optimal harvest policy is contingent upon the state of the forest at 
the time of planning but as time passes stands may be blown over, neighbouring stands 
that used to be sheltered are now exposed to the wind, and the optimal policy changes 
accordingly. The forest manager will most likely adapt to such changes almost continuously, 
abandoning the original plan and developing a new one. 
Due to the combinatorial dimensions of the problem anticipatory optimisation is not 
feasible for more than 6-8 stands at a time. In Meilby et al. (2001) we therefore suggested 
that the applied model framework might be used for creating short-term (two-period) op-
timal harvest plans and exemplified the sequential use of the method for a forest with 16 
stands. The present paper further explores this adaptive optimisation approach and exam-
ines the consequences of optimal behaviour by application of Monte Carlo simulation. As 
our point of departure we take the fact that due to the nice properties of the underlying 
volume and value functions (continuous, increasing functions with decreasing first-order 
derivatives and negative second-order derivatives), at least from age 23 the entire future 
of the forest can be dealt with optimally in a stepwise manner. At any point in time, the 
decision-maker needs only to compare the expected present value of harvesting now or in 
the next period. If the optimal time to harvest a stand is further into the future, it will 
also be optimal to wait at least one period. As illustrated for a '16-stand forest' in Meilby 
et al. (2001) this approach greatly simplifies optimisation because the number of decision 
alternatives and scenarios are reduced significantly. The fact that more optimisation steps 
are needed is not crucial. 
Models, assumptions and the adaptive optimisation approach are described in the sec-
ond section. In the third section we present results for cases similar to some of the cases 
used in Meilby et al. (2001). In the fourth section we discuss the results and conclude by 
pointing out practical implications and perspectives. 
Models, methods and assumptions 
Below we describe the applied models, methods, and assumptions. However, for details on 
the basic models we refer to Meilby et al. (2001). Basically we distinguish between models 
describing the interaction between stands, i.e. the shelter effect, and models describing the 
stochastic dynamics, i.e. windthrow probability models. 
Stand interaction 
The model complex includes four model components describing (i) shelter as a function of 
stand height; (ii) windthrow sensitivity as a function of stand height and diameter; (iii) 
50

--- Page 59 ---

exposure to wind as a function of geographical orientation and length of the borders towards 
neighbouring stands; {iv) the directional distribution of strong winds and the probability 
of gales within a year. 
The shelter provided by a given stand (i) is modelled as a sigmoid function of stand 
height. The parameters of the model obviously depend on tree species. As we focus on 
coniferous species, the parameters are chosen such that agreement with Lohmander & 
Helles (1987) is obtained. The sensitivity (ii) of a forest stand towards wind is modelled 
as a sigmoid function of stand diameter and height, similar to the model presented by 
Lohmander & Helles (1987). Unthinned stands and open-grown trees are assumed not to 
be susceptible to windthrow. As for the exposure of a stand (iii) it is assumed that the 
probability of significant wind throw damages, necessitating salvage harvest, is related to the 
relative proportion of unprotected borders rather than the absolute length of such borders. 
As regards the distribution of the wind (iv) a hypothetical model describing the directional 
distribution of destructive gales is used in combination with an annual probability of such 
occurrences. 
Stochastic dynamics 
Two stochastic dynamics models (I and II) are used. Model I stochasticity implies that the 
probability of windthrow within a given period is estimated independently for each stand 
on the basis of the state of the forest at the beginning of each period. In this model each 
scenario is uniquely characterised by a vector of de facto harvest ages and a binary vector of 
harvest specifications (salvage harvest or planned harvest). As the probability ofwindthrow 
is estimated independently for each stand, Model I only includes neighbour interdependence 
between periods and not within. Basically, this means that gales are assumed to be local 
stand-specific events and, therefore, in any period and for any stand the probability of 
windthrow is not influenced by the possible fact that one or more stands in the same forest 
are blown over during the period. However, in reality gales are large-scale phenomena that 
influence all stands within a certain region and, therefore, we have introduced Model II in 
which wind throw events are temporally coupled. Like in the case of Model I, each scenario 
includes vectors of harvest ages and harvest specifications but, in addition, a Model II 
scenario includes a binary gale vector that specifies in which periods gales occur. 
Model II is one step closer to reality than Model I but, still, the interaction between 
neighbouring stands is restricted by the fact that, within a given period, the shelter ex-
perienced by a stand is defined by the state of the forest at the beginning of the period. 
Accordingly, Model II does not include those changes of the exposure of a given stand 
that will occur during a real gale when neighbouring stands are blown over; changes that 
may lead to a 'domino effect'. In a certain sense the problem arises because the temporal 
duration of gales is not considered in any of the models. Instead gales are regarded as 
sudden 'quantum events' of no duration. Potentially a Model III, respecting the temporal 
character of gales, can be developed on the basis of Model II by dividing each period into a 
number of sub-periods and constraining the gale vector in such a way that the value of the 
gale/no-gale property is common to all sub-periods within a given period. However, due to 
the immense consumption of computational power associated with this model it is neither 
applied in Meilby et al. (2001) nor in this paper. 
Optimisation 
When solving the harvest planning problem we search for that harvest age vector which 
yields the maximum expected present value (assuming risk neutrality). If we search for 
an optimal harvest plan for n stands which are considered for T periods, then the number 
of potential solutions is rn. To determine the expected present value of each solution a 
varying - but quite often high - number of scenarios must be evaluated. 
51

--- Page 60 ---

The number of scenarios depends on the number of stands as well as the number of 
periods each stand must be considered. For instance, if the solution implies that all n 
stands are suggested to be felled in period T, then for Model I the number of scenarios is 
(T + 1)n, because all combinations of de facto harvest periods are valid, and in the last 
period we must distinguish between windthrown and surviving stands. Similarly, when all 
stands are considered for T periods, Model II includes 2T different gale vectors so, in this 
case, the number of potential scenarios is 2T(T+ 1)n for Model II. However, scenarios where 
no gales occur during periods in which stands are known to be windthrown clearly are not 
allowable and, therefore, the number of valid scenarios is considerably lower. Nevertheless, 
for Model II the computational effort is both exponential in number of stands and number 
of periods. 
Dynamic programming 
For forests with many stands, the high number of potential solutions and scenarios will ren-
der it impracticable to carry out anticipatory optimisation of the harvest policy. Here, the 
adaptive approach, only evaluating the near future, is an alternative that greatly simplifies 
the task of optimising the harvest plan. If we distinguish between stands harvested in the 
next period and later, we need to solve a two-period problem, and if harvesting immediately 
is considered realistic, then we are just facing a one-period problem. 
By reiterating the 'harvest now or later strategy' an adaptively optimal harvest policy 
arises. The procedure involves a stepwise evaluation of the (few) possible outcomes of the 
next period only, whereas the anticipatory multi-period procedure evaluates all possible 
outcomes. The adaptive optimisation approach is in fact merely an application of Bell-
mann's principle of optimality or, in other words, dynamic programming. Technically, the 
value function satisfies the Contraction Mapping Theorem and a unique solution exists 
(cf. Stokey & Lucas (1996)). At each optimisation step, the problem can be solved by 
application of: 
(4.1) 
where W F,t is the expected present value of the forest at time t, n is the number of stands, 
R(T;) is the harvest revenue plus the bare land value of stand i, and 8 is the discount rate. 
The harvest decision control is T; = {1, 2}, where 1 implies that the stand is harvested now 
and 2 implies that it is harvested in the next period. W F,t+ 1 is the expected value of stands 
not harvested in the present period, assuming that they are harvested or salvaged in the 
next. The overall result is contingent upon the course of events. Since only one period is 
considered at a time, the complexity of calculating the expected present value is determined 
exclusively by the number of stands. As the problem is still nonlinear and exponential in 
number of stands we apply simulated annealing to identify the optimal harvest age vector 
at each step (Kirckpatrick et al., 1983; Cerny, 1985). 
Monte Carlo simulation 
To examine the· distribution of present values and rotation ages we need to evaluate a 
sample of randomly drawn outcomes. Accordingly, the optimisation step must be carried 
out repeatedly, not only over model time but also for a random set of outcomes. This Monte 
Carlo simulation involves the following steps: (i) Carry out state-contingent optimisation 
of harvest decisions according to equation ( 4.1 ). (ii) Simulate wind throw events that, along 
with the current state of the forest, the growth, and the state-contingent optimal harvest 
decisions, result in a new state. (iii) Repeat (i)-(ii) for each of the following periods until it 
is optimal to harvest all remaining stands in period one of equation (4.1). This way a single 
instance of an optimal sequence of harvest decisions is obtained. (iv) Repeat (i)-(iii) for a 
52

--- Page 61 ---

large number of outcomes, thereby enabling evaluation of the present value and rotation 
age distributions associated with optimal harvest decisions. 
Case study and parameters 
Below we consider two different triangular forests also applied in Meilby et al. (2001). Both 
forests include four stands with an area of 2 ha each. The stand ages are 35, 35, 45, and 
55 years and the forests differ only by their geographical orientation. In one of the forests 
(Rotation 240°) the oldest stand is exposed to strong south-westerly winds, whereas in the 
other (Rotation 60°) it is sheltered by the other stands. The structure and orientation of 
the two forests and the predominant wind direction are shown in Figure 4.1. We apply 
the same growth and yield models and assumptions as regards the annual probability of a 
gale (0.08, based on 0stergard (1988)), discount rate (8 = 0.03), recovery rate for salvaged 
stands (0.8), and bare land value (DKK 1000/5) as in Meilby et al. (2001). In this paper 
present values and cash flows are expressed in Euros and the applied exchange rate is 7.4537 
DKK to 1 EUR. 
North 
Rotation 60° 
Rotation 240° 
t 
Figure 4.1: Structure and orientation of the two forests. The main wind direction and initial stand 
ages are indicated 
Results 
For each of the two forests in Figure 4.1 Monte Carlo simulations were carried out with 
Model I and Model II dynamics. All four simulations included 28 years and comprised 5,000 
replications. In all cases, however, the four stands of the forest were harvested in year 25 at 
the latest. Summary statistics regarding the present values of the forests are presented in 
Table 4.1 and, at least for Model I, compared with the expected present value obtained in 
an anticipatory optimisation run also including 28 years. For Model I the maximum number 
of scenarios that needs to be evaluated for a single harvest age solution (planned harvest 
of all stands in year 28) is 707,281 but for Model II the maximum number of scenarios is 
6.55 x 1012 , i.e. 9 x 106 times as many as for Model I. Hence, using the anticipatory approach 
computation of the expected present value was impracticable for Model II (indicated by 
n.a.). 
Among the four stands of the forests the one aged 55 is obviously mos't susceptible to 
windthrow. As will appear from Figure 4.1 this stand is sheltered by the younger stands in 
the 60° forest. In contrast to this, in the 240° forest it is exposed to the main wind direction. 
Therefore, it is not surprising that both for Model I and II the highest mean present value 
is observed for the 60° forest. For Model I the adaptive optimisation approach achieves a 
slightly higher mean present value than the anticipatory approach. However, the differences 
only amount to EUR 220 and 75 for the 60° and 240° orientations, respectively . .1\Ioreover, 
as the standard errors of the mean present values are EUR 87 and 82, respectively, the 
difference is significantly different from zero at the 5% level for the 60° forest, but not 
53

--- Page 62 ---

for the 240° forest. For the difference to be significant at the 5% level for the 240° forest 
roughly 25,000 replications would be needed. At any rate differences are expected, however 
small they may be, as removal of the restriction that planning is carried out at the initial 
stage - and will not be repeated - can only increase the mean present value. 
As regards Models I and II it appears from Table 4.1 that the mean present values of the 
two models are almost equal for each of the two forests. However, the standard deviations 
are greater for Model II than for Model I. This is due to the fact that windthrow events 
occur independently in Model I, whereas in Model II they are temporally coordinated. 
Note that extreme events, e.g. all stands being knocked over in the first year, are rare and 
therefore the minimum present values show no clear pattern of variation. In all four cases 
the maximum present value corresponds to the situation where all stands are harvested 
as planned. The reason why the maximum present value of the 240° forest is greater for 
Model II than for Model I is that with Model I the two stands ageing 45 and 55 years are 
felled at ages 65 and 64, respectively, whereas with Model II they are felled at ages 68 and 
65.

compared with the expected present value (EPV) obtained by anticipatory optimisation (Model I 
only). t From anticipatory optimisation 
For Model I the cumulative distribution of the present values is shown in Figure 4.2 
along with the cumulative distributions of rotation ages for each of the four stands in the 
two forests. Similar results are shown for Model II in Figure 4.3. Results for the 60° forest 
are shown in Figures 4.2a and 4.3a and results for the 240° forest are shown in Figures 4.2b 
and 4.3b. Each graph showing the cumulative distribution of rotation ages includes a curve 
showing the distribution of all harvests as well as one including salvage harvests only. For 
Model I the optimal rotation age of the anticipatory approach is indicated by arrows. 
A general observation is that for both forests and for both stochastic dynamics models 
the youngest stands are always harvested at age 60 if they are not blown over by the wind 
before this age. For these stands the adaptive optimisation result is consistent with antic-
ipatory optimisation (for Model I). Moreover, the rotation age distributions are generally 
skewed with a marked tail to the left. Therefore, the mean rotation ages of the adaptive 
approach are considerably lower than the optimal rotation age of the anticipatory approach 
(for Model I), whereas the median rotation age of the adaptive approach is quite close to the 
anticipatory result. The standard deviation of the rotation age is generally highest for the 
stand ageing 45 years. Due to its location in the centre of the forest, the role and situation 
of this stand depend much on the fate of the other stands. Similarly, for the 55-year old 
stand the standard deviation of the rotation age is considerably greater in the 60° forest 
than in the 240° forest. Finally, it should be noted that the risk of windthrow increases 
rapidly from age 45 onwards and, hence, the cumulative rotation age distribution of the 
55-year old stand is steep from the outset. 
In the 60° forest the two oldest stands are sheltered by the young ones and, hence, the 
optimal rotation ages depend on whether the young stands are blown over or not. However, 
if not damaged by wind throw, the 45-year old stand is always retained to age 62. Similarly, 
54

--- Page 63 ---

planned harvest is never carried out in the 55-year old stand before age 60. In the 240° 
forest the oldest stand is exposed towards the main direction of the wind. Therefore, the 
planned harvest of this stand does not depend on whether the other stands are blown over 
or not. Similarly, as the oldest stand is removed at age 64 (Model I) or 65 (Model II) at 
the latest, i.e. after 9-10 years, the planned harvest of the 45-year old stand is not sensitive 
towards the occurrences of the rest of the forest; at age 60 and beyond it is exposed to the 
wind whatever happens. 
It is worth noting that for all stands of the 60° forest, the overall probability that a 
given stand will be blown over by the wind before it is harvested according to plan is 
slightly greater than 40%, no matter whether the optimal harvest plan is obtained using 
Model I or II. For the 240° forest the overall probability of wind throw varies considerably 
between stands and the highest chance of being salvaged at some point in time is observed 
for the 45-year old stand. This is due to the fact that after the 55-year old stand has been 
removed, the 45-year old stand is providing shelter for the two stands ageing 35 and, hence, 
it is worthwhile keeping it for a couple of years more, although this implies an increased 
risk. To some extent it is sacrificed to protect the younger stands. 
In Figure 4.4 the temporal variation of the cash-flow distribution is illustrated for each 
of the two forests and for both stochastic dynamics models. Dark shading corresponds to 
high frequencies and the maximum frequency is equal to the number of replications (5,000). 
The dark line at the bottom of each graph reflects all those cases where the cash flow is 
generated exclusively by thinning. The dark 'islands' reflect those cases where the stands 
are felled according to the adaptively optimal plan. For instance, the dark 'islands' in 
year 16 (Rotation 60°) and year 9 or 10 (Rotation 240°) correspond to those cases where 
the 55-year old stand is felled at the optimal time. The distribution patterns clearly differ 
between the two models. In Model I wind throw occurrences are independent and, therefore, 
the event that a single stand is blown over is more likely than in Model II, whereas it rarely 
happens that two or more stands are knocked over in the same year. In Model II windthrow 
occurrences are coordinated and, hence, it may happen that two, three, or even four stands 
are blown over in the same year. As a consequence of these differences the cash-flow pattern 
of Model I is characterised by relatively few, marked peaks, whereas that of Model II is 
more diffuse. 
To provide a less ambiguous illustration of the risk of wind throw and its implications, 
the annual mean area of blown-over stands and the annual proportion of mean cash flow 
originating from salvaged stands are shown in Figure 4.5. The annual mean area of blown-
over stands is defined as a x I:;{=l n 8,;(t)/N, where a = 2 ha is the area of each stand, 
N = 5, 000 is the number of replications, and n 8,;(t) is the number of replications for 
which stand i is salvaged in year t. The annual proportion of mean cash flow originating 
from salvage harvests is defined as I:;.f=1 c8,j(t)/ I:;.f=1 ctot,j(t), where Cs,j(t) is the cash flow 
originating from salvaged stands in replication j and Ctot,j(t) is the total cash flow, both in 
year t. 
As will appear from Figure 4.5 the annual mean area of salvaged stands increases grad-
ually and when only the two youngest stands remain it reaches a level of roughly 0.2 ha per 
year. The 240° forest is clearly more risky than the 60° forest as long as the oldest stand 
remains but after its planned removal in year 9 (Model I) or 10 (Model II) there is no clear 
difference between the two forests. Above it was mentioned that the overall chance of a 
stand being blown over by the wind is roughly 40%. Therefore, it is not surprising that 
the cumulated mean areas of blown-over stands turn out to be 3.38-3.75 ha, corresponding 
to 42-47% of the forest area (8 ha). The cumulated mean areas of blown-over stands are 
greatest for the 240° forest and - for both forests - about 1.1% greater for Model II than 
for Model I. 
Except for those years where one or more stands are frequently harvested according to 
plan, the proportion of the mean cash flow generated by salvage harvests remains slightly 
greater for the 240° forest than for the 60° forest, also after year 9 or 10 when the oldest

Discussion and concluding remarks 
The framework suggested in this paper leads to results that reflect the real-world option 
of adaptive optimisation, as opposed to the anticipatory optimisation assumed in Meilby 
et al. (2001). A priori it is presumed that the expected present value of the forest will 
be greater for the former method than for the latter, as we now allow for the manager to 
react to events as they show up, instead of planning all actions many years before their 
realisation. Mathematically speaking, relaxing any sort of restriction on a maximisation 
problem can only cause non-negative changes in the maximised value function. 
For Model I it emerges that the differences between the mean present value of the 
adaptive approach and the expected present value of the anticipatory approach are only 
EUR 220 and 75 for the 60° and 240° orientations of the forest, respectively. Although 
the Monte Carlo simulations include 5,000 replications, the standard errors of the mean 
are EUR 87 and 82 for the two forests and, therefore, at the 5% level the difference is

Year 
Figure 4.5: a. Annual mean area of stands blown over by the wind; b. Annual proportion of mean 
cash flow originating from salvaged stands. Results based on Monte Carlo runs including 5,000 
replications 
only significant for the 60° orientation of the forest. One explanation for this discouraging 
result may be that we apply a relatively high bare land value in the simulations. At the 
applied 3% discount rate the bare land value is EUR 4,472 per ha. For comparison the 
average present value is roughly EUR 124,000/8 ha = EUR 15,500 per ha. Another partial 
explanation for the small difference is that the present value is quite insensitive to changes 
in rotation age. This is partly due to the fact that the value of the standing stock increases 
at a rate of 3% at age 50 and at age 60 the annual increase still amounts to 1.6%. 
A small deviation between the practical execution of the analysis reported here and in 
Meilby et al. (2001) ought to be mentioned. To reduce the time needed for the anticipatory 
optimisation, the duration of the first period was increased from one to five years in Meilby 
et al. (2001), implying that 28 years could be evaluated using only 24 periods. In the 
optimisations carried out in this paper, the 28 years were evaluated using 28 one-year 
periods. This deviation causes a small difference between the results obtained using the 
anticipatory approach in the current study (for Model I) and in Meilby et al. (2001). 
The cash-flow patterns in Figure 4.4 clearly differ between Models I and II. The differ-
ences reflect the fact that, although none of the models are perfect descriptions of reality, 
Model II is a better approximation than Model I. The potential Model III, including the 
duration (and severity) of gales, would lead to slightly more dispersed (blurred) cash-flow 
59

--- Page 68 ---

distribution patterns than those observed for Model II. However, as will appear from the 
results in Table 4.1 and Figures 4.2 and 4.3 the difference between the optimal plans ob-
tained by application of Models I and II is insignificant. Therefore, from a practical point 
of view Model III is not likely to add much - except for a lot of cumbersome work. 
The annual mean area of blown-over stands and the proportion of cash flow generated by 
salvage harvesting are observed to increase over time (Figure 4.5). Intuitively, this appears 
reasonable since the stands get older and the windthrow hazard increases accordingly. The 
240° forest is more susceptible to windthrow than the 60° forest, since in the former the 
oldest stands are facing the most frequent wind direction. Hence, it is not surprising that 
the proportion of the mean cash flow generated by salvage harvests is highest for the 240° 
forest. 
From a practical point of view, the full anticipatory harvest age solution may be useful 
for defining overall rotation age prescriptions. However, for specific forest areas anticipatory 
planning is of limited value as planning ought to be repeated every time a stand is har-
vested or salvaged in conflict with the original plan. If the risk of wind throw is significant, 
such unplanned harvests are likely to occur frequently and, hence, adaptive optimisation 
(stepwise, forward dynamic programming) is the relevant tool. 
In reality only one possible course of events will be realised and, therefore, the forest 
manager usually does not need a full description of the expected distribution of the out-
comes, such as the one computed here by means of Monte Carlo simulation. Nevertheless, 
for our purposes the use of Monte Carlo simulation in combination with forward dynamic 
programming has two important advantages. First of all, it is possible to partly circum-
vent the huge combinatorial dimensions of the problem - an aspect that renders traditional 
full-state-space backward dynamic programming useless. Secondly, when the adaptive be-
haviour of the forest manager is acknowledged, we obtain good - and perhaps more realistic 
- estimates of forest value and state-dependent optimal rotation ages. 
It has been suggested that adaptive optimisation with time frames of one or two periods 
is likely to reduce the computational effort significantly, implying that a greater number of 
stands can be managed. To obtain an idea about how many, we may assume that a certain 
maximum number of scenarios is considered computationally acceptable. For instance, if 
the maximum number of scenarios is 106 , then for Model I the maximum number of stands 
is 5 for a ten-period problem {ln(106)/ ln(10 + 1) = 5.76}, 12 (12.58) for a two-period 
problem, and 19 (19.93) for a one-period problem. Similarly, if the maximum number 
of scenarios is 108, the corresponding numbers of stands are 7 (7.68), 16 (16.77) and 26 
(26.58), respectively. Accordingly, even with a time frame of only one period the number of 
stands that can be evaluated using the adaptive approach is presumably limited to 20-25. 
To optimise harvest decisions for forests with more stands simplifications are needed. 
Finally, although the adaptive optimisation approach reduces the computational effort 
needed to evaluate a single sequence of events, a considerable effort is required to evaluate 
the distribution of present values and rotation ages. In the cases examined in this paper 
5,000 replications were needed to obtain reasonably stable mean present values and, yet, 
for the 240° forest the mean still was not statistically distinguishable from the expected 
present value of the anticipatory approach.

An econometric analysis of differences 
stumpage values using micro-level 
harvesting data 
STALE ST0RDAL AND SJUR BAARDSEN 
Abstract 
. 
In 
This paper proposes a framework, utilising micro-level harvesting data, that analyses stumpage 
values with respect to regions, seasons and harvesting size and decomposes differences between 
regions. Due to the thick-tailed distribution of the data and the presence of heteroscedasticity, 
the paper gives a comparison of ordinary least squares regression with an iterative weighted least 
squares (IWLS) estimator and a multiplicative heteroscedastic model. The results underline the 
importance of taking sample properties into account and support the use of the robust IWLS 
estimator. There were significant differences in stumpage values over regions with respect to 
both the characteristics of the timber and the valuation of the different grades. There were no 
seasonal differences in stumpage value, but the results indicate a significant but minimal loss 
from the decreasing size of the harvests. 
Keywords 
Forestry, heteroscedasticity, non-normality, roundwood markets, timber sales 
Introduction 
Knowledge of economic conditions and market characteristics is important to ensure optimal 
design of incentives. The problem that faces timber procurement planners is that the 
forest owners are heterogeneous with regard to, e.g. size of their property, timber quality 
and distance to the mill. Moreover, there may be differences between relatively small 
regions. This heterogeneity causes differences in harvesting behaviour that, in turn, creates 
difficulties for the procurement agencies in planning and fulfilling their agreements with 
the wood manufacturing industry. This paper proposes a modelling framework, utilising 
the presence of micro-level harvesting data, which analyses stumpage values with respect 
to geographical, seasonal and harvest-specific characteristics, and decomposes the regional 
differences. The empirical application considers records from individual harvests from the 
Mj!Zisen-district in Eastern Norway. 
The literature on harvesting behaviour is voluminous, from simple profit-maximising 
models, to models that incorporate the forest owners' objectives. Another strand of the 
forest economics literature concerns how cutting behaviour can be explained through con-
tractual differences, e.g. the effects of contract structure on cutting behaviour (Rucker 
& Leffier, 1988); effects of forestry consultants (Hubbart & Abt, 1989; Munn & Rucker, 
1994); effects of the presence or absence of competitive buyers in timber sales (Hardie & 
Larson, 1994); relationships between forest owner characteristics and consultant partici-
pation (Larson & Hardie, 1989); and determinants of consultant participation (Munn & 
Rucker, 1998). The theoretical framework has either had its basis in the von Neumann-
Morgenstern expected utility framework (e.g., Larson & Hardie (1989); Hardie & Larson 
(1994)), the auction theory framework (Baldwin et al., 1997), or utilising the transaction 
costs theory (Klein & Leffier, 1981; Leffier & Rucker, 1991; Leffier et al., 2000). 
Empirical tests of the models consider stumpage prices to be dependent on the tract 
characteristics (e.g., Hubbart & Abt (1989); Leffier & Rucker (1991); Hardie & Larson 
(1994)). These studies only partly attempt to analyse individual species and qualities as do 
63 
F. Helles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 63-71. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 71 ---

Bare & Smith (1999) in their eight-step procedure for the estimation of stumpage values. 
The present paper also utilises the price decomposition procedure found in Munn & Rucker 
(1995). The presence of micro-level data gives opportunity to detailed analysis. However, it 
also creates a number of methodological problems to be solved under econometric modelling 
of such data. 
In the next section we give a brief review and introduction to the models and method-
ology applied. In section 3 the data set, variables used and the empirical specification are 
given. The estimation results and properties are presented in section 4 before a discussion 
and some concluding remarks are provided in section 5. 
Method 
The model 
Sawlogs, pulpwood and other timber grades are produced jointly, but consumed by differ-
ent industries. A specific harvest can thus be viewed as differentiated factors of production 
used in the manufacture of lumber, pulp and paper, and other forest products (Munn & 
Rucker, 1994). A particular harvest differs both with respect to the timber (quantity, qual-
ity, species mix, etc) as well as its harvest-specific characteristics (size, location, period, 
logging conditions, etc). The net stumpage value (NSV) defined as gross value less measure-
ment costs and logging costs can therefore reasonably be assumed a function of the timber 
and harvest-specific characteristics. However, because the standard deviation of NSV will 
increase with the volume of the sale, a regression would appear to be heteroscedastic. In 
order to overcome this, a standard solution is to give larger volumes lesser weight by divid-
ing the function by the sales volume (Bare & Smith, 1999), estimating the net stumpage 
value per unit of the i'th sale, Pi 
Pi = f (TIMBER, LOCATION, PERIOD, HARVEST SIZE) 
(5.1) 
Ordinary least squares (OLS) estimation can be severely distorted by outlying observations 
because it gives disproportionate weight to large deviation in the calculation and, thus, 
causes the results to be sensitive to relatively small numbers of errant data points (Greene, 
2000, p.399). In particular, applications involving thick-tailed disturbance distributions are 
fairly common and have led to the proposal of "robust" estimators that are more or less 
unaffected by the outliers, e.g. the least absolute deviation (LAD) estimator. 
In order to reduce the influence from outlying observation, this paper applies an iterative 
weighted OLS algorithm (IWLS) similar to the one suggested by Schlossmacher (1973); Fair 
(1974): 
n 
min Ei = L Wi (Pi - q;f3) 2 
(5.2) 
i=l 
where q; is the vector of variables of the ith sale, f3 the corresponding vector of coefficients 
and w; = 1/IPi- q;/3;1 the weights. 
To begin iterative estimation, initial weights are chosen so that w; = 1/le;l, where the 
e; are the OLS residuals e; = p; - qib, b = (q;q;)- 1q;p;. An estimate of f3 based on these 
weights is given by (q;Wq;)-1qiWp;, where W = diag(w1,w2, ... wn)· This estimate for f3 
can be used to calculate another set of residuals and weights, which in turn can be used 
to re-estimate f3 until convergence. One problem with this procedure is that, in a given 
iteration, some of the weights may be zero, or very close to zero. This is solved by setting 
residuals equal to 0.001 when they are less than 0.001 (cf. also Fair (1974)). The weights 
are thus calculated as w; = 0.001/le;i so that small residuals have weights equal to one, 
while larger residuals lose weight proportional to the increase in their absolute deviation. 
This procedure leaves a solution close to the LAD estimates (Schlossmacher, 1973). 
The LAD-estimator minimises the sum of the residuals' absolute value. Thus, the median 
64

--- Page 72 ---

(not the mean as in OLS) is the maximum likelihood estimator and the LAD estimator is 
LaPlace distributed (density (e-lxl)/2) (Amemiya, 1985, p.71) with thicker tails than the 
normal distribution. The LAD estimator also has the useful property that it is normally 
distributed with mean (3 in large samples (Amemiya, 1985; Koenker & Bassett, 1982, pp.148-
154) Harvey (1990, p.119). 
If there is still heteroscedasticity present in the sample, the multiplicative heteroscedas-
ticity model of Harvey (1976) can be used, as. e.g. by Hardie & Larson (1994). The general 
form of the error structure is then

respective coefficients. If we let Zi include a constant term so that z; = [1, y;], where Yi is 
the original set of variables, and let 1' = [log a-2, o'], the model simplifies to (Greene, 2000, 
p.518)

(5.4) 
Price decomposition procedure 
In general, a value can be divided into two factors, price and quantity. This is utilised in this 
paper where the difference in net stumpage value is decomposed in order to analyse whether 
it is due to differences in grade distribution (characteristics) or valuation of the distinct 
grades. The price decomposition procedure applied is adapted from Munn & Rucker (1995), 
implying that the results are decomposed into (a) differences due to characteristics of the 
sale, which is the component of total differences that can be explained by different mean 
values of the explanatory variables for different region, and (b) differences due to valuation 
of the characteristics. 
as: 
First, assume that the net stumpage values (NSV) for two regions A and B are given 
pA = qA(3A + EA 
pB = qB(3B + EB 
(5.5) 
The q vectors give the relative quantity of the n different grades in region j, q1j, ... , qnj so 
n 
that L q;; = 1, Vj =A, B, while the (3 vectors embody the respective net prices, and the 
i=l 
EJ's are the vector of errors. The difference in net stumpage value (NSD) evaluated at the 
mean values is thus the difference between pA and pB, which after some manipulation and 
rearranging yields: 
where bA and bB are the vectors of estimated coefficients. The CD evaluates the difference 
in sale characteristics at mean value, while V D evaluates the difference in valuation between 
the regions. In order to simultaneously estimate these difference more efficiently, we followed 
Munn & Rucker (1995), treating the distinct samples as a single sample, estimating a single 
regression of the form: 
p = q(3 + (REGNUM X q)8 + E 
(5.7) 
where REGDUM is a dummy variable for either of the regions. The interaction term 
exhibits the difference of the (3's between regions. Since all variables are divided by total 
quantity, the explanatory variables (q's) are the proportion of a given grade in the specific 
tract. The ,B's are thus the marginal values of increasing the amount of the different grades 
in a specific tract. 
65

--- Page 73 ---

Data 
Individual records on grades and quantities were obtained from the national database of 
wood measurement information (VSOP) for a subset including members of the forest own-
ers' association Mjosen for the period 1999:1-2001:6. Furthermore, these data were linked 
with records on logging costs where available, giving a total of 2090 observations on different 
sales. 
Net stumpage value (NSV) was calculated as total value less logging and measurement 
costs. Observations with NSV less than NOK 30 per m3 were rejected (i.e., approximately 
10% of total value). These observations were supposed to include thinnings, cable op-
erations and other forestry operations that reveal characteristics that were incompatible 
with the kind of operations we wanted to study. The remaining data set consisted of 1988 
observations. 
Empirical specification 
The input variables used in the empirical analysis were: net stumpage value; quantities 
(proportion) of different grades from each harvest; total quantity; dummies (region, harvests 
during the summer period, extremely low or extremely high logging costs). From these 
variables the hedonic pricing model considered was: 
9

where Qji is the proportion of species j in contract i , REGk is a dummy for region k, 
Qror, is total volume cut in contract i, PER is a season dummy for harvests in summer 
season (June 23- Oct 1). Subscripts j = 1, ... , 9 reflect different timber grades, k = 1, ... , 4 
reflects different regions (cf. Table 5.1). COSTH is a logging cost dummy, where H = 1 
reflects logging costs less than NOK 70/cbm and H = 2 reflects logging costs greater than 
NOK 150/cbm. 
The (30 should be interpreted as the mean price of the grades not considered and, thus, 
the estimates of the (3j 's represent the deviation from the intercept. Consequently, a positive 
value of a (3i means that the price of a specific grade is higher than the intercept and a 
negative value that the price is lower. The estimate of (3r, on the other hand, indicates 
how much the net stumpage value changes when the total volume increases one m3 . The 
estimates of the (3jk 's represent the deviation of the net stumpage value in a particular 
region for a particular grade. We have only let the most important grades (i.e. spruce 
grades) vary between regions. 
Results 
Descriptive statistics for the different variables over regions are reported in Table 5.1. 
Region III had the highest mean net stumpage value (NSV) while region I had the lowest. 
There were, however, huge individual differences as can be seen from the relatively large 
standard deviations, especially in region IV. Norway spruce (Picea abies (L.) Karst.) was 
the dominant species, but also Scots pine (Pinus sylvestris (L.)) were important in region 
I and IV. Non-coniferous timber was of limited importance. Mean volume was highest in 
region I with 444 m3 , which was approximately 100 m3 higher than the other regions. Large 
individual differences were reflected by the large standard deviations. 
Only a very limited number of the tracts had logging costs less than NOK 70 per m3 
(0.5% and 0.4% in region III and IV, respectively). High logging costs were more common, 
especially in region IV where 15% of the tracts had costs greater than NOK 150 per m3 .

Initially OLS regressions were conducted; thereafter we applied the iterative weighed 
least squares where White's estimator was applied to start the iterations; lastly the multi-
plicative heteroscedastic model was applied where, following Hardie & Larson (1994). Only 
significant parameter estimates were included in the error structure (the scedastic function). 
The results from the different estimations of the hedonic pricing model are presented in 
Table 5.2. All significant parameter estimates have the same sign in the different models; 
the magnitude of the estimates is also surprisingly similar, although the OLS estimates di-
verge somewhat. The Jarque-Bera normality statistics indicate that there were substantial 
problems regarding non-normality in the OLS model. This was mainly due to excess kurto-
sis, but also some skewness. Normality could not, however, be rejected in the IWLS model. 
Homoscedasticity could not be rejected when testing the squares of the errors against the 
squares of the dependent variables. However, the Breusch-Pagan statistics (not reported) 
showed severe problems with both the OLS and the IWLS models. This was to a certain 
degree overcome in the multiplicative heteroscedastic model, but homoscedasticity could 
still be rejected. As the parameter estimates for the scedastic function show, all sawlog 
grades, the special/veneer grade pine as well as the total volume were significant and thus 
affected the variance. 
The coefficients of dummy variables for regions III and IV were significant and positive, 
indicating, on average, higher NPV than region II. Moreover, the coefficient for total volume 
was found significant and positive supporting a hypothesis of increased NPV with harvest 
size (economies of scale in logging). 
Based on the log likelihood we proceeded with the IWLS model. The slope coefficients 
of first grade spruce was lesser in all regions II-IV compared to region I, and thus, the 
marginal gain from increasing quantity of this grade was lower. The slope coefficient of 
second grade spruce was also lesser in regions II and IV compared to region I, meaning that 
the marginal loss is larger than in regions I and III. Furthermore, the slope coefficient of 
pulpwood spruce was larger (i.e. the marginal loss was larger) in regions III and IV and 
lesser in region II, as compared to region I. 
The results from the price decomposition procedure based on the WLS model are re-
ported in Table 5.3. They suggest that there were substantial differences in both physical 
characteristics as well as the valuation of the characteristics. The latter was supposed to 
be reflected by differences in logging costs. 
The last row of Table 5.3 indicates that the total differences between all regions II-IV

vs. region I were positive. This means that, compared to region I, the stumpage values are 
NOK 13, 57 and 20 higher per m3 in region II, III and IV, respectively. With the exception 
of region III, the difference in NSV was mainly due to valuation. 
A subject of particular interest is how much of the difference could be captured in the 
quality of Norway spruce timber. In order to analyse this we set the proportion of each 
grade as a share of total spruce proportion in the average tract, i.e. dividing the proportion 
of each grade in a specific region with the proportion of spruce in this region. The results 
from this decomposition are presented in Table 5.4. 
From Table 5.2 we see that first grade spruce was positive, while the other grades 
were negative. Consequently, a positive value in the columns of Table 5.4 means that the 
proportion of first grade spruce was higher and the proportion of the other grades was lower 
than in region I, i.e. the quality of the timber was better. 
From the last row in Table 5.4 we see that the overall spruce quality was worse in 
region II as compared with region I, slightly better in region IV and substantially better in 
region III. In region II a lower proportion of first grade spruce and a higher proportion of

Discussion 
This paper has provided a framework for analysing individual harvests by region, season and 
size that decomposes differences between regions. It has also examined issues concerning 
methodological problems such as non-normality and heteroscedasticity. Both the iterative 
weighed least squares (IWLS) and the multiplicative heteroscedastic model turned out 
superior to ordinary least squares estimation. This underlines the importance of taking the 
sample properties into account. The sample in this study was thick-tailed and the regressors 
significantly affected the variance. Consequently, tests based on the results may have been 
biased. The IWLS model performed relatively better than the two others. Therefore, we 
used it in the price decomposition procedure. This procedure made it possible to decompose 
regional effects on net stumpage values into differences due to characteristics and differences 
due to valuation of the characteristics. 
Based on the Mj!llsen-region data the estimates showed substantial regional differences. 
These differences were mainly caused by differences due to valuation. In the present data 
set this reflects mainly differences due to logging costs. For region III part of the total 
difference could also be explained by quality differences. Looking at Norway spruce more 
specifically, the gain from increased quality in this region stands for an average of NOK 22 
per m3 in addition to gains from higher valuation. 
Surprisingly we could not find the summer season dummy significant, meaning that the 
NSV of the summer harvests does not deviate significantly from that of the winter harvests. 
Consequently, if this is the case in general, two contradictory reasons for reduced supply 
of timber during the summer season (which is one of the largest concerns of the forest 
industry) can be put forward. The explanation may be found in models that incorporate 
forest owners' objectives because the recreation aspect may have a larger significance during 
the summer season, thus increasing the opportunity costs of harvesting. On the other hand, 
reduced supply may also be due to pure economic adjustments, in that fewer areas are 
suitable for logging as a result of reduced accessibility (meaning higher logging costs) or 
higher environmental claims because the ground is not frozen. These are important factors 
needed to understand fluctuation in timber supply left for future research. 
The coefficients of total volume were significant in all models indicating economics 
of scale in production. The magnitude of these coefficients was, however, relatively low 
(NOK 0.40-0.45 per m3). The net gain from increasing the size of the harvests is therefore 
minimal. This finding is interesting from the point of view that smaller clear-cuts are more 
environmental friendly than larger. Again, if our findings can be generalised, moderate 
69

--- Page 77 ---

rather than large-scale harvests should have minimal impact on the economy of a particular 
harvest. Thus, the possibility that the net social gain of reducing the harvest size is greater 
than the economic loss may be large. 
The large negative slope for pulpwood of pine (i.e., intercept + pulpwood pine) was 
due to the fact that the predicted NSV reduced rapidly with an increase in the proportion 
of pulpwood pine and ultimately took values less than zero. Nevertheless, observed NSV 
could never take values lower than 30 NOK per m3 in the restricted data set. The correct 
curvature of pine pulpwood may therefore be represented by some logarithmic transforma-
tion. Future research should look into the matters of transformation of data and functional 
specifications. 
The findings of the present study call for a closer look on how bonus arrangements 
could be further "customised"in order to reach a confident mix of species and qualities. 
Initially, there is a need to determine whether the species, quality and period distribution 
are satisfactory. If not, there seems to be most to gain in reducing the effects of regional 
differences, and then moving attention towards pulpwood and pine timber. 
Further work should attempt to provide more knowledge on how contracts can be used 
to ensure efficient harvest behaviour, reduce uncertainty and, thus, to increase the benefits 
throughout the value-chain. Moreover if sufficient data become available, the literature on 
harvesting behaviour should be used (e.g. Ryberg & Holthausen (1990); Kuuluvainen et al. 
(1996)) ownership characteristics should be incorporated into the model.

excellent research facilities. We are grateful also to John Claxton for shaping up the English. Funding

Afforestation as a real option: Choosing 
among options 
Bo J. THORSEN AND NIKOLAJ MALCHOW-M0LLER 
Abstract 
The theory of real options has received a considerable interest in recent years. At the core of the 
literature has been a so-called single-option model, i.e. a model where the decision-maker must 
decide the timing of a real investment given stochastic returns of the single asset in question 
and where the investment is a sunk cost. This paper extends the field of real-options theory by 
analysing the case where the decision-maker must decide on the timing of an investment facing 
the choice between two mutually exclusive real options with stochastic and possibly correlated 
returns. The paper presents a simple two-period model, outlines a continuous-time model, and 
presents some results of a numerical solution procedure for the case of afforestation. 
Keywords 
Real options, Brownian motions, stochastic control, dynamic programming 
Introduction 
The question of choosing some particular land use over another is a core problem for 
landowners and managers. Critical characteristics of this decision problem are: High initial 
costs of establishing the new land use, a certain degree of indivisibility and irreversibility 
in the decision, and a considerable amount of uncertainty related to the benefits expected 
to arise from the land uses in question. 
In their seminal studies, Henry (1974); Arrow & Fisher (1974) treated a classical case of 
land-use decisions under uncertainty. The latter study formulated the idea of a quasi option 
value related to the option to postpone an irreversible decision in order to resolve at least 
part of the uncertainty related to the future returns. Later studies on land-use decisions 
under uncertainty include among many others Zinkhan (1991); Thomsen (1992). They both 
discuss the use of the Black-Scholes formula (Black & Scholes, 1973) for valuing the option 
to harvest a forest and replace it with another land use, where the value of harvesting 
the forest is stochastic. Reed (1993) studies the option to replace an old-growth forest 
with another land use, the value of both being stochastic. In a similar study, Abildtrup & 
Strange (1999) analyse the decision to harvest a forest stand, which produces externalities 
of uncertain future values, and replace it with an intensive production, the value of which is 
also stochastic. Thorsen (1999) analyses the effects of afforestation subsidies by taking into 
consideration that afforestation may be considered a real option on an uncertain investment. 
All of these studies address the problem of replacing an existing land use with another 
in a situation where the value of one, or both, of these is stochastic. If only one value 
is stochastic, the problem becomes very similar, if not identical, to the core models of 
McDonald & Siegel (1986); Dixit & Pindyck (1994). If both are stochastic, a homogeneity 
property of the value function in the state variables can usually be invoked. This property 
allows for a reformulation of the problem, making it possible to retain the same solution 
form as in the case with only one stochastic factor. We used this approach in another 
extension of the real-options theory, taking into account the effects of repeated or infinite 
chains of real options (Malchow-M¢ller & Thorsen, 2000). 
The present paper, on the other hand, addresses the question of replacing an existing 
land use, which yields a certain return, with either of two mutually exclusive land-use 
alternatives, the value of which are both stochastic. This problem is inherently different 
73 
F. Helles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 73-80. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 80 ---

from the above mentioned studies as the state space cannot be reduced to a single dimension. 
The empirical relevance of the problem is obvious, extending beyond land-use decisions. 
Relevant problems in a forestry context are, e.g., the choice of tree species and the choice 
between development, afforestation, and preservation. Apparently, the problem of choosing 
between mutually exclusive options has not been addressed in the real-options literature 
before. The closest related ideas are found in the financial literature on derivatives, where 
numerical methods to evaluate a finitely lived option on the minimum or maximum of 
two assets have been researched by Stultz (1982). The paper is structured as follows. In 
Section 2, a simple two-period example is presented to illustrate the general idea and the 
basic intuition. Section 3 outlines a continuous-time model and describes some analytical 
properties of the problem. Since the problem cannot be solved analytically, a numerical 
solution is needed. Numerical results are presented and interpreted in Section 4 using 
afforestation as a specific example. Section 5 concludes the paper. 
A Simple Example 
This Section presents a simple two-period example to build some basic intuition for the 
results that follow. To begin, we repeat the solution to the case with only a single investment 
option, and then extend it to the case of two mutually exclusive, but possibly correlated, 
options. 
Consider an agent who has a unit of land which can be developed into some use. The 
development is assumed to be irreversible and to take place instantaneously once decided 
upon. The cost of development (the investment) is given by I, and the land use will produce 
one unit of output in the current and each future period. A unit of output in period T earns 
the price P7 , T ~ t. In the current period (period t), the price Pt is known. Future prices, 
however, are uncertain at timet. Specifically:

For an investment to be optimal, the expected NPV in Equation 6.2 should be positive. 
This implies that the investment should be undertaken in period t if and only if Pt exceeds 
or equals 1,000. 
It is by now well known that this approach ignores the value of waiting. Since in this 
example all uncertainty is resolved in the next period, postponing the investment decision 
to period t + 1 implies that the landowner has the option not to undertake the investment 
if the price drops to zero. The cost of this is that he loses the income, Pt, in the current 
period. Taking this into account, the relevant criterion for investing in period t becomes:

where the left-hand side is the expected value of investing in period t from (6.2), whereas 
the right-hand side is the expected value of postponing the decision to period t + 1. Thus, 
the investment should only be undertaken for values of Pt ~ 11,000. The option to wait is 
seen to have a dramatic effect on the investment criterion. 
The current study expands this classic problem by considering the case of two mutually 
exclusive land-use options, each with a stochastic value. Exclusivity implies that only one of

the land uses can be implemented. Thus, the landowner faces the choice between land use A 
producing one unit with the stochastic price ptA, and land use B with unit production value 
ptB. Future prices are assumed to be given by the following joint probability distribution:

into account that the investment costs, JA and JB, may differ. Assume for now that the

which holds given the assumptions in (6.5) and (6.6). For prices above ptA•, A should be 
implemented already in period t. The stopping rule in (6.8) increases in ptB and JA, and 
decreases in JB. A higher JA increases the value of postponing an investment in A, exactly 
as in the single-option case. The first term in (6.8) is simply the stopping rule from the 
single-option case. A higher Pl or a lower JB, on the other hand, increase the value of 
waiting by making land use B potentially more attractive in the future. This is captured 
by the last two terms in (6.8). Note that the effect is more pronounced with a negative 
correlation between the prices of A and B. With negative correlation, the uncertainty 
today about the future relative values of the land uses is higher, making it less attractive 
to exercise the option today. Consider, e.g., the case where r = 0.05, JA = JB = 21,000 
and ptB = 1, 000, the latter being the stopping rule for the NPV-case above. In this case, 
the stopping rule for A is Pl* = 18,500 if p = 0, and ptA• = 26,000 if p= -1. Compared to 
the single-option rule of 11,000, this is an increase of 68% in the stopping rule by adding 
an uncorrelated option with a current price of only 1,000. The increase is 136% in the 
case of perfectly negative correlation. Figure 6.1 shows the optimal stopping rule, ptA• as 
a function of Pl and p. 
The example of this Section is overtly simplified. Nevertheless, the example pinpoints 
the potentially major consequences of shifting focus from decision making under uncertainty 
with single options towards decision making under uncertainty with two or more mutually 
exclusive options. In the following Sections we outline the consequences for the general case 
of continuous infinite time. 
75

The Model 
In this Section, the general optimal stopping problem for the continuous-time case is formu-
lated. Consider a situation in which a decision-maker has a unit of land currently producing 
a constant return R per unit of time. At any time, he has the option to turn the land into 
one of two alternative uses, which are mutually exclusive and irreversible. The present val-
ues of these alternative land uses evolve stochastically over time. More specifically, assume 
that exercising the first option costs Cp and yields a (present value) return ofF, whereas 
exercising the second option is associated with the cost Co and yields a return of D. It is 
assumed that Cp and Co are positive constants, and that F and Devolve according to the 
following Brownian motions: 
dF = /-LFF + i7pFdz 
dD = !-LoD + 170 Ddw ' 
(6.9) 
where /-LF , /-LD, 17p, and 17D are the parameters of the respective processes, and dz and dw 
are the increments of two Wiener processes, possibly correlated with E(dzdw) = pdt. 
Assume that the landowner maximises the expected net present value of his land-use 
options, and that he is able to observe at each point in time the states, F and D, of the 
two processes in (6.9). The optimal expected net present value, V(F, D) , of this land-use 
problem is therefore given by the following Bellman equation: 
V(F, D)= max { F- Cp, D- C0 , Rdt + (1- odt)-1 E [V(F + dF, D + dD)]} 
(6.10) 
where o is the risk-adjusted discount rate. The expression in (6.10) says that the optimal 
value is obtained by either immediately exercising one of the options or by continuing to 
keep the options alive for at least one more short time interval, dt. Applying Ito's Lemma 
(Malliaris & Brock, 1982) to expand the expectation on the right-hand side and letting dt 
go to zero, we obtain: 
(6.11) 
This partial differential equation must be satisfied for all points, (F, D), belonging to the 
continuation region. The usual boundary conditions exist, i.e. the standard value-matching 
and smooth-pasting conditions. Unfortunately, this is not enough to allow for an analytical 
solution. Thus, we solve the problem numerically. 
76

--- Page 83 ---

Before turning to the method and results of the numerical study, we note that because 
zero is an absorbing state for either of the state variables, the values V(F,O) and V(O,D) 
are well known from the single-option case and given by: 
with associated stopping rules: 
V(F, 0) = ApFf3F + ¥ 
V(O, D) = AvDf3v + ¥ 
F*(D = 0) = 13!:_1 ( ¥CF) D*(F = 0) = !3~~ 1 ( ¥Cv) 
(6.12) 
(6.13) 
where the parameters, Ap, Av, f3F and f3v, are as in Dixit & Pindyck (1994). However, 
with two non-zero variables, even the fairly stylised set-up above becomes too complex for 
an analytical solution. 
The numerical approach 
For the problem to be solvable, the value function must be finite. In a more technical 
working paper, Thorsen & Malchow-M¢ller (2001), we show the conditions under which 
this holds. While this is typically a trivial issue in the single-option case, it becomes non-
trivial with two options, since the expected change in value depends on the maximum of 
two not perfectly correlated assets. Furthermore, it is shown that the stopping rule is given 
by two boundaries, F*(D) and D*(F). The F*(D)-boundary starts on the F-axis in the 
point F * (D = 0) from (6.13), and is increasing in D. Similarly, the D*(F)-boundary 
starts in D*(F = 0). The two boundaries will never meet, but will converge asymptotically 
towards the line given by F- Cp = D- Cv. If Cp = Cv, this is simply the 45-degree line. 
We solve the problem using a value-function iteration procedure. To increase stability 
and iteration speed, the problem is log-normalised. The central gain is that the variance 
becomes independent of the state, thereby standardising the calculation of the joint prob-
ability distribution. The problem has been solved for a number of different parameter 
combinations. In this paper, however, we focus on an illustrative empirical case treated 
earlier in the literature in a single-option framework. 
Numerical results 
In Thorsen (1999), the afforestation decision is investigated as a real single-option using the 
standard single-option approach. In Denmark, however, the choice of tree species is fairly 
free on most locations due to favourable ecological conditions. Hence, the true problem 
facing the forest owner is really a case of multiple mutually exclusive options. Since we 
are breaking new ground here and furthermore must take due consideration to the usual 
dimensionality problems of dynamic programming, we will analyse the decision problem as 
a case of two mutually exclusive options. The empirical estimates of relevant parameters 
are shown in Table 1.

Beech or oak? 
In Figure 6.2, we illustrate the value of waiting, defined as V(F,D)- max{F-Cp, D-Co} , 
for the case of beech vs. oak over part of the state space. The parameters are as in Table 
6.1 for the relevant species, except that the cost parameter is scaled to 0.2 ha to improve 
numerical accuracy. The continuation region, where the value of waiting is positive, is the 
bright area in Figure 6.2. The dark area in the upper part of the Figure, where beech 
return is high, is the stopping region for beech. The border between this region and the 
continuation region is thus the stopping boundary for beech. Similarly for oak in the lower 
half of the Figure.

Figure 6.2: The value of waiting V(b, o)- max(b- Cb , o - C0 ) for the two exclusive options, beech 
and oak afforestation. For the interval below zero, the value of waiting is exactly zero 
We see how the stopping boundaries approach each other asymptotically as the returns 
increase. The continuation region narrows down, but never disappears. Hence, when 
stopping values are close to each other, it is always worthwhile to keep the options alive. 
We also see how the optimal stopping rule for, say, beech depends strongly on the current 
return of oak. Only as the return on oak goes to zero (outside the Figure), will the stopping 
rule for beech approach its single-option rule. This, of course, has significant effects on the 
value of holding the option. Thus, when more than one land-use alternative is available, the 
single-option approach may strongly underestimate the value of the true composite option, 
the value of waiting, and the optimal investment rule. For example, V(b = 25, o = 0) = 13, 
which is equivalent to the single-option value and belongs to the stopping region. However, 
V(b = 25, o = 22) = 14.5, and continuation is now optimal. In this case, the single-option 
approach underestimates the value by approximately 10%, even though the return on beech 
is unchanged and the return on oak is more than 10% lower. 
Spruce or oak? 
In Figure 6.3, the choice between spruce and oak species is evaluated over the same part 
of the state space as in Figure 6.2. We see a much smaller continuation region in this case. 
The reason is in particular the fact that Pso is higher. A high positive correlation implies 
that the advantages related to having two investment options rather than one are reduced, 
cf. the simple example of Section 2, and hence positive correlation reduce stopping rules 
and values of keeping the options alive. 
When you choose to continue holding the options, it is because your expected gain is 
larger than the cost. With two competing options, it is the expected change in the maximum 
of the two stopping returns, which is the relevant gain. When the returns from stopping are 
close, as around the line o- C0 = s- C8 , and changes in returns are negatively correlated, 
78

--- Page 85 ---

0 0.00-3,00 
• -3,00-Q,OO 
Oak \'OIIK', 1,000 DKK/0.2 ha 
Figure 6.3: The value of waiting V(b, o)- max(o - C0 , s- C8 ) for the two exclusive options, oak and 
spruce afforestation. For the interval below zero, the value of waiting is exactly zero 
you are almost sure that the maximum will increase. This explains why continuation 
is optimal near the line o - C0 = s - Cs and why negative correlation further induces 
continuation. Furthermore, in the particular case, when moving from Figure 6.2 to 6.3 the 
beech is replaced with spruce. From Table 6.1 we see that Cs < Cb , and both of these 
will themselves decrease size of the continuation region. The first, because that means 
investment is cheaper and hence the proceeds of stopping now increase, the latter because 
it reduces the potential gains from waiting. 
In the current numerical examples none of the options are correlated negatively. How-
ever, we saw in the simple example of Section 2 that negative correlation will have a strong 
positive effect on the stopping rule and hence the value of the composite option. That 
negative correlation is good for the value of a portfolio of assets is a well-known result in 
economic theory. It is also well documented empirically. The effect stems from the fact 
that the negative correlation decreases the variance of the portfolio yield. The decreased 
variance has a value, because the valuation is performed under the assumption of risk aver-
sion. The current paper, however, values a portfolio of assets - real investment options -
under the assumption of risk neutrality. Thus, the value increase is not due to risk re-
duction. Instead, it is entirely due to an improved probability of increasing the expected 
yield by continuing to hold the options alive. The increased yield results from the fact 
that the expected maximum of changes in two stochastic assets is inversely related to their 
correlation. 
Concluding remarks 
This paper represents an extension of the research in real options as it analyses the problem 
of choosing between two indivisible, irreversible, uncertain, and mutually e~clusive invest-
ment options. We discuss and analyse the problem in the set-up of a land-use decision and 
use a forestry example. 
Land-use decisions under uncertainty have been studied before, but only cases consider-
ing the problem of replacing an existing land use with one alternative use. Decision-makers 
often have more than one investment option available, and by exercising any of them, they 
sacrifice all other options. The present paper has analysed the implications of this for 
optimal behaviour. 
The numerical results and the simple example illustrated how both the optimal stopping 
rules and the value of the options drastically change when we take into consideration the 
presence of competing options. This is the primary and most important result of the 
79

--- Page 86 ---

study. A more specific and interesting aspect of this is how the correlation between the 
stochastic variables affects the optimal decisions. It was found in a simple example that 
as the correlation becomes increasingly negative, the value of the composite investment 
option as well as the optimal stopping rules for both options increase. The numerical 
afforestation example also illustrated how the continuation region drastically shrinks as 
correlation increases slightly. Further work should explore in more depth the effects of 
varying parameter combinations. It is important to stress, however, that the gains from 
negative correlation do not arise from risk aversion, but can be entirely attributed to the 
'value of waiting'-principle, i.e. the option to wait and see if things turn out to the better 
and to do nothing should they turn out to the worse.
