The value of urban afforestation: 
A hedonic pricing case 
SIGNE ANTHON 
Abstract 
The Danish forest cover is rather low, only about 12%, and yet it is thoroughly documented that 
the Danish forests are extensively used for recreation purposes. It is believed, but only rarely 
documented, that the value of forest externalities is significant. The aim of the present research 
is to quantify the part of these benefits, which is reflected in property values for houses close 
to afforestation areas. We use the hedonic pricing method and data from two residential areas 
to calculate WTP-measures for two afforestation projects. Proximity to afforestation areas is 
found to have a significant effect on property values, implying considerable willingness-to-pay. 
Keywords 
Econometrics, environmental valuation, environmental benefits, hedonic pricing, afforesta-
tion 
Introduction 
A doubling of the forest area in Denmark has been on the political agenda since 1989. In 
the period 1989-2001 the Forest and Nature Agency initiated large afforestation projects, 
especially as urban forests in areas having few alternative recreational possibilities. With 
the increase in public expenditure on afforestation a need to evaluate the environmental 
benefits from afforestation projects has developed. 
The aim of this analysis is to examine the inhabitants' willingness to pay for proximity 
to urban afforestation projects. The method used is hedonic pricing, which estimates the 
value of environmental benefits through the analysis of house prices. Houses are composite 
goods and the selling price reflects the characteristics of the house such as size, age and 
building materials. Furthermore, the price reflects locational features such as proximity to 
public facilities and recreational areas. Therefore this method is suitable for estimating the 
recreational and aesthetic value of urban afforestation (Tyrviiinen & Miettinen, 2000). 
The hedonic pricing method has a long theoretical background, but applications are 
few in the field of urban forest and natural amenities. In Denmark, a study of existent 
urban forests was conducted in 2000, valuing four different forests (Damgaard & Erichsen, 
2000). A significant value of the forest was found in three cases, varying with distance from 
the forest edge. The method has also been used for valuation of forest benefits in other 
countries (e.g. Tyrvainen & Miettinen (2000); Powe et al. (1997); Garrod & Willis (1992)), 
showing a significant positive value of proximity to forests. 
This study differs from the above, as the valued object are two afforestation areas. 
Damgaard & Erichsen (2000) do include an afforestation project, where they estimate a 
significant increase of house prices, starting already in the planning phase of the afforesta-
tion project and increasing after the establishment of the forest. 
The current study also includes a small interview survey of the inhabitants of the two 
housing areas. The objective is to support the findings of the hedonic pricing analysis, 
especially by examining the recreational use of the afforestation areas. 
The paper is organised as follows: In the next section the methodology and central 
parts of the theory of hedonic pricing are briefly described. Following that, the case areas 
and subsequently data are described. The statistical model and other issues of the data 
analysis are described before finally, results are reported for the econometric part of the 
81 
F. Helles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 81-90. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 88 ---

study as well as the interview survey. Lastly, a discussion of results and some concluding 
remarks are presented. 
Methods and material 
A house is a composite good and the price can be described by the following general hedonic 
price function: 
P; ""P(Si,Ni,Qi) 
(7.1) 
Here Pi is the price, Si is the structural characteristics, N; is the neighbourhood char-
acteristics and Qi is the environmental characteristics of the i'th house. Structural charac-
teristics are physical characteristics, e.g. size and age, and neighbourhood characteristics 
describe the location and social qualities. The environmental characteristics are primarily 
non-market goods such as noise and recreational possibilities (Freeman, 1993). 
The aim of this study is to determine the value of the afforestation projects for the 
nearby neighbourhoods. As the value is determined from the house prices, the value esti-
mated is only locational use value, primarily local recreational and aesthetic values. Non-use 
values are not included since they do not depend upon use of the forest. Use values which 
are not dependent on the preferences of the inhabitants of the nearby housing area are not 
covered either, e.g. the recreational value for people coming from other areas (Tyrviiinen 
& Miettinen, 2000). 
To estimate the hedonic price function the following assumptions are made: 
• The house market is a perfect market, implying the presence of a large number of 
buyers and sellers, full information and no transaction costs or slack in the market 
prices. Thus, house prices directly reflect the buyers' preferences (Legget & Bockstael, 
2000) 
• Weak separability: The marginal value of each house characteristic is independent of 
the value of other characteristics. This assures that the value of the environmental 
good can be estimated separately and interpreted as the marginal willingness-to-pay 
for the good (Palmquist, 1991, 1992) 
• Stability over time: Hedonic pricing studies often include house sales in different years 
(e.g. Garrod & Willis (1992); Tyrviiinen & Miettinen (2000); Damgaard & Erichsen 
(2000)). To be able to estimate the price function it is necessary to assume that the 
house market and buyers' preferences are stable over time (Palmquist, 1992). This 
is a reasonable assumption in the short run, while in the long run the price function 
fluctuates. Data from 1980 to 2001 is used in this study. 
• One house market: The size of a house market is often discussed in hedonic pricing 
studies (Palmquist, 1992). It is assumed that in each housing area in the present 
study there is only one house market, as the areas are small. 
• Changes in the environmental quality do not make the hedonic price function shift: 
The houses which change prices as a result of the afforestation can still be included 
in the hedonic price function. This assumption is not violated as long as the studied 
good is a localised externality, only influencing a small number of houses and not the 
entire market (Palmquist, 1992). 
Case area 
Two afforestation projects have been selected: a project in Jutland called True Skov near 
the northwestern part of Arhus, the second largest city in Denmark, and a project on 
Zealand just south of Vemmelev, called Vemmelev Skov. The criteria for choosing the 
areas were primarily: 
82

--- Page 89 ---

• The age of the afforestation project, to make sure that there was enough data both 
before and after the afforestation. 
• That the forest was situated near a housing area, to make the data set as large as 
possible. 
• That the housing area was as homogenous as possible, making the variation in prices 
smaller. 
True Skov 
The afforestation area is rather large (see Figure 7.1). The planting of the forest began in 
1994 and until now 101 ha have been planted. The species are almost entirely broadleaves 
with beech and oak as the most common. In the oldest part of the afforestation area, most 
of the trees are still less then 2 metres, but some species like common alder and birch are 
3-4 metres high. The younger part near True is 1-2 metres high. 
Figure 7.1: Map of Skjoldh¢jparken and True Skov. The afforestation area is indicated by the checked 
pattern 
The nearby residential district, Skjoldhojparken in Tilst, was mainly developed through 
the 1970's and is a very typical suburban area in Denmark. It consists primarily of detached 
single-storied houses. In the old village of True there is a more diverse neighbourhood with 
a mixture of farms, old village houses and new houses. There are app. 7,000 residents in 
Skjoldhojparken and 200 residents in True. The access to the afforestation area is easy. 
The road separating the housing area from the forest is not heavily trafficked and there is 
underpass at two different places. The afforestation area can be seen from the houses across 
the road. No other recreational areas are found close to Skjoldhojparken. The surroundings 
of Skjoldhojparken consist of agricultural land to the west, and smaller industrial / com-
mercial areas to the north and south. Further to the east and south lies the rest of Arhus. 
There are some small areas of open green space for recreational use, but of no importance. 
Vemmelev Skov 
Vemmelev is a small provincial town in the municipality of Korsor in the southwestern part 
of Zealand. The afforestation area is small; app. 60 ha. As in True Skov, the main species 
are beech and oak, but the trees are smaller, not many reaching 2 metres as the forest was 
planted in 1996. The town has app. 3,000 residents. A large motorway runs right through 
the town, and the traffic is very heavy. There is a railway through the northern part of 
the town (Figure 7.2). The houses are of different ages and styles, and the housing area is 
much more heterogeneous than Skjoldhojparken. The area around Vemmelev is intensively 
cultivated agricultural land with very few recreational opportunities. The afforestation area 
83

--- Page 90 ---

is situated south of the town. The trees nearest to the town are planted on a hill facing 
south and sloping downwards from the motorway, which means that most of the forest 
cannot be seen from the town. 
Figure 7.2: Map of Vemmelev Skov. The afforestation area is indicated by the checked pattem 
Data 
The data used is collected by Kommune Data, a central housing register, and includes 
selling prices, structural characteristics and neighbourhood characteristics. The data com-
prises only owner-occupied, one-family houses used for habitation (multi-storey houses are 
included). Houses older than 1900 are excluded, and so are a number of house sales that 
had not complete or credible records. To ensure a reasonable number of house sales for the 
analysis, it was necessary to compile data from a reasonably long period, i.e. 1980 to 2001. 
The conversion of prices into present value is based on a housing price index made by the 
Danish tax authority (Told og Skat, 2001 ). In total, the sample comprised 233 houses in 
Vemmelev and 598 houses in Skjoldhojparken. 
The data from Kommune Data had a wide range of different structural variables. For 
the initial analyses, 20 variables were chosen, describing different characteristics of the 
house, such as size, building materials, number of rooms, and age. 
The environmental attribute, proximity to forest, must be modelled with a proxy for the 
utility the inhabitants get from the forest. Probably, the greatest values to the inhabitants 
are recreational value and aesthetics (Tyrviiinen & Miettinen, 2000). Since we want to 
capture the differences in value between inhabitants due to difference in the environmental 
variable, distance to the forest from each house is chosen as variable. It measures primarily 
the recreational opportunities, which can be expected to vary with distance. The distance is 
measured as the crow flies because it is a measure easily obtained in GIS. A better measure 
would be the distance along streets and paths, but as they are many in both areas and the 
forest is open from different directions (no fences or other obstacles), the direct distance 
is an acceptable proxy. Still, a number of modelling options exists and here two different 
ways are tested. 
1. A continuous variable, 'Distance', the reciprocal value of the distance to the forest, 
for houses sold after the afforestation. 
2. A discrete binary variable, 'Forest', a dummy variable for all houses within 600 metres 
of the forest, for houses sold after the afforestation. 
84

--- Page 91 ---

As there is no recreational or aesthetic value of the forest before the afforestation, the 
environmental value is zero for house sales before the afforestation. 
In the case of Vemmelev two neighbourhood variables were included: a district and 
a traffic variable. Vemmelev can be divided into two districts, since the town consists of 
two old villages on each side of the motorway, Vemmelev and Forlev. The average house 
prices in Forlev are lower than those in Vemmelev, and to capture this difference a dummy 
variable was included in the analysis. It was also necessary to include a variable for the 
houses adjacent to the motorway or the railway passing through Vemmelev, as the price of 
these houses is affected by traffic noise. 
Data analysis 
All the explanatory variables were included in a linear model for both areas. Variables 
were omitted from the analysis on the basis of t-statistics, the variables with the lowest 
t-statistics being excluded first. Multicollinearity was examined in both data sets, leading 
to the exclusion of some variables. Table 7.1 shows the variables included in the remaining 
analysis.

Explanation

Table 7.1: Explanatory variables included in the analysis 
The functional form of the hedonic price function is not prescribed by the theory, as 
the definition of the hedonic price function in (7.1) does not imply any restrictions to the 
functional form, except for the linearity implied by the assumption of weak separability. 
How to determine the functional form is often discussed in the literature (Halvorsen & 
Pollakowski, 1981; Cropper et al., 1988; Palmquist, 1991). 
Box & Cox (1964) describe a useful method for comparing possible transformations 
using likelihood ratio tests. This method is used in the present study, combined with 
examination of data and residual plots. The following linear Box-Cox model is used:

The distance variable Z4 is not transformed. The other variables including the price are 
transformed by A. Dj is dummy variables j = [1, 2, 3, 4), which vary between the two areas.

To avoid transformation of the environmental variable in the tests of Box-Cox-trans-

In the data set for True Skov, the result of the maximum likelihood estimation of (7.2) 
was .A = 0.58, in Vemmelev .A = 0.24. For Vemmelev it was tested whether the log-linear 
form (.A=O) was appropriate. The likelihood ratio test value was 1.89 and therefore smaller 
than the critical value 5.99 at the 0.05-level, and the log-linear form could not be rejected. 
In the True model, the likelihood ratio test of .A= 0 was rejected at 0.05 level (LR=l0.84). 
Instead the untransformed A=1 was tested and could not be rejected at 0.05level (LR=2.2). 
However, residual plots for the True model showed that the assumption of constant residuals 
did not hold for the untransformed model, since the residuals increased with the predicted 
price. Thus, the underlying assumptions concerning residuals are not fulfilled and the 
likelihood ratio test is questionable. Therefore, the log-linear transformation was used as 
it showed adequate statistical properties. Furthermore, the dependent variable was plotted 
against the quantitative variables to examine the functional connection between the two. 
It was decided not to transform the age variable independently in the True model, since 
the linear form was more suitable than the log-linear. 
The original data set includes house sales from 1980 to 2001. Predicted prices were 
plotted against year of sale to investigate the stability of prices over time. The visual 
investigation suggested that there was a structural shift in the period 1988-1989 in True, 
where prices were lower than in the rest of the period. Chow tests were used to determine 
whether the data set could be analysed in one model (Johnston, 1991). The test showed 
that it was not possible to analyse 1980-1990 in the same model as 1991-2001. The test 
was significant at 0.05 level (F=2.05). To solve the problem a dummy variable for all sales 
in 1988 and 1989 was included in the model. This shifted the predicted prices, and a new 
Chow test showed that the period 1984-2001 could be treated in one model. Therefore 
1980-1983 was omitted from the analysis, leaving 532 house sales in the data set. 
A similar shift was found in Vemmelev, but three years earlier 1985-86. A Chow test 
showed a shift in 1987 which is significant at 0.05 level (F=8.45). A dummy variable was 
included, but it was only significant at 0.10 level. Therefore, the period 1980-1986 was 
omitted and the data set reduced from 233 to 179 observations. 
The residuals were examined to detect any outliers. Five outliers were omitted in True 
and two in Vemmelev. 
Results 
Tables 2 and 3 show the two final log-linear models. Type A-models use the quantitative 
variable 'Distance' as the environmental variable and type B models use the dummy variable 
'Forest'. In both cases there is a significant positive effect of the afforestation. 
The quantitative variable in the final models are floor area, site size and age. The mod-
els for True include a dummy variable for the period 1988-89, whereas those for Vemmelev 
include the dummy variable traffic for houses adjacent to the motorway or railway. Vari-
ables omitted are number of bathrooms, building materials, the dummy for multi-storey 
houses and the district variable. A dummy for multi-storey houses was omitted in True 
because it correlated with the environmental variable, as many of the multi-storey houses 
are situated close to the forest edge. The other variables were excluded because they did 
not explain much of the variation, even though they were significant and did not influence 
the environmental variable. This makes the models as simple as possible. 
The two different ways of modelling the environmental variables influence the overall 
models very little. This is evident from the results, as the models show stability of the

The interview survey 
The intention of the survey was not to get independent results, but only to support the 
findings of the hedonic pricing model. 
The objective of the interview survey is to investigate the use of the forest and whether 
the inhabitants feel that the new forest has changed the neighbourhood. If the inhabi-
tants are willing to pay for proximity to afforestation projects, then the benefits from the 
afforestation projects must be a part of their preference set. Twelve inhabitants were inter-
viewed in Vemmelev and 13 in Skjoldh0jparken. The main result from the survey is that 
there are big differences in recreational use of the two afforestation projects. Most people 
in Skjoldh0jparken visit the forest several times each month or more often. The inhabitants 
of Vemmelev, on the contrary, are not visiting the forest very often. Five out of 12 have 
never visit the project, or do not even know that the state has planted the forest (Figure 
7.3). Four persons had only visited the forest once or twice since 1996.

Figure 7.3: Visiting frequency for the two afforestation projects 
This is a big difference between the behaviour of the inhabitants of the two areas. The 
rest of the survey shows the same tendency: the inhabitants of Vemmelev do not in general 
feel that the forest has made any difference to their everyday life and they feel that there 
are some serious barriers to their usage of the forest. In Skjoldh0jparken most people like 
the forest and feel that it makes a difference. The existent barriers are not very serious. 
Discussion and concluding remarks 
The estimated parameters associated with the quantitative variables can be interpreted as 
elasticities and the dummy variables as per cent of the total price. In True, the partial 
88

--- Page 95 ---

elasticity of house prices with respect to the floor area is 0.49 and 0.50, respectively. Hence 
holding other characteristics constant, a one-per cent change in floor area leads to 0.49 and 
0.50 per cent change in prices. The corresponding estimates for the site size are 0.111 and 
0.102. The age affects the prices negatively, with a price elasticity of 0.03, which means that 
new houses lose value fast, later the decrease in value slows down. The period dummy in 
the model for True Skov is significant and negative, and houses bought in 1988 or 1989 have 
11 per cent lower price. In Vemmelev, the price elasticity for the floor area is higher: 0.62, 
and the price elasticity for site size is lower: 0.09. The age parameter is not transformed 
but still negative. A change of one year in age results in a decrease in price of 0.007 and 
0.006 per cent, respectively. 
The interpretation of the environmental variable in the type A-models is complex, since 
both sides are transformed. The value of the forest depends on how far from the forest the 
house is situated, and this affects the price of the house (see Figure 7.4). In Vemmelev the 
parameter for the 'Distance'-variable is 4.9 and in True 14.0. This means that for a house 
300 metres away from the forest edge, the forest adds 1.6 per cent to its value if situated in 
Vemmelev and 4.7 per cent if in True. By construction, the environmental marginal value 
grows infinitely as the houses get closer to the forest. However, since the nearest houses are 
not very close to the forest edge this property is not a problem. In True, the closest houses 
are at least 150 metres away and the forest adds a value of 9.3 per cent. In Vemmelev, the 
house closest to the fQrest is 50 metres away and the forest adds a value of 9.8 per cent. 
Also by construction, the price of houses very far away is affected by the environmental 
variable. In Vemmelev, some houses are situated 2.5 km from the forest. To avoid the 
problem, the parameter is defined to be zero if the house is more than 1,000 metres away.

Figure 7.4: The estimated environmental value as a function of 'Distance ' from the forest 
The environmental variable in the type B-models is a dummy, and therefore the results 
can be interpreted as the percentage of the price of the house explained by the proximity 
to the afforestation project. In True, 0.041 means that people are willing to pay 4.1 per 
cent extra for a house close to the forest, in Vemmelev the figure is 6.3 per cent. 
The two different environmental variables show very different results, but nevertheless 
both models show a significant and positive result. Type A-models show a larger value of 
True Skov than Vemmelev Skov, whereas type B-models show the opposite. However, the 
interview survey indicates that the inhabitants of Skjoldhojparken value the forest more 
than the inhabitants of Vemmelev for the following reasons: 
• The visiting frequency is much higher 
• The inhabitants feel that the barriers to using the forest are few and not very limiting 
• The project is larger and there are more possibilities of nature experiences 
89

--- Page 96 ---

• The house prices are somewhat higher, which indicates that the inhabitants have a 
higher income 
• Recreation is in general viewed as a luxury good 
Therefore, the modelling of the environmental attribute with the continuous variable 
'Distance' is likely to be the best representation of the marginal values that proximity to an 
afforestation area has in the current two empirical case. Finally, it should be pointed out 
that the stability of the econometric results obtained in this study- across transformations 
and other modelling differences - is very reassuring with respect to the validity of the 
findings. 
It is safe to conclude that there is definitely a positive willingness-to-pay for afforestation 
projects in both areas. Furthermore, the higher willingness-to-pay in Skjoldh0jparken is 
likely to be explained in part by a recreationally better designed and larger afforestation 
project.

Comparison of multi-criteria optimisation 
techniques for selection of conservation 
areas 
NIELS STRANGE, VILIS BRUKAS, FINN HELLES AND PE-
TER TARP 
Abstract 
Performance of several optimisation models is compared, using genetic algorithms, simulated 
annealing, and mixed integer programming. They are applied to the multi-criteria problem of 
delineating conservation areas in a case forest in Lithuania, searching for the best trade-off 
between opportunity cost, connectivity, and proximity to swamps. The study indicates that, 
compared with the genetic algorithms and simulated annealing, mixed integer programming 
is time-inefficient for complex problem formulations that involve a large number of sites and 
consider spatial connectivity. The practical applicability of any of these techniques will to a high 
degree depend on the success of combating the professional isolationism within the science and 
practice of reserve selection. 
Keywords 
Conservation policy, genetic algorithms, simulated annealing, mixed integer programming, 
multi-criteria optimisation 
Introduction 
Despite the complexity of forestland zoning, the obvious trend is that the share of conser-
vation areas increases in most countries (Franklin, 1993; Janukonis, 1998). In Lithuania, 
different categories of conservation make up 14.6 per cent of the total forest area (Forest 
Inventory and Management Institute, 1998). Nature reserves of several thousand hectares 
each protect remnant swamp ecosystems with surrounding forests, while hundreds of small 
areas protect certain endangered species or valuable landscape elements. 
Objectives of biological conservation and biodiversity enhancement may be considered 
at various scales (Lindenmayer et al., 1999) and attained in different ways. For example, 
at stand level, through promotion of environmentally oriented silviculture (Franklin, 1988); 
at forest or landscape level, through setting aside of areas (Niemela, 1997); at country or 
regional level, through protecting large forest tracts or making core areas serve as kernels 
in bio-geocenotical networks (Pauliukevicius & Kenstavicius, 1995). 
Reserves are often designed to protect species of fauna and flora, and numerous stud-
ies have provided optimisation techniques as to fitting the largest number of rare species 
to the smallest area possible. Pioneering with Kirkpatrick (1983), this problem was often 
addressed by iterative selection algorithms, however, criticised mainly for inability to appro-
priately use information contained in previous iterations. This deficiency was remedied by 
'greedy heuristics', e.g. Bedward et al. (1992), but the sub-optimality of solutions remains 
a point of concern. Some authors advocate integer programming techniques that guarantee 
optimality (Underhill, 1994; Camm et al., 1996; Rodrigues et al., 2000; Rodrigues & Gaston, 
2002). Others contend that integer formulations might be intractable in practice, optimal 
solutions would take too long to obtain while heuristics allow achieving good solutions with 
lesser time inputs (Pressey et al., 1996; Tarp & Helles, 1997). 
A species-oriented alternative approach employs modelling and optimisation techniques 
that consider population dynamics for estimating habitat area sufficient to exceed extinction 
91 
F. He lies et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 91-102. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 98 ---

thresholds (Fahrig, 2001). However, it is increasingly acknowledged that for cost reasons 
(Franklin, 1993) all endangered species could not be dealt with one at a time (Begon et al., 
1996). Usually, various species are inventoried incidentally or in surveys of small case areas 
(Perpeet, 1995). Even if a detailed inventory is available, the question remains which species 
and taxonomic groups should be included and how should they be prioritised (Vane-Wright 
et al., 1991). 
A more habitat-oriented approach also raises important questions. Should the focus be 
on protecting the most sensitive and rare ecosystems, or on protecting a variety of ecosys-
tems? What are appropriate habitat features and size of conservation areas? (Nicholls & 
Margules, 1993; Pressey & Logan, 1998; Snyder et al., 1999). 
The subjectivity of decisions may be reduced if multiple sources of expertise and numer-
ous stakeholders are involved (Barnes et al., 1997). In this context, bio-economic evaluation 
of various reserve design alternatives becomes an important component of the decision-
making process. 
A number of studies have investigated techniques of reserve selection. Hof & Joyce 
(1993); Hof et al. (1994); Cox & Sullivan (1995) use mixed integer programming (MIP) for 
spatial and temporal optimisation of habitat management. Saetersdal et al. (1993); Church 
et al. (1996); Csuti et al. (1997); Clemens et al. (1999) use MIP to optimise species-oriented 
reserve selection, while Haight & Travis (1997); Williams & Revelle (1996, 1997, 1998); 
Snyder et al. (1999) use MIP for habitat-oriented reserve selection. Simulated annealing 
(SA) is a frequently applied heuristics in forestland zoning and reserve selection (Bos, 
1993; Csuti et al., 1997; Pressey et al., 1997; Tarp & Belles, 1997; Ohman & Eriksson, 
1998; Possingham et al., 2000; Ohman, 2000). Among these studies, only Williams & 
Revelle (1998); Ohman & Eriksson (1998); Ohman (2000) seek to simultaneously address 
opportunity cost of land, contiguity, and ecological criteria. 
The major aim of the present study is to examine the performance of genetic algorithms 
(GA), SA and MIP. 
Materials and Methods 
This study focuses on forest level, attempting to evaluate various scenarios of conservation 
area selection in the Vaisvydava Forest District, located in Middle Lithuania. The district 
is 3,101 ha, forest stands covering 2,688 ha. More than 20 tree species dominate in at least 
one of the 1,333 stands, but Scots pine (Pinus sylvestris L.) and Norway spruce ( Picea abies 
(L.) Karst.) prevail in most management units. l\Iore than half of the forest area consists 
of dry sites of average and poor soil productivity, but generally a large variety of site types 
is observed. The age class structure is rather uneven, the largest class being 50-69 years old 
stands. The average stocking is 255 m3 /ha. Besides intensive timber management, most 
parts of the district are heavily used for recreation due to the vicinity to the city Kaunas 
and its artificial lake. 
Case definitions 
Let c denote a specific forest compartment, and C represent the whole set of compartments 
comprising 3,101 ha. Let D denote the possible decision alternatives in each compart-
ment, and d(c) E D the specific alternative picked for compartment c. In the problem, 
D = {not selected = 0, selected for conservation area = 1}. A specific conservation area 
configuration can be denoted d( C) = [d( c), c E C]. Each compartment c may contain one of 
the tree species Scots pine, Norway spruce, oak (Quercus robur L.), ash (Praxinus excelsior 
L.), birch (Betula spp.), black alder (Alnus glutinosa (L.) Gaertn.), aspen (Populus tremula 
L.), and white alder (Alnus incana (L.) :vloench). 
The optimal reserve selection is pursued through minimisation of costs, and maximi-
sation of (i) a scale-ecological function and (ii) the value of proximity to swamps with 
92

--- Page 99 ---

high bio-diversity capacities, subject to an area constraint. These criteria are described in 
'selection criteria'. The problem is solved using MIP, GA, and SA that are presented in opti-
misation techniques. Sequence of the selection exercise explains the overall methodological 
approach to the optimisation procedure. 
Selection criteria 
Rationale for selection 
The first section introduced the intricacies related to the choice of criteria for reserve selec-
tion, but a resolution of the issue is beyond the scope of this study. Nevertheless, it should 
not prevent justification of the choice, which is sometimes the case in similar studies. 
First, an important decision is whether to include economic and biological criteria or 
the latter only. Consideration of the economic aspects of nature resources management 
had very low priority during the Soviet times (Brukas, 2000). After regaining independence 
in 1990 and transition towards market economy, economic criteria became important be-
cause of the changed ownership and general consideration of social welfare (Helles, 2000). 
Worldwide, conservation efforts have often failed in practice as they did not properly in-
clude socio-economic factors, e.g. budgetary constraints of the implementing authorities 
or compensations for management restrictions. It is obvious that when considering various 
alternatives of reserve network, cost-efficient provision of environmental values should be 
achieved (Shogren et al., 1999; Drechsler & Watzold, 2001). In this study, the basis for 
economic evaluation criterion is the net present value (NPV). 
The spatial location of selected reserve sites has been often neglected in the design of 
selection procedures, yet it may be critical for the success of conservation efforts (Briers, 
2002). The SLOSS debate (Single Large Or Several Small reserves) is still unresolved and 
predicted effects differ from study to study. Several authors show that large contiguous 
reserve areas may better serve conservation purposes than small and scattered reserve sites 
(Burkey, 1989; Fahrig, 2001), which is taken into account in this study by including the 
scale-ecological function. 
Mierauskas (1998) shows that swamps should be the primary object of nature protection 
in Lithuania, covering 6.4 per cent of the total land area. Fifteen per cent of the swamp 
area is protected, but scientific recommendations suggest 25 per cent (Stongcius, 1998). 
Our case area is located in a region where most swamp ecosystems have been destroyed 
(Janukonis, 1998). 
Consideration of the scale-ecological function and the swamp location captures well the 
computational complexity of the spatial optimisation. Also, it satisfies general reserve de-
sign criteria as defined by Bedward et al. (1992): (i) Reserves should be large, compact and 
connected, (ii) where choice exists, reserves should be drawn from the least disturbed areas, 
and (iii) selection of reserves should be as compatible as possible with the management of 
surrounding lands. 
Scale-ecological function 
The scale-ecological value of each compartment is denoted E( c, d( C)) and is estimated as: 
E(c, d(C)) =WE L A(c')Zc' 
e'EN( c) 
(8.1) 
where WE is a weight associated with each conserved area unit, N(c) is the set of neigh-
bouring compartments to c, A(c') is the area of compartment c' adjacent to cell c that is 
conserved. The binary variable Zc' has the value 1 if both compartment c and the adjacent 
compartment c' are conserved (i.e. d(c) = d(c') = 1), otherwise it is zero. Hence, we 
assume that the scale-ecological value is a linear function of the total conserved area of 
compartments adjacent to compartment c. 
93

--- Page 100 ---

Proximity to swamps 
The selected case area contains 13 swamps amounting to 42.4 ha. Although most of them 
do not exceed 1.5 ha, one swamp is 29.7 ha. Considering the size and distribution of 
swamps, a function has been constructed so that the closer compartments are located to 
swamps, the more attractive they become for reserve selection. We denote the distance from 
a compartment to the nearest swamp A(c). Additionally, we assume that the proximity 
value P(c) depends also on the size S(c) of the nearest swamp. Hence, the potential of 
serving as habitat increases with the size of the swamp. The proximity value is defined as: 
( 
lOOOS(c)) 
P(c) = Wp 1 + A(c)2.2 
(8.2) 
where wp is a weight associated with the combined size and distance measures. 
The proximity value is incorporated in the objective function for optimisation of reserve 
selection. This helps to adjust the network of protected areas according to the weight that 
is considered appropriate for creating buffer zones around the swamps. 
Cost function 
All forest compartments in Lithuania are assigned a soil-typological characteristic based 
on fertility and humidity levels (Brukas & Kenstavicius, 1997), to which growth and yield 
tables are linked (Kulieshis, 1993). Using Kulieshis (1993); Brodie & Brukas (1999) the 
opportunity cost of each tree species is estimated as a function of fertility, humidity, and 
compartment age. We use a real discount rate of 2 per cent that is a recommended level for 
the Lithuanian forestry (Brukas et al., 2001). The opportunity cost is denoted f(c,d(C)) 
and estimated as a polynomial function, using the non-linear regression procedure in the 
statistical package SAS. To make a trade-off between economic and biological criteria, the 
cost function f ( c, d( C)) is weighed by a factor w 1. 
Mixed integer problem formulation 
The objectives are to minimise opportunity costs, and maximise (i) the scale-ecological 
function and (ii) the value of proximity to swamps with high bio-diversity capacities, subject 
to an area constraint. The conservation policy problem in its mixed integer version is 
formulated as follows:

Optimisation techniques 
Branch and bound 
For mixed integer problems, the branch-and-bound approach is usually applied to find the 
optimal solution. In this study, the MIP approach is compared with GA and SA. We use the 
standard software package LINDO and its branch-and-bound algorithm to find the optimal 
solution to the conservation policy problem. 
94

--- Page 101 ---

Genetic algorithms 
Introductions to the general genetic algorithms procedures are presented in Goldberg (1989); 
Holland (1998). A flow-chart of the procedure applied in the present study is presented 
in Figure 8.1. In the following, the fitness function, selection mechanism, crossover and 
mutation are described.

satisfying the area constraint (equation 8.4). The penalty is formulated as a trigger(), i.e. 
a large positive number, if equation (8.4) is violated, otherwise zero.

In the present study, we use the tournament selection. Two configurations are selected with

is a winner with probability 95 per cent and loses with probability 5 per cent. In a tie, one 
95

--- Page 102 ---

of the configurations is chosen by random. The tournament selection is repeated ).. times. 
Hence, the size of the population remains constant throughout the optimisation. 
The configurations that survive the selection process undergo the genetic operations, 
mutation and crossover. Each configuration is mated with another chosen purely at random 
(Figure 8.2) and is mutated with a fixed small probability. The performance of the GA was 
found to improve when a randomly chosen segment was mutated. Mutation flips the binary 
value of a compartment belonging to the segment. The resulting configurations constitute 
the new generation. 
Simulated annealing 
From the point of view of optimisation, SA can be seen simply as a variant of a local, 
iterative improvement procedure. The SA algorithm randomly selects a compartment to be 
conserved or not. The objective value of the new candidate configuration is estimated using 
equation (8.5). If the new configuration is the best so far, it is accepted. If the configuration 
is inferior, the constant E = 1 ~ exp Q-~max is estimated. T is a pseudonym for the number 
of iterations the algorithm has run. If E is larger than a uniform, continuously distributed, 
random variable between 0 and 1, the candidate configuration is accepted anyway. So, 
selection of a new inferior candidate is possible during the search process at a decreasing 
probability. 
Crossover

Figure 8.2: Crossover and mutation ·in the genetic algorithms 
Sequence of the selection exercise 
To provide insight into the overall methodology of the selection procedure, the following 
four main stages are suggested: 
1. search for suitable model parameters 
2. minimise opportunity cost, ignoring the value of scale and proximity to swamps and 
the area constraint 
3. minimise opportunity cost, including the value of scale and proximity to swamps and 
the area constraint 
4. compare techniques and solutions 
96

--- Page 103 ---

In stage 1, appropriate model parameters must be found for each specific problem. We 
categorise the parameters into two groups: those which are critical for the performance of 
SA and GA, and those related to the value of scale (equation 8.1) and proximity to swamps 
(equation 8.2). The parameter-settings of the first group were identified from testing a 
large number of parameter combinations and selecting those that performed best. The 
parameter-settings of the second group were identified in a subjective manner, searching 
for a reasonable trade-off between biological, geographical, and economic criteria. 
In stage 2, MIP, SA, and GA were applied to find the solution that minimises NPV. 
Using this formulation, only compartments having a negative NPV are selected as set-aside 
areas. This enables to check whether the algorithms are capable of finding the optimal 
solution to a rather trivial problem that could be resolved by simply sorting the cost data.

Figure 8.3: Distribution of objective function values for a random search, simulated annealing and 
the genetic algorithm compared with the best MIP solution 
In stage 3, the optimisation model includes economic and biological criteria, and the 
area constraint as defined in equation (8.4). The performance ofMIP, SA, GA and obtained 
reserve configurations on the case area are compared in stage 4. 
Results 
Optimisation techniques 
Numerous optimisation runs were performed (on a Pentium I 200 MHz CPU) to find ap-
propriate model parameters. All three algorithms yielded the optimal solution for the 
single-criterion formulation when the losses in NPV are minimised. Finding the optimal 
MIP solution required seven minutes, whereas GA and SA found it in about 40 and 420 
minutes, respectively. Thirty-one compartments whose NPV is negative were selected and 
the total NPV savings USD 41,542. A more realistic formulation of the problem includes the 
scale-ecological function and takes into account the value of swamp proximity. Considering 
present trends in conservation policy, the target area represented by A. and A (equation 
8.4) is estimated at 295 and 300 ha, respectively, or about 10 per cent of the total case 
area. 
Weights were found from GA runs and checking the selected compartments through 
GIS in the solution space. The following set of weights made a good trade-off solution: 
97

--- Page 104 ---

WE= 10,000 and wp == 200,000. These weights are used to compare the performance of 
MIP, GA, and SA. The objective value of the optimal solution is USD 3,486,018. MIP 
finds the optimal solution during a single run taking 14 hours, while GA and SA do not 
identify this solution even after 30 hours. However, both heuristic techniques find 90-95 
per cent of the maximum value within 10 hours, and 75-80 per cent within three hours. 
Such time saving is important since many runs are needed to find a good balance between 
the selection objectives. GA and SA were run to investigate the level of variation between 
solutions, one run consisting of 5,000 generations for GA and 12,500,000 for SA, requiring 
10 hours.

Figure 8.5: Frequency of stand selection. Based on 22 genetic algorithm solutions each comprising 
1000 generations 
The obtained values of MIP, GA, SA, and the random sample, are shown in Figure 
8.3. The optimal MIP solution is obtained at higher time cost. The performance of SA is 
slightly superior to that of GA. The frequency of compartment selection by GA is shown 
98

--- Page 105 ---

in Figure 8.5, which can be compared with compartment selection by MIP (Figure 8.4). 
Reserve selection 
As shown in Figure 8.4, five conservation areas have been selected. The largest area (158 
ha), dominated by 60-90 years old coniferous compartments, almost surrounds the largest 
swamp and connects it with a smaller one. Three selected areas by the Kaunas Lake have a 
very diverse tree species composition, and the stands meet important conservation as well 
as water and soil protection functions. The last selected area is dominated by Scots pine, 
but diverse soil and forest types are found. It borders on a swamp of 3.5 ha. 
Selection of the five conservation areas implies an NPV loss of USD 1.4 from timber 
production, or 9.6 per cent of total NPV. 
Discussion and Conclusion 
There is no single answer to the question of which optimisation technique to choose. MIP 
appeared clearly superior to GA and SA when applied to the single-criterion problem: min-
imisation of NPV loss. However, this problem formulation is too trivial. When biological 
criteria and area restrictions were added, MIP still was capable of finding the optimal so-
lution, but the time input increased by 120 times. The demand for time input may deter 
from using MIP for complex and realistic problems. GA and SA yielded solutions of poorer 
but tolerable quality with lesser time input. On the other hand, substantial effort is needed 
to find appropriate model parameters for these techniques. 
Rodrigues & Gaston (2002) show that, under current capacities of computing tech-
nology, MIP could be used for efficiently solving optimisation problems as formulated in 
most studies on reserve selection to date. Yet our study indicates that inclusion of spatial 
considerations substantially lowers the performance of MIP. Here, the choice of optimi-
sation technique is case-specific and depends on the desired trade-off between time input 
and quality of solutions. The applicability of optimisation techniques in the practice of 
reserve selection is the crucial problem. Prendergast et al. (1999) discuss several reasons 
for the existing gap between theory and practice. As for the Nordic and Baltic countries, 
the primary cause is the isolationism that reigns in the research and practice of nature 
conservation. Botanists investigate the flora in a case area: species, if not a selected family 
or genus. Zoologists perform their research in a similar manner. Modellers focus on fit-
ting some phenomenon to the model investigated. Responsible agencies make little or no 
effort to integrate biological and socio-economic dimensions when developing conservation 
policies. In practice, conservation areas are often selected according to trivial criteria. For 
example, an ageing oak stand is set aside as 'it gets old enough never to be felled', but such 
'critical age' has no scientific grounding. 
We acknowledge the contention by Pressey & Cowling (2001) that selection algorithms 
can help to extract maximum value from the available data and thus improve decision-
making by managers. At the same time, we support the calls by Prendergast et al. (1999); 
Balmford et al. (2000) to put all possible efforts to coordinate and integrate conservation 
efforts at all relevant stages and levels of decision making. 
Many problems remain unresolved with regard to the optimisation-based selection of 
reserve areas. This paper adds to the sparse number of studies that attempt to compare 
the performance of diverse optimisation techniques. It also contributes with new insights 
into the formulation of a conservation policy in Eastern Europe, as opportunity costs of 
different land-uses are explicitly included. Such a pragmatic approach should warrant 
environmentally and socially acceptable solutions under the new socio-economic settings.

DEA based yardstick competition 
natural resource management 
PETER BOGETOFT AND KURT NIELSEN 
Abstract 
0 Ill 
In this paper, we discuss the pros and cons of using Data Envelopment Analysis (DEA) to 
evaluate and enhance the efficiency of natural resource management. The need for a multi-
dimensional production frontier approach is sketched, along with examples from other regulated 
multi-output industries. Also, reviews of the basic properties of DEA and DEA based yardstick 
competition are provided. Finally, we discuss the use of DEA based yardstick to evaluate bids 
in multi-dimensional procurement auctions. 
Keywords 
Regulation, incentives, performance evaluation, yardstick competition, data envelopment 
analysis (DEA) 
Introduction 
Data Envelopment Analyses (DEA) has become a tremendously popular relative perfor-
mance evaluation tool. It was first proposed by Charnes et al. (1978, 1979). A recent 
bibliographic survey (www. deazone. com) identified more than 1000 papers from all sec-
tors of society, including several studies within agriculture, forestry, and fishery. Many of 
these have been published in high quality economics, management science and operations 
research journals. 
Regulators soon realized the usefulness of DEA. DEA studies have for many years 
been informing their decision making. More recently, DEA has also been used more di-
rectly (or mechanically) to define regulatory incentives. In particular, it has been used 
in incentive regulation of energy utilities. For example, in the regulation of electricity 
distribution, countries like Norway (www.nve.no), Holland (www.dte.nl), and Finland 
(www.energiamarkkinavirasto.fi) have introduced DEA based revenue and price cap 
systems. Furthermore, DEA has - together with more traditional statistical methods -
been used to determine reasonable cost norms in countries like Australia, England, New 
Zealand and Sweden. 
Given this trend, it is natural to discuss the potential use of DEA in the regulation of 
natural resource management. This is the aim of the present paper. To the best of our 
knowledge, there has been no formalized regulation of natural resource management using 
DEA. 
Farmers and forest owners produce - and use- environmental goods like recreation, clean 
ground water, and habitats for indigenous plants and animals. However, as no conventional 
market exists for these goods, the unregulated production levels are presumably below their 
social optima. A private farmer or forest owner is likely to prioritize the production of 
marketable goods, e.g. corn or timber. Environmental regulation plays an important role 
in trying to correct this market failure, one of the means being subsidies. Subsidies are 
usually granted on a flat-rate basis, say per acre, or they are determined from assessments 
of opportunity costs. Due to asymmetric information, the better-informed farmers and 
forest owners will usually extract information rents. Part of the problem in environmental 
regulation is therefore to limit these rents. Relative performance evaluation using, e.g. 
DEA may be an important tool for just such a purpose. 
103 
F. Helles et al. ( eds.), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 103-125. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 110 ---

In this paper, we discuss when and how DEA based relative performance evaluations 
can support the regulator. 
DEA can be used to model costs and can hereby assist in the design of an ex-ante 
regulation, i.e. a system where subsidies are based on past data. ex-ante systems like 
"CPI-x-xi", where the compensation over the years is increased by a (consumer) price 
index (CPI) and decreased by the general productivity development (x), as well as possibly 
the individual improvement (xi), are commonly used in many sectors. DEA can assist in 
the determination of the general and individual productivity developments, x and xi. 
DEA can also be used in an ex-post regulation, where the additional information ac-
quired during the regulation period is used to set reasonable costs. The principle here 
is that the ex-ante commitment to ex-post regulation effectively creates a pseudo-market 
among the agents, each of which is trying to do at least as well as the others. 
DEA can finally support procurement design. While both ex-ante and ex-post regulation 
seek to reduce the costs of producing given outputs, the focus in procurement is on the 
choice of agents (to operate in a market), as well as their multiple dimensional output 
vectors. 
The outline of the paper is as follows. We first provide a brief literature review in the 
second section, and we identify some key issues in natural resource incentive provision and 
regulation in third section . Next, we give a non-technical introduction to DEA in fourth 
section. In fifth section, we discuss why DEA has become so popular among regulators, 
as well as some of the main criticism that has been raised against the use of DEA for 
regulatory purposes. We then discuss earlier research on DEA based incentive plans, using 
this to suggest a reimbursement plan for natural resource management regulation in sixth 
section. In seventh section, we suggest that DEA can be used to evaluate and screen bids, 
rather than actual performances. We discuss how this could form the core of an auction 
design, e.g. when a government wants to procure forest and landscape qualities, reduce 
nitrogen leaching, etc. Final remarks are provided in eighth section. 
Environmental Regulation 
The theory of market-based instruments to enhance environmental benefits is growing. 
The theory originates from the traditional theory of economic incentives for environmental 
protection, cf. e.g. Baumol & Oates (1988); Hanley et al. (1997). 
A significant body of research is concerned with problems in European and US agri-
culture. Intensive cultivation of the land conflicts with a growing demand for public en-
vironmental goods. This increases the need for incentives. The literature focuses on the 
evaluation of existing schemes, cf. e.g. Shoemaker (1989); Whitby et al. (1998); Roberts 
et al. (1996); Vukina et al. (2000). It also contains more theoretical contributions, e.g. 
Chambers (1997). 
With hidden information and a high social value of environmental benefits, private 
landowners are in general overcompensated, i.e. the compensation they receive exceeds the 
opportunity costs of producing the environmental benefits. The hidden information allows 
landowners with low opportunity costs to imitate landowners with high opportunity costs. 
The extraction of such information rents can be reduced by using auctions or yardstick 
competition, or a combination thereof, as incentive mechanisms. 
There is a large amount of literature on the theory and practice of auctions. Important 
theoretical contributions include Maskin & Riley (1984, 2000); McAffee & McMillan (1987); 
Laffont & Tirole (1987), while Klemperer (1999) offers an overview. Within agriculture, 
studies on the use of auctions include Baneth (1995); Latacz-Lohmann & van der Hamsvoort 
(1997, 1998); Vukina et al. (2000). Auctions as incentive instruments are used in few cases, 
the most significant being an American set-aside scheme (CRP), Shoemaker (1989); Vukina 
et al. (2000). 
There is also a great amount of literature on relative performance evaluations. This 
104

--- Page 111 ---

has been an important theme in the agency literature ever since the seminal contribu-
tion of Holmstrom (1982). Relative performance evaluation as yardstick competition was 
introduced by Shleifer (1985). The extension to multiple dimensional performances and 
the combination with frontier models like DEA was initiated in Bogetoft (1997, 2000) and 
expanded upon in Agrell & Bogetoft (2001); Agrell et al. (2002a,b). The uses of relative 
performance evaluations and yardstick schemes are numerous. Performance based payment 
schemes, where a manager's bonus depends on his performance relative to the sector or the 
market in general, is a prime example. Yardstick competition has, e.g. been used to regu-
late transportation and electricity distribution, cf. Dalen & Gomez-Lobo (1997); Resende 
(2001). Similarly, elements of relative performance evaluation and yardstick competition 
are found in several production contracts in agriculture, see e.g. Bogetoft & Olsen (2002) 
and in particular Olsen (2002). 
Key Characteristics of Natural Resource Regulation 
In most countries, the use of natural resources is heavily regulated. The regulatory aims 
range from the protection of endangered species to the assurance of reasonable incomes to 
farmers. Also, the regulatory means range from direct interference in works practices to 
more indirect monetary incentives. 
At the general level, the regulatory motivations (externalities, market power, etc.) and 
tools (restrictions, subsidies, etc.) hardly differ from one sector to another. This suggest 
that the regulatory innovations in one sector will find their way to other sectors as well. On 
the other hand, the different sectors may have particular characteristics that may motivate, 
if not new mechanisms, then at least a somewhat different mix of regulatory tools. 
What then are some of the key characteristics of the natural resource management 
sector? More precisely, and in line with the new economics of regulation as formulated 
in principal-agent settings, cf. Crew & Frierman (1991); Laffont (1994); Laffont & Tirole 
(1993), we ask if the informational, preferential and technological characteristics of the 
natural resource management sector make certain regulatory tools more appropriate than 
others? 
In terrns of information, some scholars have suggested that the sector is faced with 
extraordinary uncertainty from both production space (biology, weather, etc.) and the 
market place. The theoretical and empirical basis of this, however, is not clear. Indeed, the 
conditions in many other sectors, e.g. the financial and biotech sectors, are very volatile as 
well. 
Others have emphasized the extreme degree of informational asymmetry. This seems a 
likely hypothesis given the diverse activities involved in many natural resource operations. 
It is a complex matter to characterize the cost function of, e.g. a farm, and it is likely, 
therefore, that external investors and regulators will be severely disadvantaged in terms of 
information about the local conditions faced by farmers. The ability farm managers have 
and the effort they make, is therefore difficult to infer. This may explain the survival of 
very small firms, e.g. family farms. 
In terms of preferences, there is again non-trivial asymmetric information about what 
values the individual players may have. An agent's personal value gained from working 
with animals, from creating beautiful landscapes or from protecting the environment, etc 
is hard to know and to measure. 
Also, in many cases there seem to be fuzzy social priorities and numerous potential con-
flicts between the stakeholders of natural resource management systems. It is e.g. difficult 
or politically sensitive to make explicit trade-offs between different generations, species, 
regions, etc. Since there are no markets for many of the goods produced, a single criterion 
of overall welfare cannot be constructed. Moreover, the preferences are most likely dynamic 
and contingent on the possibilities, i.e. the ability to reduce one or the other externality 
will influence the values attached to the outcome. This creates the need for evaluation. 
105

--- Page 112 ---

In terms of the technology, it clearly varies between agents facing different environmental 
conditions. Also, the natural resource production is characterized by a high degree of 
jointness. This means that the input or output we may want to regulate or incentivize, 
may affect the marginal products or costs of other inputs and outputs as well. To capture 
such dependencies, it is necessary to work with rather complex multiple input/multiple 
output production structures with easy and flexible allowance for non-controllable factors. 
Last but not least, at the sector level we typically have many "similar" units. This 
facilitates practical modeling. Also, it facilitates incentive provision by allowing for relative 
performance evaluations to reduce rents. In general, we also have large amounts of high 
quality data collected, using standardized natural science procedures. This makes the 
pure (idiosyncratic) noise elements less important than the uncertainty of the underlying 
structural relationship between inputs and outputs. 
In the rest of this paper, we examine the design and usefulness of DEA based evaluations 
and incentives in such contexts with a complex technology, rather good data but consid-
erable amounts of asymmetric information, unclear social priorities, non-trivial impact of 
local conditions, and conflicts of interest among the agents in the sector. 
Data Envelopment Analysis 
In this Section, we provide a short, not too technical introduction to the main ideas and 
constructs in Data Envelopment Analysis (DEA). For a textbook introductions, see Charnes 
et al. (1994); Coelli et al. (1998); Cooper et al. (2000). 
Rational Ideal Evaluations 
Consider the problem of evaluating a given production unit or production plan. In the 
DEA literature, the evaluated units, say the farmers or forestry estates, are usually called 
Decision Making Units (DMUs). One can think of the DMUs as actual organizational units, 
as production plans or more generally, as multiple dimensional performance descriptions at 
a given time. Note that there is no technical difference between times series and panel data 
in the DEA model. Past performances of a unit may therefore be used to evaluate current 
behavior. 
A DMU transforms resources into products and services. The transformation is affected 
by non-controllable variables as well as non-observable skills and efforts in the organization. 
The inputs in a farm could e.g. be the employees, the machinery, the buildings, the fields, 
the animals or the pesticides. The outputs might include the crops produced, the animals 
feeded, the sales revenue or indeed, the landscape preserved. The non-controllable variables 
will depend on the time horizon, etc. but could include large parts of the fixed costs, the 
weather conditions, the genetic health conditions of the herd, and the market conditions. 
Taking the standard micro-economic approach, we would ideally like to evaluate the 
performance of a given DMU by its ability to choose the best means to pursue its aims. 
The rational ideal performance evaluation can, e.g. be summarized by comparing the 
actually attained goal level to the maximum goal level that can be achieved. Figure 9.1 
illustrates this idea in the case of a fixed bundle of inputs and non-controllable variables. 
Here, the goal is U(.) and the possibilities are given by the set T. The effectiveness ofDMU 
Dis evaluated by its obtained utility level U(D), compared to the maximally obtainable 
utility level U (ideal) given the set of possibilities. 
In real evaluations, it is not easy to apply the micro-economic cookbook recipe. In 
the typical evaluation, we lack clear priorities U as well as clear information about the 
production possibilities T. DEA provides a way of overcoming the informational limitations. 
The idea is illustrated in Figure 9.2 and commented on below. 
106

--- Page 113 ---

Crop Earnings 
Effectiveness 
Actual Performance 
Ideal Performance 
U(D) 
U (ldealj 
Biodiversity 
Figure 9.1: The Rational Ideal Evaluation 
From Effectiveness to Efficiency 
Consider first the lack of clear priorities as to how the resource spent and the products 
created, should be evaluated and traded-off against each other. In Figures 9.1 and 9.2 this 
corresponds to a lack of information about the U function. The lack of priority information 
includes the problem of trading-off the different benefits created by a landowner. DEA 
overcomes this problem by moving from an evaluation of effectiveness, i.e. goal attainment, 
to an evaluation of efficiency. Efficiency here is broadly defined as the production of the 
most outputs using the least inputs. The efficient plans in Figure 9.1 are all the plans on 
the northeast frontier. 
To quantify the extent of inefficiency, the DEA literature uses different measures of the 
distance between a given DMU, say DMU D , and the frontier of efficient plans. Most studies 
use the so-called Farrell (1957) measures that take into account the multiple dimensional 
character of the inputs and outputs by looking for proportional expansions and contractions. 
Thus the Farrell output and input based measures are: 
• F = largest proportional expansion of all outputs that are possible without using 
additional inputs 
• E = largest proportional contraction of all inputs that are possible without reducing 
any output 
Thus, e.g. F = 1.2 means that all output could be increased by 20%, while E = 0.6 
means that all inputs could have been reduced by 40%. In Figure 9.1, the Farrell base 
output efficiency is approximately 200%, therefore all outputs could have been increased 
by 100% without introducing additional inputs, namely by moving from D to the Farrell 
projection plan Fl. 
The resulting evaluations can also be interpreted in the following manner. The lack 
of a priori information about priorities like U in Figure 9.1 is overcome by choosing the 
priorities that put the evaluated DMU in the best possible light. For DMU D in Figure 
9.1, this would be the priorities corresponding to the stipulated preference structure uP 
Hence, in DEA, each and every DMU is evaluated according to prices or priorities that 
make its effectiveness look as high as possible. The lack of knowledge about priorities is 
handled by allowing for all possible priorities, corresponding to all possible slopes of the 
indifference curves in Figure 9.1. Of course, if some perhaps partial preference information 
is available, this can be used to refine the evaluations. 
107

--- Page 114 ---

Figure 9.2: The Basic DEA Idea 
From Absolute Efficiency to Relative Efficiency 
Consider next the other fundamental problem in practice, namely the lack of sufficient a 
priori information about the underlying, potentially complex technology. In Figure 9.2 this 
corresponds to the set T and it reflects the technology in a broad sense, i.e. the socio-
technical possibilities of transforming combinations of inputs into combinations of outputs. 
DEA overcomes this problem by estimating the technology T* from observed historical or 
cross-sectional actual plans. Performance is then evaluated relative to the performance of 
other DMUs, rather than relative to an absolute norm. 
The idea of substituting an underlying but unknown production possibility set with an 
estimated one is of course not unique to the DEA approach. It is also done in performance 
evaluations using traditional statistical methods, accounting approaches, etc. What is 
particular about the DEA approach is the way the approximation of the technology is 
constructed and the resulting properties of the evaluations. 
The technology is estimated using a so-called minimal extrapolation principle. By this 
we mean that DEA constructs the smallest possible set of production plans that constrain 
the observed ones and satisfy a set of (weak) regularity conditions. By constructing the 
smallest set containing the actual observations, the method extrapolates the least. 
The minimal extrapolation idea is illustrated in Figure 9.2 by the set below the piecewise 
linear line. Effectiveness, if we know the priorities U, or efficiency F if we do not know 
U, can now be evaluated relative to T* rather than T . Since we evaluate compared to an 
empirical norm set by the other DMUs and since we do not compare to an absolute norm, 
we say that we evaluate relative effectiveness or efficiency. In the case of DMU D, the 
relative efficiency is now approximately 1.3, suggesting only a 30% improvement potential 
since we now compare to the point F 2. 
Different DEA models are distinguished by the set of production economic regularities 
imposed on the set 1'*- They typically include some of the following: 
• Al: Free disposability, i.e. the ability to produce less outputs using less inputs 
• A2: Convexity, i.e. the ability to make weighted averages of production plans 
• A3: s-Return to scale, i.e. the ability to scale freely (crs), down (drs) or not (vrs) 
108

--- Page 115 ---

The four most commonly used DEA models are then the original constant returns 
to scale (crs) DEA model proposed by Charnes et al. (1978, 1979) invoking A1, A2 and 
A3(crs), the decreasing returns to scale (drs) and (local) variable returns to scale (vrs) 
models developed by Banker (1984); Banker et al. (1984) with appeal to Al, A2 and A3(drs) 
and A1, A2 and A3(vrs), respectively, and the free disposability hull (fdh) model proposed 
by Deprins et al. (1984) by invoking only Al. The resulting models in a single input single 
output case are illustrated in Figure 9.3.

Figure 9.3: The free disposable hull (fdh), variable returns-to-scale (vrs), decreasing returns-to-scale 
(drs), and constant returns-to-scale (crs) technologies for DMU A, B, C, D, E 
In the multiple output case the vrs, drs and crs models could look like the T* technology 
in Figure 9.2, while the fdh technology would correspond to a step-function between points 
A, B, C and E. 
To understand the flexibility of the DEA approach, it is worthwhile noting that the 
fdh model simply assumes that production and cost functions are increasing. The vrs 
model simply assumes that production functions are increasing and concave, and that cost 
functions are increasing and convex. The remaining models add the scaling possibilities, 
but even the crs model is much more flexible than a linear model (as soon as there are 
multiple inputs or outputs). 
To summarize, DEA copes with two fundamental problems in real evaluations. The 
lack of clear preference or priority information is handled by moving from effectiveness to 
efficiency, while the lack of technological information a priori is handled by making weak 
a priori assumptions, by doing the estimation via the minimal extrapolation principle, and 
by evaluating efficiency relative to be best practice. Figure 9.2 summarized the basic ideas. 
109

--- Page 116 ---

Using Mathematical Programming to Assess Relative Efficiency 
To calculate the Farrell efficiencies in a realistic, many dimensional context, it suffices to 
solve simple linear programming (LP) problems. Let us assume that we have for each 
of n production units, DMUi, i = 1, ... , n, the following data available: The inputs used, 
perhaps just as costs, xi E ll~.P, and an r-dimensional environmental improvement vector, y E 
m.r. In addition, the regulator and the firms know a series of non-controllable, environmental 
variables z E Rq like the type of land, weather conditions, and distance to reservoirs, etc. 
In the formulations below, we model the non-controllable variables as inputs. 
Now, to formalize the basic ideas of DEA, we may think of an underlying, but unknown 
production possibility set:
