Pros and Cons of DEA 
We have argued that relative performance evaluations &nd regulation are necessary in sev· 
era! sectors, including the n~ttural resource sector. We have also described how DEA solves 
some of the fundamental problems in real ev~tluatlons, namely the lack of preference and 
po~sibility information. We shall now look a little closer at smm' of the implied pros and 
cons of DEA. 
Pros 
The way we estimate the production possibl!ities ill DEA has several implications. The 
use of the minim&! set conta.ining the actual points suggests that DEA provides an inner 
approximation of the underlying production possibility set. The (in)efficiency estimates 
are therefore cautious or conservative in the sense that the potential output expansions or 
input savings are underestimated. We have already seen this for PMU D in Figure 9.1 
where the expansion possibilities were estimated as 30% with T* and 100% with T. 
The use of the minimal extrapolation principle and hereby, the construction of the 
largest inner approximation, also implies that the technology identifies so--called best prac· 
tice. This is attractive in many calitls, since the methods and procedures of the best units 
are more likely targets for other units. Thus, e.g. if D in Figure 0.1 Is to learn, It would 
probably find little to learn from looking at F. It would be more interesting to look at 
what units like B and perhaps E have done differently. A further consequence of using 
the DEA approach is that rea.! peers are identified. In Figure 9.1, D has two peers, B and 
E, since F2 is located on the line between these two units. B is the primary peer, since 
F2 is located close to B. Of course the construction of best practice norms, as opposed to 
average norms, must also influence the way we design incentive schemes. \Vc shall return 
to this below. 
The third and in many cases most important implication of the DEA estimation ap--
proach is its ability to work with weak a priori assumptions and associated extremely 
111

--- Page 118 ---

flexible models of the technology. DEA models generally allow for the underlying best 
practice production structure to take many different forms. If we estimate a cost function 
using DEA, e.g. we may assume that it is simply any increasing function - or any increasing 
convex function. We do not need to assume that the substitution possibilities between the 
outputs are, e.g. fixed. No parametric statistical model or any cost function constructed by 
different accounting practices allow for a similar flexibility in the technology model. There 
are many versions of the DEA approach, corresponding to the introduction of different 
combinations of a priori assumptions. In all cases, however, the imposed a priori regulatory 
is mild compared to competing approaches. 
Cons 
The single most problematic feature of DEA is the risk of mistaking noise for efficiency or 
inefficiency, and the luckiest practice for the best practice. 
If a DMU by chance faces particularly favorable circumstances that are not accounted 
for in the model, or if the registration of the outputs by luck (or intent) is biased upwards 
and the inputs downwards, the units will appear to have performed particularly well and 
have little if any inefficiency. Similarly, there is a risk of non-favorable circumstances 
or registrations leading to groundless claims of inefficiency in a DEA analysis. The case 
of overly optimistic registrations is particularly problematic, since it might influence the 
evaluation of others that may now face tougher standards by being compared to a unit with 
a windfall gain. 
These observations have led theorists as well as practitions to question DEA and ad-
vocate instead the use of statistical methods, including so-called stochastically frontier 
analyses SFA. SFA is like a traditional statistical model except that the noise is composed 
of two terms, a one-sided (in)efficiency term and a two-sided traditional noise term. 
The appropriateness of DEA depends to a large extent on how well it is executed and 
in which contexts it is used. DEA is - similar to any other operations research technique -
just a tool that can be used with success, if put in the right hands and used optimally. 
To be well executed, a DEA analysis must involve careful data collection, serious sensi-
tivity analysis (using Monte Carlo techniques, peeling techniques, alternative technologies, 
etc), perhaps stochastic programming and if possible specification and significance testing. 
There are by now numerous contributions involving re-sampling, bootstrapping, and asymp-
totic test theory, cf. Simar & Wilson (2000) for a recent survey. Still, the state-of-the-art 
in this respect is still lacking compared to what can be done in parametric models. 
In terms of context, we note that in a regulatory context, it will often be at the regulator's 
discretion how much inefficiency to eliminate in the coming periods. In such cases, by acting 
generously, the regulator may effectively create a safeguard against noise. Also, we note 
that given the flexibility in the production structure, individual noise or outlier problems 
may only have a local impact. Lastly but most importantly, we suggest that the impact 
of noisy registrations and mis-specified models should also be viewed with the uncertainty 
about the underlying (average) production structure in mind. If we have very little a priori 
information about the technology and if it is potentially complex, the DEA approach may 
have clear advantages over parametric statistical methods and simple accounting models. 
Intuitively, it would seem natural to conclude therefore that if one faces a simple tech-
nology and very noisy data, the use of parametrical, statistical models are preferable from 
an inference perspective. If on the other hand, we have relatively high quality data, but a 
complex technology with considerable uncertainty about the structure of the input-output 
correspondences (the rates of substitution, etc.), DEA is preferred. More formal models 
of the pros of DEA in regulation with considerable structural uncertainty will be surveyed 
below. 
A couple of more pragmatic observations are relevant here as well. DEA is easy to use 
given the existing computer implementations, the limited a priori assumptions needed and -
somewhat counter intuitively - the lack of good standard indicators of mis-specified models! 
112

--- Page 119 ---

Also, DEA may be considered easy to defend. Again, this rests on the mild regularity 
assumptions, the ability to handle multiple inputs and outputs and the apparent ease of 
explaining DEA. Counter to these properties is the fact that the generation of explicit 
peers may not always be attractive. If, e.g. a regulated firm questions the regulator's 
decision in court, the existence of explicit peers make the regulator vulnerable, since it 
seems straightforward for the firm to find circumstances by which the regulated firm deviates 
non-favorably from the peer units. Using instead an econometric model, the exact basis 
of comparison becomes blurred, actually creating strategic advantages. Casual empiricism 
from the use of DEA in energy regulation suggests that this is more than an academic 
possibility. Hence, although we do like the explicitness in the DEA analyses, we realize that 
in the less than ideal world of reality, even a black box approach may have its advantages. 
Also, the risk possibly entangling slack and noise, inefficiency and noise, may make the 
DEA approach harder to defend. 
Another drawback of the DEA approach, as of efficiency studies in general, is the lack 
of focus on the goals of the organization. The impressing progress that can be made in the 
evaluation without much preference information should not lead one to forget the impor-
tance of doing the right things and not just doing things right. It may be better to move 
slowly in the right direction than to run fast in the wrong direction. The importance and 
potential gains from giving more attention to preference modeling and less to the evalua-
tion with possibly naive priorities, has been emphasized in the multiple criteria literature, 
cf. e.g. Bogetoft & Pruzan (1991). Also, it has gradually been included into the DEA 
literature as well, c.f. e.g. Ali et al. (1991); Golany (1988b,a); Halme et al. (1999); Joro 
et al. (1998). 
Summing Up 
To summarize our discussion, we have identified a series of pros and cons of DEA. The pros 
include: 
• Requires no or little preference, price or priority information 
• Requires no or little technological information 
• Handles multiple inputs and multiple outputs 
• Provides real peers 
• Identifies best practice 
• Cautious or conservative evaluations (minimal extrapolation) 
• Supports learning and - as we shall argue in the next Section - planning and motivation 
and the cons include 
• Relatively weak theory of significance testing (sensitivity, resampling, bootstrapping, 
asymptotic theory) 
• Lack of focus on goals 
DEA and Incentives 
We now give a brief introduction to some key results about incentive provision using DEA 
analysis. 
113

--- Page 120 ---

The Literature 
The first conjectures as to the likely responses to DEA control go back to Banker (1980); 
Banker et al. (1989). They provided game theoretical interpretations of the scoring problem 
in the standard DEA models given realized inputs and outputs. 
The study of the ex ante motivation game of choosing inputs, outputs, efforts, skills etc 
using formal agency models was initiated by Bogetoft (1990). It has subsequently been the 
subject of several papers and books including Bogetoft (1994a,?, 1995, 1997, 2000); Agrell 
& Bogetoft (2001); Agrell et al. (2002a,b). The main results concern the use of so-called 
super-efficiency, cf. Bogetoft (1990, 1994a,?, 1995), the design ofstatic incentives with noise 
and risk adverse agents Bogetoft (1994) and - as we shall focus on below- the design of 
incentives for risk neutral agents in a context with considerable technological uncertainty 
and asymmetric information about a regulated agent's actions (moral hazard) and working 
conditions (adverse selection), cf. Agrell & Bogetoft (2001); Bogetoft (1997, 2000), and in 
a dynamic setting Agrell & Bogetoft (2001); Agrell et al. (2002a,b). 
Similar ideas have been used in other studies, including Bowlin (1997) proposal for 
designing employment contracts for government managers, Dalen (1996) analysis of the 
interaction between performance measurement and bureaucratic slack, and Dalen & Gomez-
Lobo (1997, 2000) cost estimation and yardstick analyses of buses, Resende (2001) study 
of yardstick competition in electricity distribution, Sheriff (2001) use of DEA in the design 
of (agrarian) contracts, Thanassoulis (2000) analysis of DEA and its use in the regulation 
of water companies, and the Ph.D. dissertation by Wunsch (1995) on peer comparison and 
regulation of mass transit firms in Europe. 
The Setting 
The basic problem addressed in this literature is the following: Given a cross section, a 
time series or panel information on the multiple inputs and outputs of several DMUs, what 
should a manager, a regulator or an owner ask the DMUs to do in the future, and how 
should he motivate and compensate the DMUs for their effort and other private costs? 
The answer to these questions depends intimately on the organizational context and in 
particular on the technological, informational and preferential assumptions of the parties, 
i.e. the regulator (principal} and DMUs (agents). 
In general, we consider the case where the principal (regulator) faces considerable un-
certainty about the technology. In a single input multiple output cost setting, he may, 
e.g. know that the cost function is increasing and convex, but otherwise have no a priori 
information about the cost structure. 
The general case also empower!! agents to take private actions, which the principal 
ca.nnot observe. The action could, e.g. be to reduce costs or increase the quality of the 
work. This leads to the usual moral hazard problem since the principal and the agents 
may conflict as to which actions the agents should take. The traditional setting depicts 
the agents as work averse, tempted to rely on their good luck, and to explain possibly bad 
performances with unfavorable circumstances. In general, however, it is simply one way to 
model the underlying conflicts giving rise to a motivation problem. 
Usually, we also consider the possibility that the agents have superior information about 
the working conditions, before contracting with the principal. A farmer may, e.g. have good 
information about the production conditions on his land and therefore the likely loss in crop 
revenue from reduced N application. The regulator trying to reduce N-usage, on the other 
hand, may have little information about the cost at a specific farm. This leads to the 
classical adverse selection problem, where an agent will try to extract information rents by 
claiming to be operating under less favorable conditions. 
As regards the preference of the parties, we generally assume that the principal is risk 
neutral and that the agents are either risk averse or risk neutral. The principal's aim is to 
minimize the costs of inducing the agents to take the desired (hidden) actions in the relevant 
114

--- Page 121 ---

(hidden) circumstances. An agent's aim is usually to maximize the utility from payment 
minus the disutility from private effort. The general set-up and timeline is illustrated in 
Figure 9.4.

DEA based Yardstick Theory 
In Bogetoft (1997, 2000), we consider the combined adverse selection and moral hazard 
context. We assume - in the simplest possible version - that the only observable input is 
the realized costs, i.e. the input is one-dimensional xi = ci E JR., i = 1, . . . , n. The question 
is how much, B, to reimburse a DMU using costs c to produce y E JR.r with environmental 
(non-controllable) variables z E ]R.Q. 
In terms of technology and information, we assume that there is considerable uncertainty 
and asymmetric information about the underlying cost structure. The DMU is supposed 
to have superior technological information. In an extreme case, it knows with certainty the 
underlying true cost C(y; z), i.e. the costs of producing y under environmental conditions 
z. Of course, we do not have to assume that the DMU knows these costs for all possible 
output profiles y and environmental conditions z. In fact, it will ease the design of good 
schemes if it has only local information, say the costs for a limited set of possible output 
vectors and given its specific local conditions. 
The regulator, on the other hand, only knows the general nature of the cost function a 
priori, say that 
C(.; .) : JR.r+p--> JR. 
(9.11) 
belongs to a set to a class C of possible cost functions. The classes we consider here are 
C(crs) = { c(.; .) : JR.r+q --> !R.Jc( .; .) is in-(de)-creasing in y (and z), convex, crs} 
C(drs) = {c(.; .) : JR.r+q -->!R.Jc(.; .) is in-(de)-creasing in y (and z), convex, drs} 
C(vrs) = {c(.; .) : JR.r+q -->!R.Jc(.; .) is in-(de)-creasing in y (and z), convex} 
C(fdh) = {c(.; .) : JR.r+q -->!R.Jc(.; .) is in-(de)-creasing in y (and z)} 
(9.12) 
where C(.; .) being crs means that C(ky; kz) = kC(y; z) for all k 2: 0 and C(.; .) being drs

--- Page 122 ---

We note that the analysis below can be undertaken using different assumptions about the 
information available to the regulator. Thus, e.g. the regulator may know that there are 
fixed unit costs of the different outputs, but be uninformed about the exact unit costs, cf. 
Bogetoft(2000). Also, the classes of possible cost functions could be extended, cf. Bogetoft 
(1994a, 1997). 
In terms of preferences, we assume that the DMU is risk neutral and has limited liability, 
and that it seeks to maximize a weighted sum of profit and slack. The risk neutrality is a 
simplification compared to, e.g. Bogetoft (1994a,?). Assuming that the incentive payments 
only introduce marginal variations, however, it is not an invalidating assumption. Moreover, 
the DMU's liability is assumed to be limited in the sense that it will only participate in 
the schemes if the resulting utility exceeds a minimum value, Q, with certainty. This may 
reflect risk aversion as well. 
The DMU's resulting utility is assumed to be the utility from payment and disutility 
from effort 
U(B, c, y, z) = (B- c)+ p(c- C(y; z)) 
(9.14) 
when it uses costs c to produce y with environmental variables y and is reimbursed B. The 
first term, B- c, is the profit. The second term is the excess costs or slack, c- C(y; z), 
multiplied by the value of slack compared to profit, p. 
The aim of the regulator is simply to minimize the costs of inducing the DMU to produce 
output y in the context of z. 
Assuming that the realized costs c, outputs y and environmental variables z are all 
verifiable, the regulator's problem for any given output vector y and any given environment 
z is one of minimizing the expected costs of making the agent accept implement y given 
z. This can be formulated as one of designing a (x,y,z) contingent reimbursement plan 
B(x, y, z) that solves the following contract design problem 
minEc[B(x(C,y,z),y, z)] 
x(., ., .) 
s.t. 
B(x(C, y, z), y, z)- x(C, y, z) + p[x(C, y, z)- C(y; z)] 2 QVC(., .) (IR) 
B(x(C, y, z), y, z)- x(C, y, z) + p[x(C, y, z)- C(y; z)] 2 
B(x', y', z)- x' + p[x'- C(y'; z)]VC, x', y': x' 2 C(y'; z) 
(IC) 
(9.15) 
In this problem, x( C, y, z) is the cost level chosen by the DMU when the cost function 
is C, the output is y and the environmental variables are z. This is a usual contract 
design problem, where the individual rationality constraint (I R) ensures that the DMU 
will participate for all possible cost functions, while the incentive compatibility constraint 
(IC) ensures that it is a best response for the DMU to pick cost strategy x(., ., .) and 
produce y with environmental variables z. 
If some of the variables ( c, y, z) can not be contracted upon, additional constraints may 
be introduced. Thus, e.g. if the actual costs cis non-verifiable, we impose B(x, y, z) = 
B(y,z)Vx. 
The solution to this problem can be derived following the lines in Bogetoft (2000), ex-
tended by the introduction of the non-controllable environmental inputs z. Let cDEA(y; z)

--- Page 123 ---

be the DEA estimated cost function of producing y given z, i.e.

where s=crs, drs, vrs or fdh depending on the regulator's assumed a priori knowledge about 
the class of underlying costs functions. (This problem may not have feasible solutions. Ways 
to deal with this are discussed in Bogetoft (1997, 2000)). 
Now, when the actual costs cannot be contracted upon, B(x,y,z) = B(y,z)Vx, the 
optimal solution is to use the following revenue cap with non-verifiable cost information: 
B(y, z) = Q + cDEA(y; z) 
(9.17) 
i.e. the optimal reimbursement equals a lump sum payment to cover the reservation utility, 
Q, plus the DEA-estimated cost norm for the given output y and environmental variables 
z. 
If instead, we assume that the actual costs of the DMU can be contracted upon, the 
optimal reimbursement scheme becomes 
B(c, y, z) = Q + c + p(CDEA(y; z)- c) 
(9.18) 
i.e. the optimal reimbursement equals a lump sum payment, Q, plus the actual costs, 
c, plus a fraction p of the DEA-estimated cost savings, p( cDEA (y; z) - c). 
The structure of this payment scheme can be interpreted as a DEA based yardstick 
model: Using the performance of the other DMUs, the regulator creates a cost yardstick 
and the regulated DMU is allowed to keep a fraction p of his saving compared to the 
yardstick costs as his effective compensation. Figure 9.5 illustrates this reimbursement 
scheme. 
Several extensions and generalizations of these results are possible. Most significantly, 
we show in Bogetoft (1997) how the above setting can be extended to a simultaneous game 
among multiple DMUs in the spirit of traditional yardstick competition, cf. Shleifer (1985). 
The contract design problem in this case becomes a multiple agents model, where the (IC) 
constraints define a Bayesian equilibrium among the agents. Assuming verifiable costs 
information, the resulting yardstick scheme is like the one above. For a given DMU, one 
just needs to interpret the DEA based cost function as the cost model that can be derived 
ex post from the observation of the other units. This corresponds to the use of so-called 
super-efficiency in a usual DEA model, cf. also Bogetoft (1997). Hence, in the simultaneous 
yardstick model, the regulator commits himself a priori to making a DEA super-efficiency 
evaluation ex post, and to let this evaluation determine the revenue to the DMUs. 
In Bogetoft (1997), we also show how to extend the yardstick setting to cases, where 
the regulator can observe more detailed input consumptions and possibly different factor 
prices. In this case, the DEA cost function used above shall simply be derived from the 
underlying detailed DEA model, i.e. the DEA based cost norm for DMUi becomes 
(9.19) 
where w is the factor prices for DMUi, and TDEA-i is the DEA approximation of the 
technology based on detailed data from all units except unit i. 
117

--- Page 124 ---

In Bogetoft (2000), we also show how the structure of the optimal scheme is essentially 
unaffected by introducing decentralized decision making (where the D:VIU, e.g. the forest 
owners, decide on the output mix), as well as participatory budgeting arrangements (where 
non-verifiable costs estimates are communicated a priori).

Figure 9.5: The DEA Yardstick Model in the Production-Cost Space 
In Agrell et al. (2002a,b), we introduce a time dimension in the yardstick model. The 
dynamic perspective gives rise to several new issues. One is the possibility of accumulating 
and using new information. Another is the need to avoid the ratchet effect, i.e. deliberate 
sub-performance in early periods to avoid facing standards that are too tough in the future. 
Nevertheless, the structure of the optimal dynamic scheme is similar to the ones devel-
oped above. Thus, the optimal revenue cap for a DMU is found as a DEA based yardstick 
norm. Assuming verifiable actual costs, the optimal scheme taking into account the genera-
tion of new information, the ratchet effect and the possibility of technical progress becomes: 
(9.20) 
i.e. the optimal reimbursement to the DMU in period t, B1(c1, y1, z1), equals a lump sum 
payment, Q, plus actual costs in period t, c11 plus a fraction p of the DEA estimated cost 
savings in period t, p(CP~A(y1 ,z1 )- c1), using all the information from the other DMUs 
generated in periods 1 through t. 
In Agrell et al. (2002a,b), we also consider how to modify the schemes to take into 
account the possibly of limited catch-up capacity, i.e. the fact that it may take time for a 
DMU to learn the best practice and the possible cost of innovation (frontier movements). 
We close this Section by noting that the use of yardstick schemes is not new to farmers. 
Elements of yardstick competition have long been part of existing production contracts. 
Yardstick competition in these contracts, however, is typically introduced to cope with a 
single aspect, say the impact of sowing time or feed quality, c:f. Bogetoft & Olsen (2002); 
Olsen (2002) for recent and rather advanced examples. To the best of our knowledge, 
however, the use of multiple dimensional yardstick schemes like the ones suggested have 
not previously been part of the natural resource management literature. To the extent 
that multiple dimensional aspects have been dealt with, it has been done by introducing 
a common aggregation of the different dimensions a priori, rather than by using agent 
specific, endogenous aggregations as it is implicitly done in the DEA approach. 
118

--- Page 125 ---

DEA and Procurement Auctions 
In the traditional DEA literature, the focus is on the evaluation of past performances using 
past production data. In the DEA incentive literature, the focus is on the use of historical or 
future production data to monitor the agents and to motivate them to take proper actions 
by committing ex ante to a payment principle ex post. 
We now introduce a third potential use of DEA, namely to evaluate non-realized multi-
dimensional bids (as opposed to realized production plans) in a procurement setting (as 
opposed to a control setting). In particular, we suggest that an allocation and price setting 
mechanism along the lines of the DEA based yardstick schemes can be a useful generaliza-
tion of a second price sealed bid auction mechanism. 
Multi-Dimensional Auctions 
Although there are many practical instances of multidimensional auctions, e.g. the con-
servation reserve program in the USA, cf. e.g. Vukina et al. (2000), and the Department 
of Defence procurement auctions for weapon systems in the USA, cf. e.g. Che (1993), the 
theoretical literature on multi-dimensional auctions is sparse. 
In a standard auction or procurement context, where a single quality product is supplied, 
the revenue equivalence between first price and second price auctions is the most central 
result. It was suggested by Vickrey (1961), but remained a puzzle until 1981 where Riley 
& Samuelson (1981); Myerson (1981) simultaneously solved the problem. They show that 
in an independent private value model, the different mechanisms give the same expected 
revenue (or costs) to the principal. 
Che (1993) shows how the existing theory can be generalized to multidimensional auc-
tions. He considers allocating contracts containing a price p and a one-dimensional quality 
parameter q. The principal's utility function is U(p,q) = V(q)- P, where V(q) is a 
concave function that values quality. An agent DMUi that wins a contract earns profit 
1ri(p, q) = p- c(q, B;), where pis the price he is paid, q is the quality he must deliver, B; is 
his type and c( q, B;) is his costs of producing quality q. The principal selects a quasi-linear 
score function: S(p, q) = s( q) -p. The agents with highest scores are offered a contract. The 
exact terms of the contracts depend on which mechanism is chosen. Che (1993) considers 
two different mechanism: 
• First score auction - the bidder with the highest score wins and the winner has to 
meet the highest score. A first score auction can be compared with the first price 
auction. 
• Second score auction - the bidder with the highest score wins and the winner has to 
meet the second highest score. A score auction can be compared with the second 
price auction. 
He shows an equivalence theorem for the two types of score auctions. Both auctions are 
optimal second best mechanisms. 
DEA Based Procurement 
We now propose a DEA based procurement scheme that generalizes the second price scheme 
to a multiple dimensional context. It leads to truthful revelation of costs and works with a 
broad class of underlying cost functions like the DEA based yardstick scheme. 
We consider a principal who wants one or more of n agents or DMUs to improve the 
environment. To determine which agents to call upon and the compensation to award 
them, the regulator organizes a multiple dimensional, multiple unit procurement auction. 
Initially, the agents submit bids, and based hereon the regulator determines which offers 
to use and how to compensate the corresponding agents. Next, the agents pick the actual 
119

--- Page 126 ---

production plans, including slack, and payment is realized when the promised outputs are 
delivered. 
A bid from DMUi is now an r-dimensional environmental improvement vector yi E ~r 
and a cost ci E ~- In addition, a series of non-controllable variables zi E ~q like type of 
land, distance to reservoir, etc. is common knowledge to the agents and the regulator. The 
underlying costs of producing yi in the context of zi, C(yi, zi), is private information to the 
agents. The regulator simply knows that the costs originate from a common cost function 
C(.; .) from a class C(s), where s = crs, drs, vrs or fdh. 
To keep things simple at this stage, we assume that DMUi can only choose one pro-
duction plan yi. Also, we assume that the production plans y = (y1, ... , yn) and non-
controllable context or state variables z = (z1, ... , zn) can be perfectly verified and hence 
costlessly contracted upon. (In a generalized setting, each DMUi will have a whole set yi of 
technically feasible production plans given its other activities and each DMUi will submit 
multiple bids corresponding to the different productions in Yi). 
We assume that the agents are risk neutral and that they maximize profit and slack 
with a relative value of slack compared to profit. That is, when DMUi gets compensated 
Bi for producing yi and when he actually uses (xi, zi), he is left with a utility of Bi - xi + 
p(xi- C(yi; zi)). 
Also, we assume that the regulator maximizes the value of environmental gains U(., ... ,.) 
minus the costs of inducing the agents to undertake the production. The costs needed to 
pay the agents are inflated with (1 + k) > 1, to reflect the economy wide misallocations 
resulting from the generation of the necessary funding via tax payments. 
Before formalizing the regulator's problem, we note that the cost (types) of the different 
DMUs are correlated. We know that the actual costs of the DMUs all originate from the 
same underlying cost function. The set of possible cost functions, however, is very large. 
This means that types are not perfectly correlated. On the other hand, they are also not 
independent. We argue that the assumed correlation is very natural by its relationship to 
production theory. The underlying cost function may be interpreted as the long run cost 
function while the costs of the individual DMUs may be thought of as originating from 
local, short run cost curves. 
We will now formalize the regulator's problem, suggest a procurement procedure and 
discuss the basic properties of the procedure. 
To do so, we introduce decision variable di, i = 1, .... , n to reflect which agents are 
actually selected to produce the desired outputs, { i E {1, ... , n}ldi = 1 }, and which are 
not, { i E {1, ... , n }ldi = 0 }. Note that in general, the decision variables will depend on all 
the bids of all the agents, di = di ( c, y, z). Using the revelation principle, we can without 
loss of generality impose truth-telling constraints, i.e. assume that the costs reported by 
DMUi together with a (yi, zi) is ci = C(yi; zi). The regulator's problem (with a variable 
budget) can therefore be formulated as 
maxd(.),B(.),x(.) 
. 
. 
Ec [U(d1(c, y, z)(z1 , y1 ), ... , ~(c, y, z)(zn, yn))- (1 + k) 2::; d'(c, y, z)B'(c, y, z)] 
s.t. 
Ec-'lc' [di(c, y, z)((Bi(c, y, z) + p(xi- C(yi; zi))- Qi] ;::>: 0, \:/C E C(s), i = 1, ... , n (IR) 
Ec-'lc' [di(c, y, z)((Bi(c, y, z)- xi)+ p(xi- C(yi; zi))- Qi] ;::>: 
Ec'lc' [di(ci•, c-i, y, z)((Bi(ci•, c-i, y, z) -xi*)+ p(xi• - C(yi•; zi)) - Qi]

(9.21) 
The objective function is the expected environmental value minus social costs. Expec-
tation is taken with respect to the underlying, unknown costs, c, to the DMUs of producing 
the outputs. The regulator's choice variables concern which DMUs to accept in the pro-
gram, d, and what to pay, B. In addition, the regulator must predict the actual costs that 
120

--- Page 127 ---

the accepted agents will use, x. The first set of constraints is the individual rationality 
constraints. They ensure that all DMUs, given their private information about their costs, 
expect to get at least their reservation utility of Qi if they are selected. Note that in the 
chosen formulation, we assume that the DMUs only know their own costs, not the costs of 
the other DMUs. The second set of constraints is the usual incentive compatibility con-
straints. They say that no agent would ever like to deviate from truth-telling about costs, 
ci, and from choosing actual costs according to xi. 
Consider now the following DEA based procurement auction to deal with this problem: 
Stage 1: Bidding. The DMUs submit (cost, context, output) bids (ci, zi, yi), i = 
1, ... ,n 
Stage 2: Cost Norms. The regulator uses the submitted bids to determine DEA 
based cost norms. The cost norm for DMUi , ctextDEA- i(yi; zi) is determined, based on 
the bids of the other units 
CDEA- i(yi; zi) =mine 
s.t. 
c ~ 2::>jd 
#i 
zi ~ 2.:>-.izJ 
j#i 
yi ~ 2.:>-jyj 
j#i 
.A E A(s) 
Stage 3: Selection. The regulator selects DMUs by solving 
maxU(dl(zl,yl),d2(z2,x2), ... ,dn(zn,yn) _ (1 + k) L_idi(Qi + cDEA-i(yi;zi) 
s.t. 
diE {0,1},for i = 1, ... ,n 
(9.22) 
(9.23) 
Stage 4: Payment. If DMUi is selected, di = 1, it is instructed to produce yi and it 
is paid Bi(c, z, y) = Qi + cDEA- i(yi; zi). 
The idea of this procedure is simple. The regulator uses the bids from the bidding round 
to estimate DEA based cost norms for the individual DMUs. Using these cost norms, the 
regulator then makes the necessary trade-offs between the environmental benefits and the 
costs of acquiring them. Finally, the payments to the selected DMUs are settled as the 
DEA estimated cost norms plus reservation utilities, just like in the yardstick competition 
model with non-verifiable actual costs. 
The DEA based procurement auction gives a feasible and cost efficient solution to 
the regulator's procurement problem. To see this, assume truthful cost revelation to all 
DMUs but DMUi. It follows from the minimal extrapolation principle that C(yi; zi) ~ 
cDEA-i(yi, zi). Now, since the value of slack is less than 1, the best response of DMUi is to 
choose ci = C(yi; zi) and xi= C(yi; zi), i.e. to reveal the true costs and to produce outputs 
at least possible costs. We therefore have that the suggested scheme is 1) individually 
rational and 2) incentive compatible. Moreover, we note that the resulting solution is 3) 
cost efficient in the sense that no DMU would like to introduce slack in the final production 
plan. 
Also, the DEA based procurement auction will sometimes be an optimal solution to the 
regulator's procurement problem. To see this, consider a case with considerable environ-
mental benefits B such that we would ideally like all DMUs to produce the environmental 
goods. In such cases, it cannot be part of an optimal solution to ration production, i.e. to 
forgo production in some cases, i.e. for some cost types to make production for other cost 
types cheaper. Now, to make sure that it is individually rational for all to produce under 
all possible cost functions, we cannot pay less that Bi(c, z, y) = Qi + CDEA- i(yi; zi) since 
for some cost types, we will have Qi + cDEA- i(yi; zi) = Qi + C(yi; zi). Therefore. if he 
was paid anything less, it would not be a best response to accept the offer. Since other cost 
121

--- Page 128 ---

types can imitate this one, no one can be paid anything less than Qi + coEA- i(yi; z1), i.e. 
the solution is optimal when rationing cannot be accepted. 
Finally, we suggest that the DEA based procurement auction will often be near-optimal. 
To see this, note that the DEA approximation of the cost structure will provide a close fit 
in many cases. Having observed n -1 observations on the C(.; .) curve, the piecewise linear 
approximation will typically not deviate too much for points close to the observed ones. (If 
every DMU could submit multiple bids, the approximation would even improve). 
The DEA based schemes above can of course be extended in several directions. In 
particular, one could combine the initial bidding with an ex-post evaluation and verification 
of actual costs using a yardstick scheme like in the previous section. Also, to save on the 
information rents, it may be attractive for a regulator with not overly large benefits to ration 
away some production. As suggested in Bogetoft (1997), the optimal rationing procedure 
would correspond to including artificial plans in the estimation of the DEA cost norms. We 
leave such extensions to future research. 
Final Remarks 
In this paper, we have discussed the pros and cons of using Data Envelopment Analysis 
(DEA) to evaluate and enhance the efficiency of natural resource management. 
Natural resource management problems often involve complex production structures, 
with joint production of multiple products simultaneously. Moreover, there are non-trivial 
inputs to thE> production process that cannot be controlled. There are also non-trivial 
dements of asymmetric information about the conditions and preferences of the different 
landowners. Last but not least, there are often several entities performing similar oper-
ations, and this allows the recording of relatively good and detailed data on practices in 
a large number of units. The DEA modeling is particular useful in these circumstances 
because of its ability to handle multiple inputs and outputs, to work with flexible produc-
tion structure, to incorporate local variables, and to work with limited or no preference 
information. Moreover. the need in DEA for good data from several, similar units is often 
possible to fulfill. 
To cope with delegated production and incentive problems, the DEA approach can also 
be useful. We reviewed some basic results on DEA based incentive schemes. These schemes 
can be used in motivating landowners to take desired decisions, e.g. to reduce N-leaching. 
Finally, we indicated how a DEA based procurement procedure could be used to select 
farmers and forestowners for a program, e.g. to enhance environmental qualities. 
There are several, relevant extensions of the research reported here. We suggest that 
future research should focus in particular on the development of multi-dimensional pro-
curement auctions. The discrepancy between practical ad hoc procedures in coping with 
multiple dimensions and the simplified, usually single dimensional theoretical models, is 
particularly striking. We believe that developments along the lines of the DEA based 
auctions may lead to new approaches that can solve real problems using sound theory.

Part II

--- Page 133 ---

A multi-output restricted profit function 
for Norwegian sawmilling 
SJUR BAARDSEN 
Abstract 
A short-run translog profit function with three variable outputs, seven variable inputs and a 
restricted capital input was estimated for Norwegian sawmilling at a five-digit disaggregated 
industry level, using a panel of 3,025 individual observations of sawmills over the period 1974-
1991. The parameter estimates were used to calculate price elasticities of supply and demand, 
shadow price elasticities and related measures for capital. Also the short-run elasticity of size 
and the rate of technical change were calculated. The estimated short-run supply and demand 
functions differed between regions, probably due to different export and import opportunities. 
Non-jointness was rejected for inputs and for outputs. Separability tests indicated that some 
further aggregation could have been possible. There were decreasing returns to size and negative 
technical change for the average sawmill in the short run. 
Keywords 
Price elasticities, elasticity of size, non-jointness, separability testing 
Introduction 
The objectives of this paper are to estimate input demand, output supply, returns to 
size, and technical change in Norwegian sawmills in a coherent econometric context. We 
use duality theory to determine production characteristics from simultaneous estimation 
of a flexible, restricted and normalised translog profit function and a set of three output 
supply functions, seven factor demand functions, and a shadow price function for capital. 
Technology tests regarding non-jointness and separability are also made, and own-price 
elasticities are estimated for three different regions. 
Previous studies taking a profit function approach to modelling sawmill technologies 
include Constantino & Haley (1988); Murray (1995); Stevens (1995). All three studies use 
a flexible, normalised and restricted profit function, but only Constantino & Haley (1988) 
employ the multi-output approach. They do, however, implicitly assume exogenous input 
prices at the industry level, an assumption which is valid only at the firm level. 
To our knowledge this is the first multi-output flexible profit function approach to 
modelling sawmill technologies in Europe. Thus, the focus is set on the estimation of 
the function, on the derived elasticities and on aggregation issues rather than on possible 
implications for policy and further research. 
We conclude that the estimated supply and demand functions seemed to be well be-
haved, with the exceptions of symmetry and homogeneity in prices, and that they differed 
between regions. Non-jointness was rejected for inputs and for outputs. Separability tests 
indicated that some further aggregation may be allowed. There were decreasing returns to 
size and negative technical change for the average sawmill in the short run. 
Theoretical model 
Let the efficient technology for a representative Norwegian sawmill be described by a multi-
output restricted production function L ==-Xi= F(X, Z), where Xi is the quantity of an 
arbitrarily chosen variable input (Lau, 1978; Shumway, 1983, p.170), and the variable netput 
129 
F. Helles et al. (eds.), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 129-139. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 134 ---

vector X= [X1, ... , Xm, Xm+l, ... , Xn] consists of X1, ... , Xm variable output quantities 
and Xm+l, . .. , Xn variable input quantities. The r fixed or quasi-fixed input quantities are 
gathered in the vector Z = [Z1, ... ,Zr]· By convention, outputs are positive and inputs 
are negative. L is minimised for given values of X and Z, such that the production plan 
(X, Z, - L) is feasible, and the restricted production function F(X, Z) is in accordance 
with the following conditions (Lau, 1976, pp.134-136): the effective domain is a convex set 
containing the origin, F(X, Z) is non-negative, and closed and convex in {X, Z}. 
Some of the net puts are aggregates. The underlying technology is assumed to be weakly 
separable in these, since aggregates exist only under weak separability (Berndt & Chris-
tensen, 1973). We further assume that the individual sawmill is a price taker in all markets, 
and that it maximises profits. Besides, we assume exogeneity also of aggregate netput prices, 
although the characteristics of firms' aggregation procedures may question the exogeneity 
of such aggregates (Fuss, 1977). 
According to duality theory, all characteristics of the given production technology can 
be uniquely represented by a normalised restricted profit function 7r* = H(P, Z) (Lau, 
1976, pp.138-139), where 7r* is returns over variable costs with competitive behaviour and 
short-run equilibrium, and P = [p1, ... ,pm,Pm+l,.,. ,pn] are the normalised prices corre-
sponding to optimal quantities of the variable netputs X*= [Xi, ... ,X;';.,,X,';,+1, ••. ,X~]. 
The effective domain of this function is a convex set containing the origin, the function 
is lower-closed, convex in normalised prices for every fixed input, concave in fixed inputs 
for every normalised price, non-negative lrt normalised prices for zero fixed inputs, and 
non-positive in fixed inputs for zero normalised prices. 
Shifts in the production function due to technical change are assumed to be explained 
by a technology index T. We make no convexity assumptions over this variable. Thus 
F(X,Z,T) is convex in {X,Z}, and H(P,Z,T) is convex-concave in {P,Z}, for all T 
(Lau, 1976, p.139). 
Applying a generalised version of Hotelling's lemma (Hotelling, 1932, pp.590-594)(Lau, 
1976, p.142), and assuming differentiability everywhere, the expression

Translog profit function 
The chosen translog restricted profit function has the following form

Estimation 
Equation (10.10) and the eleven equations in (10.12) and (10.13) define the technology as 
a system of equations. Multivariate normally distributed residuals with mean vector zero 
and constant covariance matrix are added, and all restrictions in (10.13) imposed. The 
use of symmetry restrictions requires a full information estimator, and we have chosen 
Zellner (1962)'s seemingly unrelated regressions estimator. Since the shares in (10.12) sum 
to one, any of them is determined by the others, and, since the sum of residuals must 
equal zero for each observation, the covariance matrix is singular and non-diagonal. We 
therefore drop one of the share equations, and divide all input prices by an arbitrarily chosen 
variable netput price numeraire to maintain the homogeneity restriction. All results are 
independent of which share equation is dropped, since we use iterative Zellner estimation, 
which is asymptotically identical to maximum likelihood. The remaining coefficients are 
calculated residually using the restrictions in (10.14). 
Data 
Almost all data are from Statistics Norway's annual census of manufacturing statistics, SIC 
33111 Sawing and planing of wood, for the years 1972-1992. There exist no comparable 
data for later years. Statistics Norway provided a data base consisting of 7,229 observations 
on certain variables according to our specifications. The original variables used directly (as 
variables) or indirectly (to define new variables) are year, reference number, number of 
employees, number of employees and owners, man-hours performed, sawlog quantities and 
costs, lumber input quantities and costs (for planing), fuel oil quantities and costs, wood 
residues quantities and costs, electricity quantities and costs, material costs, labour costs, 
total costs, fire insurance values for machinery, fire insurance values for buildings, lumber 
output quantities and revenues, planed wood quantities and revenues, chips revenue, and 
total revenue. All input costs include transport, insurance, freight, duty and so on (F AS-
delivery), except when these services are performed by the sawmill's own employees. Output 
revenues relate to prices for produced and intermediate goods at millside, including packing 
and transport services performed by the sawmill's own employees. 
When quantity consumed of a certain input that belonged to the restricted data set was 
zero (X; = 0), the corresponding input price was set equal to the arithmetic average price 
over the mills where X; > 0, for that certain year, and X; was set equal to one to make 
In X; = 0. The same simple procedure was followed for outputs. 
Four time-series variables were needed for the analysis. First, the consumer and whole-
sale price indexes (CPI and WPI), and the nominal rate of interest on loans from Nor-
wegian savings banks were collected from Statistics Norway: Historical Statistics, and the 
lower bound marginal tax rates from Bank of Norway. 
Monetary values were inflated to 1993-NOK, using the WPI. This was done to make 
descriptive statistics interpretable, but the inflation effect was removed when the relative 
prices were calculated. Unit prices were calculated as values divided by quantities where 
this was possible. 
A restricted data set of 3,025 observations was defined from the original one. The 
rejection procedure and a more detailed data description can be found in Baardsen (2000), 
where an unrestricted cost function is estimated on almost the same data set (in this paper 
we also rejected observations with profit shares more than 10 times away from the average).

R2 (Berndt, 1991) was estimated as 0.91. This statistic had to be estimated without

restricted capital variable (6;z = 0 for all i), and profit-neutral technical change (PiT = 0 
fori= m + 1, ... , n) could all be rejected.

Short-run price elasticities 
The calculated short-run price elasticities of supply and demand are shown in Table 10.2. 
All own-price elasticities are significant at the 1% level, and with correct signs. This 
indicates that the bordered Hessian is positive semi-definite, and thus that convexity in 
prices holds at the mean point. However, to be sure on this point, we calculated the matrix 
<I>, whose elements are defined as E;jSk (Sckokai & Mora, 1996). The results confirmed 
convexity. There is a total of 100 calculated price elasticities; 90 significant at the 1% level, 
134

--- Page 139 ---

and 8 more at the 5% level. We note that the own-price elasticities have larger magnitudes 
in the demand than in the supply, in general, and that especially the demand for lumber 
·input, but also the demand for other materials and other inputs are rather price elastic. 
This probably reflects the flexibility in the underlying production process when it comes to 
planing.

Table 10.2: Short-run price elasticities of supply and factor demand, evaluated at the means of the 
profit shares. Own-price elasticities and standard errors in semi-bold. Standard errors in parenthe-
ses, * indicates significance at 5% level, * * at 1% 
All three pairs of output cross-price elasticities define gross complementarity, especially 
between lumber output and chips output. From the total of 17 pairs of significant input 
cross-price elasticities, 15 indicate gross substitution and 2 indicate gross complementarity. 
The gross complementarity is between other materials on the one hand, and the two energy 
variables electricity and fuel oil on the other. All cross-price elasticities from inputs to 
outputs are negative, while all are positive in the opposite direction. We also note that 
a 1% increase in the price of lumber output increases the demand for saw logs by 1.2%, 
and that this relationship is reversed when the price of sawlogs increases. The same kind 
of symmetry holds for chips output and sawlogs, with an elasticity of about 1.3, and for 
planed wood and sawlogs, but this time with only 0.4. Finally, we note that the own-price 
elasticity for sawlogs and the cross-price elasticities between sawlogs and lumber output are 
rather close to the ones obtained by Constantino & Haley (1988). 
Short-run shadow-prices and elasticities of capital 
The shadow own-price elasticity of capital was estimated as -1.88, with a standard error of 
0.05. Estimated shadow price elasticities of capital with respect to prices of variable netputs 
are shown in Table 10.3, and quantity elasticities (intensities) of the variable netputs with 
respect to the quantity of capital are shown in Table 10.4. 
If any of the three output prices increases with 1%, the shadow price of capital increases 
with more than 1% (Table 10.3). We also note that a 1% increase in the input price of 
sawlogs decreases the shadow price with 1.6%. Thus, short-run output expansion points -
ceteris paribus - indirectly at the need for more capital by increasing its value, while short-
run input expansion does the opposite, especially for sawlogs. And a marginal increase in 
the use of capital decreases the use of all outputs and inputs marginally in the short run 
(Table 10.4).

Economies of size and technical change 
The short-run elasticity of size was 0.62, evaluated at the means of the data. Thus, a 1% 
increase in use of all variable inputs resulted in 0.62% increase in output. The standard 
error was 0.01, so the estimate was different from 1 at the 1% level of significance. This 
result indicates that profits can not be increased by size expansion as long as the capital is 
fixed in the short run (here, size expansion means increased production using the existing 
capital equipment). Increased profits may be obtained in the long run through investments, 
but that is not a topic in this paper. There are no comparable analyses when it comes to 
short-run returns to size. We re-estimated the model for different size classes. The short-
run returns to size remained in the interval 0.58 - 0.68, with a tendency to show larger 
values for larger sizes. 
Estimated at the means of the data, the rate of technical change was -0.033 (-3.3%) p.a., 
with a standard error of 0.003. Technical recession is somewhat unlikely for the average 
sawmill. Although situations may occur in which wear, ageing etc. are not compensated by 
repairs or new investments, as has been the case in some developing countries, we should 
look for other explanations for Norwegian sawmills. Previous studies show almost all kinds 
of results for technical change. To cite Stier & Bengston (1992, p.147): 
If there is any generalization that can be made about these studies, it is that 
they are characterized by widely conflicting results. 
Rates of technical change biases are shown for the whole sample in Table 10.5. The table 
also includes "biases" for the outputs. Lumber input has no significant bias, other materials 
have been has been increasingly saved, while all other inputs have been increasingly used. 
Lumber output and chips output increased their shares compared with planed wood. These 
results should be interpreted with caution since the technical change measure employed is 
not exact.

Non-jointness and separability 
Both kinds of non-jointness were rejected at the 1% level of significance, input non-joint ness 
with a Wald X~ = 558, and output non-jointness with x~ 1 = 261. Production of lumber, 
planed wood and chips from sawlogs, electricity, fuel oil, lumber input, other materials, 
labour and other inputs is thus joint all over. Separate modelling of output supplies is 
therefore not recommendable, neither is separate modelling of derived demands. 
We made a few tests of weak separability to investigate some aggregation issues. First, 
the separability of lumber output and chips output from planed wood could not be rejected 
at the 1% level of significance ( xi = 0.02, t = v'xi has an asymptotic standard normal 
distribution under the null hypothesis). Neither could the separability of electricity and fuel 
oil from other materials ( xi = 1.00). But the separability of electricity and fuel oil from 
the fixed capital input was rejected at the 5% level ( xi = 4.38). No general conclusions 
could be drawn from these marginal tests, but they indicated that some further aggregation 
may be possible. To be sure about this would, however, have required several sets of joint 
hypotheses. Since separability testing was not the main goal of this paper, we refrained 
from a more rigorous treatment of this subject. 
Regional short-run own-price elasticities 
In order to investigate possible own-price differences between different regions, we divided 
the restricted sample into three regions: (I) South-East Norway, (II) Middle-Norway, and 
(III) Other Regions. The model was re-estimated for each of the regions. The short-run 
own-price elasticity results are shown in Table 10.6. Convexity in prices was not obtained 
for Middle-Norway, indicated by the significant negative own-price elasticity of planed wood 
supply. This is a strange result for which we have no explanation, but we note that the 
lumber input is far less price elastic in this region than in the other two regions, so the 
unexpected result might have something to do with collinearity. The share of mills that 
produced planed wood was almost equal in the three regions (85%), but the average sawmill 
size with respect to this output decreased from 4,600 m3 jyr in region (I), to 3,600 m3 jyr in 
region (II) and 2,400 m3 /yr in region (III). And the ratio between planed wood and lumber 
output was significantly higher in region (II) (58%) compared to region (I) and region (III) 
(50%). 
Lumber supply is most price elastic in region (III), and sawlog demand in region (I). 
The latter result may be due to the relatively easier access to sawlog imports in region (I). 
We also note that the demand for the two energy inputs electricity and fuel oil, and the 
demand for other materials are most price elastic in region (III).

Discussion and conclusions 
The objectives of this paper were to estimate input demand, output supply, returns to size, 
and technical change in Norwegian sawmills, employing a flexible, restricted and normalised 
translog profit function. Focus was set on the estimation of the profit function, on the 
derived elasticities and on aggregation issues rather than on possible implications for policy 
and further research. The estimated function seemed well behaved with the exceptions of 
symmetry and homogeneity in prices. 
Outputs were complements, and almost all pairs of dependent inputs were substitutes. 
Short-run price elasticities had larger magnitudes in the demand than in the supply, in 
general, and lumber was rather price elastic in the demand. 
Short-run output expansion points at the need for more of the restricted capital variable 
by increasing its value, while short-run input expansion does the opposite, especially for 
sawlogs. And a marginal increase in the use of capital decreases the use of all outputs and 
inputs marginally in the short run. 
The existence of short-run diseconomies of size indicates that increased production was 
non-profitable for the average sawmill in the short run. No attempts should therefore be 
made to stimulate increased production in the short run. But we cannot conclude anything 
about size economies in the long run. Thus investments in increased size may very well 
have been profitable, cf. Baardsen (2000). 
Technical change was estimated at -3.3% p.a. for the average sawmill. Is it plausible 
that technical recession has taken place? It is a common opinion in the sawrnilling industry 
that large investments were made in the mid and late seventies when the real (after tax) rate 
of interest was negative. As positive interest rates followed in the eighties and nineties, and 
tax rates changed, the user price of capital increased considerably. It became unprofitable 
to invest in new equipment before wear and tear had reduced the productivity of the old 
equipment considerably. Thus it is likely that the majority of the sawmills were relatively 
modest in adoption of new technology during the last 10 years of the period analysed (1982 
- 1992). 
Non-jointness was rejected for inputs and for outputs, and separability tests indicated 
that some further aggregation could have been possible. 
Even though the estimated elasticities for different regions were rather similar, they also 
showed some important differences. This may be due to the different sizes of the average 
sawmills, but also to the fact that the opportunities for lumber and planed wood export 
and sawlogs import differed between regions. 
Acknowledgement 
Many thanks to Steven Dunlop for linguistic assistance.

The use of sawnwood within 
the end-use and producer sectors 
in former West Germany 
MATTI FLINKMAN 
Abstract 
The data on the use of sawnwood is compiled for six end-use sectors and nine related pro-
ducer sectors involved in the West German wood supply chain for construction (Hochbau). The 
time-series identified cover the period from 1970 to 2000 and are used for econometric analysis, 
and forecasting the wood use within the subsectors. For each sector a causal model relying 
on intermediate demand theory and the theory of combined factor and consumer demand is 
estimated for the years 1970 to 2000 applying Ordinary Least Squares (OLS). Then, Seemingly 
Unrelated Regression (SUR) is used to estimate a system of subsectors in order to increase the 
efficiency of model estimations. For the purpose of forecasting, the projections for exogenous 
variables are needed and are here accomplished through Vector Auto Regression (VAR) mod-
elling. Incorporating projections into the models then results in sectorwise forecasts, as well as 
the grand total for end-use and producer sectors respectively. 
Keywords 
Sawnwood markets, OLS, SUR, VAR 
Introduction 
Traditionally, long-term analyses of sawnwood consumption are carried out on an aggre-
gated level (Kallio et al., 1987; Martin et al., 1988). These studies typically focus on either 
demand or supply issues of wood products at the demand or supply end, not enabling the 
description of interlinkages along the wood supply chain. The earlier studies are mainly 
consumer or factor demand models, or combined factor and consumer demand models. 
While viewing the models from the statistical/ methodolocial perspective it is convenient 
to divide them into time-series models or econometric models. The latter can be further 
divided into (i) models based on time-series and/or cross-sectional data, (ii) short- and 
long-term models, and (iii) univariate and multivariate models. 
However, the sawnwood market in a country comprises several subsectors with different 
market conditions and developments over time as well as various actor groups involved 
in specific businesses. Therefore, there has been a demand for developing analysis and 
projection methods towards more differentiated studies of subsectors for the benefit of 
actors. The studies of Baudin & Solberg (1986, 1987) in Norway, of Baudin (1989a,b, 
1990), ofLonner (1991) and Dackling (2002) in Sweden, ofBaudin (1992, 1993) in the UK, 
and of Flinkman (1993, 1994, 2001) in Germany have been carried out in that direction, 
contributing to more detailed analysis and projections than those based on traditional 
methods. Describing the inter-linkages between the wood product providers and consumers, 
i.e. the flow of wood products from the primary producers (sawmills) via intermediary, i.e. 
producers of e.g. windows, doors etc, to end-users and uses in e.g. residential housing in 
modelling demand and supply, should even facilitate a better use of market information 
within practitioners involved in wood product business. 
The present paper aims to analyse and describe the demand and supply of wood prod-
ucts along the wood supply chain to construction activities, and to display interdependences 
between various wood providing and consuming subsectors in the former West Germany 
141 
F. Helles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 141-152. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 145 ---

1970 to 2000. First the methodological background including data sources is outlined, then 
the achieved results are displayed, and discussed. 
Methods 
In the context of modelling the demand for sawnwood in end-use sectors, the estimation of 
econometric models relies on the theory of combined factor and consumer demand (Baudin 
& Solberg, 1989). The theoretical starting point for the model specification is the theory 
of intermediate demand for (production) goods, where the demand for input goods in a 
manufacturing process can be studied applying factor demand models. Thus, the activities 
of a firm are analysed from the point of view of how the production factors are transformed 
by the firm into products for delivery to the markets. Besides deriving the factor demand 
from this theory the defined end-use sectors can be seen as producer sectors where input 
goods are transformed and made ready for final use, e.g. to be used in the construction of 
dwellings and/or non-residential buildings in various ways. 
Accordingly, the production unit, the firm or as in this case the sector, utilises n input 
goods ( v1, ... , vn) to manufacture m products ( Qn+l, . .. , Qm) in a manufacturing process. 
This can be expressed in the form of an implicit production function as follows: 
(11.1) 
Equation (11.1) expresses how the input factors (vj) are transformed in processing to output 
products (q;). 
According to the theory of implicit functions (Chiang, 1984) it can be 
assumed that F has the continuous partial derivates (F{, ... , F~; F~+l, ... , F:,.). It is also 
possible to express equation (11.1) in an explicit form as a twice differentiable production 
function (11.2) which for the sectors gives the production conditions according to following 
formulation: 
(11.2) 
where Qst is production volume in sector sat time period t, Vst is input volume of sawnwood 
for production in sector s at time period t, Zst is a vector of other inputs to sector s at 
time period t, t is time index and indicator for technological progress t = 1, ... ,T, and 
s = 1, ... , Sis the number of sectors. 
The production volume of Qst in sector s is determined by the consumer demand which is 
derived by maximising the utility function of a single consumer whose demand alternatives 
are restricted by income and product prices. The total comsumer demand is shown, e.g. 
in Baudin & Solberg (1989), to be identical to the production volume of Qst in equation 
(11.2). Thus, the model formulation for the studied subsectors is: 
Vst = hs(Pvst,Pzst, Yt, t) 
(11.3) 
where h, is the functional form, Pvst is the price of input goods, i.e. sawnwood, Pzst is the 
price vector of other input goods, and Yt is the average per capita income. 
Equation (11.3) is a combined factor and consumer demand model, which fits in with 
the objective of the study, i.e. to make sectorwise analyses. Besides general macro economic 
factors, (sawn)wood consumption within sectors respectively is also assumed to be influ-
enced by supply and demand related factors which are specific for each subsector. After 
specification of the models based on equation (11.3) the OLS-estimation of models is done 
in log-linear form. Thus, the elasticities are given directly by the estimated coefficients. 
The model estimations are based on the following log-linear form: 
In Qt = a + (31 In X1t + (32 In X2t + ... + (3p In Xpt + Ut 
(11.4) 
where lnqt is the natural logarithm of the endogenous variable at time period t, lnxlt, ... , 
In Xpt are natural logarithms of the exogenous variables, a, (31, ... , (3p are coefficients ( elas-
ticities) to be estimated according toOLS, and Ut is a random error with zero expectation 
and constant variance u at every time period t. 
142

--- Page 146 ---

In vector form equation (11.4) for sector scan be expressed as 
Q, = X 8/38 + U 8 , 
s = 1, ... , S 
(11.5) 
where Q8 is a (T x 1) vector of endogenous variables in a log form for sectors with typical 
elements Q8t, t = 1, ... , T, Xs is a (T x p8 ) matrix of exogenous variables in a log form for 
sectors, sis a (p. x 1) vector of coefficients (elasticities) for sectors, and u 8 is a (T x 1) 
vector of random errors of the model with zero expectation and constant variance a at 
every time period t. 
OLS-estimation is made individually for sectors s. Because altogether 15 sectorwise 
estimations are made it is probable that wood consumption in sectors close to each other 
is to some extent connected with and influenced by the same independent factors outside 
the model system, e.g. business cycles and construction activity. Therefore, there is reason 
to examine the sectors (the estimated models) in a systems context. 
One econometric tool with the possibility of examining dependencies between models is 
SUR-methodology (Seemingly Unrelated Regression)(Zellner, 1962). A system of equations, 
starting with equation (11.5) for sectorwise estimations, can be written in terms of vector 
and matrix representation as follows: 
Q=X/3+u 
(11.6) 
where Q is a (ST x 1) vector of all (S sectors) the equations' endogenous variables, X is a 
(ST X '£ 8 Ps) matrix of exogenous variables, s = 1, ... S, j3 is a ( Ps x 1) vector of coefficients 
(elasticities), and u is a (STx1) vector random errors with expectation 
E(ust)=O t=1, ... ,T 
(11.7) 
Furthermore, to make predictions the developed causal models require input data in 
terms of projections of the exogenous (independent) variables. Here, projections for all the 
independent variables are derived using Vector Auto Regression (VAR) modeling. This is 
an attempt to establish a holistic view, which means that the projections for a number of 
exogenous variables are treated in a systems context. 
Let Yt be the value for a time-series at time period t. The h step forecast for the period 
T + h, at the end of the period T is 
(11.8) 
where f is the functional form. 
Now, if Yt is regarded as a vector of random variables this estimator is an optimal 
forecast based on the vector autoregressive (VAR) model which can be expressed: 
Yt = v + AtYt-1 + ... + ApYt-p + Ut 
(11.9) 
where Ut = ( Utt, ••• , UKt) fonns a sequence of independently identically distributed random 
K-vectors with zero mean vector. 
Here, the VAR-approach (VAR) is applied to the historical time-series data of the 
exogenous variables from 1970-2000. The parameters At, ... , Ap are estimated according to 
the maximum-likelihood method. As a result ofVAR-estimation forecasts for the exogenous 
variables are obtained to the year 2010. 
Data 
The time-series data for the calculation of wood consumption within various end-use sectors 
were obtained from Statistisches Bundesamt and OECD. As shown in the next section the 
German data on building volume of residential and non-residential buildings, as well as the 
143

--- Page 147 ---

OECD data on economic indicators cover the years 1970 to 2000. Other German studies 
(Mantel & Schneider, 1967; Kroth et al., 1991) were then used to identify the wood volumes 
utilized for various construction purposes. Initially, the data is here used to describe and to 
structure the wood consumption of different end-use and producer sectors within the wood 
supply chain. The disaggregation of end-use, and the wood utilization within preceding 
production phases, yields altogether 21 subsectors of which 15 with manufacturing mainly 
for the construction sector are considered in the present study. 
Results 
Subsector models 
The results obtained for subsector models are based on SUR estimation and given in ap-
pendix 1. For the sectors 1-6 the dependent variable is respectively expressed as building 
volume (m3). The transformation of building volume to volume of wood for construction 
is made using the key figures (Flinkman, 1993), which express the wood volume for various 
input products per cubic metre building volume. Thus, in sectors 7-14 the endogenous vari-
ables are given in cubic metres of wood. The price and cost items are deflated and given 
in 1985 monetary values. For estimations where the lagged dependent variable is included 
the long-term elasticity is calculated. In these cases the Durbin-Watson test (DW) is also 
replaced by Durbin h test (Durbin, 1970). 
The models chosen for the producer sectors (7-15) (which mainly sell their output to 
the construction sector) are characterized by independent variables which exhibit the signs 
according to the theory. On the other hand, in some of the models autocorrelation is 
indicated as well as some other weakening factors regarding model technical aspects, which 
is also observed in the consumer sector models (1-6). However, this study aims at analysing 
and forecasting the wood consumption within subsectors in a systems context. Seen from 
this system viewpoint and focusing less on the single model estimations, it is argued that 
the estimated models can be accepted for that purpose. 
Projections of exogenous variables 
When the exogenous variables are viewed in a systems context the VAR-modelling can be 
used to generate alternative projections for these exogenous variables included in the model 
system. The forecasting alternative displayed here draws on the assumption that a smaller 
number of subjectively chosen (significant and relevant) variables is able to forecast the 
development of each exogenous variable. In this study one composition of projections for 
the exogenous variables is utilised to generate forecasts for wood products. Further, the 
selected alternative for the (exogenous) projections is achieved through a conscious choice 
of possible development paths for exogenous variables that in this displayed case predicts a 
kind of balanced scenario for the economic development up to 2010. Altogether 9 exogenous 
variables have been used for estimation of the model system described above. However, 
for one of these time-series the projections have been endogenously determined within the 
model system. The variables are as follows: Real long-term interest rate, Nominal long-term 
interest rate, Nominal short-term interest rate (Figure 11.1). Price index of manufacturing, 
Index of construction activity, Index of credits, Index of share prices (Figure 11.2) Inflation 
rate and Unemployment rate (Figure 11.3). 
Wood consumption 1970 to 2000 and projections to 2010 
The past consumption, and forecasts, for wood products are first displayed for the new 
construction. Furthermore, it is shown how the wood volume, e.g. for one-family dwellings 
(Figure 11.4) (as also for all the other end-use sectors) can be broken down into the input

When the wood volume of a specific input product, e.g. windows, used within the 
different end-use sectors, e.g. one-, two- and multi-family dwellings etc., is gathered and 
put together, the total volume of wood used for this product (window) can be calculated 
for new construction. While the total window market, i.e. apparent consumtion of windows 
is also known through the branch statistics, it is possible to even assess the window volume 
targeted to RMI (Repair- Maintenance - Improvement) activities (Figure 11.5). 
After adding together all the wood volumes over intermediary subsectors, e.g. windows, 
door, floors etc., and adding the manufacturing waste volume, the gross volume of the 
producer sectors, i.e. the input volume to the value-adding processes is obtained (Figure 
6). 
Discussion 
The construction sector is characterised by a number of subsectors, where e.g. wood prod-
ucts of different value-added grades are used. Usually, these products have already been 
manufactured within the "preceding" production phase. As regards wood consumption 
it is thus also important to analyse these manufacturing steps, e.g. within window and 
door manufacturing, as part of the "end-users" of sawnwood. Strictly speaking it is diffi-
cult to define the border-line between the "end-user" and the "preceding" manufacturing 
phase, thus there is a motive for including all "user phases" in the analysis. This will 
provide an opportunity for a more consistent estimation of the ( end)-use within different 
phases/sectors and show inter-linkages, i.e. "where the wood is used" on its way to the 
final point of consumption. 
The methodological starting point for the analysis is the "wood supply chain", which 
is created on the basis of the historical description and aims to illustrate schematically the 
different end-use sectors and interdependencies between them in a market context. When all 
the 15 sectors identified have been estimated individually according to OLS, the estimation 
146

--- Page 150 ---

Figure 11.5: An example of the wood volume (m3) used and delivered by the intermediary producers 
of windows; estimated 1970- 2000, and projections to 2010

1970-2000, and projections to 2010 
147

--- Page 151 ---

procedure is supplemented with SUR-methodology (Seemingly Unrelated Regression), i.e. 
the whole system of sectors is estimated simultaneously in a systems context. This is 
motivated by the fact that the sectors are not independent of each other. In the context of 
econometrics this means that the residuals of the estimated sector models are correlated. 
By using SUR-methodology, where the covariance matrix is included in the estimation 
process, it is possible to improve the model estimations. 
When the estimated system of equations consists of causal models, where the exogenous 
variables generate projections for the wood consumption, predicted values are needed for 
the system of included exogenous variables in forecasting. Predictions have to be obtained 
for the exogenous variables. VAR-processes for estimation of the future development of the 
exogenous variables are used here to generate a system-consistent projection alternative for 
these independent variables. 
The projections of exogenous variables incorporated in the sector models then generate 
forecasts for the wood used in the different sectors, and by aggregation over the subsectors, 
a projection for the total consumption of sawnwood in construction is derived. Hence, the 
model system and the individual sector models can be seen as a simulation model with 
defined constraints (specified models). The model system reacts to exogenous stimulus 
caused by exogenously definied variable values. The model system can therefore be used 
as an instrument for testing the effects of alternative scenarios of exogenous variables on 
endogenous variables indicating future trends for wood product uses. This is in the line of 
future research, i.e. a conscious planning for the future, where the main task is to produce 
knowledge about alternative future opportunities and limitations. The forecast alternative 
displayed here can be regarded as a result of assuming a balanced economic development 
for the economic indicators (exogenous variables) selected for the subsector models, i.e. in 
this chosen scenario the balanced economic development will, in general, result in moderate 
and stable demand for wood products up to 2010.
