Backward vertical integration in the Swedish 
forest sector - six case studies 
LARS LONNSTEDT 
Abstract 
In this paper arguments for and against backward vertical integration, i.e. integration from 
pulp or saw mills to timberland ownership, are studied. Three interviews are made with pulp 
and paper Chief Executive Officers and three with lumber company CEOs. The theoretical 
background is taken from transaction cost theory, a theory that explains, among other things, 
why vertical integration exists. As the main reason for backward integration, the pulp and paper 
companies' CEOs stressed the possibility to affect prices while the lumber companies stressed 
secure deliveries. All three presidents of the lumber companies expressed a wish to buy more 
timberland, i.e. to become more backward integr;ted. Two of the presidents of the pulp and 
paper companies expressed an interest in selling timberland far away from the mills and buying 
timberland closer to the mills. 
Keywords 
Timberland, ownership, scope, transaction costs, roundwood market 
Introduction 
During recent decades in Sweden business analysts and journalists among others have ques-
tioned the pulp and paper companies' ownership of timberland. The question they are 
raising is why should the companies have so much capital invested in timberland? They 
claim that the rate of return on this capital is quite low compared with downstream in-
vestments, i.e. in paper machines. Probably the origin of this discussion emanates from 
the U.S. where, for example, Georgia Pacific in a first step formed a separated stock ex-
change listed company for its timberland; a company that later merged with a real estate 
investment trust company. Also some of the Swedish chief executive officers (CEO) have 
expressed their opinions. For example, Sverker Martin-Li:if, CEO of the biggest Swedish 
pulp and paper company, SCA, in an interview says that he could imagine selling part of 
or all SCA's timberland if the company could find a suitable organizational form ("Dagens 
Industri" 19 November 1999). However, the new arrangement must be better than the 
existing one. The synergy effects must be retained. Mr. Martin-Li:if says that the actions 
taken by Georgia Pacific may be a good organizational solution in the U.S. South but not 
automatically in Sweden. He also points out that it will be difficult to find buyers of 2.3 
million hectares of forestland worth 1.6 billion USD. Furthermore it is of importance that 
the effect on the price of forestland will be minor. 
Among pulp and paper companies, ownership of timberland has a long tradition. Selling 
this land would mean a major change to the ownership and organizational structure of the 
Swedish forest sector. We want to understand better what is behind the discussion. This 
may hopefully give some insight into what will happen. Our purpose is to study how 
interesting this issue is for the forest industry and to reveal arguments for and against 
industrial ownership of timberland. Thus, we conducted a set of interviews with chief 
executive officers of both pulp and paper and lumber companies. As a base we used the 
transaction cost theory, a theory that explains, among other things, why vertical integration 
exists. Our interest is backward integration, i.e. integration backward from pulp or saw 
mills to forest. 
153 
F. He lies et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 153-164. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 157 ---

A concept related to vertical integration is scope (Kotler, 1988; Porter, 1985). Four 
different dimensions are distinguished. Of interest in this context is vertical scope, i.e. 
the degree to which different activities of a supply chain are made internally in the same 
company. Thus, scope defines the borders within which the company is operating. The 
scope for a company can also be seen as its position in the supply chain. Galbraith (1983) 
has presented a model of the supply chain for manufacturing companies (Figure 12.1). From 
a company's position it is possible to judge the center of gravity, i.e. the key competences 
and the heart of the business cultural. A firm's center of gravity usually influences the 
development of the organizational culture. 
Raw 
materials 
* 
Primary 
manufacturing 
* 
Upstream 
Fabrication 
* 
Product 
Marketer 
Retailer 
producer 
distributor 
* 
* 
* 
Downstream 
Figure 12.1: Model of the supply chain for manufacturing companies (Galbraith, 1983) 
Mintzberg et al. (1995) defines vertical integration as expansion to closely related parts 
of the supply chain (see also Andersen et al. (1990)). Integration can take place in two 
directions, backward (upstream) or forward (downstream). Our interest is backward inte-
gration. A company characterized by backward integration can be said to prefer production 
rather than buying or selling (Mintzberg et al., 1995). Related to self-sufficiency of inputs 
or continued processing, the integration can be total or partial. According to Galbraith 
(1983), the main purpose of vertical integration is usually to support that part of the supply 
chain where the company has its focus. In Sweden, pulp and paper companies generally own 
timberland but not to the extent that they are self-sufficient. Lumber companies generally 
do not own timberland or only marginally. 
Theoretical and empirical base 
Theoretically the boundary of a company is defined by balancing the costs for using the 
market solution, i.e. the transaction costs, compared with the costs for internal coordi-
nation, i.e. vertical integration (Coase, 1937; Williamson, 1985). Transaction costs are 
defined as the costs for running the economic system, i.e. costs besides production cost. 
Sometimes transaction costs are seen as the economic equivalent of friction in physics. If 
the market is internalized, i.e. the boundary of the company is widened; other costs will 
arise, such as costs for administration and non-optimal allocation of resources. There is a 
tendency that these management related transaction costs will increase in proportion to the 
size of the company, exponentially with geographical expansion and degree of dissimilarities 
of integrated transactions. It should be pointed out that the internal transaction costs are 
dynamic in size and character. Changes of economies of scale, possibilities for organizing 
geographically dispersed activities and new management techniques will result in larger 
companies. However, these types of innovations will also influence the market solutions. 
Other alternatives than the market solution and internalization of the transaction costs 
exist for organizing the economy; some examples are collaboration agreements, long-term 
contracts, minority ownership, joint ventures, alliance and license production (Richardson, 
1972; Bjuggren, 1985; Wikstrom et al., 1994; Mintzberg et al., 1995). 
Transaction costs are related to concepts such as contact, contract and control (Bjug-
gren, 1985; Williamson, 1985): 
• Costs for contacting, i.e. cost for finding information about possible products and 
154

--- Page 158 ---

sellers/buyers (ex ante) 
• Cost for contracting, i.e. cost for negations about conditions and writing a contract 
(ex ante) 
• Costs for controlling, i.e. cost for implementation and supervision of the contract (ex 
post) 
The base for Williamson's theory is that the individual in his/her behavior is character-
ized by bounded rationality and opportunism (compare with Simon (1961)), i.e. transaction 
difficulties that may be present in the exchange process. In an uncertain world it is im-
possible to forecast all possible outcomes of a decision, which means that uncertainty ex 
ante may be costly ex post. This explains the existence of bounded rationality. Oppor-
tunism means that some people periodically may be selfish at the expense of other people. 
For example, information impactedness may give them the possibility to take more than 
motivated of the profit. 
For a specific transaction it is important trying to identify which organizational form, 
inter- or intrafirm transaction, is the most efficient. According to Williamson, three di-
mensions distinguish different types of transactions: 1) transaction specific capital (asset 
specificity), 2) uncertainty (about the future) and 3) frequency (of transactions). The first 
dimension is the most important. Transaction specific capital refers to lasting investments 
that have a specific use or user, i.e. the investment costs are "sunk" because no alternative 
use exists. A special concept, quasi rents, is related to transaction specific capital. This 
refers to the difference between the value of an asset at its best use compared with its 
second best use. For very specific purpose assets, the second best use value can be close 
to salvage value (Bjuggren, 1985). One consequence of transaction specific capital may be 
less competition; in extreme cases one buyer and one seller will characterize the market 
situation. From the economic perspective of a transaction cost this is only of importance 
when assuming bounded rationality, opportunism and uncertainty (Williamson, 1985). 
The transaction cost theory offers an explanation why integration, especially vertical, 
normally offers the most efficient form for realizing potential synergy effects. The expla-
nation is that high quasi rents make other solutions expensive. Ansoff (1965) defined the 
concept synergy as the effect from combined production instead from separate parts. The 
sum is bigger than the parts, i.e. 1 + 1 > 2. The explanation is economies of scale in a wide 
meaning. Ansoff distinguishes four types of synergies: 1) selling, 2) operative, 3) investment 
and 4) management synergies. Other types of synergies mentioned in the literature are for 
example, financial and cartel synergies (Chatterjee, 1986). It should be pointed out that 
the effect of synergies could be positive as well as negative. 
In addition to the above references basically presenting the theoretical base for trans-
action costs and vertical integration, there are a large number of empirical studies. Some 
are more general while others study more specific aspects. However, the number of em-
pirical studies about backward integration between forest and the first conversion step are 
limited. A recent study is that of Schmelzle & Flesher (2001). From three case studies 
of Mississippi based lumber companies they conclude that the main reason for backward 
integration was to gain better control over, and ensure a constant supply of, raw materials. 
"It was only when the company purchased its own timberland that it finally removed the 
problem of uncertain raw material supplies." Globerman & Schwindt (1986) present what 
they characterize as a verbal test of the transaction cost approach. They observe that 
(at that time) all but one of Canada's largest (in term of sales) forest product enterprises 
were integrated back into the ownership of timber rights. They conclude that the trans-
actional considerations, particularly asset specificity, are robust empirical determinants of 
governance structures. Murray (1995) studied the effects of vertical integration for the 
U.S. pulpwood markets. He found that spatial factors such as the cost of transporting 
products between sellers and buyers can mitigate the forces necessary to support perfect 
competition. This is particularly the case in markets for bulky, land-intensive inputs such 
155

--- Page 159 ---

as forest products. In these situations, the market power is typically thought to favour the 
relatively few buyers of the pulpwood over the relatively many timber-growers, and thus 
the imperfectly competitive market structure can be characterized as oligopsonistic. Mur-
ray also points out that pulpwood processing companies may have various other incentives 
besides oligopsony power for vertically integrating into timber production. Perhaps the 
most prominent motivation is to provide some stability to their pulpwood procurement in 
light of the natural supply shocks in pulpwood markets due to weather and other random 
events. The penalty for a wood procurement shortfall can be substantial (e.g., temporary 
mill closure). Examples of articles dealing with vertical integration between pulp and paper 
are Eklund (1967); Ohanian (1994). Examples of interesting studies from other industries 
in this context are Hennart (1988); Al-Obaidan & Scully (1993). 
Based on the transaction cost theory of Porter (1985); Mahoney (1992), we have for-
mulated a set of hypotheses about backward integration of the forest industry: 
Advantage of increased negotiating power and lower factor prices 
Backward integration is one way of tackling the problem with powerful suppliers of round 
wood such as the forest owners' associations in Sweden, i.e. distribution of the quasi rent 
and risk of opportunistic behavior. Thus, backward integration may be one way for a forest 
product company to lower the round wood price. Furthermore, backward integration will 
reveal the production cost structure that can be used in price negotiations. 
H1 The backward integration of forest companies can been seen as a means of ensuring 
that most of the profit does not end up in forestry. 
Advantage of secured supply 
A main reason why the forest product companies do strive for backward integration has 
long been to secure deliveries of wood, which reduces the risk of production disturbances. 
This is important for capital intense industries such as the forest industry. 
H2 A backward integrated forest product company secures, at least to a certain extent, 
deliveries of round wood. 
Control and coordination advantages 
Costs for planning and coordination of different operations as well as the time it takes for 
reacting to unforeseen happenings may be shorter for an integrated forest product company. 
A backward vertically integrated pulp or lumber mill has a possibility to reduce the time 
in round wood inventories and for production. 
H3 The backward integration of forest companies has been compared with the control 
and coordination advantages of non-integrated companies. 
Information advantages 
Lately, not the least for lumber companies, it has become increasingly important already 
in the forest operations to observe the requirements from later stages of the supply chain. 
Environmental considerations and the necessity to use fewer and less chemicals and still 
keeping retain the brightness of pulp and paper is another example of an increasing need 
of information about wood quality. 
H4 It is easier and cheaper for backward vertically integrated forest product companies 
than non-integrated companies to get accurate and quick information from wood producers. 
Disadvantage of tying up capital 
Backward integration means that capital is tied up in timberland; capital that has an 
alternative use later in the supply chain. If the investment is big compared with the ability 
156

--- Page 160 ---

of the company to finance the investment, the integration may expose the company to 
strategic risks in other parts of its businesses. In capital intense industries, such as the 
forest industry, the need for investments in new technology and advanced machinery is 
big. Timberland ties up large sums of capital, capital that may be needed downstream for 
improving the competition position of the company. 
H5 Return on capital is negatively effected by ownership of timber land. 
Structure of timberland ownership 
Sweden's total land area is about 41 million ha of which about 21 million ha (52 %) is 
timberland. The standing wood volume is 2.6 billion m3 or 121m3 per ha (2001). Softwood 
(Norway Spruce and Scots Pine) is dominating (85 %of the total wood volume). Almost 
half ( 49 %) of the timberland is owned by private persons, pulp and paper companies own 
39 %, the State owns 3 % and other owners, for example the church, own the rest. In 
South Sweden, private persons own 80 % of the timberland compared with only 35 % in 
the north. In 2000 the total production of sawn softwood was 14.8 million m3, of wood 
pulp 11.5 million tons and of paper and board 10.8 million tons. Other users of wood raw 
material are energy plants, not the least of cutting residues such as tops and branches. 
For the last decades, through merges and purchases, the ownership structure of the 
Swedish pulp and paper companies has changed quite dramatically. Few companies are left 
and some companies with a long Swedish tradition are no longer Swedish. Today most of 
the companies are global or at least international. One consequence for the round wood 
market is that regionally a pulp and paper company can be dominating. Even the structure 
of the Swedish lumber companies has changed, but less dramatically. The size has grown 
but they are still based in Sweden. Sawmills that are not run by the pulp and paper 
companies are often owned by one "family". In one region many lumber companies can 
be competing for the saw timber supply. Thus, from a demand perspective an obvious 
difference between pulp and paper and lumber companies is the number of companies. 
The supply side looks different. The number of private persons owning timberland in 
Sweden is a couple of hundred thousand. The number of estates is about 200,000 which 
means an average size of about 50 ha. About half of the estate owners are members of forest 
owners' associations. Each association is related to a specific region. Merges have also taken 
place among forest owners' associations. Today there are only five associations. In South 
Sweden the largest association also owns pulp and lumber mills. The other associations 
only own lumber mills. The goal for the associations is to help the members to sell their 
wood at a good price. 
The pulp and paper companies have quite substantial deliveries from their own timber-
land; they also buy from forest owners' associations and from private forest owners. The 
lumber companies are only marginal owners of timberland and thus must buy most of their 
saw timber on the market. In addition to domestic cuttings, quite a substantial import 
of pulpwood, 15-20 % of the total used, takes place from Russia, the Baltic countries and 
other countries. 
Due to logistic considerations quite a substantial exchange of wood takes place. Thus, 
a company can exchange wood with another company and in this way reduce its trans-
portation costs. Pulp and paper companies exchange saw logs for pulpwood with sawmill 
companies, and the other way around. After the processing, the lumber companies sell 
residues to the pulp and paper companies. About one-third of one cubic meter of saw logs 
is sold as residues to the pulp and paper companies. (It takes about 2 m3 of saw logs to 
produce 1m3 of lumber.) This business is quite favorable for the pulp and paper companies 
as they later buy the residues for about 60 % of the original price; residues that can go 
directly into the pulp boiler. However, competition for these residues may exist locally 
between energy plants and pulp mills. 
157

--- Page 161 ---

Methodological approach 
Population and sample 
Two different types of forest industries have been studied, pulp and paper and lumber 
companies. All companies operate in Sweden although one of them has its headquarters 
outside the country. From each industry, a subjective sample of three companies has been 
made, three pulp and paper companies and three lumber companies. All companies belong 
to the larger ones in Sweden. Typically, the pulp and paper companies own extensive 
areas of timberland while the lumber companies own timberland only to a marginal extent. 
However, one of the selected lumber companies is an exception even if the ownership of 
timberland cannot be compared with that of pulp and paper companies. This company 
was quite recently formed by two pulp and paper companies and their contributions were 
timberland. 
The target person in each company was the CEO. However, in one case the interview 
was made with the vice CEO. The reason for the wish to interview the CEO is that the 
ownership of timberland is a strategic question. Apart from the chairman of the board, the 
CEO is perhaps the person who has the best knowledge about company strategy. Two of 
the lumber companies' CEOs also represented the main owners. 
Data collection 
The first contact was made through a telephone call to the secretary of the CEO. In most 
cases the next step was e-mail with a short presentation of the project and the issues we 
wanted to discuss. After that, the time for the interview was decided. In all but one case 
personal interviews were made. Due to time constraints one of the interviews was made 
by phone. The issues to discuss were decided beforehand. However, the order varied. All 
interviews were recorded and later typed. After the typing, the interviews were sent to the 
rnanager for comments. The analyses are based on the typed interview. Later, each analysis 
was also sent to the president for eventual comments. All interviews are anonymous, i.e. 
the names of the companies are not presented or the result. Some of the presidents did not 
bother about this aspect but others did. Thus, all interviews had to be anonymous. 
Results 
As the main reason for backward integration the pulp and paper companies' CEOs stressed 
the possibility to affect the prices while the lumber companies stressed secure deliveries. 
All three presidents of the lumber companies expressed a wish to buy more timberland, 
i.e. to become more backward integrated. Two of the presidents of the pulp and paper 
companies expressed an interest in selling timberland far away from the mills and to buy 
timberland closer to the mills. However, in Sweden this possibility is restricted for compa-
nies. According to the law, a company cannot buy timberland without permission. Another 
problem is the high prices of timberland: Prices that are much higher than motivated by 
the marginal production value. Today, it seems as if buyers are prepared to pay a lot for 
hunting possibilities, recreational possibilities, and just for the feeling of being a landowner. 
Pulp and paper companies 
None of the three CEOs expressed any intention to change the present backward integration 
or to rely on a market solution to a bigger extent. Reasons for this were: 
• Influence on pricing 
The strongest argument for backward integration, i.e. for these three pulp and paper 
companies to own timberland, was that this gives them a possibility to affect the price of 
158

--- Page 162 ---

pulpwood. In order to understand this argument, the reader must remember that these 
companies are major owners of timberland. Regionally, they often are dominating, i.e. they 
are the only buyer of pulpwood. Thus, the market can be characterized as a monopsony. 
• Reduction of delivery uncertainty 
Another argument for the CEOa is the control of supply and selling given by the ownership 
of timberlanda. Two aspects of this lsaue were mentioned. One is the reduction of delivery 
uncertainty due, for example, to bad weather, which may cause poor ground carrying 
capability. Thia problem cannot easily be dealt with by offering higher prices. In this 
situation many small-scale forest owners will probably wait a week or two with the cuttings 
inatead of risking ground damage. However, the pulp mills are extremely capital intensive, 
i.e. high quasi rents, and need a continuous flow of wood raw material. One way of avoiding 
this is through inventories but nowadays the inventories are at a minimum level. Besides, 
the wood needs to be fresh in order to reduce the use of chemicals. 
• Retaining negotiating power 
Another aspect is to avoid weak negotiating power when the market is characterized by 
a shortage of supply. The auppliers and the forest owners' associations are well aware of 
the possibility for the pulp mills to pay very high marginal prices for pulpwood in order to 
avoid a stop In production, This Jlleans that, at least theoretically and in the short run, it 
would be possible for the suppliers substantially to reduce the pulp mills' profits. 
• Insecure import 
All three companies Import pulpwood from Russia and the Baltic cpuntries. However, in the 
long run this supply is considered to be uncertain. One reason is the possible expansion of 
pulp production in these countries; another is inaecurity about the political development. 
A third reason Is that Finland is situated closer to these suppliers and thus is likely to 
out-compete Sweden in a situation chi:U'acteri:.:ed by shortage. 
• Exchange of timberland 
Two of the companies express a wish to sell or exchange timberlands located far away from 
their own mills and instead buy timberland close to the mills. 
Lumber companies 
In the Interviews, the three CEOs expressed a positive view on backward integration and 
also a wish to buy more timberland. 
• Secure deliveries 
The main reason for this view was secure deliveries. During a year the supply of saw timber 
VIU'ied conaiderably. Not least during the fall period it was difficult for all three companies 
to buy saw timber to the extents they wanted. ThU$, during the fall, and also when ground 
conditiona are bad, timberland owned by a company can be decisive for the possibility 
to produce. Recently, two of the companies invested in new production facilities. The 
presidents said that these investments would not have been justified if they did not own 
timberland. 
• Better information facilitates planning 
159

--- Page 163 ---

Another common argument that the three CEOs used for vertical integration was that it 
made it easier to plan the production and also meet the requirements of the customers. The 
information the companies have about their own forests is much better than information 
about saw timber purchased from external suppliers. This information is valuable for 
meeting customer requirements and also when exchanging pulpwood for saw logs. 
• Negotiating power 
The CEOs considered their companies as price takers for both lumber and saw timber. The 
opinion is that the suppliers take too large a share of the profit. The competition for wood 
supply is very servere. The situation would be even worse if it were not for the company's 
own timberland. The pulp and paper companies as well as the forest owners' associations 
primarily deliver to their own mills. 
Summary 
The results give strong support for at least the two hypotheses that reasons for backward 
integration are a wish to influence pricing (H1) and to secure deliveries (H2). Furthermore, 
the CEOs of lumber companies give support for the hypothesis about information advan-
tages (H4). It is interesting to note that this is not stressed by the CEOs of the pulp and 
paper companies. The same applies to control and coordination advantages (H3). Could it 
be that these advantages are marginal compared with the influence on pricing and secure 
deliveries? Neither is the issue about return on capital and alternative use commented. Is 
the explanation the same, i.e. that the CEOs judgement is that this cost is outweighed by 
the advantages of pricing and secure delivery? 
Discussion 
Issues related to all the above transaction specific capital and opportunistic behaviors were 
brought up during the interviews. The pulp and paper managers' major concern was 
the pulpwood prices whereas the lumber managers stressed secure deliveries. However, it 
should be emphasized that both these issues were highly ranked for both industries. It 
is interesting to note that at the bottom line this is evidence of deep mistrust between 
suppliers and consumers of round wood. 
Both industries have transaction cost. specific capital and high quasi rents. However, 
the sums we are talking about for a pulp mill are much higher than for a sawmill. Pulp and 
paper machines are running day and night throughout the year. For a sawmill it is easier 
to vary the capacity utilization through increasing or decreasing the number of shifts. In 
order to understand the situation it must also be mentioned that both industries sell on 
international markets; markets characterized by tough competition. Thus, the possibility to 
compensate high factor prices through an increase of product prices does not exist. Given 
the markets for the products and high quasi rents it is not surpriseing that factor prices are 
a major concern. Maybe this also can explain why most pulp and paper companies own 
timberland but lumber companies don't? In Sweden, the first sawmills were established in 
the middle of the 19th century. At this time most of the timberland had no or only small 
value. The production and thus also the consumption of saw logs cannot be compared 
with the volumes of today. Thus, it was no big deal to get the wanted deliveries. The first 
Swedish pulp mills were established at the beginning of the 20th century. Also in this case, 
the size of the mills cannot be compared with the sizes of today. But, for that time, a lot 
of capital was involved. The quasi rents were high. Perhaps the owners were not willing 
to take the risk of temporary stops due to supply shortages? In fact, the pulp companies 
buying timberland from non-industrial private forest owners resulted in a law restricting 
the possibilities for companies to buy land from this owner category; a law that is still 
existing. 
160

--- Page 164 ---

If we return to the situation today, we find it somewhat surprising that the pulp and 
paper companies' CEOs concentrate so much of their interest on pulp wood prices, even if 
the concern for quasi rents is taken into account and costs for wood raw material account for 
a major part of production costs. Often on a regional round wood market their company is 
the only, or at least the dominating, buyer of pulpwood. Deliveries from the company's own 
timberland are substantial. Besides, import of pulpwood gives some security for smooth 
deliveries and also puts a downward press on prices, although the buying of these marginal 
volumes does not affect the regional price. For the lumber companies the situation on the 
round wood market is different. In some regions the competition is tough. Many business 
economic analysts say that the companies are paying too much for saw logs. Besides, 
delivery possibilities from the companies own forests are limited. 
From a business economic perspective it is easy to understand that a company has an 
incentive to integrate backward to avoid bidding up the price as more and more purchases 
are made, and it instead obtains the additional units of the input at their marginal cost 
(Perry, 1989). Using the same arguments, it is easy to understand why the forest owners' 
associations try to restrict the supply in order to avoid the prices falling more than the 
marginal costs rise. One way for the associations to control supply has been for them to 
establish their own conversion facilities. At least regionally, the pulpwood market may 
be monopsonistic or at least oligopsonistic. However, it should be remembered that even 
if small-scale forest owners own most of the timberland in South Sweden, about half of 
them are not members. On the other hand, the association owns all three major pulp 
mills in this part of Sweden. It should also be remembered that transportation costs put a 
limit on the possibilities to transport wood raw material to other regions. From a business 
economic perspective, it is also important to remember the cash flow. For a company to 
own timberland means that the savings in cash flow is the amount that the marginal costs 
for the company's own cuttings differs from the market price. Cash flow is also saved when 
saw logs are changed for pulpwood or residues. Selling of saw logs to lumber companies 
generates a cash flow. 
This description fits well with the conclusion of Andersen et al. (1990) when they stress 
that the basic driving force behind vertical integration is to reduce transaction costs through 
replacing the market pricing mechanisms with internal decisions. Especially if the market 
is characterized by a high degree of uncertainty, combined with transaction specific capital, 
ownership of forests is often seen as the only way to protect the company against oppor-
tunistic behavior when dividing the profit of the supply chain. Blair & Kaserman (1985) 
support this conclusion when they argue that a company has an incentive to vertically inte-
grate between two stages of production if both stages are monopolized prior to integration, 
but not if they are competitive. If they are competitive then the market will be efficient 
and the price the upstream company would charge the downstream company for the input 
is marginal cost. However, if both are monopolized, then by vertically integrating, the 
intermediate product can be transferred from the upstream division to the downstream 
division at its marginal production cost instead of at the monopoly price. The cost of 
producing the product will thereby be reduced and the effect of the monopoly eliminated, 
leading to an expansion in final product output and a corresponding reduction in price. 
The profits of the vertically integrated company will be greater than the combined profits 
of the two independent and unintegrated companies, since joint profit-maximizing behavior 
can be carried out over the two stages of production, which would not previously have been 
possible. Murray (1995) points out that on an oligopsonistic market the inequality between 
the input's market price and its value to the purchaser causes an asymmetry of signals for 
integrated versus open market suppliers. Because the extra incentive for vertically inte-
grated suppliers to produce the input derives from the distortion of market prices rather 
than from technological advantage in production, this negatively affects resource allocation 
and social efficiency in production. The core concept is that the oligopsonistic equilib-
rium introduces a margin between the factor's market price and the processing company's 
161

--- Page 165 ---

marginal willingness to pay for the factor. Since the processing company values the input 
more than the market price, it has an incentive to supply itself with inputs at a marginal 
cost in excess of the market price. 
The lumber companies' CEOs stressed the importance of smooth deliveries. This is 
a problem not least during the fall period and when weather conditions mean a risk of 
ground damage. These are strong reasons for them to own timberland. However, this is 
not so much a question of opportunistic behavior, the main reason for the suppliers not 
to deliver is concern about their timberland. This result can be compared with the study 
of Schmelzle & Flesher (2001), where they found that in three Mississippi based lumber 
companies the main reason for backward integration was to gain better control over, and 
ensure a constant supply of, raw materials. 
As the literature review shows, costs will be involved irrespective of whether a market 
solution or a management related solution is chosen. The market solution involves transac-
tion costs, such as costs for contacting, contracting and controlling. If, instead, a vertical 
integration solution is chosen, management related costs such as costs for administration 
and non-optimal allocation of resources will arise. It should also be remembered that these 
costs very much depend on the type of transaction that we are talking about, i.e. the 
industry we are analyzing. Examples of administrative costs are costs for control, coor-
dination and information. These costs will exist in all companies. Thus the question is 
if the sum will be lower if two companlel!l merge into one (compare with the above about 
synergy effects). Economies of scale in a broad sense may mean that these costs per unit 
will be lower. Non-optimal allocation of round wood may take place. A company owning 
timberland is likely, at least in the short run, to deliver timber from the land to its own 
mills even if it would be more profitable to sell it on the round wood market. Another 
example of non-optimal allocation concerns capital. The rate of return for a company may 
increase if, for example, capital was reallocated from upstream to downstream investments. 
In fact, many business analysts and journals claim this. 
In the interviews, none of the CEOs, talked about any of these costs. There was 
no difference between pulp and paper companies and lumber companies. It should be 
remembered that none of the industries are self-sufficient when it comes to deliveries of 
wood raw material, especially not the lumber companies. Thus, in any case both external 
and internal transaction costs will arise. This means that it must be possible for the 
companies to calculate costs for the two different organizational solutions. 
The pulp and paper CEOs did not bring up the issue about non-optimal allocation of 
resources. When we did, they said "Maybe you have a point here but many other factors 
complicate the picture". It is not possible to make a straightforward business economic 
calculation, as benefits due to landownership, through low wood prices, will be a disad-
vantage for the forestry division and an advantage for the pulp division. Something that 
complicates the possible selling of timberland is to find buyers without totally destroying 
the timberland market. Secondly, taxes also complicate the issue. Given the low prices 
once paid for the land the capital gains and thus the taxes would be substantial. 
The lumber company CEOs, in two cases also owners, expressed a clear wish to increase 
timberland ownership. Before making such an investment they made an investment calcu-
lation and compared it with investment alternatives. This meant that the actual number 
of opportunities decreased quite substantially. Even if they found it profitable to buy a 
forest estate offered on the market, there is no guarantee that they can buy it because of 
Swedish legislation. 
In summary, the results give strong support for the transaction cost theory. Backward 
vertical integration of the forest industry may be explained, not least for the pulp and paper 
industry, by transaction specific capital, i.e. high quasi rents. The fear for opportunistic 
behavior was expressed by both pulp and paper and lumber CEOs. The fear is that such 
behavior may affect both prices and deliveries. 
The six interviewed CEOs have clearly considered the strategic question about forest 
162

--- Page 166 ---

ownership. They present good arguments for keeping the backward integration even if it is 
surprising to notice that few comments are made about the alternative costs for the capital 
invested in timberland. The issue may be complicated by the loyalty of the CEOs, i.e. the 
company or with the shareholders? From a company perspective, there is certainly less risk 
involved with ownership of timberland. Maybe, the shareholders would prefer a higher risk 
and higher profits? This could be achieved through a split into two separate companies; a 
timberland company with less risk and lower profit and a processing company with higher 
risk but also higher profit. It is then up to the shareholders to decide the mix of their 
portfolio. Could the lumber companies' CEOs interest in increasing timberland ownership 
at least partially be explained for private reasons. Is this a good mixture from a portfolio 
perspective? Perhaps also tax reasons can explain part of the interest? 
The interviews do not give an answer to the question how much timberland a company 
ought to own in order to achieve the benefits of vertical integration. It is also obvious 
that some key figures based on annual reports do not give a correct answer about the 
business economic advantages and disadvantages with backward integration for the forest 
industry. The benefits of owning timberland for controlling prices and to secure deliveries 
are accounted for in the conversion divisions but not for the forest division. That is why 
simple key figures do not give the full or true answer. Thus, we whould like to quantitatively 
estimate what timberland ownership means from a business economic perspective. This will 
not be a simple task as it is unlikely that the companies will allow us to examine their books. 
One option may be to compare the economic results for companies with varying timberland 
ownership.

Part III 
Markets

--- Page 169 ---

The impact of government investment 
assistance on forest investment and timber 
supply 
MIKAEL LINDEN AND JUSSI LEPPANEN 
Abstract 
A theoretical model is derived in the utility maximum framework where the representative for-
est owner maximises his consumption utility. The owner optimises his forest investments and 
cuttings to obtain this target. It is shown that government cost sharing (loans and grants) has 
positive private investment effects. These results are not rejected in an econometric analysis of 
a Finnish regional panel data from Forestry Board Districts in years 1983 to 2000. Significant 
negative cost sharing effects on timber supply are not found in the data. Unit costs of invest-
ments increase wood supply. A strong complementary is found between forest investments and 
sawlog and pulpwood supplies. 
Keywords 
Non-industrial private forest, utility maximisation, public financial aid, reforestation, har-
vesting 
Introduction 
Forestry investments constitute an important part of forest management. Effort and ma-
terial inputs in silvicultural activities are targeted to increase the productivity of timber 
production. For the forest owner the investments in improving output of his bare lands 
(reforestation) and forest stands are an activity from which benefits (fellings) are obtained 
after many years. As the investment activities are costly and slowly maturing the socially 
optimal level of investments are not warranted. In the long term the continuous and sus-
tainable national level of timber supply and forest investments are not necessarily provided 
by the private small forest owners without government intervention. 
The private incentive for forest investments may be low for many reasons. The relatively 
low return of forest capital makes alternative investment projects more attractive than wood 
production. Self-financed investments reduce current consumption. The long maturity of 
forest investments and risks involved therein are difficult to reduce. As the stand grows at 
its natural rate even without major investments, the forest owner can take quite a passive 
role towards forest investments. Typically, the social or the government discount rate is 
lower than the private rate. This is also valid for the forest wealth. In order to sustain the 
socially optimal level of wood supply the government must finance the private forest owners 
to keep their forest investments at the desired level. Grants, loans with interest rates below 
market rates and tax concessions are the parts of this cost-sharing policy with respective 
technical assistance. However, the optimum level or the economic efficiency of cost-sharing 
rules are seldom asked. Generally the incentive structure has been one wherein, once the 
private forest owner starts his forest investment project, the government will support and 
partly cover the financial costs. 
The relevant forest economics literature provides some guidelines concerning the effects 
of investment assistance on harvesting and investment decisions. Theoretical papers on 
assistance are few but larger literature exists on the effects of investment cost on wood 
supply. Chang (1983) provides comprehensive analysis of site preparation and planting 
cost effects on timber supply and initial planting density (i.e. management intensity) in 
167 
F. Helles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 167-179. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 170 ---

the forest rotation framework. The rotation age increases when the site preparation cost 
increases. However, the effect on management intensity depends on the complementary 
between the rotation period and management intensity inputs T and Jvf in quasi-concave 
"production" function Q(T, A!) of per acre stand volume. Similarly, effects of planting cost, 
stumpage price and interest rates on wood supply (i.e. rotation period) and management 
intensity depend on the sign of cross derivative, QrM, and on the magnitude of difference 
between current annual value increment and discounted land value. If the target of forest 
investment and management is to reduce the rotation period (i.e. Qnvi < 0) then the 
increase in investment costs reduces the level of forest management and higher stumpage 
prices increase it. The effects on the rotation period are opposite. Interest rate effects are 
uncertain in both cases. Amacher et al. (1991) derive related results in a more general 
rotation model. The results obtained depend on the sign of Q ET where E stands for initial 
stand investment. 
The general paper on behaviour of non-industrial private forest (NIPF) landowners 
by Ryberg & Holthauser (1989) builds on the utility maximisation framework and makes 
comparison with the profit maximisation model (i.e. rotation model). Special interest is in 
both harvesting and reforestation decisions. Stumpage price and the cost of reforestation 
effects on investments are similar in both models (positive and negative). The rotation 
length effects are unclear in the utility model but price effects are negative. Investment 
cost effects are positive in the rotation model. Ovaskainen (1992) derives some results of 
interest in utility maximisation and forest stock production framework. He shows that, 
in the short run, forest management intensity is related positively to the next period's 
stumpage prices. Interest rates and investment unit cost have negative effects. Current 
price effects are uncertain. The effects on timber supply are opposite, but current price 
has a positive effect on supply and unit cost effects are uncertain. The long-run effects 
depend on the complementary properties of production function. If complementary is 
found between forest stock and management intensity, timber supply is negatively related 
to investment unit costs and interest rates. Vehkamiiki (1986) derives similar results with 
a related model in optimal control framework. 
The only paper that dwells theoretically deeply on government support, timber produc-
tion and cost sharing of forest investments is provided by Boyd (1984). He forms a general 
equilibrium model typically used in the international trade literature to analyse harvest-
ing and investment decisions. He shows that the financial aid part of the cost-sharing 
programme to forest owners is distortionary, affecting capital improvement more than the 
decision to harvest mature timber. Technological changes provided by forest assistance 
have unambiguously positive effects on timber supply. Likewise, the investment effects of 
cost-sharing programmes are positive. 
On the general level the theoretical results show that it is difficult to obtain clear har-
vesting results concerning the investment and involved cost effects on timber supply. Dif-
ferent models give partly contradictory or ambiguous results. Thus it is not surprising that 
empirical analysis has found many opposite results. Briinnlund et al. (1985); Briinnlund 
(1990) analyse the effects of subsidised regeneration measures on timber supply. They use 
aggregate time series data from Northern Sweden from 1954-1983. The empirical results 
show that subsidies have a non-positive effect on short-run timber supply despite the op-
posite theoretical results provided by the authors. Clearly positive harvesting effects are 
found by Ryberg & Holthauser (1989) based on survey data on Georgia NIPF landowners. 
The analysis is based on the logit analysis of planting and harvesting decisions. Technical 
assistance, reforestation costs and knowledge of public cost sharing measures had statisti-
cally significant positive effects on harvesting probability. However, timber price effects are 
negative. Reforestation is related positively to timber price, technical assistance and cost 
sharing. Investment cost effects are negative. Boyd (1984); Royer (1987) report similar 
results with the same type of data and econometric methods from earlier period. However, 
Boyd did not find significant cost-sharing timber supply effects. Carlen & Lofgren (1986) 
168

--- Page 171 ---

report that knowledge of thinning subsidy did not have a significant effect on harvesting 
decisions in the Tobit model using questionnaire data among 2500 NIPF owners in Sweden 
1975-80. 
One problem with these studies is in their data generation process. The data is based on 
personal communication and questionnaires with forest owners concerning their harvesting 
plans. The problem of measurement errors is often present in such microdata. Secondly, the 
reported econometric estimates are not based on the actual allocation and use of government 
subsidies in forest management. Some other studies concentrate on cost-sharing effects on 
forest investment, not on timber supply. Brooks (1985) finds, using panel data from 1950-
83 among Southern USA states, that planting cost has major negative effects on acres 
planted but cost share subsidy payments reduce the effects. de Steiguer (1984) finds, 
using related panel data, that government cost-sharing payments do not affect the private 
autonomous tree planting expenditures of NIPF investors. The investment expenditures 
are positively determined by personal income and negatively determined by interest rates. 
Coefficients of stumpage price and subsidy variables were non-significant. Vehkamiiki (1986) 
reports that lagged stumpage price has a significant positive unit elasticity effect on the 
tending of young forest (measured in hectares). The data used is aggregate time series 
from Finland 1955-1979. Tikkanen (1981) uses time series data from the Forestry Board 
District of Kainuu from 1954-1979 to show that expected forest income and government 
aid (grants and loans) have significant positive effects on seedling stand improvement work 
(in hectares). Estimated models included control variables for business cycles and trends. 
Modelling Forest Investment and Harvesting Decisions 
Background 
Forest investment is costly for the forest owner. In the short run he must allocate part 
of his wealth and income in investment project that reduces current consumption. The 
forest investment and costs involved therein most likely increase current timber supply. If 
the forest owner's income comes from wood selling and access to bank loans is limited, the 
financing of forest investment must be done with increased fellings. However, this is not 
necessary if he can adjust his current consumption in favour of savings and investments. 
The postponed consumption needs to be discounted and if the private discount rate is too 
high the investment plan may be too costly. If bank loans are allowed, the case for under-
investment still remains because the gains and returns from forest investment are typically 
lower than loan costs. However, the effects of forest investment on the consumption plan 
are reduced with bank loans. The investment effects on timber supply may be very small. 
Irrespective of these short run timber supply effects, the intervention of government cost 
sharing is preferred as the private discount or market interest rates are at a non-optimal 
level for private forest investors. The cost sharing reduces the private costs of investment 
and sustains the incentives for them. 
In the long run, the situation can be quite different since the forest investments induce 
positive forest stock and wealth effects. Thus, in long run, the wood supply will increase 
and in the presence of investment cost-sharing, the effects can be even larger, inducing 
also a decrease in timber price. This means that net social gains from government cost-
sharing programmes can perhaps only be evaluated in the long term. However, they are 
implemented in the short run to correct forest capital and income distortions caused by 
forest investments. Thus they must also have some observable short run effects. 
In the following we analyse the short run effects of cost-sharing policy of forest invest-
ments in the consumption utility maximisation model. Bank loans are not permitted. We 
show that under typically neo-classical economic theory assumptions the increased govern-
ment cost sharing has positive effects on private forest investments and timber supply. 
169

--- Page 172 ---

The Model 
It is assumed that the representative forest owner simply gets utility from the consumption 
goods and disutility from the forest management and forest investment goods. Thus we 
have a continuous and differentiable utility function

The assumption of disutility effects of investment stems from the fact that forest man-
agement is effort demanding and most of the forest investment goods are not consumable 
although some of them can have positive utility effects (i.e. forest roads). Note that the 
disutility assumption is quite important for the results derived below.

owner's consumption, investments and harvesting decisions affect his next period forest 
wealth. The investment and harvesting decisions are not done in continuous time but in 
discrete time, having effects on coming periods. In this sense it is more natural to include 
in the model next period capital stock and expected market price than use only the current 
period variables. The forest stock capital function G(I, S- H) has the following properties

The economic interpretation of the results needs some clarification. Our analysis implies 
that A> 0 and Uc > 0: the budget constraint is binding as long as the forest consumer's 
utility from consumption is positive. If the price of wood p increases the consumer's wealth 
increases and W > 0 and A = 0. This means that the forest owner reduces his harvesting 
(~~ < 0) in order to keep his utility maximisation at the optimal level. The argument for 
171

--- Page 174 ---

~~ < 0 is similar. Note that ~~ > 0. i.e. wood supply increases if expected wood price 
increases. When the expected market price increases, the value of forest owner's wealth 
increases. Now the harvesting rate can be increased without distorting the optimal plan.

the stock effects from harvesting are non-linear, G IIH > 0 and the investment returns on 
marginal harvesting are non-zero, G HI < 0, public cost sharing will affect forest invest-
ments and wood supply. However, under these marginal conditions interest rate effects on 
investments are ambiguous (see App. II). Only when GHH > 0 and GHr = 0 will the 
interest rate effects on investments be negative. When GHH = 0 and HHI < 0, i.e. the 
stock effects of harvesting are linear and the investments have marginally strong effects on 
the stock effects of harvesting, we have ;¥,: > O.The outcome corresponds to the case where 
the proposed investment incentive problem does not exist. The gains from investments are 
large. Thus the proposed incentive problem is valid only when the private forest owner 
considers that marginal investments do not increase the effectiveness of fellings ( G HI = 0). 
Note that the direct investment effects on wood supply ( fr) are not analysed in this con-
text. However, in Linden (2002) it is shown that the consumption maximisation needs only 
that <ijf < 1. 
Empirical Analysis 
Data 
\Vc use cross section time series data in the econometric testing of implications of the model 
derived above. The data consists of observations from the period 1983-2000 in 19 regional 
Forestry Board Districts in Finland. All the observations come from Statistical Yearbooks 
of The Finnish Forest Research Institute (METLA) and Statistical Yearbooks of Finland 
(Statistic in Finland). Some transformations for regional consistency in time have been 
made. The following variables were used: 
• H = timber supply (agreement on amount of fellings in m3 between the wood sellers 
and buyers): H1 =sawlog supply, H2=pulpwood supply 
• 19= geometric mean of investment outputs measured in kilometres and hectares 
• S= unit price of government financial aid, grants and loans to private forest invest-
ment (in current FIM) 
• C1= unit costs of private forest investments (in current FIM) 
• P= observed market price (in current FIM); P1= sawlog price, P2=pulpwood price 
• pe= expected market price for next period derived from forecasts of distributed lag 
model of observed prices (in current FIM); Pf=sawlog price, P:f=pulpwood price 
• TT= market interest rate for loans 
• D1= dummy variable for collective timber price agreements in period 1979-1990 
• D2= dummy for year 1992 adjustment period for change in forest income taxation 
• D3= dummy starting in year 1993 for alternative forest income taxation system 
• D4= regional percentages of alternative forest tax formulas adopted since year 1993 
Some variables need clarification. As part of forest investments are only measurable in 
the form of hectares (e.g. reforestation and fertilisation) and some in the form of kilometres 
(investments in forest roads and ditches) the geometric mean of these, 19 , was found to be 
the best measure for volume of forest investments in preliminary analysis. Unit costs and 
172

--- Page 175 ---

unit assistance prices of investments were derived by dividing the corresponding total sums 
of costs, and for simplification also government support, by I 9 . The Finnish tax system for 
NIPF owners changed in 1993. The forest owner was forced to choose, in the transition 
period 1993-2005, between the old system based on the owned forest area (site-productivity 
tax) and the new system based on the income from wood selling (wood sales profit tax). 
Depending on the particular age structure of the forest this caused some adjustment fellings 
in 1992 among some NIPF owners. 
All the price variables were changed into real prices by dividing them by the cost-of-
living index. Interest rates were adjusted by the inflation rate to obtain real interest rates. 
Observations of regional timber stocks were not available. All variables except the real 
interest rate and D1-D4 were logarithmically transformed. 
Econometric formulation 
Our target is to estimate and test NIPF owners' forest investment and timber supply 
equations derived from the model above. The model entailed the following predictions for 
exogenous variables. 
Investment equation:

The first signs in the investment equation mark the variable effects under the assump-
tions that the stock effects of harvesting are non-linear (GHH > 0) and investments and 
fellings affect forest stock independently (CHI= 0). 
Our theoretical model is based on the fact that the forest owner optimises with respect 
to the level of his forest investments and harvesting (timber supply) simultaneously. Thus 
we add to the above models endogenous variables H and I in equations for I and H. Assume 
that we can approximate the true reaction equations with the following linear econometric 
models 
In H1 =ao; + D1li + D2li + D3li + D4li + f3li In P + f32ipe + f33irr + (34; ln S+ 
(35; lnC1 + c; 1ni9 + E; 
(13.13) 
The consumption price variable PC is not needed anymore as the monetary variables are 
measured in real terms. q, E2 and E3 are NID errors of models that may correlate with each 
other. The endogeneity of variables H and I9 leads us to following estimation strategy. We 
estimate first the above models, without these variables as explanatory variables, with the 
fixed effect OLS (LSDV) model. In the second stage we estimate the models added with 
endogenous variables using 2SLS method and generalised instrumental estimation method 
(GMM). The models are estimated both for sawlog and pulpwood observations (i = 1, 2). 
Any cross effects between sawlog and pulpwood supplies are not modelled. 
Results 
Tables 13.1 and 13.2 give the estimation results with regional panel observations for in-
vestment and timber supply equations. The common time effects were not usable as they 
correlate perfectly with the dummy variables. The results for the regional fixed effects 
173

--- Page 176 ---

model (LSDV) show that the price agreement dummy variable D1 and the tax system ad-
justment dummy D2 are not statistically significant, i.e. if the effects were present, they are 
not regionally consistent. However, the period dummy for tax system alternative D3 and 
the regional share dummy D4 are significant. The results indicate that the introduction of 
alternative tax systems has decreased both wood supplies and forest investments, but the 
regional specific tax effects seem to moderate this effect. 
A closer look at the investment equation results (Table 13.1) reveals that price expec-
tations, In Pf and In Pi, are not significant for forest investments. Only the current timber 
prices of pulpwood have significant positive effects, both in the statistical and economic 
sense. Interest rate effects are not found. However, the unit cost (In ct) and cost-sharing 
(InS) effects are significant and are in line with the proposed theoretical model. The 
increase of the unit cost of forest investments lowers investments. 
The cost-sharing estimate has a value of 0.17 4. The related private forest investment 
unit cost estimate is -0.574. The former is approximately 30 % of the latter in absolute 
value. 
The LSDV model diagnostics show that regional effects are important, but residual au-
tocorrelation tests reveal that some model dynamics are missing. The relatively low value 
of R2 indicates also that some omitted variable problem may be present in the estimated 
models. To respond to these problems we added in the LSDV model both lagged dependent 
variable and endogenous explanatory variables as the investment and harvesting decisions 
are made simultaneously by a NIPF owner. However, these additions led to new problems, 
since the inclusion of lagged endogenous variable in the model with short panels introduces 
bias in OLS/LSDV parameter estimates. Similarly, OLS/LSDV with endogenous explana-
tory variables give inconsistent estimates. One solution is to use the standard instrumental 
variable estimation method. In this context we first appky 2SLS method that uses all 
exogenous variables and lagged values of endogenous variables as instruments. Thus, we 
do not include in the estimation, at this moment, the lagged investments directly as an 
explanatory variable. 
A more general solution to these problems is to use the GM~-estimation method. The 
GMM method is a general instrumental variable estimation method that is built on the 
moment conditions like E [(y- X f3cM M )X] = 0 that give consistent parameter estimates. 
In the following we use the GMM panel estimation method for differenced variables since 
we are not so much interested in regional fixed effects that can be correlated with omitted 
variables. We use lagged values up till 4 period as instruments for all endogenous variables. 
The Sargan test is used to test the validity of instrument set. 
The results of 2SLS and GMM estimation are found in the middle and on the right-hand 
side in Table 1.3.1. \Vood supplies have a major role in determination of forest investments. 
The effects from sawlog supply are stronger than from pulpwood supply. The 2SLS and 
GMM difference model approaches change the magnitude of parameter estimates somewhat 
but their signs remain unchanged compared to LSDV results. The tax effects are lost in the 
difference model. Nonetheless diagnostics show that model estimates are well determined. 
Concerning the results of wood supply equations, we can observe that positive and 
statistically significant timber market price effects are not to be found. These are lost in 
the GMM estimations but they were present in 2SLS estimations (not reported for the 
sake of simplicity). Tax effects similar to the investment equation are present here also. 
The cost-sharing coefficient is positive with some statistical significance, 0.013 (1.69), for 
sawlog in LSDV estimation. This is in line with our theory's predictions. However, the 
unit cost coefficient is in all estimated supply equations statistically significant, but it has 
the wrong sign. Higher private unit costs of investment increase wood supply. The result 
has some rationality in a dynamic theory framework, but in this context it is against our 
static theory predictions. 
The estimated direct investment effects on wood supplies in the GMM framework are 
significant and surprisingly large. Investment elasticity is 0.569 for sawlog supply and 0.568

At the general level we have obtained econometric results that reject some of our the-
oretical model predictions. This is not a surprising result in this context where different 
theoretical and eqlpirical results have often been obtained. Irrespective of these problems, 
the empirical analysis conducted shows that forest investments and investment costs are 
an important part of NIPF owners' wood production and supply. The results imply that 
harvesting effects on forest stock are nonlinear ( Q H H > 0) but harvesting and investments 
enter the forest stock generation function separately ( G HI = 0), Finandal assistance on 
private forest i):lVestments does not have secondary effects on investment decisions. The 
cost-sharing effects on timber supply are non-negative. Thus government cost-sharing pro-
grammes have, to some extent, solved the incentive problems found in NIPF owners' forest 
management. 
Conclusion 
The relationship between forest investments and timber supply was analysed in the static 
forest owner's consumption maximisation model. It was argued that the private forest 
owner has a low incentive for self-financed forest investment due to low forest capital re-
turn and postponed consumption. The government cost-sharing programmes are introduced

Preservation and employment 
PETER BERCK AND SANDRA HOFFMANN 
Abstract 
Preservation of forests and other natural lands is a major aim of national environmental policies, 
yet it is perceived as coming heavily at the expense of local employment. Forest planning requires 
estimates of these employment effects. The effects are likely to be felt both in the affected 
industry and more generally in the local economy. In this paper, we consider different methods 
and issues in estimating employment changes from preservation, including SAM, CGE, and time 
series methods and provide some results for California on the magnitude of various preservation 
policies. 
Keywords 
Employment multipliers, regional modeling, VAR, SAM, CGE 
Introduction 
California forests and wildlands are the target of extensive efforts to preserve habitat and 
conserve species. In the first half of 2002, planning for one large publicly owned forest 
included sustained yield harvest alternatives ranging between zero and 30 million board feet. 
Another tract of land capable of producing 20 million board feet per year on a sustainable 
basis was being purchased entirely for conservation purposes. While preservation may have 
far reaching benefits, it also has costs. This paper is about those costs, specifically the costs 
in terms of local employment. 
There are many ways of estimating the local costs of the withdrawal of land from goods 
producing activities. The ways differ in requirements for data and in their assumptions 
about the structure of local or regional economies. The standard methods of analysis 
utilize input-output tables augmented by tables of transactions of consumers and other 
agents. These Social Accounting Matrices (SAM) can be used in a straight forward way 
to produce linear multipliers for total jobs lost as a function of jobs lost in the subject 
industry. Using additional information on elasticities of demand and supply, SAMs can 
also serve as the database for non-linear computable general equilibrium models (CGE). In 
most applications, these models use prices to equilibrate supply and demand and otherwise 
mimic an economists view of a neoclassical economy. In the next section of this paper we 
discuss these models. We then expand the models to include decreasing costs as an element 
of the scale of a local economy. The new economic geography emphasizes the advantages of 
proximity, multiplicity of types of goods, and economies of scale and agglomeration provided 
by cities in fostering regional economic growth. This paper focuses on the related problem 
of diseconomies of scale and agglomeration associated with maintaining infrastructure and 
operating firms in a small community, so that decreasing the size of the local economy also 
increases the average production costs of local firms and the average cost of government 
services. 
Social Accounting Matrix 
A linear model of the impacts of industrial change is derived from a Social Accounting 
Matrix (SAM.) The SAM is a tabular record of all transactions that occur in an economy 
at a particular time. These transactions occur among the agents in the economy: producers, 
181

© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 183 ---

owners of primary factors of production, households, government, and rest of the world.

Table 14.1: A conceptual Social Accounting Matrix 
In the SAM, producers are represented as industrial sectors (aggregates of similar indus-
tries), which include manufacturing, service and natural resource industries. Households 
usually are disaggregated by income or other demographic characteristics, government by 
level of government. It is the inclusion of households, government and other institutions 
that primarily distinguishes SAMs from the older input-output models1 . Primary factors 
of production include labor and capital and also may include land or even multiple natural 
resource factor accounts, such as stumpage, agricultural land, and water. 
The columns of the SAM represent payments by these actors and the rows represent 
their receipts. The first row of Table 14.1 shows industry receipts for intermediate inputs 
from other industries and the rest of the world, for capital equipment from the investment 
account, and for final products from households, government, and the rest of the world. 
The first column of Table 14.1 shows industry payments to other industries and the rest of 
the world for intermediate inputs, to primary factors of production for their services, and 
to government as taxes. Since the SAM is based on current accounts, capital equipment 
is sold to an "investment account" which has transfers and savings as its income sources. 
Industries purchase the current use of capital equipment and plant from the investment 
account. Industries buy labor, land and capital from primary factor accounts, which in 
turn pay households and other owners of primary factors of production. Total payments 
equal total receipts, so the row totals and column totals for each agent are equal. 
To derive multipliers from a SAM, the SAM is converted into a linear model of the econ-
omy's transactions. For simplicity, let a J+1xJ+1 SAM matrix, S, be partitioned into a Jx 
J matrix of endogenous transaction accounts, T = [t;j], bordered to the right by a column 
10ne will also encounter extended input-output models in the literature. These are extensions of input-
output models moving in the direction of a full SAM and were developed prior to SAMs (personal commu-
nication with Adam Rose, Pennsylvania State Unversity, Dec. 2001). 
182

--- Page 184 ---

vector of exogenous demand, x = [xt, ... Xi, ... XJ, OJ= [sl.J+l, ... si,J+l ... SJ,J+l, SJ,J] and 
at the bottom by a row vector of exogenous accounts, 
As a practical matter, the SAM is simply rearranged so that the exogenous trade column 
and row are at the far right and bottom of the SAM. Let y be the vector of row totals 
of S representing total receipts by each actor in the SAM. Total receipts for any one of 
the J accounts in the region's economy (yi) is made up of payments from other actors or 
institutions in the economy (ti]), and payments from exogenous demand (xi): 
Yi = L tij + X j. 
j=l,J 
In order to convert the SAM into a linear model, the endogenous part of the SAM, T, is 
transformed into a matrix of fixed "requirements coefficients", A, with elements aij = ~· 
Substituting for t, 
or in matrix notation, 
Inverting,
