that is, regional, sectoral and household income (output) are a multiple of exogenous de-
mand. The matrix (I- A)-1 is generally referred to as the multiplier matrix. 
As an example of how SAMs are applied, we will consider counties that have experienced 
a decrease in timber harvest. Conventionally in economic analysis of U.S. forest policy, this 
decrease in supply due to environmental regulation and timber depletion is interpreted as 
a corresponding decrease in demand for timber exports from the region (Burton & Berek, 
1996; Alward & Palmer, 1983). This assumes that the region is a small supplier of timber 
that cannot affect timber prices and that the entire reduction was sold to export. The ques-
tion of how environmental regulation and depletion of timber supply affects employment is 
addressed by looking at the affect of the corresponding change in timber export demand, 
b.x, on the equilibrium represented by the SAM. In this case box would be a vector with 
only one non-zero element, the change in the level of forest product exports. The change 
in economic activity (or output), boy, is then computed as boy= (I- A)- 16-x. Finally, 
one would convert the change in output to a change in jobs by dividing the appropriate 
elements of y by the average number of dollars of sales per job, w. One could then add 
together the elements of b.y that correspond to industrial outputs to get a total output 
multiplier (or if one had converted to jobs, a total job multiplier). 
An example from Mendocino County 
The first step in evaluating the employment effects of a decrease in timber output is to 
find the direct effect of the decrease on timber and wood products employment. One 
approximation is to use labor productivity to convert changes in timber cut to associated 
timber-related jobs. In Mendocino, there were 10,718 jobs per million board feed of harvest 
(MBF) during the period 1996-2000. This figure is derived by averaging the timber harvest 
from 1996 to 2000, averaging employment in the lumber industry during that period, and 
then dividing the averaged employment by the averaged harvest. The figure of 10,718 jobs 
per MBF can be used to estimate the employment effect of a reduction of timber harvest 2 . 
2The use of average labor per output will lead to understatement of the effects of decreasing harvest 
because mills with the largest labor to output ratios will likely be eliminated first with decreased harvests. 
183

--- Page 185 ---

Each job in the lumber industry has a multiplier effect in other industries that supply 
goods and services to the lumber industry. A job that is created in the lumber industry 
creates jobs in other industries; a job that is lost in the lumber industry causes the loss of 
jobs in other industries. The model assumes that the product of the target sector is exported 
(and not further processed in the county.) The SAM for this study was constructed using 
the IMPLAN system, which is ultimately based upon the Census of Manufactures and the 
employment by industry data. 
The SAM multiplier for logging is 2.57. One additional job in logging leads to 2.57 
jobs in the county, including the one logging job. The SAM multiplier for sawmills is 3.41, 
for wood products it is 3.08. The jobs created include both jobs providing supplies for 
the work of logging, sawmill, or wood products (indirect effects), and jobs resulting from 
consumer spending by the workers employed in the logging, sawmill, or wood products 
employment (induced effects). The countywide jobs per million board feet are 10,718. A 
weighted average of the SAM multipliers is 3.13, so each million board feet in Mendocino 
County is associated with 33.2 jobs (direct, indirect, and induced.) 
In addition to employment and payroll, forestry has a significant financial contribution 
to both the State of California and the County of Mendocino. Various county entities 
receive 2.6% of the 2.9% the state yield tax, a tax of the gross sales from county timber. 
Recent prices for the mix of timber harvested in the county are approximately $500/MBF. 
Thus the county loses $13,000 in taxes for each million board foot preserved. There is also 
an associated loss of sales taxes from income no longer spent in the county. 
Further thoughts on local impact 
The calculation of employment multipliers begins by finding the lost direct employment 
per thousand board foot not cut. Literally, this is the derivative of the conditional factor 
demand for labor with respect to timber output. In a long run analysis, with constant 
returns to scale, this derivative function is linear. In that case, the usual and feasible 
method of computing the ratio of labor expenditure to output provides the estimate of 
employment changes. 
The more likely case, however, is that there are many different vintage mills, distin-
guishable by their labor productivity. In this case, the withdrawal of timber leads to the 
closing of the least efficient mill, so that the labor unemployed exceeds the estimate one 
would get from area-wide labor productivity measures. This will be the result for all cases 
where the marginal cost exceeds average cost. 
While economic theory asserts that markets provide efficient allocation of resources and 
therefore that the purchase of timber for preservation purposes is socially optimal, that 
makes an unwarranted assumption about the mobility of other factors of production. In 
the case of a remote place, like Del Norte county, California, the stock of housing, retail 
outlets, restaurants, county government, etc., is fixed. Removal of an industry makes this 
community capital redundant. More specifically, the removal of an industry shifts the 
demand for community services inward. Assuming that the average cost for community 
services is U-shaped and started near the bottom of the average cost curve, the result is 
that the community service sector is then operating on the decreasing cost portion of their 
cost curve. The remaining residents are then forced to pay those increased average costs. 
While SAM multiplier analysis is easily accomplished, the analytic framework has a 
number of limitations which can be overcome in the more general models described next. 
3If one knew for sure that the lumber would be milled in the county then the 3.41 number would be 
correct. 
184

--- Page 186 ---

Economy-wide, non-linear simulation (CGE) models 
Like the SAM multiplier model, a Computable General Equilibrium (CGE) model is a gen-
eral equilibrium model of an economy4 . It uses the SAM as its database, and represents the 
same transactions as the SAM model. But unlike the SAM, CGE models permit non-linear 
relationships between actors in the economy and adjust through changes in relative prices 
rather than quantity (Rose, 1995). As a result, the CGE allows for substitution among in-
puts in production and goods in consumption. This permits a more realistic representation 
of the adjustment process and results in less extreme assessments of employment impact. 
In the case of our California CGE, the Dynamic Revenue Analysis Model (DRAM), there 
are more than 1,100 equations (Berek et al., 1999). This is computationally expensive, and 
only recently has the availability of computer capacity allowed for the widespread use of 
CGE models5. Use of non-linear relationships also means more decisions must be made 
about functional form and choice of parameter values. In practice, while these choices 
are sometimes based on estimated relationships, they more often draw on the modeler's 
judgment and a stylized understanding of the economy being examined6 . This has been a 
major criticism of CGE models (Abler et al., 1999). Yet the parallel criticism of linear SAM 
models is that these same relationships are modeled under the arbitrary assumption that 
they are linear. CGE modeling can advance in accuracy over time through more extensive 
estimation of the parameter values needed to describe relationships in the economy. 
A correlate of the fact that CGE models generally have a neoclassical closure, and that 
markets clear through changes in relative prices, is that there is no involuntary unemploy-
ment. In contrast, fixed-price, linear SAM models adjust through changes in quantity, 
including changes in total employment. This can be thought of either as labor migra-
tion or, on the downside, unemployment. Since all relationships are specified in the CGE 
model, intermediate (non-neoclassical) cases with stickiness in labor market adjustment 
and therefore unemployment can be created. 
The investment, migration and trade equations that specify the closure of the economy 
to the rest of the world receive particular attention in CGE models. In the case of DRAM, 
none of the equations needed to connect California to the rest of the world were ideal. 
Within the U.S., there is very limited data on the flow of goods between states. As a result, 
trade flows must be inferred from other data. California sectoral output was derived from 
the I-0 portion of a U.S. SAM by using California's share of national sectoral employment 
to determine California's share of national sectoral output. U.S. Consumer Expenditure 
Survey data was used to estimate consumer demand functions for goods and services. 
The difference between output and demand was taken as trade. In addition, imported 
and domestically produced goods are not perfect substitutes in DRAM. The model uses 
an Armington specification that requires an elasticity of substitution between foreign and 
domestic goods used in the production of domestic consumption goods. This elasticity 
cannot be estimated from U.S. data and is instead taken from international trade studies. 
Similarly, migration between U.S. states or within a state is not directly measured. In the 
U.S., significant efforts are being made to estimate interstate migration from tax records. 
Capital plant and equipment investment is not tracked by the state in which it is installed. 
Similarly, capital investment is not traceable by state from primary sources. Of investment, 
migration, and trade, it turns out that DRAM is most sensitive to the assumptions one 
makes about imports, particularly the substitutability of foreign and domestic goods. 
4Dervis et al. (1982); Sadoulet & de Janvry (1995); Lofgren et al. (2001) provide good introductions to 
CGE modeling. 
5CGE models have a long history of use in international trade and national policy analysis. In the 
U.S. they have recently begun to be used for state and multi-state analysis. Lack of availability of data, 
uncertainty about parameter estimates, and greater appropriateness of linear functional form has limited 
their use at the sub-state level (Partridge and Rickman 1998). 
6CGE models are usually created by adapting existing models to current needs. This reduces the modeling 
burden, but can add opaqueness and makes it easy to adopt prior modeling choices uncritically. 
185

--- Page 187 ---

To find the change in jobs implicit in a policy, one could run the CGE model twice, 
once with and once without the policy in place. The model would track the labor market: 
how many are working, how many are not working (the wage rate changes labor force 
participation), and how many migrated. Take for example, a restriction in timber harvest. 
It is common in forest policy analysis to assume that forestry sector output is a linear 
function of stumpage. Since it is assumed that output is produced in fixed proportion to 
stumpage input, one can model a decrease in stumpage as an increase in stumpage price 
through a tax on timber output. In actuality, most of the money paid for stumpage goes to 
out-of-state capital owners. Modeling the stumpage price increase as a federal excise tax 
has the same effect: the net price of the output goes down and the money raised leaves the 
state with no effect on any other variable. To find the right level of the tax, one runs the 
model with different size taxes and finds the resulting size of the timber sector. One picks 
that tax that corresponds to the timber sector size one wants to achieve. The resultant 
tax take divided by the stumpage input use is the increase in the price of stumpage that 
would occur if the quantity were restricted. Table 14.2 shows the effects of a 10, 20, and 30 
percent tax on the "Other Primary" industrial sector, the sector that includes non-energy, 
minerals, fishing, and forestry.

California Environmental Computable General Equilibrium Model 
From Table 14.2, one can see that to achieve a 50% reduction in sectoral output, one 
would require a tax of a little over 30%. The industry would have roughly 3,400 fewer 
workers, while the state as a whole would have only 1,800 fewer people in the workforce. 
This is the opposite of the logic in the multiplier models. Here, many of the people who 
stop working in the other primary sector start working in other sectors. The employment 
multiplier is less than 1. Again looking at the Table, there is a considerable increase in 
imports as a result of the tax. If the elasticity of substitution between foreign and domestic 
produced goods were higher, this effect would be more pronounced, as would the job losses 
in the primary sector. 
Time series approaches to deriving impact multipliers 
In the United States, monthly time series data on employment by industry are collected as 
a consequence of the unemployment insurance programs. The use of time series models, 
estimated with these data permits the direct estimation of employment impacts. These 
estimates capture the actual adjustment that occurred in local employment in response to 
186

--- Page 188 ---

a past change in labor market conditions. In this section, we examine three time series 
approaches to estimating multipliers. 
Data 
We present an example in which the data for employment are a monthly time series of 
county employment beginning in 1984 and ending in March of 2002. The county time 
series included are farm employment (Farm), timber employment (SIC 24 only) (IS24), 
service employment (Service), government employment (G), and other goods producing 
manufacture employment (N24). In addition, the county unemployment rate and two series 
for the State of California, total state employment (CAT) and the state unemployment rate 
(CAU), were included. Not all models utilized all of these measures. These series were 
collected for two very different counties, Mendocino and Humboldt. Both have substantial 
timber employment, but Mendocino has a prominent arts and recreation industry and is 
located at the edge of the San Francisco urban area while Humboldt is centered around the 
towns Arcata and Eureka and is totally out of commuting distance. 
Vector autoregression 
We develop vector autoregression (VAR) models of the county economies. A VAR is simply 
a regression of a group of variables on their lags. The VAR models developed here can be 
interpreted as the solution to a simple general equilibrium model of a county economy open 
to interactions with the state economy. The method chosen for estimating the VARs was the 
Litterman-Doan Bayesian auto regression, as implemented by Doan in RATS (ESTIMA, 
2000). The number of parameters per equation is the number of variables times the number 
of lags, so the use of long lags is not feasible with moderate sized data sets. With monthly 
data it is desirable (though not always necessary) to use at least 13 lags to account for 
the seasonal nature of the data. With 6 variables, this would account for 78 degrees of 
freedom per equation, where our data sets have 219 observations. A Bayesian VAR (BVAR) 
alternative is to use the long lags and impose a prior that the size of the coefficients decays 
at a harmonic rate and that the on-diagonal elements are larger than the off-diagonal 
elements. The BVAR process was implemented with Doan's suggested settings for the 
hyperparameters that control the tightness and decay of the prior. 
The estimated BVARs were then used to calculate two types of impact multipliers for 
timber employment on other variables, particularly total employment. The first, "impulse 
multipliers", were calculated as the results of a one standard deviation shock in the first 
period, the impulse response. (It is calculated from the moving average (MA) representation 
of the VAR (see e.g., Hamilton (1994)). A second set of multipliers were calculated from 
the BVAR model as a conditional forecast, the conditioning being that the level of timber 
employment was increased by one standard deviation and constrained (on a six monthly 
basis) to remain one standard deviation above the unconditional forecast. The difference 
in the other series between the conditional and unconditional forecast gives the short-run 
"conditional" BVAR multiplier. 
Humboldt County 
The BVAR for Humboldt consisted of equations for county timber employment (IS24), 
county non-timber goods manufacture employment,(N24), county unemployment (U), county 
government employment, (G), county total employment (TOT), and two statewide vari-
ables, California total employment (CAT) and California unemployment (CAU). County 
timber employment includes only employment in the wood products industry, SIC 24). We 
use two approaches to looking for evidence of cointegration in this VAR. First we use stan-
dard extended Dickey Fuller tests. On this basis, each of the seven series appear to be J(1). 
We then regress TOT on the remaining six variables. The error vector of this regression is 
187

--- Page 189 ---

I(O). In other words, this particular linear combination of time series that have unit roots 
does not have a unit root, i.e. it is cointegrated. We also use the Amax and Trace tests 
of Johnanssen to search for evidence of cointegration among the seven time series. These 
tests indicate the existence of three cointegrating vectors. Ultimately, we would like to use 
time series analysis to derive long-run employment multipliers. Since there is cointegration 
among the time series, estimation in error correction form theoretically could be used to 
derive long-run multipliers. This is examined more completely in Berek et al. (2000). In 
practice, given that we have a cointegrating rank of three and seven time series, we can-
not derive long-run multipliers directly from the cointegrating space by using comparative 
statics to examine the impact of an exogenous change in timber employment (SIC 24) on 
the remaining time series. Even if we were to make use of the fact that Humboldt is very 
small relative to California as a whole by assuming that the state-level variables remained 
fixed throughout the study period, we would still have one too many degrees of freedom 
to perform a comparative statics analysis of the impact of a change in county-level tim-
ber employment on the other county-level variables. With three cointegrating equations 
and the two state-level variables fixed at their 2002 levels, there are a total of five linear 
relationships among the seven time series. In order to perform comparative statics, one 
needs six relationships among the seven variables. The case of Mendocino county, below, 
is, however amenable to this type of analysis. 
Since the cointegrating space alone does not provide multipliers in the case of Humbolt 
county, we turn to the VAR in levels for estimates of the dynamics. Fuller (1976) showed 
that there is no increase in efficiency in estimating an error correction model over a VAR 
in levels, even when the series are cointegrated. 
All R2s were .99 or higher, which is to be expected in a monthly VARin levels. None 
of the series of residuals showed autocorrelation (Durbin-Watson statistic). In all but one 
equation, an F-test rejects the omission of all of the lags of CAT. In line with the BVAR 
prior, the exclusion of own lags is always rejected by the F-Test. Also in line with the prior, 
exclusion of the lags of variables other than CAT is almost always accepted. However, a 
Chi-squared test for the omission of all non-state, non-diagonal terms rejects that joint 
hypothesis. 
The decomposition of the errors analysis shows that IS24 errors are (at 5 years) equally 
influenced by their own innovations and by those of CAT, statewide employment. This 
is in line with the findings of Burton & Berek (1996) that macro variables drive timber 
harvest. The error variance analysis for TOT at 1 quarter is dominated by the government 
term, while at 5 years CAT, G, and N24 all account for more than 10% of the forecast 
error. The impulse response to an addition of 88 jobs (or one standard deviation from the 
one period ahead forecast) to IS24 is an increase in total jobs of 31 at 5 months, cycling 
between net additional and net loss, and damping so that the effect at 5 years is 7 jobs. 
The standard error in this forecast is 526 jobs at 1 quarter increasing to 1,395 jobs at 5 
years. The impact multiplier for IS24 on IS24 declines rapidly to zero at 7 months and then 
also exhibits damped cycles with a value of 9 jobs at 5 years. Other series show a similar 
pattern. The standard error of forecast for IS24 is 313 at 5 years. 
The second multiplier model (the "conditional multiplier")is for a permanent increase 
in the number of jobs. To accomplish this, the series were first forecast for 5 years from the 
VAR, these are the unconditional forecasts. A second set of forecasts was made with IS24 
constrained to the value of the unconditional forecasts plus one standard error, 88 jobs. 
Figure 14.1 shows the conditional and unconditional forecasts for total county jobs. 
At 5 years the difference between the two series is 416 jobs. The implied multiplier is 
4. 73, which is higher than the SAM estimates. 
Mendocino County 
The same steps were taken to estimate a VARin levels for Mendocino as for Humboldt. 
Again, the series were individually J(1) by a Dickey Fuller test, but in this county there is

Figure 14.1: Conditional and unconditional forecasts of total jobs for Humboldt County, California 
no satisfactory cointegrating regression, at least as measured by the Dickey Fuller test. 
Again the regressions in levels fit with very large R2 (.99+ ). The own lag terms dominate 
in the VAR, though again the test of excluding the remaining county variables rejects 
exclusion. Both impulse and conditional multipliers are calculated. An impulse of 80 jobs 
(one standard deviation to the one period ahead forecast) to IS24 declines to 0 jobs in 
7 quarters. The maximum impact on TOT is at 5 quarters and is 62 jobs. Both TOT 
and IS24 then cycle between positive and negative and the cycles in IS24 damp, though 
within the 5 years those in TOT are undamped. Figure 14.2 shows the conditional and 
unconditional forecasts for forecasts for total county jobs. 
The difference between the conditional and unconditional forecasts at 5 years is 202 for 
an implied conditional multiplier of 2.53, nearly the same as the SAM multiplier. 
A VAR model for y = (Farm, Service, IS24, N24, CAU and CAT) was estimated using 
five lags and a cointegrating rank of three. The method of choosing the lags was to increase 
the lags until there were no large changes in the rank test from one lag to the next. An 
alternative method of choosing lags is to use a likelihood ratio test on an unrestricted VAR. 
This method, in our previous experience, chooses too few lags to produce stable estimates 
of the equation. The problem of lag length in monthly data could also be solved by using a 
number of lags long enough to account for the seasonality, 13 lags in this case. That solution 
is prohibitive because, with 6 variables, 13 lags would account for 78 degrees of freedom, 
where the sample has 214 observations. A better solution would be to use the BVAR 
methods, described above, to shrink the coefficients of the longer lags and particularly of 
the longer off-diagonal lags, while using the maximum likelihood methods of Johansson 
(1995). So far, this estimation challenge has defeated us. 
Two tests are used to determine the rank of the cointegrating space, the A max test and 
the Trace test. With five lags in the model the Amax test rejects a rank of two in favor of a 
rank of three, but does not reject a rank of three in favor of a rank of four at the 90% level 
of confidence. The trace test does not reject a rank of two in favor of higher rank, also at 
the 90% level of confidence. Based on these results we have chosen a rank of three. The R2 
189

Figure 14.2: Conditional and unconditional forecasts of total jobs for Mendocino County, California 
for farm output and services are over 80 percent, while those for other goods and timber 
are between 56 and 76 percent. 
Of the six series only the California unemployment series was weakly exogenous at the 
10% level of significance. A variable is weakly exogeous to a dynamic system if it plays 
no role in the system's adjustment back to equilibrium after a shock. Both the service 
sector and the total of California employment were weakly exogenous at the 5% level 
of significance. Although total California employment would be expected to be weakly 
exogenous to the county variables, there is no reason to believe it is not influenced by 
changes in state-level unemployment. California unemployment, similarly, should respond 
to state employment. The exogeneity of the county service sector is not at all an anticipated 
result. Economic base theory asserts the opposite: county non-service employment should 
determine service employment. The definition of the service sector includes industries like 
health care as a large component and these industries don't have the same business cycle 
properties as timber, farming or goods production, hence the potential for weak exogeneity 
in the data. 
It is not surprising that both state variables, California employment and unemployment, 
were weakly exogenous to the county variables. The level of economic activity in Mendocino 
county is extremely small relative to California as a whole. This result can be used in 
deriving estimates of long-run county multipliers directly from co-integrating space through 
comparative statics. For Mendocino county, the variable space is six dimensional. We have 
determined that the cointegrating space has a rank of three. The results of the weak 
exogeneity tests support the assumption that the state-level variables will not change in 
response to changes in county variables, like timber employment. As a result, it is reasonable 
to look at the impact of changes in county variables in isolation from changes in the state-
level variables. Assuming that the two state-level variables are fixed at their 2002 levels, 
there is one degree of freedom remaining in the system. This permits direct calculation 
of the multiplier affect of an exogenous change in county timber employment on the other 
190

--- Page 192 ---

three county variables by comparative statics. The multipliers for timber are computed as 
dy 
( 
)-1 
d(IS24) = -
/3goods, /3rru:m, f3services 
f3timber· 
This method differs from the previous two time series methods in both holding state vari-
ables constant and in forcing a permanent additional timber job, rather than adding one 
additional timber job and allowing it to decay over time. 
The results for multipliers derived by comparative statics are that timber jobs have a 
negative effect, decreasing all other varieties of employment. Goods producing manufacture 
employment has a total multiplier (including its one own job) of 3.6 and farm employment 
of 7.3. The latter number is spectacularly large by multiplier standards. 
Conclusions 
The conclusion from this work and from our previous work on cointegrated models of 
employment impacts is that job multipliers exist, but are surprisingly difficult to pin down. 
Different methods, both simulation and time series, result in different empirical estimates 
for job multipliers. 
On the theoretical side, the often-used SAM analysis leaves out too many economic 
equations to be a totally satisfactory description of a local economy. The assumption of no 
migration or capital formation and of exogenously given demand are all heroic, as are the 
assumptions of linearlity. In these counties the omission of sectoral substitution in the labor 
market seems most vexing, as people shift their employment from the timber sector to other 
sectors. The CGE models are more satisfactory in this respect, however the lack of data on 
interstate trade and migration leaves considerable scope for sensitivity to choice of these key 
parameters. Time series methods provide direct estimates of the employment adjustment 
process both through all routes, migration, intersectoral relocation, and unemployment. 
On the empirical side, the two BVAR multipliers give answers most in line with prior 
experience, which is largely conditioned by working with CGE and SAM models. The coin-
tegrated models permit direct testing on long run relationships, however they do so at the 
cost of using a pre-test for rank. Forecasting experience shows that imposing cointegration 
when the root!> are only nearly one is costly in terms of mean-squared error (see e.g. Berek 
& Roberts (1996)). It appears that the use of these equations for calculating impact mul-
tipliers is also subject to these qualifications. It may also be that the use of shorter lag 
lengths in the cointegrated VARs leads to the lack of numerical stability of some of those 
results. 
The economic conclusion from the VAR work is that multipliers on the order of the 
SAM multipliers seem reasonable. While in these results it appears that VAR multipliers 
are the same order of magnitude as SAM multipliers, more estimates are needed to confirm 
the empirical relationship between these two classes of multipliers. Those concerned about 
the practical policy implications of these multiplier estimates should note that this entire 
discussion has been phrased in terms of jobs. The situation in terms of payroll is less 
advantageous to the counties, because the mill jobs pay about a third more than the 
average county job.

Issues and prospects in global forest sector 
modeling 
JAMES TURNER AND JOSPEH BUONGIORNO 
Abstract 
Increased globalization and the multiple uses and functions that world forests are expected 
to meet mean that policy makers are faced with increasingly complex decisions. To help in 
making these decisions global forest sector models need to incorporate more detailed information 
concerning countries, timber supply, techniques of production, and trade flows. This paper 
reviews differences in the concepts and information used by current global forest sector models. 
The potential for improving the predictive power, descriptive detail, and the usefulness of these 
models is then discussed in terms of the availability of reliable data, guiding economic theory, 
and practicality of implementation. As an example, potential enhancements to the Global Forest 
Products Model are discussed to show how greater realism and usefulness might be obtained. 
Keywords 
Forest sector, international, world, policy, modeling, econometrics, markets, trade. 
Introduction 
In the past, forest sector issues focused on assessing forest timber resources, and formulating 
strategies in relation to the needs of forest industries. This was reflected in the concern 
of forestry organizations such as the Food and Agriculture Organization, and the USDA 
Forest Service with the "gap" between forest product demand and supply (Haynes, 1993). 
A shift from top-down government planning to increased participation of various interest 
groups has led to a change in the focus of forestry issues. Policy makers must now consider 
the variety of goods and services provided by forests, the different economic motives of 
forest users, the linkages between the forest sector and other sectors, the role of forests in 
sustainable development (FAO, 1997, 2001b), and the distribution of policy effects among 
regions and sectors (Reichelderfer, 1993). 
Forest sector issues have also taken on a global perspective, due to stronger links between 
nations through trade liberalization, and international treaties. Key issues in the forest 
sector today are the impact of climate change, the effects of carbon management strategies, 
biological diversity conservation (FAO, 2001b ), causes of tropical deforestation, and the 
effects of trade liberalization (FAO, 1999). Faced with these issues, policy makers are 
asking for greater detail in international forecasts in terms of regions, commodities and 
subsectors (Allen et al., 1992; Reichelderfer, 1993). 
This paper is organized as follows. First, selected global forest sector models are re-
viewed in terms of the modeling approach used, the policy issues they have been applied 
to, and their level of regional, commodity and sector detail. The next section discusses the 
potential for improving the predictive power and usefulness of global forest sector models 
in terms of the availability of reliable data, guiding economic theory, and practicality of im-
plementation. In particular, the Global Forest Products Model is examined in more detail 
in terms of possible enhancement of structure, commodity coverage, and parameters. The 
conclusion deals with the key issues and prospects in global forest sector modeling. 
193 
F. Helles et at. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 193-205. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 195 ---

International Forest Sector Models 
In reviewing past work on global forest sector models, the models are discussed in terms 
of the modeling approach, the policy issues they have been applied to, and their level of 
regional, commodity, and sector detail. Existing forest sector models range from detailed 
single country models with limited international interaction (e.g., the Timber Assessment 
Market Model of the United States forest sector (Adams & Haynes, 1980, 1996)) to global 
models broadly describing the forest sectors of most countries in the world (e.g., the Global 
Forest Products Model, which covers 180 countries and 14 commodities (Zhu et al., 2001)). 
In addition to the spatial description of the forest sector, existing models differ in the 
way they describe temporal interactions such as the effects of forest investment and harvest 
decisions on future forest stock (e.g., the Timber Supply Model (Sedjo & Lyon, 1990), which 
uses optimal control theory). 
The forest sector models discussed here are broadly grouped according to their economic 
scope, from general equilibrium models covering all economic activities, to timber supply 
models dealing mostly with raw material supply, through spatial equilibrium models dealing 
with forest sector detail in multiple regions. Particular attention is given to models with a 
global coverage. 
Computable general equilibrium models 
Computable general equilibrium (CGE) models emphasize the links between the forest 
sector and the macro-economy. The central component of a CGE model is the social 
accounting matrix; a matrix of production coefficients accounting for all economic flows in 
an economy. Empirical data in the social accounting matrix is used to calibrate a nonlinear 
set of production, consumption, and transfer equations with endogenous prices (Taylor & 
Howitt, 1993). The effect, in terms of prices, employment, gross domestic product etc., of 
policy changes is estimated by recalibrating the model for changes in the empirical data in 
the social accounting matrix. 
The use of CGE models in the analysis of the forest sector is justified when there 
are strong links between the forest sector and the rest of the economy. For example, a 
full analysis of the impacts of carbon taxes requires consideration of substitution between 
forest products and products such as steel, aluminum, fossil fuel energy, etc, as well as 
the allocation of tax revenues (Hoen & Solberg, 1997). Also, in regions where the forest 
sector is an important contributor to employment and/ or gross domestic product, the 
effect of changes in the forest sector on the macro-economy may be of interest (Binkley 
et al., 1994; Alavalapati et al., 1997, 1999). Alternatively the effect of policy changes in 
non-forestry sectors on the forest sector may be more important (Haynes, 1993; Prestemon 
& Buongiorno, 1996). 
An example of a global computable general equilibrium model is the Global Trade Anal-
ysis Project (GTAP) (McDougall et al., 1998), based at Purdue University. The GTAP 
model is the product of research and support from a consortium of national and interna-
tional institutions including the OECD Development Centre, the World Trade Organiza-
tion, the US International Trade Commission, the Federal Agricultural Research Centre in 
Germany, and the Australian Bureau of Agricultural and Resource Economics. The GTAP 
model was recently used as part of an APEC study to assess the effects of the removal 
of specific non-tariff barriers to forest product trade, on country gross domestic product, 
welfare and trade. In particular; the effect of environmental certification of tropical wood 
products, the Indonesian log export ban and prohibitive export taxes on sawnwood, the 
Canada-United States softwood lumber agreement and trade liberalization were analyzed 
(NZFRI, 1999). 
The GTAP database combines detailed bilateral trade, transport and tariff data, which 
characterize regional linkages and input-output tables, which describe inter sector linkages 
within regions. The level of descriptive detail used for the APEC study was 24 regions, with 
194

--- Page 196 ---

most detail for the APEC economies, 11 sectors, three of which are forest sector related, 
three primary input factors (labor, land and capital), and a single government entity. 
A CGE model of similar principle, but much smaller geographic scope was used by 
Binkley et al. (1994) to analyze the economic impact of reductions in the Annual Allowable 
Cut (AAC) in the Canadian province of British Columbia. This was justified because 
the forest industry is a major economic activity in British Columbia, and the authors 
were interested in the impact of reductions in the AAC on macroeconomic variables; gross 
domestic product and employment. A CGE model was also used by Lin (1996) to model 
the Indonesian economy, in which forestry plays an important role. In contrast, following 
methods developed by Vincent (1992) for Malaysia, Manurung & Buongiorno (1997) used 
a non-spatial equilibrium model to simulate the impact of Indonesia's log export ban on 
domestic and international markets for logs, sawnwood and plywood. 
Spatial partial equilibrium models 
Spatial partial equilibrium models are the most common approach to international forest 
sector analysis. This type of model is based on the work of Samuelson (1952); Takayama 
& Judge (1971), and is characterized by the endogenous determination of demand, supply, 
trade flows, and prices. Examples of global spatial equilibrium models include the CIN-
TRAFOR Global Trade Model (Kallio et al., 1987), and the Global Forest Products Model 
(Zhu et al., 1998, 2001). The Timber Assessment Market Model (Adams & Haynes, 1980, 
1996), though more limited in geographic scope is similar in structure. In addition, the 
spatial equilibrium framework has often been used to investigate trade issues with one or 
two commodities as, for example, in Adams (1985); Boyd & Krutilla (1987); Buongiorno & 
Manurung (1992); Schirmer & Buongiorno (1985). 
The CINTRAFOR Global Trade Model 
The CINTRAFOR Global Trade Model (CGTM) was originally the product of research at 
the International Institute of Applied Systems Analysis (IIASA) and has continued to be 
developed at the University of Washington (Kallio et al., 1987; Cardellichio et al., 1989). 
The CGTM was the first forest sector model to provide a global coverage of forest products 
and markets and their many inter linkages (Cardellichio & Adams, 1990). The CGTM has 
been applied to a number of forest sector issues, including assessment of the likely effects 
of reduced timber harvests in the Pacific North West on forestland use in other countries 
(Perez-Garcia, 1995), the impacts on the global forest sector of proposed United States 
climate warming mitigation policies (Perez-Garcia, 1994), the economic impacts of climate 
change on the global forest sector (Perez-Garcia et al., 1997), and an assessment of the 
competitiveness of the Japanese forest sector (Eastin et al., 2002). 
The CGTM projects production, consumption, prices and trade for ten forest products 
in 43 log producing regions and 33 product consuming regions. Over 400 trade flows link 
the supply and demand regions in the CGTM. Products considered in the model range 
from hardwood and softwood sawlogs to wood pulp. The CGTM is an integrated model 
since it describes all aspects of forest products production: forest growth, wood supply, 
processing capacity and final demand (Perez-Garcia et al., 1997). An interesting aspect in 
the descriptive detail of the CGTM is the use of four different wood supply specifications 
representing regional differences in forest policy influencing wood supply. For example, for 
China, eastern-Europe and United States federal lands, wood supply is exogenously set 
each period, to reflect the fact that planning, rather than prices determine harvest levels 
(Perez-Garcia et al., 1997). Dynamic elements in the CGTM are changes in forest inventory, 
which shift wood supply, and changes in production capacity. 
195

--- Page 197 ---

The Global Forest Products Model (GFPM) 
The GFPM was developed at the University of Wisconsin, Madison, based on the Price 
Endogenous Linear Programming System (FELPS, see e.g., Zhang et al. (1993)). The first 
major international application of the Global Forest Products Model was to produce the 
1997 FAO provisional outlook for global forest products consumption, production and trade 
to 2010. Using revised exogenous assumptions and an improved GFPM model structure, 
projections to 2010 of global forest products consumption, production, trade and prices were 
made for the FAO 1999 Global Forest Products Outlook Study (Zhu et al., 1998). Since the 
application of the GFPM to these descriptive and forecasting studies, the GFPM has been 
applied to several policy issues including the effects of accelerated tariff liberalization (Zhu 
et al., 2001), United States waste paper recycling, Asian economic crisis, United States 
timber harvest restrictions, and regional trade agreements in New Zealand (Turner et al., 
2001). 
The GFPM integrates the four major components of forest sector models (Kallio et al., 
1987): wood supply, processing industries, product demand, and trade. The GFPM is able 
to project the general future trends in quantities and prices under different scenarios for 180 
countries and 14 commodities, from fuelwood to paper and paperboard. Commodities are 
interrelated by supply and demand equations, and manufacturing input-output coefficients 
and costs. Countries are linked by imports and exports from and to a world region. Inter 
temporal linkages in the GFPM are both exogenous and endogenous. Changes in potential 
wood supply, wastepaper recovery rates, trade tariffs and techniques of production are set 
exogenously reflecting anticipated forest sector policy and technological progress. Changes 
in manufacturing capacity and trade inertia are determined endogenously. Manufacturing 
capacity growth is a function of the previous periods' production and the shadow price of 
additional capacity. Trade growth is partly determined by growth in country gross domestic 
product (which is set exogenously). 
The demand equations for the end products in the GFPM are based on derived demand 
theory for raw material inputs (Varian, 1992). The demand models are characterized em-
pirically by elasticities of demand for each forest product with respect to national income 
(measured by real GDP) and real product price (Buongiorno, 1978; Baudin & Lundberg, 
1987). 
The GFPM model expresses short-term wood supply (for a given level afforest inventory 
and other variables that may influence wood supply), using a Cobb-Douglas functional form 
as a local approximation of the quantity-price relation (Binkley & Dykstra, 1987). The 
wood supply curves also shift over time, depending on changes in the level of inventory 
and other relevant variables. In the GFPM the shift in wood supply is exogenous. These 
rates of shift are estimates of how much industrial roundwood production would change 
without a change in price. They vary by country, and are based on information regarding 
past production, forest area and stock, growth rates, extent of plantations and the policies 
of each country. 
The GFPM simulates the transformation, in each country, of wood and other raw mate-
rials (other fiber pulp, wastepaper) into end products (sawnwood, panels), or intermediate 
products (pulps), which are in turn transformed into end products (paper and paperboard). 
These successive transformations and the attendant supplies and demands are represented 
by activity analysis, consisting of the input-output coefficients and manufacturing cost pa-
rameters. These coefficients were estimated for the base year, with the methods described 
in Buongiorno et al. (2001). 
The GFPM model's reliability has been tested, in part, by simulating the global forest 
sector from 1980 to 1994 with 14 commodities and 162 countries. The simulations were done 
conditional on known exogenous changes (GDP growth rates and roundwood supply shift 
rates) between 1980 and 1994. The model projections for each year were then compared with 
actual consumption, production, imports, exports, net trade, and prices. This validation, 
by replicating past observations, suggests that the GFPM projections are at best reasonable 
196

--- Page 198 ---

in terms of general trends, but by no means in terms of annual detail. The validity of the 
GFPM model was also tested by using it in extreme conditions, and judging the consistency 
of the results with theoretical knowledge of competitive markets. The extreme conditions 
included applications of the model to a single country, to two countries, removing either 
trade activities, or production or consumption activities, and extreme exogenous shocks 
(such as sharp supply drops). 
The ability of the GFPM to give acceptable predictions of the general trends in produc-
tion, consumption, trade, and prices over long time periods, combined with the agreement 
of the model predictions with a priori expectations in various conditions, suggests that the 
model can be used to address numerous forest sector policy questions, but the results must 
be viewed with strong caveats regarding their precision. 
Global timber supply models 
In contrast to the models described above that try to describe the entire forest sector, from 
raw material production to end product demand, some models have concentrated in more 
detail on timber supply. In particular, the Timber Supply Model (TSM, Sedjo & Lyon 
(1996)) was developed to study the transition of the world's forests from old growth stands 
to second-growth and plantation grown industrial wood. The overall modeling approach 
uses control theory (Chiang, 1992) to determine the economically optimal transition. The 
focus of the TSM on the sources of world industrial wood supply means that most descriptive 
detail in the model is provided to accurately describe the wood supply sector. 
While the models discussed above are static (e.g., GTAP), or incorporate dynamic 
elements that link each period's solution (e.g., CGTM, GFPM), the TSM model solves 
for an economically optimal transition over time by maximizing the sum of consumer and 
producer surplus in transition. This approach is based on rational expectations; as such the 
solution assumes economic agents on average correctly anticipate future conditions (future 
timber demand, interest rates, etc.) (Sedjo & Lyon, 1990, 1996). 
The TSM models industrial wood supply for eight regions, one of which is "unrespon-
sive" (the level of wood supply is independent of market conditions). The seven" responsive" 
regions are subdivided into 22 timberland classes. Each land class is described in terms 
of the land class quality, location, accessibility, growth and yield functions; existing inven-
tories and their age distribution; silvicultural response to investment inputs; and harvest, 
transport, growing and establishment costs. 
The use of control theory in the TSM means that wood supply is described in terms 
of a set of "initial conditions" , "laws of motion" and "control variables" which together 
determine the optimal transition of the world's forests. The "initial conditions" in the TSM 
are forest inventory by region, age group and land class. The "laws of motion" in the model 
describe the rate of growth of the forest in each land class, and the "control variables" are 
the area harvested by age group and land class, investment in regeneration and magnitude 
of regeneration input by land class. The "control variables" are endogenously determined 
as part of the solution of the TSM. As well as projecting the transition from old-growth to 
plantation grown industrial wood in terms of the optimal harvest and regeneration effort, 
the TSM also projects timber prices from the intersection of wood supply with exogenously 
determined demand shifts, and trade flows. 
In contrast with the TSM model, with its economic motivation, the Global Fibre Supply 
Model (GFSM) has a purely biometric structure in which prices have no explicit effect on 
timber production. The GFSM was developed for the FAO to forecast potential industrial 
fiber supply, and aid forest policy development through the analysis of industrial fiber 
sources (Bull, 1998). The GFSM forecasts fiber supply by forest type and country for 
natural forests, industrial plantations, non-wood fibers (e.g., bagasse, bamboo, hemp, etc.) 
and recovered fibers (e.g., waste paper) using several yield equations. Forecasts of fiber 
supply levels from natural forests and other wooded land are based on the forest area 
available for wood supply, logging intensity and growth statistics. Fiber supply levels from 
197

--- Page 199 ---

industrial plantations are based on mean annual increment and plantation area statistics. 
Non-wood fiber supply forecasts are based on estimates of the percentage non-wood pulping 
capacity and country total pulp production. Recovered fiber supply is estimated from 
percentage waste paper recovery and total paper production statistics (Bull, 1998). Total 
fiber supply is simply the sum of these alternative fiber sources. 
The GFSM allows the analysis of alternative fiber supply futures by varying exogenously 
specified policy variables; natural forest deforestation rate, sustainable management policies 
as expressed by the cutting cycle, forest area set aside as legally protected, plantation 
afforestation rate, improvements in industrial plantation yield arising from research and 
development, changes in non-wood fiber pulping capacity and waste paper recovery rates 
(Bull, 1998). 
Assessment of Existing Models 
This brief review of global forest sector models highlights the fact that there are few truly 
global forest sector models. Other than the GFPM, CGTM, GFSM and GTAP, most 
models have a strong regional focus with either exogenous trade, e.g., TSM or only a 
few endogenously determined trade flows, e.g., the studies by Manurung & Buongiorno 
(1997) of Indonesian log export bans and by Binkley et al. (1994) of the British Columbia 
forest sector. The regional focus of most forest sector models is perhaps a reflection of the 
historical development of most models for analysis of national level forest policies, and the 
sources of funding for model development. 
The review also highlights the variability in not only the modeling techniques used, but 
also the level of detail. Most models have a particular regional, sectoral, and/ or commodity 
focus, with the focus of each model influencing the sorts of policy issues that the model 
may be used for. The GFPM and CGTM with their global coverage of the forest sector are 
suited to analyzing the distributional effects of policies that have an international influence, 
such as trade liberalization or the Kyoto Protocol. Models with global coverage may also 
capture the unintended consequences of national level policies on other regions of the world, 
or the mitigation of forest sector policy impacts in a country due to opposing responses in 
other regions (Cardellichio & Adams, 1990). 
An important aspect of forest sector models is the description of the elements of wood 
supply, including forest stock, forestland area, and harvest volumes. Both the links be-
tween these elements and, the linkages between wood supply and forest product demand 
are important. The forest sector models discussed differ greatly in their description of wood 
supply. CGE models tend to have the simplest wood supply specification due to the inclu-
sion of all sectors of the economy making these models large. The ability to increase the 
realism of wood supply dynamics in spatial partial equilibrium models containing complex 
trade flows is also limited by model size (Cardellichio & Adams, 1990). In these models a 
simple growth-drain approach (e.g., CGTM), or exogenously determined wood supply shift 
(e.g., GFPM) is used to model wood supply. Of the forest sector models discussed only the 
TSM endogenously determines regeneration effort as well as harvest levels. This complex 
specification of the wood supply sector in the TSM is, in part, achieved by simplifying the 
rest of the sector and the description of trade flows, due to the high computational demands 
of inter temporal optimization (Sohngen & Sedjo, 1998). 
Given the large number of policy issues spatial, equilibrium models have been applied 
and appear to have some advantages over the other approaches. The utility of spatial 
equilibrium models for policy analysis is a reflection of the model structure's flexibility in 
accommodating a range of scenarios, by including appropriate constraints or by changing 
the objective function. The ability to include additional constraints in spatial equilibrium 
models also allows consideration of a priori knowledge which limits the deviation of the 
model results from some set value (Taylor & Howitt, 1993). The trade inertia bounds 
in the CGTM and the GFPM, which limit the period-to-period change in interregional 
198

--- Page 200 ---

trade flows, are an example of such constraints. Constraints of this kind are useful when 
performing policy analysis as they can be used to generate a "realistic" base scenario 
(Reichelderfer, 1993). For example, in applying the GFPM to forecast the impact of regional 
trade agreements on the New Zealand forest sector, constraints were used to restrict New 
Zealand's expansion of pulp capacity, reflecting the limited potential for development of 
new pulp mills (Turner et al., 2001). Disadvantages of such a priori constraints to consider, 
are that poor judgment in setting them will result in poor decisions (Reichelderfer, 1993), 
they may reduce the consistency of model results with the models' competitive behavior 
assumption (Cardellichio & Adams, 1990; Taylor & Howitt, 1993) and they complicate the 
interpretation of model results (Cardellichio & Adams, 1990). 
Prospects for Improvements 
There is much potential for improving the predictive power, descriptive detail, and use-
fulness of global forest sector models, but they are all conditional on the practicality of 
implementation. Practicality of implementation largely relates to the amount of time taken 
to formulate and solve larger and more complex models, and to the question posed. Very 
often, one has to choose which feature to describe in detail, and which one to sketch su-
perficially. For example, whether to improve the description of trade flows or the timber 
supply component depends both on the difficulty of implementation, and on the question 
being asked from the model. Modern economic theory is sufficiently rich to serve as a basis 
for many different model structures. However, data availability is a major limit to imple-
mentation of all but the simplest theory. Ultimately, data limitations influence critically 
the choice of model structure, and the ability to estimate the necessary parameters. 
Below, we discuss potential improvements to the Global Forest Products Model, in terms 
of commodity coverage, parameter improvements, and model structure, as an example of 
the prospects for global forest sector modeling. 
Commodity coverage 
Using the GFPM to study the global timber market Tromborg et al. (2000) demonstrated 
the importance of making the distinction between pulpwood and saw logs (at present these 
commodities are aggregated as industrial roundwood in the GFPM) in terms of the differ-
ences in price projections for these commodities. Aggregation of saw logs and pulpwood into 
industrial round wood may result in misrepresentation of a country's competitive advantage 
if that country has an abundance of one type of roundwood relative to the other. Turner 
et al. (2001) using the GFPM to study the New Zealand forest sector, found unexpected 
results regarding New Zealand production of sawnwood, which may have been due to the 
inability, in the current specification of the GFPM, to adequately represent New Zealand's 
relative abundance of sawlogs. Making the distinction between sawlogs and pulpwood in 
the GFPM would increase the descriptive detail of the model and quality of representation 
of manufacturing activities by allowing the use of saw logs in the solid wood and pulp sectors, 
and pulpwood in the pulp and fiberboard sectors. 
Incorporating sawlog and pulpwood supply into the GFPM would require estimation 
of supply equations for these commodities, manufacturing coefficients for their use in the 
production of solidwood and pulp products, and base period commodity production, con-
sumption, trade and prices. While data are available for sawlog and pulpwood production, 
there are no trade data for pulpwood, but there are trade data for wood chips, which may 
represent the main form of pulpwood trade. 
Improving key parameters 
The base year, from which the GFPM makes projections, needs to use data that reliably 
reflect the present situation in the global forest sector. Buongiorno et al. (2001) employed 
199

--- Page 201 ---

a goal programming formulation to ensure that base year production, consumption, and 
manufacturing coefficients in the GFPM were consistent, and to estimate data that were 
missing. The estimation method used, however, assumed that trade statistics were accu-
rate. Incorporation of bilateral trade flows into the GFPM, as was done in the study of 
New Zealand forest sector impacts of regional trade agreements Turner et al. (2001), was 
hampered by the inconsistency between total country imports and exports, and imports 
and exports from and to individual countries. In a number of cases, the sum of bilateral 
trade flows exceeded the reported total trade flows. The methodology of Buongiorno et al. 
(2001) could be extended to provide more consistent estimates of base year trade flows in 
the GFPM. Adding unknowns to the problem increases the possibility of multiple solutions, 
but this may be handled with an entropy objective function (Lence & Miller, 1998). 
Country manufacturing capacity for each manufactured commodity is another key pa-
rameter in setting the base year data. Currently, manufacturing capacities in the GFPM 
are set to be 10% above the base year production of that commodity for lack of reliable 
data on manufacturing capacities. Base year capacities influence the evolution of coun-
try manufacturing capacities, and hence the evolution of manufactured commodity prices. 
Were a country's manufacturing capacity underestimated, a small increase in commodity 
production to meet increased demand could result in a dramatic increase in the price of 
the commodity when capacity becomes binding. 
More reliable base year capacity estimates would, therefore, provide more realistic price 
and capacity expansion trends. The lack of country level production capacities may be 
overcome by estimating capacities from historical production data, for example with a 
data-envelopment method. 
Improving model structure 
The main improvement in the structure of the GFPM would involve making the manu-
facturing coefficients, waste paper recovery rate, and long-term wood supply, endogenous. 
There are two advantages in doing this. Firstly, the possible interactions among variables 
via price changes are increased. This allows representation of the full impact of policy 
changes on the forest sector. Secondly, variable changes modeled endogenously allow more 
elastic responses to price changes. This is of particular importance given the length of the 
planning horizon in forest sector models. 
Timber supply 
At present industrial roundwood supply in the GFPM is endogenously responsive to price 
in the short run, but shifts exogenously over time. In contrast, the CGTM, and TSM 
incorporate the change in forest stock as a shift variable so that previous periods' harvests 
influence current roundwood supply via changes in forest stock. In the TSM potential 
supply is also determined by the price of roundwood via its influence on the regeneration 
decision. Tromborg et al. (2000) incorporated a price responsive wood supply shifter into 
the GFPM and found such a specification to have a significant effect on pulpwood price 
projections. 
Ideally, specification of wood supply in the GFPM would incorporate forest stock and 
forestland area, with inter-temporal relations linking current forest stock to past harvest and 
regeneration decisions, and growth. Such a specification would make long-term roundwood 
supply price responsive via the effect of timber prices on past harvest and regeneration 
decisions. There are, however, several difficulties with incorporating endogenous shifts in 
potential roundwood supply. Firstly, reliable data on forest stocks for the 180 countries in 
the GFPM are scarce. Secondly, the effect of forest stock on wood supply is uncertain, with 
many models, including the CGTM, using a unit elasticity for lack of a reliable estimate 
(Binkley, 1987; Cardellichio & Adams, 1990). Finally, political as well as economic forces 
influence countries' forest stocks (Tromborg et al., 2000). In the current version of the 
200

--- Page 202 ---

GFPM, the exogenous supply shifters represent the most likely scenario regarding political 
and economic influences on forest stock, based largely on expert knowledge of a particular 
country, and on the GFSM projections (Bull et al., 1998). 
The important influence of a nation's forest sector policy on forest stock suggests forest 
inventory and area are important policy variables in timber supply models. Forest area 
in particular is the key variable linking forestry to agriculture. Forest stock is essential to 
monitor forest health and to study policies involving carbon storage. Incorporating forest 
stock and forest area in models such as the GFPM would capture the long-term response 
of trade, for example, to policies affecting forest area and stock. 
The current exogenous specification of shifts in potential supply has serious deficien-
cies for some applications. Modeling harvest restrictions as limiting the shift in potential 
roundwood supply does not allow for a possible increase in potential roundwood supply in 
other countries due to increased roundwood prices encouraging expansion of forest area, 
through increased regeneration effort. So, in spite of the difficulties involved in incorpo-
rating endogenous shifts in roundwood supply into the GFPM, the importance of effects 
on roundwood supply for making policy decisions suggests it is an improvement worth 
pursuing. 
Although the theory of wood supply is central to forestry economics, and has reached 
some sophistication (Johansson & Lofgren, 1985; Wear & Parks, 1994), feasible empirical 
models, especially at the international level, are limited. This is in part a reflection of the 
lack of reliable country level data with which to estimate forest stock and forest area price 
responses. The lack of country level data also limits the ability to set base year forest stock 
and forestland area, and growth equations. Data limitations are further exacerbated by 
the multiple management objectives of different forest owners (Binkley, 1987). A useful 
first step to gathering the forest resource data needed to model wood supply across many 
countries are the Forest Resource Assessment studies of the FAO (1995, 2001a). 
Technical change 
Currently the GFPM incorporates exogenously specified technical change, and this specifi-
cation is used to simulate anticipated increases in wastepaper utilization in the manufacture 
of paper and paperboard. However, it would be expected that roundwood input to the solid-
wood and pulp sectors would be price responsive (Tromborg et al., 2000), implying that 
roundwood demand in the current GFPM specification is too inelastic, and that price trends 
possibly project greater price increases than are likely to occur in reality. An endogenous 
specification of technical change would provide more believable price projections and allow 
incorporation of the important influence of technical change on round wood demand. 
Technically, the PELPS structure allows for such substitution by defining different tech-
niques of production for each product. However, the practical implementation of this feature 
in the GFPM is limited, due to the lack of country data on the cost of alternative techniques 
of production. 
As well as the limited specification of technical change in the GFPM, the current model 
of the evolution of waste paper supply is also restrictive. Short-term supply is an endoge-
nous function of price, up to an upper bound set by the recovery rate and the previous 
year paper consumption. Currently, the upper bound on the waste paper recovery rate 
is exogenous reflecting best estimates of the increase in the recycling effort in individual 
countries. Allowing waste paper recovery rates to respond to changes in the price of waste 
paper would make the supply of this commodity more realistic. This is of particular impor-
tance given the growing contribution of recovered paper to fiber supply for pulping (Mabee, 
1998). 
201

--- Page 203 ---

Conclusion 
Forest sector issues have become increasingly complex over the last few decades with an 
increase in the goods and services provided by forests and the linkages between the forest 
sector and other sectors. Forest sector issues have also taken on a global perspective, due to 
stronger links between nations through globalization, trade liberalization, and international 
treaties. 
Existing forest sector modeling methodology has much potential to analyze current 
environmental and trade issues. There are, however, few truly global forest sector models 
with sufficiently detailed coverage of countries and products to answer specific questions, 
such as for example, the impact of China's admission to WTO on Canadian or Swedish 
pulp exports. 
Adequate representation of timber supply is a particular weakness of existing global 
forest sector models. At the very least the models must be made richer by explicit pre-
dictions of forest area and forest stock, in addition to forest harvest. Given the very weak 
existing data on international forest area, stock, and drain, even this modest goal will not 
be easy to achieve with any degree of accuracy, but it must be pursued. There is much 
to be gained from improving country data. This data collection needs to be guided by a 
model structure within which the data will be used. The model structure must in turn be 
based on a careful definition of future international forest policy issues.

Inventory and price movements of 
coniferous sawnwood in Europe 
ANDERS BAUDIN 
Abstract 
Lack of data is an obstacle for analysing the interaction between sawnwood inventories and 
prices. There are, however, methods to obtain indicators of sawnwood consumer inventory 
movements. The starting point is to obtain an indicator of real consumption of sawnwood. 
Since data on apparent consumption generally are available, the two time series can be combined 
to get an indicator of inventory change. The paper shows a method by which an indicator of 
inventory change can be obtained. Furthermore, VectorAutoRegression (VAR) is applied as a 
method for simultaneous dynamic modelling price and inventory movements. A cointegration 
approach is applied, where no indication of a long-term equilibrium is found. The hypothesis 
of stationary inventory movements is consequently confirmed, which means that inventories are 
mainly used for short-term adjustments. 
Keywords 
Consumer inventory changes, price movements, vector autoregression, cointegration analy-
sis, end-use sectors 
Introduction 
Producer and consumer inventories constitute the two general types of inventories with 
possible intermediate forms, such as importers' inventories. In this study the focus is on 
consumer inventories. The role of inventories, due to cautious actions, is to absorb specula-
tion, cover exaggeration of market up- and downturns and disguise failed supplier relation-
ship (Iwand & Rosenbaum, 1991). Inventories also provide perceived profit opportunity 
where elements of speculation exist. Inventories can also occur as the result of improper 
production, logistics and sales management (Gilbert, 1995). Inaccurate consumption and 
delivery forecasts are some reasons for this deficiency. 
The project was initiated in a situation when producers of sawnwood in Sweden found 
substantial decrease of export deliveries while indicators of European construction activities 
did not show any sign of decrease. Since the big bulk of sawnwood enters the construction 
sector, the obvious conclusion was that the consumers, mainly construction companies, 
fulfilled customer demands by reducing their inventories. Due to lack of data little is 
known about the inventory movements in general and in particular their relation to price 
variation. The objective of this paper is: 
• to present a methodology by which indicators of consumer inventory movements can 
be obtained, 
• define and estimate a model system by which short-term forecasts of prices and in-
ventories can be obtained. 
Driving forces of inventory movements 
Short-term price movements occur as a consequence of the discrepancy between demand and 
supply (Rosenbaum, 1989). The decision-maker tries to adjust by controlling the inventory, 
i.e. the production process. But inertia in economic and industrial systems cause inventories 
207 
F. Helles et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 207-218. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 208 ---

to be built up in recessions and vice versa in business cycle peaks. The correlation between 
inventories and prices is expected to be negative; inventory increase is expected to occur 
simultaneously with decreasing prices. A fraction of inventory changes, however, occur as a 
result of speculation. As prices increase, the consumer may expect prices to increase further 
and therefore, in contrast to the arguments given above, the consumer intends to reduce the 
purchase and, as a consequence, the inventories increase (Lord, 1991). Simultaneously, a 
countermove exists; increasing prices is also a sign of favourable business cycle opportunity, 
which means that demand is high. Consequently inventories may be reduced since sales 
increase more rapidly than production. Although these forces tend to be counteracting, the 
dominant pattern is expected to be the negative correlation between price and inventory 
movements (Figure 16.1). Furthermore, all these reactions do not occur instantly and, 
consequently, lagged effects should be accounted for in econometric modelling. 
An example of the (negative) relationship between sawnwood producer inventories and 
sawnwood prices is given in Figure 16.1, where the diagram presents monthly data of spruce 
prices and spruce producer inventories in Sweden.

A method to obtain inventory indicators 
Outline 
Data on consumers' inventories are rarely available. One reason is that measuring consumer 
inventories would require substantial statistical resources and would, realistically, be based 
on sample data. Furthermore, it would require considerable reporting burdens for the in-
dustry and the branch organisations. For sawnwood there are, however, some opportunities 
to get some measure of inventory changes. Below one such method is proposed. The usual 
demand indicator is apparent consumption: 
Apparent consumption = Production+ Imports - Exports, 
(16.1) 
while real consumption is defined as 
Real consumption = Apparent consumption - changes in inventories 
(16.2) 
Data on apparent consumption of sawnwood are generally available for most countries 
in the world. Consumer inventory changes would, by definition, be the difference between 
208

--- Page 209 ---

apparent and real consumption. The problem is that data on real consumption are generally 
not available. An indicator of real consumption can, however, be obtained. 
The largest amount of coniferous sawnwood is used in the building sector (approximately 
70 per cent, United Nations (1986)). An index of building and construction activities would 
therefore give a reasonably good indication of real sawnwood consumption. By constructing 
an index for building and construction activities and by transforming apparent consumption 
into an index, it would be possible to subtract real from apparent consumption. This 
difference would be considered as an indicator of consumer inventory changes. The last 
step of the calculation procedure would be to transform from changes of inventories to 
levels of inventories. 
Instead of using construction as the only indicator a more refined method can be outlined 
based on information of the major uses of sawnwood. It is then required that all activities 
be measured in one common unit, an index with the same mean and standard deviation. 
It is also required that there exists an end-use study that provides the relative weights of 
the end-use sectors regarding their shares of sawnwood consumption. 
The technique is presented below for the case of United Kingdom. 
Step 1: Obtaining indicators of sawnwood use. Monthly time series are collected for all 
activities that are connected to sawnwood consumption. The sectors, the indicator time 
series and weights 1of the sectors are: 
1. Dwellings, joinery and furniture. Indicator: Housing starts. Thousand units. Relative 
weight: 26.7 per cent 
2. Localities. Indicator: Orders of new work less infrastructure. Million GBP in 1990 
prices. Relative weight: 11.9 per cent 
3. Other construction. Indicator: Infrastructure. Million GBP in 1990 prices. Relative 
weight: 13.0 per cent 
4. Repair-Maintenance-Improvement (RMI). Indicator: Investment in Repair-Maintenance-
Improvement. Million GBP in 1990 prices. Relative weight: 9.4 per cent 
5. Packaging. Indicator: Index of manufacturing production, trend adjusted. Index. 
Relative weight: 11.4 per cent 
6. Do-It-Yourself (DIY) and other uses. Indicator: Private Consumption Expenditure 
in 1990 prices seasonally adjusted2. Relative weight: 27.6 per cent. 
Step 2: Forming indices. To get a comprehensive view of real sawnwood consumption 
it is necessary to convert into a common unit of measurement. All indicators in (1) - (6) 
above are transformed into an index with mean 100 and standard deviation 53. 
Step 3: Forming an index of real sawnwood consumption. The indices are denoted h 
to h for the six sectors given above and the relative weights4 are denoted w1 to w6. Then 
the composite index of real sawnwood consumption is: 
6 
RCI= Lwii;

(16.3) 
RCI will be transformed into an index with mean 100 and standard deviation 5 if it is not 
so directly.

--- Page 210 ---

To make comparisons possible, apparent consumption is transformed into an index with 
mean 100 and standard deviation 5. The index is denoted ACI. 
Step 4: Obtaining an indicator of inventory changes. From (16.2) it is evident that an 
indicator of inventory changes, denoted CSit at time period t, is given by 
!:::..CSlt = AClt- RClt t = 1, 2, ... T 
(16.4) 
Step 5: Obtaining an indicator of inventory movement. With an initial level of inventory, 
denoted CSio, the inventory levels are given as 
CSh = CSio + CSh 
CSh = CSh + CSh etc. 
The initial value can arbitrarily be set as 100. For the purpose of further comparisons the 
inventory series can be transformed to have mean 100 and standard deviation 5. 
Data 
Data are obtained from Housing and Construction Statistics and National Accounts data 
in Germany and the United Kingdom. Swedish Wood gives the inventory data. Data are 
given monthly and generally from 1986 and onwards. A special problem is encountered 
in Germany, where data exist separately for the two countries before the re-unification in 
1990. After 1990 data exist for total Germany, while data series before 1990 are given for 
the 'old and new states'. To avoid problems, all calculus and analysis is based on data from 
January 1991 in Germany. 
The United Kingdom will serve as an example of the procedure given in Steps 1 to 5. 
In Figure 2 an index of housing starts with mean 100 and standard deviation 5 is presented 
and considered as an indicator for housing, joinery and furniture.

Figure 16.2: Index of housing starts, private and public in the United Kingdom 
The other indicators are treated similarly. Some additional comments are, however, 
necessary. In Step 1 the indicator for packaging is the index of manufacturing production. 
It is motivated by the fact that wood for packaging generally is used for deliveries of 
industrially processed goods (often for exports). Before entering (3) the time series is trend 
adjusted. 
210

--- Page 211 ---

For Do-It-Yourself (DIY) and other uses of sawnwood the selected indicator is private 
consumption expenditure since the remaining wood products can be referred to as consumer 
goods. DIY constitutes an important use of sawnwood, particularly as sales at Builders 
Merchants and DIY Outlets. Both non-professionals and small contractors are included in 
this category. It refers to domestic purposes - often garden and other outdoor applications, 
small tables and chairs etc. Private consumption expenditure was only available on quar-
terly basis, so a smoothing procedure was applied by which monthly data were obtained, 
as if they had been seasonally adjusted. 
The composite index of real consumption, denoted RCI in (3), is given in Figure 16.3 
together with apparent consumption of coniferous sawnwood. 
120 -
