Figure 16.3: Index of real consumption (thick line) and index of apparent consumption {thin line) 
of coniferous Sawnwood in the United Kingdom 
Figure 16.3 indicates that the two indices of real and apparent consumption are related. 
The differences between the two series are consumer inventory changes, which means that 
when apparent consumption is larger than real consumption there is an inventory increase, 
and vice versa. The difference between apparent and real consumption refers to consumers' 
inventories (Figure 16.4). 
By setting the starting value, Clo, equal to 100 the consumer inventory indicator series 
is obtained in Step 5 of the calculus procedure. The resulting series is given in Figure 16.5. 
In the United Kingdom a time series of monthly observations for importers' inventories 
of coniferous sawnwood is available. Importers' inventory, transformed into an index with 
mean 100 and standard deviation 5, is given in Figure 16.5 together with the indicator of 
consumer inventories. 
The interpretation of Figure 16.5 is that the series are negatively correlated with a short 
time lag. Because of their respective roles in the supply chain they can be considered as 
communicating barrels. The time series for Germany are essentially obtained the same way 
as for the United Kingdom. The resulting inventory index and the importers' inventory5 
index are given in Figure 16.6. 
50nly annual data were available for importers' inventory, given by the end of the year. The seasonal 
coefficients of the inventory index were applied over the rest of the year.

The relationship between prices and inventories 
The negative correlation between prices and inventories was demonstrated in Figure 16.1 
for spruce in Sweden. It has to be born in mind that it refers to producers' inventories 
and export prices. It is not obvious that the same close relationship holds for importing 
countries, where import price variation is related to domestic price variation and where 
the consumer inventories are obtained from a calculus procedure. The first problem is to 
select an appropriate indicator of price. The alternative to domestic or import prices is 
export prices in the countries that are major exporters to the United Kingdom or Germany. 
Figure 16.7 presents deflated imports prices of soft sawnwood and export prices of spruce 
from Sweden, expressed in GBP, deflated and transformed in to an index. 
The development of the two price series are strikingly similar, with the Swedish price as 
leading indicator one to eight months ahead. The conclusion is that the Swedish spruce price 
can be used as an indicator of domestic prices in the United Kingdom. It is transformed 
into the domestic currency, deflated and made into an index. The relationships between 
prices and consumer inventories are presented below for the United Kingdom. 
Figure 16.8 gives an impression of the negative relationship between prices and consumer 
inventories. It is, however, not obvious which of the series is the leading indicator. 
The relationship between importers' inventories and prices seems to be positive with 
price as a leading indicator (Figure 16.9). 
The stationary - non-stationary properties as well as the appropriate ARIMA-model 
specification are of interest, e.g. for the test of non-stationarity, the Dickey-Fuller (D-F) 
test or the augmented D-F test. The autocorrelation- and partial autocorrelation functions 
are presented in Baudin (1998), which gives the argument that all three time series will 
not give significance in the unit root test and hence there is support for the hypothesis of 
non-stationarity. 
Model Approach 
The hypothesis is that there is a multivariate dynamic relationship among price and inven-
tory variables. The direction of causality is expected to exist both directions: Inventory 
movements depend on price variation and vice versa. The Vector AutoRegression (VAR) 
approach (Sims, 1980; Liitkepohl, 1993) is therefore a suitable tool for analysing dynamic 
multivariate relationships. An alternative, and identical, definition of the model system is 
212

--- Page 213 ---

liS

Figure 16.5: An indicator of coniferous sawnwood inventory (thick line) and importers' inventories 
(thin line}, both with mean 100 and standard deviation 5 in the United Kingdom 
a set of transfer function, or ARMAX, models (Box & Jenkins, 1976). It can be written as 
Zt = A1Zt-1 + ... + AkZt-k + Ut 
(16.5) 
where Zt is an (n x 1) vector, each Ai is an (n x n) matrix of parameters and tis a time 
index for discretely obtained observations on the vector of random variables z at equidistant 
intervals, t = 1, ... , T. This can also be seen as a reduced form dynamic econometric model 
(or final form) with k equations. The (n x 1) vector of disturbances (or random errors) 
are assumed to be stationary and identically normally distributed with mean vector 0 and 
covariance matrix I; ('white noise'). 
Cointegration analysis is a method by which long-term equilibrium as well as short-
term deviation from it can be modelled. Since there are signs of non-stationarity among 
the variables it is natural to consider this alternative. In the framework of cointegration 
analysis equation (16.5) can be reformulated: 
~Zt = f1~Zt-1 + · · · + fk-1~Zt-(k-1) + IIzt-k + Ut 
(16.6) 
where ri = -(I- A1 - ... - Ai) , (i = 1, ... , k) and II = -(I- A1 - ... - Ak)· This 
specification includes both the short- and long-term (equilibrium) adjustment to changes 
in Zt , via the estimates of ri and II, respectively. 
For the application of (16.5) or (16.6) testing stationarity is crucial according to the 
Engle-Granger procedure (Engle & Granger, 1987). The standard test is the augmented 
Dickey-Fuller test (Dickey & Fuller, 1979), where the null hypothesis is non-stationarity 
behaviour of the time series ('unit root'). It has been demonstrated in a number of applica-
tions that the test is quite weak (e.g. Campbell & Perron (1991)). The alternative to this 
procedure is the Johansen approach (Johansen & Juselius, 1992). In their procedure the 
starting point is estimating the coefficients of (16.6). The first question raised is whether II 
is a matrix of zeroes. If so the cointegration vectors would be unnecessary to estimate and 
the appropriate model would be VARin first differences involving no long-run components 
(Harris, 1995). 
Cointegration vectors, IIzt-k, exist when II = af3' has reduced rank, which means 
that there are r ::=:; (n- 1) cointegration vectors present in {3. Testing for cointegration 
amounts to finding the number of r linearly independent columns of II. This is equivalent

to testing the hypothesis that the last ( n- r) columns of a are constituted of zeroes. In the 
reduced rank estimation procedure set out by Johansen (1992) n eigenvalues are obtained, 
.5.1 > .5.2 > ... > .5.n, and corresponding eigenvectors. 
The test of the null hypothesis that there are at most r cointegration vectors (and thus 
(n- r) unit roots) is equivalent to testing the null hypothesis

where only the first r eigenvalues are non-zero. The hypothesis can be tested using the

None of the hypotheses are rejected for different values of r and for different test statis-
tics at 90 (or 95) per cent level and, consequently, there is no indication of non-zero elements 
in the cointegration matrix II. Since we have not found any evidence of non-zero elements 
in the IT-matrix, it can be concluded that it is appropriate to accept a VAR-model without 
any error correction term. Thus there is evidence that there is no long-run (equilibrium) 
relationship between the variables in levels and that, essentially, all price and inventory 
movements are consequences of short-term disturbances. 
In the case of the inventory and price series, there are no obvious trends in the time 
series. In spite of this, running first (and later on second) order autoregressions give high

Figure 16.7: United Kingdom. Deflated import prices of soft sawnwood (thin line) and export prices 
of spruce from Sweden (thick line), tmnsformed into GBP, deflated and made into an index 
coefficients for the first-order lag. These coefficients are all near one and, therefore the null 
hypothesis of the unit root test will not be rejected. A first-order differencing of the data 
is consequently appropriate. The selected approach is VAR-modelling (5) based on first 
differences of the data. Since all variables in the system of equations are endogenous, the 
model system itself is sufficient for generating forecasts. 
VAR-Estimation 
In the 'Model approach' Section it is concluded that it would be appropriate to apply VAR-
models on differences. In general prices depend on inventories and vice versa. Furthermore, 
there is a cross country dimension that is accounted for by including Swedish price and 
inventory variables in the German an UK models and vice versa. 
For the United Kingdom and Germany a model is formulated with three variables, all 
of them are endogenous denoted 
DINVIND = first differences of the inventory index 
DIMPINV = first differences of importers' inventories 
DSWEPX =first differences of Swedish spruce price (given in local currency and deflated) 
In the Swedish VAR model differences of producer inventories and prices are included as 
dependent variables. Consequently the VAR-system consists of 8 variables. As an example, 
the model for the inventory index in the United Kingdom is 
A(L)DINVINDt = B(L)DIMPINVt + C(L)DSWEPXtt + Ut t = 1, 2, ... 'T 
(16.8) 
Here, A(L) denotes the lag structure of the dependent variable in equation 1, A(L) = 
(1- a1L- a2£2 - ... - apLP). B(L) and C(L) are defined synonymously. 
As an example Table 16.2 gives estimated results for one model, where the dependent 
variable is differences of consumer inventories. The observed time series and the model fit 
are presented in Figure 16.10. 
Discussion 
The study shows that it is possible to obtain indicators of consumer inventories. The 
precision of the suggested calculus method depends on the quality of the input data. It 
215

--- Page 216 ---

115 
110 
105 
" 
] 
100 
95 
90 
85 
Year;Month 
Figure 16.8: United Kingdom. Consumer inventory index {thick line) and Swedish spruce price {thin 
line) tmnsformed into GBP, deflated and tmnsformed into an index 
is necessary that the chosen indicators are appropriately reflecting the activities in the 
wood-using sectors. Adding to this is the requirement that the weighting procedure also is 
reasonably accurate, which in turn requires an end-use study with good precision. 
Furthermore, the study shows that dynamic models can be specified and estimated. 
Cointegration analysis is applied as a means to account for possible trends in the data, 
but no long-term equilibrium between prices and inventories is indicated. The absence 
of equilibrium may be due to the inherent role of the inventories - to adjust for large 
discrepancies between demand and supply. Consequently a VAR-model is applied based 
on first-order differences. The model has the advantage of being directly applicable for 
forecasting purposes. The quality of data is, of course, crucial for the model and forecast 
precision. For Sweden, with accurate data (Figure 16.1), the prospects of success is greater 
than for the United Kingdom, where consumer inventory data have been estimated.

The project was initiated and financed by the Swedish Wood Exporters' Organisation.

--- Page 219 ---

Quality dimensions of wood products-
perceptions of German organisational 
customers 
RITVA TOIVONEN AND ERIC HANSEN 
Abstract 
This study examines quality dimensions of wood products, and compares wood and other con-
struction materials with respect to those dimensions. Interviews conducted during 2000-2001 
provide data from German companies or business units trading construction materials. Quality 
dimensions were analysed using factor analysis. Even though similarities can be found in the 
quality dimensions of various materials, product quality appears to take somewhat different 
dimensions regarding different products. Thus, it is difficult to define strictly uniform dimen-
sions, or measurement tools for product quality. This is a challenge for marketing and product 
development: The quality measurement tools should be defined product by product, rather 
than using some general quality measurement instrument. However, research may contribute 
to develop a general frame for identifying quality dimensions that helps in constructing product 
specific quality measurement instruments. 
Keywords 
Product quality, wood, construction materials 
Introduction 
Product quality concept 
High product quality is increasingly a prerequisite for entry into global and even local 
markets. Still, quality clearly remains a significant method for differentiation in the mar-
ketplace. This may be especially true for wood-based industries, given the variability of 
the wood material. 
In European construction material markets, wood has been losing its position to other 
materials for decades. However, during the 1990s the use of wood has increased again, and 
significantly in some countries even though the total global consumption has not. In spite 
of the positive development in the consumption of wood in Europe, wood products are 
continuously under severe pressure from other construction materials. Successful attempts 
to improve the competitive situation of wood and enhancing its use require high or even 
superior quality in comparison with other materials. Overall, high product quality may 
still be regarded as an important cornerstone for a competitive advantage in the forest 
industries. 
It has been difficult to construct a measure of quality, and quality has thus seen nu-
merous definitions. Recent literature, however, recognises that the customer is the key to 
defining quality: A product can only be of high quality if the consumer feels it is so (Garvin, 
1984; Shetty, 1987; Takeuchi & Quelch, 1983). Hansen & Bush (1996, 1999) have studied 
the quality concept particularly in connection with sawn wood products. They suggest that 
matching the producer's internal definitions of quality to customer requirements is one of 
the biggest steps to gaining competitive advantage through quality. 
Recognising that product quality must be defined from the perceptions of the customer 
is a major step in defining the concept of total product quality. However, a simple definition 
of quality is still missing. From the customers' perspective quality is something they know 
when they see, but not something they can easily articulate a definition for. 
219 
F. Helles et al. ( eds.), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 219-226. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 220 ---

Recent research in the field of quality has tended to conceptualise quality as being 
composed of multiple dimensions (Brucks et al., 2000). Garvin (1984) developed a set of 
eight dimensions that he suggested to represent the concepts underlying product quality. 
His work has been used extensively in recent studies. For example, Sinclair et al. (1993) 
assessed product quality in wood furniture and found dimensions very similar to those of 
Garvin. Hansen & Bush (1996, 1999) followed this study and used Garvin's dimensions 
as well as service dimensions from Parasuraman et al. (1988) to create a model of total 
product quality. They reduced the quality dimensions first into five and then four (Table 
17.1). 
Garvin (1984) 
Hansen & Bush (1996, 1999) 
Sinclair eta/. (1993) 
8 
5/4 
7 
Performance 
Lumber performance 
Performance/features 
Features 
Lumber characteristics 
Reliability 
Reliability 
Supplier characteristics 
Conforma nee 
Conforma nee 
Supplier services 
Durability 
Durability 
Service/perceived quality 
Serviceability 
Aesthetics 
Aesthetics 
Economics 
Perceived quality 
Table 17.1: Product quality dimensions based on findings of several authors 
Some authors see quality management incorporating issues such as integrity, social re-
sponsibility and environmental protection (Madu & Kuei, 1995). From this perspective 
quality becomes a very holistic concept that incorporates basically every action of a com-
pany. With its connection to firm performance, quality is a matter that companies cannot 
ignore. 
It has been argued that some of the reasons for increasing favour of wood among Eu-
ropean consumers are the environmental and social characteristics of wood material and 
wooden products. From this point it is particularly interesting to evaluate how these char-
acteristics are integrated with the customers' view of quality, and how these characteristics 
relate to other key quality characteristics of wood. Because of the natural variability of 
the wood raw material, quality is potentially a larger challenge for forest industry than for 
many other industries. 
It has been suggested that achieving competitive advantage through quality requires an 
understanding of customer requirements, a method for measuring how well the company 
meets those quality requirements, and a strong commitment to do both (Hansen & Bush, 
1999). Unfortunately, the literature suggests that this situation often does not exist. Roper 
et al. (1997) found that firms nearly always gave themselves a higher quality rating than 
their customers. The authors concluded that what was most needed by the firms was 
more market research to identify the quality requirements of the customer. In a study of 
the Western North American sawn wood industry Weinfurter & Hansen (1999) also found 
significant gaps between producer and customer views of quality, even between lumber mills 
and their closest customers. 
Objectives 
In this study we attempt to assess the dimensionality of quality as viewed by German 
wholesalers and retail traders of wood and other construction materials. As a new aspect, 
we seek to determine if environmental characteristics can be seen as a product quality 
dimension. We explore first the quality dimensions that German organisational customers 
perceive for four construction materials: wood, plastics, steel and concrete, in order to 
check whether any uniform product quality dimensions are present. 
Then we analyse wood in more detail, and compare these dimensions qualitatively with 
findings of earlier studies on wood products, and the eight theoretical dimensions developed 
220

--- Page 221 ---

by Garvin. Finally, based on the findings, we provide insights into how wood product 
suppliers could better assess the quality of their products from a customer perspective and 
improve their product quality based on this information. The ultimate motivation is that 
this might be useful for firms to enhance their competitive position. 
The German market was chosen for empirical study because it is the largest construction 
material market in Europe and is generally considered to be environmentally sensitive. 
Measurement of Quality 
Quality is related to products and therefore defining quality requires a definition for the 
product. The product model defined for this study follows the specification of Toivonen 
(1995). She used a component model based on several authors' findings. This model 
separates tangible and intangible product components, and introduces environmental con-
siderations as a separate sub-component of the tangible product. The model also introduced 
product information as a sub-component of the intangible product component. 
In this study, the tangible product component is defined to include technical product 
properties, environmental (green) properties, and a visual component. The intangible com-
ponent includes product information, supplier characteristics and services. Intangible and 
tangible product components are related with each other. It is assumed that the dimen-
sions of product quality reflect the quality of various product components or possibly a 
combination of these. 
Measurement of quality was based on a set of 32 variables describing product char-
acteristics evaluated using a five-step Likert scale. The product characteristics included 
were derived from earlier studies analysing image and quality of wood and other building 
materials, wood in particular (e.g. Cohen & Kozak (1996)). The background literature, 
the construction of the questionnaire, and the questionnaire itself are described in detail in 
Jarvinen et al. (2001). 
Data and Analysis 
Over 2,600 companies traded construction materials in Germany in the year 2000. The 
size and the product palette of the companies vary a great deal. However, some tens of 
large company chains cover a major share of the value of the traded products, and several 
of these companies also operate outside Germany. The aim was to collect information 
from all the largest companies and to sample the smallest companies that are mostly wood 
products wholesale businesses. These are quite commonly organised into co-operatives 
that operate as an umbrella for the members. The co-ops may, for example, take care of 
the procurement of wood products. For this study, two large co-ops were contacted and 
their members surveyed. After identifying the population, a total of 95 retailer/wholesaler 
companies were included in the study target group. The company information was based 
on available lists of German DIY- and construction material companies, and member lists 
of wood traders' co-operatives. 
The final data includes 75 responses (79 % response rate). The data was collected 
through personal interviews (48 responses) by two interviewers, and a mail survey that 
resulted in 27 responses. The data collection took place between 29.5.2000 and 14.2.2001. 
A similar structured questionnaire was used both in the mail survey and in interviews. A 
total of 11 interviews took place in Austrian offices. The results of the mail survey did 
not differ markedly from the personal interviews when tested statistically. Clear differences 
were not detected between the interview data collected by different persons either. 
The data collection procedure: First, a cover letter was sent to each target company 
explaining the purpose of the study and asking for co-operation. Second, a telephone 
contact was made and the time for the interview was agreed or the mail survey questionnaire 
was sent. The questionnaire was sent beforehand to the persons to be interviewed if they 
221

--- Page 222 ---

so wished. Finally, a summary of the results was sent to respondents. Persons responsible 
for product purchasing were specifically targeted. 
The annual turnover of the 75 respondent companies covers about 75% of the to-
tal value of the construction material market in Germany (which was EUR 38.4 billion, 
2000). Respondents were classified into three groups: DrY-companies/Home Centres (37 
respondents), construction material retailers/wholesalers (18), and wood product whole-
salers/ retailers ( 20).

Table 17.2: The respondent companies/business-units 
The data was analysed performing statistical multivariate methods and using SPSS sta-
tistical software package. Principal axis factor analysis was used for studying dimensionality 
of quality of all four materials. Maximum likelihood method was also used in analysing the 
quality dimensions of wood in more detail. The results were very similar regardless of the 
method. 
The minimum factor loading of 0.4 was chosen as the criterion for including a variable 
into a factor solution, and the eigenvalue of 1.0 for accepting a factor into a solution. The 
minimum communality value of 0.2 was used as the criterion for including a variable into 
the analysis. Coefficient alpha was used for testing internal consistency of certain factors. 
Following Sinclair et al. (1993), 0.60 was used as a critical value. The "confirmatory" phase 
of the factor analysis remains very simple in this study. It includes only forcing a certain 
number of dimensions for each material, and testing the fit of the resulting factor structure. 
The factor solutions were tested using Chi-square test statistics. The number of factors 
was based on the findings in earlier studies on wood product characteristics and the eight 
theoretical dimensions by Garvin. The conclusions of whether the resulting dimensions 
could be regarded as similar remained qualitative. 
Results 
Quality dimensions of construction materials 
In the first phase of the study, all four materials, wood, plastics, concrete and steel, were 
analysed using the initial set of 32 variables and performing Principal axis factoring with 
Varimax-rotation. In the first factoring trials it became clear that the communality-criterion 
excludes three variables from the 32-variable set (modern, old-fashioned, non-flammable) 
for all or almost all materials. Therefore the analyses were carried out for each material, 
starting with an identical 29-item data set. 
The results showed that the four materials did not have exactly the same structure. 
Thus, the procedure was continued in an explorative way by searching for the best solution 
for each of the four materials. The results are shown in Tables 17.3-17.6. 
The resulting dimensions were quite logical, and the degree of explained variation was 
satisfactory regarding all materials. However, the results do not indicate totally similar 
quality dimensions in the cases of the four materials, even though similarities can be ob-
served. Most clearly, aesthetics (visual and style related characteristics) seem to form a 
separate dimension for each material. Visual characteristics are not related to other char-
acteristics that were assumed to reflect the tangible product quality, however. 
222

--- Page 223 ---

Characteristics related to the tangible product - technical properties, features and per-
formance - seem to organise as one or several dimensions depending on the material. This 
result does not clearly follow Garvin's theoretical model. The result is closer to the sug-
gestion by Hansen & Bush (1996, 1999) that the quality of the tangible product may be 
one dimension. Product related economic aspects, such as price, do not form one clear 
dimension either. Instead, the results show that economic considerations relate somewhat 
differently to the other product characteristics from material to material. This is not nec-
essarily against Garvin's assumption which was that price is a function of quality and thus 
it is not a separate quality dimension. 
Environmental characteristics are related to health aspects in the cases of steel and 
concrete, but not in the cases of wood or plastics. Thus, the question of a "green quality 
dimension" remained vague on the basis of this study. Instead, product information and 
information services also seem to form one dimension or two dimensions (contents of infor-
mation/availability of information) for all materials. However, the composition of variables 
within these dimensions also varies somewhat between the materials.

None of the solutions was immediately perfectly fitting for the data, but an eight factor 
solution provided the best degree of explained variation. This solution was used as an 
initial phase, and it was further condensed till it did not improve on the basis of explained

variation or the internal consistency of the dimensions, and all of the variables were loaded 
to a dimension. In the final phase, only variables with loadings of 0.5 or higher were 
included in the interpretation in order to get as clear dimensions as possible. 
Technically the best result was a 5-dimensional model including 23 variables (Table 
17.7). This result was also fairly content valid based on earlier studies. However, in the last 
phase of the process the two particularly interesting variables, namely environmental and 
health considerations, were excluded using the 0.5 minimum loading criterion. Since our 
interest was particularly on environmental issues and the loadings of both variables were 
over 4.0, environmental and health variables were kept in the chosen solution. This did not 
change the interpretation of other factors. 
All dimensions had sufficiently high internal consistency, and the Chi-square statistics 
(p 0.110) suggested that the solution was just acceptable. The five factors were named 
according to Garvin's quality dimensions: 
• 1. Factor 1: Perceived quality. This is the strongest factor with regard to the 
degree of explained variation 
• 2. Factor 2: Product information 
• 3. Factor 3: Technical quality of the tangible product (Garvin's "features" are 
concluded to include such matters as health considerations and easiness to care) 
• 4. Factor 4: Aesthetics (the factor however also includes matters reflecting certain 
style) 
• 5. Factor 5: Supplier (information) service and Economics (this factor might 
reflect more generally supplier characteristics and service, but the original data set 
only allows very narrow interpretation through information related to supplier and

Discussion and Managerial Implications 
Empirical results of German organisational customers' perceptions of four construction 
materials (wood, plastics, steel, concrete) are in accordance with the assumption of multi-
dimensionality of product quality. It also appears that eight to five product quality di-
mensions suggested by earlier studies fit construction materials quite sufficiently. Product 
quality dimensions are somewhat similar from material to material, but not totally uniform. 
Aesthetics is most clearly a separate dimension for all materials. Product information and 
perceived quality are quite clearly separable dimensions. Earlier studies did not identify 
product information as a separate dimension, but it seems feasible to assume that the signif-
icance of product information has increased and it is still increasing. Furthermore, findings 
of survey studies are always somewhat tied with the original variables, and in this study 
the information related variables were perhaps included more clearly than in the earlier 
ones. 
It seems possible to identify most of the quality dimensions that Garvin (1984) theoret-
ically suggested from the results of this study. Still, each of Garvin's dimensions does not 
form a separate dimension in the cases of the four construction materials. The technical 
product properties that relate to the tangible physical product (performance, features, con-
formance and durability) seem to form only one or two dimensions. This is in accordance 
with the conclusions by Hansen & Bush (1996, 1999). 
Economic, environmental, social and health considerations were not included in Garvin's 
original model. Overall, the results of this study provide vague implications regarding those 
potential "new" quality ingredients. Particularly regarding wood, the environmental con-
siderations did not seem to form a separate quality dimension. Despite this, the contribution 
of environmental and health considerations to quality of wood products deserve attention: 
Wood is clearly perceived environmentally friendly material (Jarvinen et al., 2001). This 
225

--- Page 226 ---

study points out several needs for further research. The degree of explained variation in 
the factor analyses varied between 55% and 61% from material to material. This suggests 
that product quality has dimensions that the original set of variables was not covering well 
enough. For example, supplier characteristics and aspects related to services would require 
more attention. Survey data may be somewhat sensitive to subjective interpretation, too. 
This or other studies have not indicated totally uniform product quality dimensions, 
and therefore, in real marketing situations it may be necessary to design product specific 
- perhaps even customer specific - quality assessment instruments. Still, similar lines can 
be revealed in the product quality dimensions observed in this study, and in those carried 
out earlier. This suggests a need for identifying major product quality dimensions at 
least for various product groups, such as wood products. This would help wood product 
producers to develop practical quality assessment instruments for their specific products. 
This study indicates that the quality of wooden products should be measured using the 
following guidelines: Technical properties, visual aspects, product information, and overall 
perceived quality reflected through general image and use value. Finally, supplier services 
and economic matters should be also considered.

Analysis of trade 
in non-timber forest products 
CARSTEN SMITH OLSEN AND THORSTEN TREUE 
Abstract 
Recent studies indicate a huge annual trade in medicinal plants from the Himalayas. Based 
on a national level survey of trade, this paper investigates trade in one of the most important 
commercial medicinal plant species from Nepal, Swertia chirayita (Roxb. ex Flem.) Karst. The 
survey was conducted along the marketing chain from the hills of Nepal to the plains of India 
and included standardised open-ended interviews with 58 harvesters, 56 local traders, 45 central 
wholesalers, 26 regional wholesalers, and 16 production companies. The annual supply of air-
dried unprocessed whole plants of 5. chirayita from Nepal, almost all harvested in the wild, is 
estimated at 300- 1,500 tonnes with an export value of USD 0.7- 3.5 million; trade in the case 
year 1997/98 is estimated at 849 tonnes valued at USD 2.0 million. The latter is estimated to 
provide almost 25,000 households with around 20% oftheir annual cash income. Nepal is found 
to be the main global supplier (74 ± 13%) with Bhutan (19 ± 10) and India (8 ± 7) being the 
only other supply countries. Total international trade in 1997/98 is estimated at 1000- 1,400 
tonnes with a CIF value of USD 2.3 - 3.2 million. The validity and reliability of findings are 
discussed and it is concluded that the trade figures are likely to represent conservative estimates. 
Keywords 
National survey, medicinal plants, markets, chiraito, Nepal 
Introduction 
The need to involve local people in forest management was increasingly recognised in the late 
1970s (FAO, 1978) and people-oriented forestry and sustainable development concepts have 
become widely accepted in the past 15 years (Arnold, 1992; Brundtland, 1987; Westoby, 
1989). These developments have attracted attention to non-timber forest products (NTFPs, 
also known as non-wood forest products, special forest products or minor forest products) 
and there has been a huge increase in the number of NTFP publications since the late 
1980s. The attention was fuelled by hope that NTFPs could simultaneously contribute 
to meet both conservation and development objectives (Olsen et al., 2001): Firstly, it 
was argued that NTFPs could contribute positively and significantly to improving rural 
livelihoods through subsistence uses, such as indigenous plant-based primary health care, 
and commercial uses, such as selling plant parts in demand as spices. Secondly, NTFPs 
were seen to be able to contribute to local, regional and national level economic growth, 
e.g. through the establishment of processing industries or export earnings. Thirdly, NTFPs 
were believed to be able to contribute to improved natural resources management, both in 
terms of conservation and preventing under-utilisation, e.g. as utilisation of NTFPs were 
argued to be ecologically less destructive than timber harvesting. The evidence of NTFP 
based development, however, has been mixed as there are both stories of success and failure 
(see Arnold & Perez (1996); Perez & Byron (1999)). 
In line with global development, there has been an increasing focus on NTFPs in the 
Himalayas since the early 1990s. Much of the attention has focused on a sub-set of NTFPs, 
the medicinal and aromatic plants. These can contribute to improve rural livelihoods in 
two ways (Olsen & Bhattarai, 2000): Firstly, through increased welfare as products are 
used to sustain or improve daily life, e.g. the use of medicinal plants in primary health care 
can serve to improve local health conditions where alternatives are not available or are too 
227 
F. He lies et al. ( eds. ), Recent Accomplishments in Applied Forest Economics Research, 227-239. 
© 2003 Kluwer Academic Publishers.

--- Page 228 ---

expensive. Secondly, by improving rural incomes as many medicinal and aromatic plant 
products can be sold through well-established marketing chains. This paper will only deal 
with commercial medicinal and aromatic plants. This group includes all traded medicinal 
a.nd ~trornatic plant products as well as related products, such as species used as spices, 
that IU'e traded along the same channels. 
The trade in plant products is huge across the Himalayas; each year tens of thousands of 
tonnes composed of hundreds of species and products are traded (Edwards, 1996a; Murty, 
1993). Recent studies have provided information on the medicinal plant trade from Pakistan 
in the west to Bhutan and Arunachal Pradesh in the east (e.g. Dobriyal et al. (1997); 
Fa,rooquee & Saxena (1996); Holley & Cherla (1998); Mulliken (2000); Nawang (1996); 
Olsen (1998); Rai et al. (2000)). Hundreds of products are gathered by rural harvesters in 
forests and other vegetation types in the mountains and subsequently traded along well-
established marketing channels to cities in the south. The main market is India; wholesalers 
based in India have suppliers throughout the sub-continent. They import products from all 
the Himalayan range states and distribute them to producers and retailers, mainly in India. 
Many authors argue that the demand for medicinal plant products is increasing both in 
the sub-continent and internationally (e.g. Herach-Martinez (1995); Kate & Laird (1999); 
Olsen (1998)). Most of the trade is in raw materials, such as barks, leaves, tubers, roots 
and fruits, though trade in semi-processed products, such as essential oils, also takes place. 
The annual value of trade is millions of USD; however, no studies have yet estimated the 
total value at the sub-continental or national levels. 
Nepal is the Himalayan country from which most information is available on medicinal 
plant trade; more than 100 studies have been conducted. Accordingly, Nepal was chosen as 
case country for the present study. In a review of the contribution of commercial medicinal 
plants to household income, Olsen & Bhattarai (2000) cautiously estimate the annual value 
of commercial medicinal plant collection to rural collectors at USD 11-35 million. This 
corresponds to an average income in collecting households of USD 28-90 hh-1 yr-1 equiv-
alent to 12- 41% of annual household cash income. The number of collecting households 
were roughly estimated at 312,000 - 470,000 representing 10-15% of rural households. A 
generalised model of the flow of medicinal plants from Nepal to India is provided in Figure 
18.1.

Existing trade related information on Swertia chirayita ( chiraito) 
Available trade studies (Table 18.1) indicate that the product known in Nepalese as "chi-
raito" is among the most important income generating plant products for rural households, 
especially in eastern Nepal. Bhattarai & Acharya (1998) found two traded chiraito prod-
ucts: (i) pothi chiraito consisting of more than 95% Swertia chirayita (Roxb. ex Fleming) 
Karsten (syn. S. chirata Buch.-Ham. ex C.B. Clarke), and (ii) bhale chiraito consisting of 
a mix of eight other Swertia spp. They recorded prices paid to collectors for pothi chiraito 
228

--- Page 229 ---

as four to five times higher than bhale chiraito and found that pothi chiraito made up ap-
proximately 80% of the traded chiraito volume. This study focuses on S. chirayita chiraito 
only. All trade is in the air-dried whole plant. According to CSIR (1985), the drug chiretta 
is obtained from the plant and useful as a tonic or mild febrifuge; it is widely used in Indian 
medical systems. Apparently, chiretta is also exported from India. However, there have 
been no systematic studies of end uses of chiraito or chiretta and thus factors influencing 
demand are unknown. An overview of existing trade information on S. chirayita is provided 
in Table 18.1; it does not include village level studies.

The studies are of varying quality and often the methodological descriptions are not 
detailed. However, the studies do indicate that EDR is the main supply region but that 
significant amounts of S. chirayita may also be traded from CDR and WDR. It is also clear 
that the officially registered amounts (Malia et al., 1995) are too low when compared with 
trader derived estimates. The studies also indicate that regionally focused studies, such as 
Burbage (1981), can not be used to derive valid national level estimates. The most reliable 
estimate of national level trade in chiraito is the study by Bhattarai & Acharya (1996, 
1998) specifically aimed at establishing such an estimate. However, their methodological 
description is unclear and their findings should be treated with caution, e.g. it appears 
that the study has not considered trade among wholesalers. 
229

--- Page 230 ---

According to Hara et al. (1982), S. chirayita is found in the Himalaya from Kashmir 
to Bhutan and Assam. However, according to Shrestha & Joshi (1996), the species is only 
found in central and eastern Nepal. This is contradicted by Bhattarai & Acharya (1998) 
who found that Dadeldhura District in Far-western Development Region (FDR) and Baitadi 
District in MDR were important sources of S. chirayita - thus it appears likely that the 
species is found throughout Nepal. The distribution of S. chirayita to vegetation types 
is generally not known; Olsen (1996) found, in Gorkha District in the WDR, the species 
in mid-montane forests only (Quercus spp., Quercus semecarpifolia and mixed hardwood 
forests). Using the definitions developed by Lillesp et al. (2001), S. chirayita is distributed 
in the upper half of the sub-tropical ecological zone and the lower half of the temperate 
ecological zone. 
The aim of the present paper is to contribute to the understanding of the South Asian 
medicinal plant market by analysing the trade in S. chirayita, in particular (i) providing 
a national level estimate of the trade in and from Nepal, (ii) estimating the importance 
of Himalayan range states as suppliers, and (iii) estimating the amount and value of the 
international trade in the species. 
Methods 
Data was collected through a country level investigation of the medicinal and aromatic 
plant trade in and from Nepal. Field work was conducted from August 1998 to September 
1999 and covered a set of 32 focal products identified on the basis of literature (Burbage, 
1981; Edwards, 1996a; Hertog, 1997; Karki et al., 1997; Malia et al., 1995; Olsen, 1997; 
Pradhan & Maharjan, 1994), including the air-dried whole plants of S. chirayita. Emphasis 
was on including the products and species that were traded in the largest quantities and/or 
had a high unit value. General data as well as data for the case year 1997/98 were collected. 
Field work consisted of five separate activities: 
• Activity one was a district survey covering 15 districts, one randomly selected in each 
of the 15 cells defined by the five development regions (east-west variation; precip-
itation) and the three main physiographic regions (north-south variation; altitude). 
For S. chirayita, 56 local traders and 58 harvesters involved in trade of the species 
were interviewed. For each district, all traders purchasing medicinal plants directly 
from harvesters were interviewed, including traders based outside the chosen districts. 
Harvesters, gathering medicinal plants in the chosen districts, were randomly met en 
route in each district. The standardised open-ended interviews included detailed 
questions on the trade, such as variations in volume and prices. 
• Activity two was a central wholesaler survey interviewing all 90 exporters of medicinal 
and aromatic plants to India in the case year of 1997/98 of which 45 were involved in 
export of S. chirayita. Central wholesalers were found in all five development regions. 
Data collected was similar to that of the local traders mentioned above. 
• Activity three was a regional wholesaler survey covering 53 wholesalers in India, of 
which 26 were involved in S. chirayita trade, located in the seven main cities importing 
medicinal plants from Nepal (Delhi, Lucknow, Kanpur, Kannauj, Siliguri, Tanakpur, 
and Calcutta). Besides collecting background and trade data, interviews also focused 
on investigating the origin of supplies. 
• Activity four was an investigation of export records to examine whether S. chirayita 
was exported to countries other than India and Tibet in the case year 1997/98. Copies 
of invoices, including details of products exported, amounts and prices, must, in 
principle, be submitted to the Department of Foreign Trade. These records were 
analysed. 
230

--- Page 231 ---

• Activity five was a survey of domestic Nepalese producers using medicinal and aro-
matic plants. A total of 16 production companies across the country were identified 
and interviewed. The focus was on obtaining an overview of the domestic demand for 
the focal species. 
Thus, assuming no stock piling or depletion in the chain of commerce, data allows a com-
parison of two estimates of the same trade: (i) the annual amount entering trade, derived 
from the district level survey, and (ii) the sum of the export to India and Tibet (derived 
from the central wholesaler and district level surveys), the domestic industrial demand, and 
the export to third countries. An overview of development regions, physiographic zones and 
selected districts is provided in Figure 2.

Data analysis 
The district level data (15 districts) was generalised to the national level (75 districts) by 
extrapolating the findings in each district to the other districts within the same cell, e.g. 
Dolakha District represents the three districts in the Central Development Region (CDR) 
High Mountains cell. The number of districts in each cell varies from 2 - 10. District 
level data is generalised to the national level by assuming that the vegetation quality and 
harvesting pressure is similar for the districts within each cell. Two methods were employed 
to generalise district findings. First, a simple extrapolation of findings based on the total 
area of each district. Second, by taking into consideration the size of the potential growing 
area of the species in each district and using an ecological zone adjustment factor as a proxy 
for the relative distribution of forest area between districts: 
(18.1) 
Where Qij is the estimated amount (kg) of product from species i (in this case S. chirayita) 
traded from district j , j = 1, 2, ... , 75; Pij is the estimated potential growing area of species 
i in district j; Pic is the estimated potential growing area of species i in case district c, 
c = 1, 2, . .. , 15, in the cell containing district j; ejc is the ecological zone adjustment factor 
calculated as the forest area in district j divided by the forest area in the case district c 
in the cell containing district j; Qic is the recorded amount (kg) of product from species i 
traded from the case district c. 
231

--- Page 232 ---

Estimating the potential growing area was done in three steps: (i) first the altitudi-
nal range of the species in Nepal was estimated based on the voucher specimens in the 
Kathmandu National Herbarium, Godovari and informal discussions with botanists, (ii) 
this range was related to the altitudinal distribution of ecological zones as described by 
Lilles!ZI et al. (2001), and (iii) the area of the relevant ecological zones were calculated for 
each district using the percentage distribution of ecological zones for all districts in Nepal 
provided by Lilles!ZI et al. (2001). In step one, the altitudinal range of S. chimyita. was 
estimated at 1500 - 2500 mas!. This estimate is identical to that for the entire distribution 
range (Hara et al., 1982) and for Nepal (Shrestha & Joshi, 1996). The work by Lilles!ZI et al. 
(2001), focusing on developing a potential vegetation map of Nepal, synthesises the last 
30 years' vegetation studies in Nepal and combines this with analysis of extensive climate 
data to arrive at the best available ecological classification of Nepal. In step two, using 
this system, the species occur in the sub-tropical (1000 - 2000 mas!) and temperate (2000 
-3000 mas!) ecological zones. In step three, the altitudinal range and distribution across 
ecological zones is combined with the district size to calculate the potential growing area of 
the species in the district; e.g. 39.4% of Baglung District is found in the temperate zone; 
this gives a potential growing area of (0.5·0.394·district size) in that ecological zone. 
The ecological zone adjustment factor is used as there may be differences in the actual 
size of the forest area between districts that have a similar potential growing area. Ideally, 
the factor should be calculated based on the actual area of the forest types in which the 
species occurs while taking into account the species' altitudinal range. However, this infor-
mation is not available. Instead, the total forest area in each district is used as a proxy; 
forest area figures are drawn from ISRSC (2001). 
The total supply from Nepal for species i is calculated as the sum of estimates from each 
of the 75 districts. Note that data collection allows Q;j to be calculated as the minimum 
amount traded in a bad year, the maximum amount traded in a good year, as well as the 
amount traded in the case year 1997/98. 
Results 
The findings on volume and value of trade in S. chimyita are presented in six parts: (i) 
export from Nepal, (ii) domestic processing in Nepal, (iii) annual volume entering trade 
in Nepal, (iv) origin of supplies across range countries, (v) estimation of all international 
trade, and (vi) importance to rural livelihoods. 
Export from Nepal 
Export to India 
Figures for export of unprocessed air-dried whole plants of S. chimyita from Nepal to India, 
based on interviews with central wholesalers, are presented in Table 18.2. There is a large 
variation in yearly amount and value of the export; the annual export ranges from 230 to 
790 tonnes. The export in the case year 1997/98 was 352 tonnes. The average value of the 
export, using the 1997/98 price level, is approximately USD 1.2 million, while the export 
value in the case year was below average at around USD 817,000. 
Export to Tibet 
As part of the central wholesaler and district surveys, the export to Tibet was investigated 
(Table 18.3). The direct trade with Tibet is far smaller than trade with India; only 12 
traders in the Eastern and Central Development Regions are involved. The selling price, 
including transport from Nepal, obtained by central wholesalers is 146.7±2.9 Nr/kg and 
comparable to that obtained in India (Table 18.2). The annual export is estimated at 20-

Export to other countries 
Data provided by the Department of Foreign Trade were analysed to obtain information on 
the products exported to countries other than India and China. In only two cases, trade in 
S. chimyita were reported: two samples of one kg each sold to Japan. This supports the 
finding below that there is no processing of chiraito in Nepal. Even if the main part of the 
trade is not reported to the Department, it appears that the value of processed exports is 
very limited. Many items reported to the Department are merely listed as "herbs" or are 
medicinal compounds with unknown contents; however, the export of all items related to 
herbs is very limited with a total value in the case year 1997/98 of USD 247,000, including 
spices, soaps, oils, teas, ayurvedic medicine, herbal pillows, morels, incense and lycopodium 
powder. 
Domestic processing in Nepal 
The survey of the domestic industries did not record any demand in Nepal for processing 
of S. chimyita whole plants. Processing industries are dominated by essential oil manufac-
turers that do not process this species. 
Annual volume entering trade in Nepal 
To check the export data obtained from the central wholesalers, district level data was 
collected on the annual amount of S. chirayita entering trade in Nepal. An overview is

to the high mountains physiographic zone of the Eastern Development Region, using the 
two calculation methods described earlier. Trade in S. chimyita was recorded from seven 
of the 15 investigated districts.

Table 18.4: Annual amount of unprocessed Swertia chimyita whole plants entering tmde in Nepal 
based on the district level survey. Only the seven cells where tmde in S. chimyita was recorded are 
included 
Table 18.4 confirms that EDRis the most important source area, supplying more than 
50% in 1997/98, followed by CDR and WDR. Most of the supplies originate from the high 
mountain physiographic zone, supplying around 80% in 1997/98, followed by the middle 
hills. The total annual traded amount of unprocessed whole plants is estimated from 300 -
1,500 tonnes with 750 - 850 tonnes being traded in 1997/98. 
The low and high estimates are comparable at cell, physiographic zone and development 
region levels except for the EDR middle hills (Udayapur) and WDR high mts. (Mustang) 
cells where calculation method 2 estimates are much higher than in calculation method 1. 
This reflects that the two districts representing these cells are not sufficiently representative 
for all other districts in their cell, e.g. the potential growing area of S. chimyita and the 
forest area in Mustang are very small while they are huge in Gorkha District, which also 
belongs to the WDR high mts cell. This indicates a need to adjust findings using other 
available case studies and developing a more refined sampling strategy. 
As expected, the results show that no S. chirayita is harvested in the Terai districts. 
However, it is surprising that no trade is recorded in the MDR; although traded amounts 
are small they have previously been recorded from this area (Malia et al., 1995; Bhattarai 
& Acharya, 1998; Rawal & Poudyal, 1999). This is due to the fact that the case districts 
(Humla and Surkhet) do not cover the distribution area of the species. Again, this indicates 
a need to adjust findings and to develop a more refined cell system (stratification of the 
supply area), e.g. by using the work of LillesiZl et al. (2001) which was not available during 
the design of this study. 
Origin of supplies across range countries 
During field work it was also attempted to estimate the importance of different countries 
as supply sources for chiraito. Only Nepal, Bhutan and India were mentioned by regional 
wholesalers as supply countries. Of the identified 26 regional chiraito trading wholesalers in 
India, 20 provided information on distribution of supplies (Table 18.5). Nepal is estimated 
to supply 74±13% and is clearly the main global supplier; all 20 wholesalers obtain at 
least 50% of their supplies from Nepal. Bhutan is the second largest source of supply, 
providing 19±10% followed by India supplying 8±7%. The variation between wholesalers, 
as expressed in the min-max ranges, reflects the geographical location of wholesalers, with 
the easternmost obtaining comparatively more from Bhutan and the westernmost obtaining 
comparatively more from India.

Estimate of all international trade in chiraito 
Using the information on origin of supplies in Table 18.5 and the Nepal national level 
trade data (calculation method 2) in Table 18.4 allow an estimation of the total amount of 
unprocessed air-dried whole plants of S. chirayita entering international trade (Table 18.6). 
The international trade in 1997/98 is estimated at 1,000 - 1,400 tonnes with a CIF value 
of USD 2.2 - 3.2 million.

Importance to rural livelihoods 
According to local traders (n=56), the average harvester price in 1997/98 was 71.7±16.5 
Nr/kg; according to harvesters (n=19 groups of harvesters) the average price was 63.7±15.6 
Nr/kg. The average amount sold per harvester (n=54) was 34.7±7.4 kg. The above har-
vester prices are in the same range and assuming an average price of 67 Nr/kg, the annual 
average income from sale of S. chirayita per harvester was Nr 2,325 (USD 34.2). Using 
calculation method 2, the 849,000 kg traded in 1997/98 were then harvested by approx-
imately 24,500 harvesters to whom the harvest had a value of USD 837,000. Given an 
average annual household cash income of small farm rural households in rural hills of Nr 
11,076 (NHDP 1998), and assuming one harvester per household, the harvest of S. chirayita 
provides almost 25,000 households with around 20% of their annual cash income. 
Discussion 
Comparing trade figures 
A comparison of the two trade estimates (the figure for annual amount entering trade 
derived from the district level survey and the sum of the figures for export and domestic 
demand) is provided in Table 18.7. The figures provided by exporters are lower than those 
derived from district level trader interviews and the latter is found to be the most accurate. 
This indicates an annual supply of chiraito from 300- 1,500 tonnes from Nepal, almost all of 
which is harvested in the wild. This is higher than previous estimates (except the national 
estimate by Burbage (1981) based only on field work in EDR). This emphasises the need to 
determine sustainable harvest levels of chiraito in Nepal- there is presently no information 
available on sustainable harvest levels. This information is important for assessing the 
future potential of wild chiraito as a source of income and to address conservation concerns. 
Given the consistent high price levels of chiraito in India (Edwards, 1996a; Olsen & Helles, 
1997; SION, 2002), there is also potential for increased integration of chiraito domestication 
235

--- Page 236 ---

and cultivation in agricultural systems and community forestry. So far, only a few studies 
have investigated this potential (Edwards & Maharjan, 1996; Khadka et al., 1994; Maharjan, 
1994).

Validity and reliability of findings 
Chiraito exporters, the central wholesalers, are relatively few but quite visible. Studies 
have indicated that much trade in medicinal plants takes place illegally (Edwards, 1996a; 
Hertog, 1997; Malia et al., 1995; Olsen, 1997): (i) officially registered amounts of medicinal 
plant harvest at the District Forest Offices are lower than estimates derived from trader 
interviews, and (ii) large amounts are transported across the Indian border illegally, e.g. 
because export of unprocessed products is not permitted or because the required papers 
are not in order. It appears that exporters can lower costs by jumping the time consuming 
official procedures for medicinal plant export (collection permit, transport permit, export 
permit, royalty payment, and custom duties); therefore, it is likely that they are understat-
ing the amounts traded. Local traders, on the other hand, are generally not involved in 
extensive transport and export of medicinal plants and would therefore have less incentive 
to underestimate the amounts traded. Harvest and export of S. chirayita in WDR illus-
trates this: the exporter interviews estimated the annual range of export at 5-26 tonnes 
(Table 18.2) while Olsen & Helles (1997), on the basis of local trader interviews, estimated 
the annual amount entering trade from Gorkha District in WDR at 25-57 tonnes. It appears 
that exporters have underestimated the annual amounts traded from WDR. The exporter 
derived estimates for S. chirayita trade thus appear too low, while the scenario 2 findings 
are more in line with existing case studies. 
The central wholesaler estimates may also be too low if not all wholesalers were located. 
However, as they are quite visible and easily located, this is not found to be a major 
problem. Estimates may also be too low if there is significant stockpiling or weight losses 
during storage. This appears unlikely due to the high prices for S. chirayita which should 
result in low average storage time. Olsen & Helles (1997) estimate average weight losses 
for chiraito stored by central wholesalers at 5%. 
As there is no authorised list of local traders, it may be that not all of them were located 
and interviewed during the district survey - accordingly, the data set for the generalisation 
scenarios should be considered conservative. Not all the selected case districts are represen-
tative of all other districts in the same cell, which excludes some districts where trade in S. 
chirayita is known to take place. The opposite does not appear to be problematic: available 
case studies (Table 18.1) do not indicate that trade in case districts are extrapolated to 
districts with no trade or with different trading patterns. 
In calculation 2, it is assumed that vegetation quality and harvesting pressure is similar 
across districts. Clearly, this is not always the case. For instance, the income earning 
opportunities from tourism in Mustang and Manang Districts would probably lead to less 
harvesting pressure than in similar vegetation types in upper Gorkha District where there 
are fewer income options. Unfortunately, as the distribution of S. chirayita to vegetation 
types is not known and as there are few studies of village level utilisation and management 
of medicinal plants, it is not possible to assess whether the assumption generally holds true 
or not. Furthermore, in calculation 2, the ecological zone adjustment factor is calculated 
on the basis of the total forest area in each district rather than the area of forest types 
in which S. chirayita occurs. If deforestation patterns are similar across districts in the 
236

--- Page 237 ---

same cell, this is not problematic. Available information on deforestation in the hills and 
mountains of Nepal (Gilmour, 1988; Soussanj et al., 1995) do not suggest that deforestation 
patterns generally vary across neighbouring districts. 
All harvesters interviewed stated that their harvest of S. chirayita was undertaken in 
the wild in government forest. None had domesticated and/or cultivated chiraito. However, 
it appears that middle altitude, high value medicinal and aromatic plants are increasingly 
integrated into agricultural production systems (Hertog, 1997; Khadka et al., 1994; Mahar-
jan, 1994; Olsen, 1997) that may become an important source of chiraito in the future if 
prices are maintained. 
Conclusion 
There is a large international trade in air-dried S. chirayita whole plants harvested in the 
wild. Most of the plants originate from Nepal where the sale generates substantial producer 
benefits that are likely to be important in rural livelihood strategies. The national trade 
survey provides an approach to investigating trade in medicinal plants that may be used 
at state level in other parts of the Himalayas to derive basic trade information. Even if 
the approach is likely to produce conservative trade estimates, it can form the basis for 
collecting data that are comparable across cases and boundaries. If, as in the case of for S. 
chirayita, it is found that trade in other medicinal plant species and products is larger and 
more valuable than previously estimated, these may form an important avenue to increase 
rural incomes in the Himalayas. However, before medicinal plants can be used in longer 
term larger scale attacks on poverty in the Himalayan region, it is important to establish 
sustainable harvest rates and to ensure that national policies, legislation and administration 
support such initiatives. It should also be kept in mind that trade in Himalayan biodiversity 
probably results in substantial consumer benefits, e.g. as consumers in South Asian cities 
gain access to Ayurvedic medicinal products, and some degree of labour absorption, e.g. 
through processing industries and retail outlets. 
Acknowledgement 
All the wholesalers, traders and harvesters who participated in the study are thanked for their time and 
helpfulness. Nirmal K. Bhattarai was instrumental in data collection and Malene Hall is thanked for her 
contributions to data handling. Helie 0. Larsen is thanked for useful comments on the draft manuscript. 
The study was funded by the Council for Development Research, Ministry of Foreign Affairs, Copenhagen.
